Thus, it breaks through the primordial chaos, and the infant is born.

The root of nature in Prior Heaven is *Qian*, which is Heaven; that which originates from Heaven ascends, hence it floats to the crown. The *qi* of Prior Heaven within Posterior Heaven is life, and the stem of life is *Kun*, which is Earth; that which originates from Earth descends, thus it sinks and settles into the Gate of Life. The Great Ultimate transforms into *Qian* and *Kun*. The nature that floats to the crown is “existence within non-existence”; the life that sinks into the Gate of Life is “non-existence within existence.” The Gate of Life is also called the “Secret Portal” or the “Northern Culmen Abyss.” Once nature and life are established and inner and outer communicate, the infant can eat, speak, move, see, and hear.

The above describes the principles governing the fetus’s birth and its stages as infant, baby, young child, and youth. Upon the breaking of the body, the situation shifts from Prior Heaven to Posterior Heaven: *Li* and *Kan* replace *Qian* and *Kun*, occupying south and north, while *Qian* and *Kun* are arrayed east and west. The four trigrams form a surrounding enclosure, and the central Spirit Chamber lies empty and at rest. Only the intermediate *yin* assumes its proper position and function, but with the opposition of root and dust, knowledge unfolds daily, and the intermediate *yin* transforms into the consciousness-spirit, increasingly harming nature and life.

When the body breaks, true *yin* ascends and true yang descends; nature turns into emotion, and emotion turns into nature. The *qi* of Prior Heaven—its material form, desires, and appetites—combines with the *qi* of material form, desires, and appetites carried by the intermediate *yin* through countless kalpas. Thus, the heart grows ever more restless, and worldly desires multiply. Once generative essence arises, the child’s body naturally breaks—this need not await the union of male and female, for the breaking of the body is the natural work of Heaven and Earth. On the day when “five thousand four hundred births complete the Yellow Path,” pure yang and pure *yin* reach their fullness. After this day, the body breaks of itself: in males, generative essence is produced; in females, the water of celestial menses appears. One white, one red—precisely one *Kan* and one *Li*, the image of external leakage.

At the inception of the Yellow Path, the spirit is radiant, the countenance luminous. In males, a warm *qi* often courses through the spine; in females, a cool fluid often flows beneath the breasts. Those who attend to bodily experience will know this. If one cultivates at this stage, what flows is original *qi* and original essence. Simply concentrate the spirit in the *qi* cavity, and it will generate endlessly. Within non-action, the fire of the microcosmic orbit circulates naturally. Empty the heart, and the abdomen will fill of itself; illuminate the heart, and nature will manifest of itself.

Without contrivance or effort, the elixir forms in the realm of unknowing and unawareness—most simple, most easy. Yet once the body is broken, true yang has already dispersed; without the work of accumulation, one cannot return to youth and lay the foundation for cultivation.

Here follows the principle of the broken body: On the five thousand four hundredth day of the Yellow Path’s generation, yin and yang conjoin and the body is broken. At that time, the original *qi* of the Gate of Life (the life within *Kun*) surges upward along the spine to meet the ancestral *qi* at the crown (the single yin within *Li*). The ancestral *qi* in the crown descends through the Crimson Palace straight into the Gate of Life, where it merges with the original *qi* of the Gate. This is *Kun* yin ascending into *Qian*, *Qian* yang sinking into *Kun*—yin and yang inverted, *Qian* hollowed into *Li*, *Kun* solidified into *Kan*. The original *qi* once present in the Gate of Life is pressed downward into the space between the Gate and the yin heel vessel, where it borders the void outside the body, as if external—hence the alchemical classics speak of it as “fathomlessly deep.”

Once heaven and earth have conjoined, the yin of *Li* becomes hollow and restless, ever drifting outward. A single spark of original emotion courses through the central palace’s spirit chamber, stirring the acquired emotions and perceptions. These rise to the crown, where they merge with the nature within *Li*, arousing desire. Descending again to the Gate of Life, they generate tangible essence. Nature and emotion blend, the original spirit transforms into the acquired spirit, and the postcelestial entirely takes charge under the rule of the acquired spirit.

The domain of the original spirit is occupied by the acquired spirit, squeezed out from the lesser void to dwell between inner and outer. Its condition mirrors that of the life within the original *qi*—pressed outward, as if external—thus the Daoist texts often speak of “seeking without.” This is why Sun Wukong in *Journey to the West* is called “External Grandfather.”

When lust and desire flourish and the six senses rage unchecked, the original spirit becomes as if nonexistent—like the sun obscured by clouds, where only clouds are seen and not the sun. In unbroken children, the original spirit still resides: though the acquired spirit governs their actions, in moments of stillness, the unknowing original spirit remains whole within, a pure innocence ever manifest. But once the original spirit is consumed by the acquired spirit, the world becomes one of the acquired spirit alone. For those with deep-rooted vitality, the original spirit may yet persist at times. If one carries karmic roots from the intermediate state, and if one cultivated the Dao in past lives, those root-nature remain, and the original spirit may endure to some degree. But if what one brings are only karmic obstructions, the original spirit is entirely absent, leaving only karmic consciousness.

Those with wisdom roots possess the original spirit; those without are wholly acquired spirit—such is the principle. There is also a special case: Confucius said, “Only the highest wisdom and the lowest stupidity cannot be changed.” The highest wisdom refers to those with wisdom roots, as described above. As for the lowest stupidity, not only do they lack wisdom roots, but they also lack intelligence and perception; their acquired spirit is dull, and the original spirit cannot persist even to a small degree—hence they “cannot be changed.”

Ordinary people of middling capacity rely entirely on the acquired spirit for governance. Confucius said, “By nature, people are close; by habit, they grow apart.” Habit and nature become one, deeply ingrained. All day, they are entangled in the seven—joy, anger, sorrow, fear, love, hate, desire—and submerged in the six—form, sound, scent, taste, touch, and dharmas—adrift in the cycle of birth and death until the original spirit is wholly eroded. As Laozi said, “Of ten, three are companions of life; of ten, three are companions of death”—this is what he meant.

The eight trigrams of the child’s body are *Qian* south and *Kun* north, *Li* east and *Kan* west. At the time of the Yellow Path’s generation, the eight trigrams become *Li* south and *Kan* north, *Qian* east and *Kun* west. Now, the eastern and western *Qian* and *Kun* transform into *Zhen* and *Dui*. *Zhen* is the eldest son, *Dui* the youngest daughter, replacing their father and mother. The father *Qian* and mother *Kun* retreat into the void of the southwest and northwest, no longer active. Thus, the alchemical classics speak of *Zhen* and *Dui* rather than *Qian* and *Kun*.

After the body is broken, *Li* and *Kan* occupy the upper and lower positions, while *Zhen* and *Dui* stand at the eastern and western gates. Thus, water and fire are unregulated, metal and wood are estranged; what flames upward grows ever more fierce, what flows downward sinks ever lower. Spirit leaks above, and essence and *qi* follow; essence leaks below, and spirit and *qi* follow; *qi* leaks in the center, and essence and spirit follow. What was received from the prenatal is ceaselessly lost, while the postcelestial desires of the world incessantly obscure. With each fraction of yang consumed, yin grows by a fraction. By the time a woman reaches forty-nine (seven sevens) and a man sixty-four (eight eights), true yang is exhausted. If one harms it excessively, it may be depleted before this time; if one preserves it well, it may endure beyond—all depends on the sway of worldly desires.

The rustic folk of the countryside often enjoy their full span of years, while those of wealth and nobility frequently suffer from illness—this is the reason. When the human body resides in the womb, it is called the prenatal; before receiving *qi*, it is the prenatal of the prenatal. Upon birth, it becomes the postnatal; after the body is broken open, it becomes the postnatal of the postnatal, also known as the body of postnatal turbid yin.

The prenatal exists before heaven and earth, and is the Supreme Ultimate. The prenatal of the prenatal is the Non-Ultimate. The Non-Ultimate is the extremity of nothingness, the utmost of emptiness. Before a person’s parents unite, there is not the slightest sign of existence—this is the Non-Ultimate. After death, when the form is extinguished and the nature returns to nothingness, it is again the Non-Ultimate. To know death is to know life; if one does not know life, how can one know death? Before birth and after death, all is one emptiness. In between, there is form and appearance, color and substance—merely a few decades. The Buddhist scriptures say, *"What has form must ultimately decay; what is formless is true"*—this means that the body perishes in mere decades, while what is neither born nor destroyed is only the nature of the primordial ancestral *qi*.

But why does this nature not perish? Because its original essence is empty nothingness. Though it moves and awakens, in stillness it is as if never born. Speaking solely of nature, true emptiness is its substance, wondrous existence its function; wondrous existence is numinous awareness. This awareness arises from sensation and moves with *qi*; separated from *qi*, it returns to emptiness, and without sensation, it remains forever void. Precisely because it is utterly nothing, it does not arise or perish—hence the saying, *"Heaven and earth may decay, but this does not."*

How could emptiness ever decay? Thus, it transcends eternal kalpas. Cultivating and nurturing it serves to preserve it; to preserve it is to preserve one’s heavenly mandate. The heavenly mandate is heaven’s numinous radiance, which, at the moment of awareness, enters the mother’s womb. Upon entering the womb, it reverts to the body of true emptiness. From this, form is completed and birth occurs. At every moment, when emptiness and numinosity respond, awareness of the ten thousand existences and thousand realities arises—all things in the three realms can be known, yet none can know it. Later, it is obscured by the intermediate existence and usurped by emotional consciousness.

Though its numinous radiance is dimmed, in moments of extreme emptiness and profound stillness, nothing remains hidden. After death, it returns to its original true emptiness, dwelling motionless within heaven and earth. The Confucian teaching of *"preserving what is received in full and returning it in full"* means precisely preserving the numinosity received at the beginning. To preserve this numinosity, one must undergo a process of refinement, allowing the *qi* of wondrous existence to merge within. Then, in life, one is empty and numinous; in death, one remains empty and numinous—furthermore, expanding its capacity to boundlessness. When released, it pervades the six directions; when concealed, it retreats into profound obscurity—this is preserving what was received and returning it in full.

The Song Confucians, not comprehending the principles of the *Yijing*, believed that fulfilling human affairs constituted *"preserving what is received in full and returning it in full."* They did not realize that emptiness without existence is mere dead emptiness, and voidness without numinosity is not true void. The mandate received at the beginning has already been lost imperceptibly—how can one speak of preserving it in full?

Moreover, the true yang of the prenatal is the principle-*qi* of the Supreme Ultimate, the source from which the ten thousand things are born. Utterly nonbeing yet containing utter being, utterly empty yet truly numinous—sages’ innate knowledge and numinous awareness arise solely from nurturing this *qi*. Though it resides within the human body, it cannot always respond because people cannot remain constantly still; it cannot always be numinous because people cannot remain constantly empty.

The poem of the True Man of Great Mystery says:
*From parents’ birth, a single numinous spark,*
*Yet numinous no more once form takes shape.*
*Form obscures the body of radiant light—*
*Let go, and clarity returns in full.*

The poem of Chan Master Zhi Jue says:
*Bodhisattvas never leave the true,*
*Yet from the start, delusion estranges them.*
*If one can sit in stillness, turning light within,*
*One sees the former master of one’s prior life.*

The poem of Chan Master Miao Xu says:
*Ever wakeful, the master within,*
*Abiding motionless in the numinous palace.*
*If nothing obstructs this inner realm,*
*The natural body is itself empty void.*

The poem of Scholar Zhang Zhuo says:
*Radiant light, still illumination, pervades the Ganges’ sands—*
*Ordinary and sacred, originally one family.*
*When not a single thought arises, the whole body appears;*
*The moment the six senses stir, clouds obscure it.*

For the primordial ancestral *qi* is nature in humans. Once the fetus is formed, it mingles with the *qi*-nature and material nature of the parents, as well as the karmic desires of the intermediate existence, obscuring its clarity and filling its emptiness—thus its numinous radiance is lost. As the *Cantong Qi* says, *"The One is obscured, and the world does not know it."* Yet when attached to form, its emptiness and numinosity are obscured—why then, when speaking of death returning to emptiness and awareness dissolving, is this so?

It is said: The former speaks from the perspective of the self, the latter from the perspective of the other. The self is formed by this *qi*, which is the prenatal master of the self. When the intermediate existence usurps the host as a guest, the original nature’s numinosity is obscured, yet it still resonates with the true emptiness beyond form. Only the prenatal primordial *qi* within the postnatal remains to cling to it, so it does not immediately depart from the body. When ordinary people die, their nature returns to emptiness, reverting to the Supreme Ultimate of heaven and earth. Though it is neither born nor destroyed, for one’s lifetime of awareness and the sufferings of beings in the three realms, it is as if unrelated—like awakening from a great dream, where the former illusions and bubbles are no different from the emptiness before birth.

From the perspective of the self as the subject, there is truly no numinosity or awareness; from the perspective of the other as the subject, it still gestates the ten thousand things.

In reality, the "I" has already perished, yet emptiness remains emptiness—was the primordial *qi* I first received not already dispersed, reduced to nothingness? How then can it be called "wholly returned"? Only by preserving the true self, dissolving the illusory form of the corporeal body while ensuring the formless true self endures, does one become a great person of luminous virtue, or the sage who transcends through transformation, or the spirit beyond comprehension—this is why the Three Teachings emphasize the cultivation of the body.

The Way of cultivating the body begins by drawing that which was the master back to its rightful place, while the "I" retreats to the position of guest. Only then can the "I" be transformed into emptiness and merge with it, so that the "I" that was not numinous becomes numinous, and the "I" that was not empty becomes empty. Abiding in constant stillness yet ceaselessly responsive, like the Most High traversing seven kalpas of primordial chaos to repeatedly expound the Dao, or like Śākyamuni pervading the ten directions and three realms to eternally ferry sentient beings.

The scriptures’ discourse on "that" and "I" arises in three contexts: first, from the standpoint of duality; second, because emptiness is difficult to describe, and so the term "that" is used as a substitute; third, because the "I" is the master of the body, while emptiness is the master of the "I"—both being masters, hence the terms "that" and "I" generally do not exceed these three meanings. Later generations, misunderstanding a single "that," have spawned countless transgressions—what could be more lamentable?

Moreover, Daoist texts sometimes refer to "that household," which carries the meaning of "the other shore," as in the *Journey to the West* verse: *"Prajñā-pāramitā arrives at that household."* Ancient sages, recognizing that people misconstrued the word "that," appended the character "household" to clarify. "Household" here aligns with the *Awakening to Reality* line, *"This supreme treasure is possessed by every household,"* and Zhang Sanfeng’s poem, *"Every household has, every household has."* It is said "every household has" rather than "every person has" because emptiness is humanity’s true home. The colored body is form, a substantial entity; the original nature is formless, emptiness itself. The colored body is nurtured within emptiness, and without emptiness, it cannot live—thus, humanity takes emptiness as its home and the numinous as its sovereign.

The union of emptiness and the numinous is called the "Valley Spirit." "Valley" denotes the hollow cavity, while "Spirit" signifies *"that which is wondrous beyond all things."* The scriptures state: *"Heaven and earth are one great valley; caves are a middle valley; orifices are a small valley."* My master, Perfected Wang, said: *"Extending this to the body, the Valley Spirit governs all: the ears cannot hear without emptiness, the eyes cannot see without emptiness, the nose cannot smell without emptiness, the tongue cannot speak without emptiness, the hands cannot grasp without emptiness, the feet cannot walk without emptiness, and the body and mind cannot be at ease without emptiness—otherwise, illness arises. Moreover, if the mouth and nose cannot breathe, death follows."*

I further extend this reasoning: clothing cannot be worn without hollowness; a dwelling cannot be inhabited without hollowness; the mouth and stomach cannot ingest food and drink without hollowness. All things in the myriad realms occupy space, and within their forms, orifices arise to connect with emptiness, embodying the principle of the *"Three Thieves"* in the *Yin Fu Jing*. Only by emptiness communicating with emptiness can there be numinosity and movement. Without the Valley Spirit’s governance, there can be no emptiness; without emptiness, there can be no numinous communion, and the mechanism of transformation ceases.

The scriptures say: *"The Dao is simply emptiness."* Ancestor Zhang Sanfeng declared: *"Observe me: breath naturally gathers and closes, naturally lifts and raises. Before father and mother were born, that single point of true yang moved and stilled, cultivating roots and nurturing the stem within emptiness, establishing life and securing its foundation within emptiness—this is the primordial tone we speak of, the luminous principle."* He Yangzi said: *"My body came from emptiness; my body should return to emptiness."*

Within emptiness, departure and arrival unfold;
Within emptiness, the tree of karma is sown.
Once the karma-tree’s roots strike deep,
Its branches and boughs choke the paths of coming and going.

Yingchanzi says:
To become immortal, Buddha, or sage—
The three teachings transmit but one emptiness.
From ancient times to now, those who transcend
All cultivate their work within emptiness.
To study immortality: emptiness and stillness are the elixir’s core.
To study Buddhism: abiding in emptiness completes the task.
If you ask how to study the sagely way,
Within emptiness, self dissolves to reveal Heaven’s principle.

The Dao-body’s emptiness is wondrous beyond measure;
Heaven and Earth operate in emptiness, qi harmoniously fused.
Yin and Yang’s transformations surge through emptiness;
Should one abide in emptiness, all becomes malleable.

Master Wei Kuan says:
I urge you to study the Dao, seek not greedily—
With no-mind, all things align with the Dao’s head.
Only no-mind can embody the no-mind Dao;
Embodying no-mind, even the Dao rests.

The *Xingming Guizhi* states:
At the moment parents’ thoughts unite in union,
Round and luminous, radiant and bright,
A single point of primordial luminous light strikes the mother’s womb—
Simply thus: ○.
The Confucians call it “benevolence,” also “Wuji”;
The Buddhists call it “the pearl,” also “luminous clarity”;
The Daoists call it “the elixir,” also “spirit-light.”
All refer to this primordial qi, the primordial essence of chaos—
The true source of embodied life,
The inception of received qi,
The foundation of nature and destiny,
The ancestor of all transformations.

When parents’ union concludes,
Essence and blood envelop it externally—
Thus: ●.
This is the Supreme Ultimate.
According to this diagram, emptiness and fullness still divide into five layers,
As in the diagram I previously explained.
Fearing the full revelation of Heaven’s mechanism,
I speak only of three, concealing two.
Pondering the diagram’s meaning, one should readily awaken.

"Benevolence" (*ren*) means "two persons"; "two persons" signifies the blending of yin and yang—its meaning identical to the previously discussed "luminosity," "elixir," and "ease." The union of yin and yang is the empty body of the primordial Supreme Ultimate. When one attains this emptiness, it is quiescent as if absent, yet active as if present; thus, the fruition of the heart is also called *ren*. Two segments conjoined are yin and yang, while at their center, a subtle hollow point appears—this is the Supreme Ultimate. Human conception follows the same pattern.

When the intermediate yin enters and takes control, stillness turns to motion, and the consciousness of the intermediate yin seizes the handle of the Valley Spirit. Buddhists also call the intermediate yin the "nature of arising and ceasing," which stands in contrast to the unarising, unceasing nature of emptiness. Thus, it becomes the master of the postnatal form-body, and only through it does the form-body begin to move—yet once the form-body moves, the Valley Spirit dies.

Those who cultivate the Dao must let the heart die and the spirit live, thereby communing with Heaven and Earth and becoming indestructible as vajra. The postnatal form-body differs entirely from the prenatal Dharma-body. To preserve the prenatal, one must serve the prenatal during the postnatal. Serving the prenatal means grasping the mechanism of movement and stillness.

The *Book of Changes* states: *"Serve Heaven’s timing in the postnatal."* It also says: *"To know the subtle is to be divine!"* And further: *"The subtle is the incipient movement, the forerunner of good and ill fortune."* (See *Xici* II.)

The Valley Spirit is empty and quiescent, while the consciousness is floating and active. When consciousness dominates, the Valley Spirit vanishes. If one extinguishes consciousness, within that emptiness and stillness, the radiant, unobstructed illumination is none other than the Valley Spirit. Yet from birth onward, people are forever driven by worldly desires, causing the primordial spirit to lose its numinosity and never recover.

The Great Dao of the prenatal is simply the restoration of this primordial emptiness. Emptiness enables spiritual communion, and spiritual communion allows transformations to follow the heart—thus, Heaven and humanity unite in manifestation.

The postnatal form-body is but a skin-bag, destined for eventual destruction. Once the body is broken, Zhen and Dui occupy the positions of Qian and Kun. Qian’s true yang and Kun’s true yin retreat into disuse, deeply concealed and invisible. As the *Canticle* says: *"The primordial essence is too subtle to behold; the formed is easy to measure, while the unmanifest is difficult to contemplate."*

Zhen consists of two yin and one yang; Dui, two yang and one yin. The three yin and three yang are jumbled and impure, hence not true yin and yang—yet true yin and yang must emerge from within them.

Buddhists speak of "inner nurturance," while Daoists speak of "outer refinement." Inner nurturance means nurturing this quiescent extinction; outer refinement means refining this emptiness—both are one and the same. The Dao says: *"It is not in the colored body, yet not apart from the colored body."* Buddhism says: *"Neither inside nor outside, form is emptiness, emptiness is form"*—again, one and the same.

Since the postnatal form-body has lost its ancestral nature and lacks the prenatal, one must serve Heaven’s timing to seek the path of return. The method lies first in seeking primordial chaos to restore the undifferentiated Supreme Ultimate, then advancing from chaos to emptiness to restore the true emptiness of the Supreme Ultimate. Only then does wondrous existence arise from true emptiness, returning one to the original face before parents’ birth.

Terms like *"gathering the Five Agents,"* *"harmonizing the Four Images,"* *"the three families meeting,"* and *"converging the three into one"* all refer to the restoration of the undifferentiated Supreme Ultimate. Hence the discourse on the *"cauldron."*

The cauldron is the heart, which is Qian; the vessel is the body, which is Kun. Qian is *"having within non-being"*; Kun is *"non-being within having."* Between Qian and Kun lies the *"empty center."* The empty center is neither being nor non-being, yet both being and non-being—this is true intention.

Body, heart, and intention are humanity’s three families, yet they are not the physical body, heart, or intention. Only those whose postnatal bodies are broken, at the initial stage of practice, must temporarily rely on the colored body as a framework, employing only the postnatal body, heart, and intention.

One must forget the form to reveal its function; extreme emptiness and profound stillness are the practice. Thus, the *Canticle* says: *"What is natural is not a deviant or false Dao."* The *Daodejing* states: *"Heaven follows the Dao, the Dao follows spontaneity."*

In cultivation, body and heart are the substance, spirit and qi the function; intention is the substance, true intention the function. The body is the yang qi of the Gate of Life, located between the Gate of Life and the yin heel vessel—hence it is called *"body"* rather than *"Gate of Life."*

The *"heart"* is not the fleshy heart but the numinous, unwavering primordial spirit, dwelling neither inside nor outside, its essence originally empty. Kan is the qi-body, with Kan’s single yang (the true qi within the body); Li is the spirit-heart, with Li’s single yin (the primordial spirit within the heart). When they govern within the body, both become consciousness. Why not call it *"consciousness"* but *"heart"*? Because when emotional consciousness is extinguished, the Heavenly Heart appears, and the primordial spirit is restored—hence it is still called *"heart."*

Transforming being into non-being is the Dao-heart; failing to sever worldly passions is the human heart—both are metaphorical, not the physical body or heart.

Intention is the two earths, Wu and Ji. When the two earths merge, they form the true intention of the central palace, the third entity born of the union of spirit and qi. In a state of undifferentiated chaos, neither intending nor not intending, heart and breath interdepend, spirit and qi unite, no thought arises, no intention scatters. When harmony and congealment occur, the two earths form a jade tablet, and true intention appears.

The Buddhist school refers to it as *"lucid solitary radiance,"* symbolizing its state of non-being. The *Cantong Qi* speaks of *"sitting with warmth descending,"* while Ancestor Lü describes *"the gentle warmth of the lead cauldron, light piercing the curtains"*—these symbolize its state of being. Both instances of *"warmth"* arise from observed phenomena. When spirit and qi merge and lead has entered the cauldron, within the empty aperture there appears a wisp of misty qi, a subtle warmth faintly perceptible. When light pierces the curtains, whether the eyes are closed or open, illumination enters the eyelids. Yet this only occurs upon reaching the state of *"sitting with warmth descending"*—all these are tangible manifestations of undispersed true intention.

The *Daodejing* states:
*"Vague! Elusive! Yet within it there are images.
Elusive! Vague! Yet within it there are entities.
Dim! Obscure! Yet within it there is essence.
Its essence is utterly real—within it there is sincerity."*
The images, entities, and essence here all refer to the wondrous being within true emptiness—all are phenomena arising after the cessation of thought. These may be contemplated in moments of extreme emptiness and profound stillness. Yet these few lines encompass myriad phenomena, which will be elaborated later.

**Lecture Four: Internal Nurturance of Emptiness Externally Generates True Yang**
*"Though it governs as master, its pairing is perfect—*
*what the common folk use daily yet remain unaware of"*
refers precisely to this spirit and qi.
Spirit governs as master, qi enables movement.
Empty yet numinous, hence called *spirit*; existing as if non-existent, hence called *qi*.
When flowing outward, the six roots become the portals through which spirit and qi enter and exit. Through these portals, one discerns the myriad forms and colors, and the consciousness-spirit takes charge—thus spirit and qi are harmed.
The consciousness-spirit is the master of the six consciousnesses. The eyes, ears, nose, tongue, body, and intent are the six roots; form, sound, scent, taste, touch, and dharmas are the six dusts. When roots and dusts meet, the six consciousnesses arise. The six consciousnesses are in truth one consciousness, with spirit concealed in the root of intent. If this root of intent is extinguished, then as Mañjuśrī said, *"When one root returns to its source, the six roots attain liberation."*
The *"world"* in Avalokiteśvara’s name refers to this world of consciousness. When this world of consciousness is dissolved, the six roots naturally become pure, and the consciousness-spirit reverts to primordial spirit. Primordial spirit is the Valley Spirit, and the Valley Spirit is fundamentally empty—thus it does not die and endures eternally.
*"Returning to the source"* means withdrawing vision and reversing hearing. The *Cantong Qi* states:
*"The three treasures—ears, eyes, and mouth—*
*seal them tightly, let nothing pass.*
*Silence seals the trigram Dui,*
*rare words align with primordial chaos."*
This is the intent of sealing the six roots. When heart and intent are emptied, one naturally returns to primordial spirit and does not die.
The Buddhist scriptures say: *"With a single thought turned back, one instantly regains the original attainment."* *"Turned back"* means mouth-to-mouth alignment—great emptiness with small emptiness. If one recognizes this mechanism and turns back a single thought, one becomes identical to one’s original attainment. It also says: *"Directly accept and uphold."* The top of the character *"uphold"* resembles a head; the *"mouth"* is emptiness, and the *"field"* is the elixir field. Within the human body, only the ancestral aperture is vertical—all else is horizontal. (This is the ancestral aperture.) If one can directly accept and uphold from below, one naturally returns to the source and reverts to the root.
The *Heart Sutra of the Jade Emperor* states: *"The three superior medicines are spirit, qi, and essence."* What humans use daily are precisely these three substances: essence, qi, and spirit.
Spirit is formless and belongs to yang; qi has form and belongs to yin; essence is corporeal. The essence exchanged between man and woman is lustful essence. Before intercourse, essence is stored in the Gate of Life. When desire arises in the heart, a qi descends from the brain to the Gate of Life, transforming into essence and being expelled. This occurs because the heart stirs, emotions follow, spirit moves, and qi accompanies it—spirit and qi unite to form essence, the union of one and two producing three. Thus, the essence of intercourse is *shun* (sequential), generated by the stirring of desire that rouses spirit and qi. For the Dao cultivator, the *huo zi* (living midnight) is *ni* (reversed), formed by the surge of yang qi in heaven and earth. Yang nature ascends, thus it can rise from below to above, returning essence to replenish the brain—this is the distinction.
Qi is the treasure of life for the corporeal body; the physical form relies on qi for movement. Originally external, it is drawn in through the mouth and nose to become the body’s master. When breathing ceases, death follows immediately. Its arrival is summoned by the body’s postnatal prenatal qi. This postnatal prenatal qi first resides in the Gate of Life, later in the Yin Heel Vessel. Though it differs in purity from external qi, its nature is the same—both are qi. Thus, by the principle of like attracting like, it is drawn inward. This is akin to a magnet attracting iron or two electrical charges drawing each other. This qi arrives at the moment of parental intercourse, enabling the summoning of external qi to initiate respiration.
Daoist texts say: *"If even a trace of yin qi remains in the body, one cannot become an immortal."* Yin qi refers to the external qi of postnatal breathing. *"If even a trace of yang qi remains, one does not die."* Yang qi refers to the postnatal prenatal qi of the Gate of Life and Yin Heel Vessel. Its presence enables breathing and sustains vitality, hence one does not die.
The breath’s qi enters from outside, first reaching the lungs. The *Huangting Jing* names the lungs *"White Origin"* and also *"Formed by Emptiness,"* for the lungs are empty, formed by the accumulation of qi. Thus, within the body, the lungs open and close with each inhalation and exhalation; the qi’s entry and exit cause the lungs to open and close. The lungs’ opening and closing follow the qi’s movement in and out.

The qi’s exit and entry are in truth summoned by the postnatal prenatal qi. This qi is also called *"original qi"* (元气), stored in the Yin Heel Vessel, communicating with the crown of the head. Though seemingly unmoving, it is in constant motion—rising and falling, mutually interacting—thereby drawing external qi into the lungs. The lungs open and close in response, all by the power of this qi.

Modern Western medicine likewise recognizes that qi enters the lungs from outside, causing the lungs to open and close. Yet if asked how the qi comes, they cannot answer, for they are unaware of the invisible principle by which original qi summons and governs it.

When an infant descends to earth and external qi enters its mouth, Daoist texts call this *"a single arrow piercing the Primordial Chaos."* Lü Dongbin said: *"When speaking of the moment of receiving qi at birth, marvel not that the celestial mechanism is fully revealed."* For the reception of qi in humans is divided into prenatal and postnatal.

At conception, the primordial ancestral qi one receives pertains to the state before parents’ birth—one’s inherent empty nature. At birth, the qi drawn in through breathing pertains to the moment of being born by parents—the precious life of the formed body. The four characters *"receiving qi at birth"* (生身受气) reveal the mechanism of both prenatal and postnatal.

From the intermediate yin, one comes into being, occupying the Central Palace. Nature ascends to the crown, while life enters the Gate of Life. The Supreme Ultimate divides into the Two Modes, giving rise to life below. Once the umbilical cord is cut, air rushes into the mouth and nose, and breathing begins. From then on, each breath remains connected, and this breathing qi becomes the means to nourish the formed body. The five senses and four limbs thereby gain movement; with every inhalation and exhalation, there is no moment of cessation.

Thus, postnatal breathing clings to the original qi, causing it to congeal and not disperse. Because it does not disperse, it can continue to summon and govern the qi of breathing. The two qi mutually sustain each other, enabling human existence—a ceaseless cycle.

The original qi resides between the navel, the Gate of Life, and the Yin Heel Vessel. The *Inner Classic* names it *"Pass of Origin"* (关元) and *"Sea of Qi"* (气海). *"Pass of Origin"* refers to the barrier that contains this original qi, or the pass formed by the original qi itself. When the fetus takes shape in the womb, the original qi already dwells here, allowing communication with the mother’s breathing.

This original qi arises from the harmonious fusion of the parents’ qi, sharing the same essence as the generative qi of the Supreme Ultimate. After the union of father and mother, the father’s essence and mother’s blood enclose the prenatal qi and the postnatal prenatal qi, forming this body. Once formed, this qi resides 1.3 inches below the navel, becoming the core of this form.

Yin and yang intermingle, moving to open and close, becoming fetal breathing. Fully attuned to the mother’s respiration, it can summon and govern the external postnatal qi. Daoist texts say: *"Yang expands, yin contracts."* With each opening, yang expands; with each closing, yin contracts. Yang corresponds to exhalation, yin to inhalation—this *"opening and closing"* is but a figurative expression. In reality, its movement is like a wheel, turning ceaselessly, which Daoist texts call the *"River Chariot"* (河车).

The formed placenta is called the *"Purple River Chariot"* (紫河车). *"Purple"* denotes its red hue. Why is it called *"River Chariot"*? The *Canon* states: *"The River Chariot of the North."* The Tathāgata says: *"The true qi of the North is called the River Chariot."* The North corresponds to water, driven by its flow, hence the name *"chariot."* The qi of Heaven’s One descends in the North to generate water. Ren-water is formless—qi; Gui-water is formless, first transforming from qi into water. This qi is born of Heaven’s One, hence the name *"River."*

Buddhist texts also call it the *"Dharma Wheel"* (法轮), speaking of the *"Dharma Wheel ever-turning"* and *"turning the great Dharma Wheel"*—all referring to true breathing. True breathing arises from fetal breathing, and respiration follows, hence the saying: *"to return to the root and restore life."* *"Returning to the root"* means returning to the source of breathing; *"restoring life"* means restoring the life of the Receptive (坤) Origin.

Those who cultivate the Dao must first nurture this fetal breathing to establish the foundation. After birth, fetal breathing ceases, the River Chariot stops turning, yet the original qi below the navel remains congealed as a single mass, called the *"Life Stem"* (命蒂). The qi of breathing returns to the stem with every breath, reaching the Gate of Life. The true fire of the Gate of Life rises daily, while the heart’s fluids descend daily. Heart and kidneys daily harmonize, and the body and consciousness grow. From infancy to childhood, through the 5,400 days of the Yellow Path’s cycle, postnatal qi reaches fullness and breaks through the body. Then the postnatal prenatal qi sinks into the Yin Heel Vessel and can no longer ascend.

Though the qi scatters and sinks, its power to summon remains unchanged. Moreover, when transforming essence, it must rely on this original qi. When desire stirs, spirit descends and unites with qi, transforming into essence and discharging externally. Thus, each stirring of desire depletes essence, qi, and spirit once; when exhausted, one dies. Human life truly depends on these Three Treasures—essence, qi, and spirit.

In daily life, all activities deplete spirit and qi. The *Classic* says: *"The eyes are dazzled by the five colors, the ears confused by the five sounds."* The saying goes: *"The mouth opens and spirit-qi scatters; the tongue moves and disputes arise."* Here, *"mouth"* refers not merely to the organ of eating but to the gateway of the six senses. Only *"the tongue moves and disputes arise"* specifically refers to the mouth of eating.

For speech is the voice of the heart, and the tongue its sprout—thus, the arising of right and wrong largely stems from the mouth and tongue. Moreover, dazzlement and confusion at the gates of the six senses leak and dissipate spirit and qi. Daily indulgence in desire causes these leaks to grow ever greater, and so one gradually weakens, ages, sickens, and dies—this is the natural course of creation. Thus, to follow this course without change is to be born and to die; to reverse it and return is to attain longevity and become an immortal.

The *shen* of consciousness grows more acute the more it is used. All post-natal wisdom arises from this *shen* of consciousness, yet the sharper it becomes, the deeper one falls. Deceit, crime, fraud, and falsehood—all are governed by this *shen* of consciousness. The original spirit (*yuan shen*), however, is otherwise: unknowing and unperceiving, it follows the decree of the Emperor and moves in accord with the spontaneity of creation, thereby achieving numinous communion. The *Yinfu Jing* speaks of a single word—“stealing”—referring to the theft of the primordial qi of Heaven and Earth, which is the work of the original spirit. In *Journey to the West*, Sun Wukong descends to the world as a stone monkey: the stone is solidity, born of emptiness within. Yet he steals weapons, steals the golden-hooped staff, steals peaches, steals wine, steals elixir—nothing he does not steal. When he steals weapons in the Country of Aolai, these are still ordinary weapons, mere metal—post-natal. But when he steals the golden-hooped staff from the Crystal Palace, it is true lead, metal within water—primordial qi. Stealing peaches is wood; stealing wine is the water of the tenth Earthly Branch; originally belonging to the east, it is born in the west, where metal and wood converge. Stealing the elixir is the harmony of yin and yang. Thus, the more he steals, the greater his spiritual powers grow. Like one born below, by the day of “five thousand four hundred births in the Yellow Path,” there is no day he does not steal—all is done in unknowing and unperceiving, to complete the body of a pure yang youth. Qian is pure yang, is Heaven, hence “Equal to Heaven.”

The *Yinfu Jing* says: *“When the three thefts are fitting, the Three Powers are at peace.”* Even Heaven, Earth, sun, and moon are thieves—they steal the qi of the Great Ultimate to become the qi of creation, the principle of which is the same. Primordial qi comes from without to become the master within me; post-natal qi also comes from without to become the master of my body. Where there is an outer, there must be an inner: the inner is spirit, the outer is qi. The original spirit shares the same substance as the primordial nature of Qian; when it later transforms into the *shen* of consciousness, it too becomes a master within me. All movement and action are governed by spirit, yet without qi, it cannot communicate or command the physical body. Thus, spirit is the master—the formless master, the master of the heart; qi is the master—the embodied master, the master of the body. Spirit can be the master of the Dharma-body, and when harmonized with primordial qi, it forms the yang spirit. This primordial qi can also return the *shen* of consciousness to the original spirit, giving birth to the fetal immortal.

The *Huangting Jing* speaks of “threefold zither-heart,” which is the harmonizing of spirit and qi. For the qi of breathing is not the root of life, yet the root of life resides within it; the spirit of thought is not the root of nature, yet the root of nature lies within. Thus, through the post-natal one returns to the primordial—emptiness without substance, not following the dictates of the *shen* of consciousness. When nature abides and qi returns, the Way is attained.

In humans, spirit is internal and qi external; spirit is the host and qi the guest. When qi moves, spirit moves; when breath turns, thought arises. With each inhalation and exhalation, the six sense objects—form, sound, scent, taste, touch, and dharma—come into being. Spirit, drawn outward by qi, daily follows this course, scattering spirit and depleting essence until qi is cut off. Thus arise aging, sickness, and death—this is the common path. Though one may bear sons and daughters, the original body is never without decay.

The immortal path, however, reverses this: inner emptiness draws outer fullness; outer emptiness gives rise to inner fullness. That which is originally void, when I make it solid, becomes real; that which is originally guest, when I welcome it, becomes host. By inverting host and guest, they return together to the Great Ultimate of emptiness, to recreate nature and life. Hence, the alchemical classics repeatedly speak of “recreating Heaven and Earth,” and the *Wuzhen Pian* says: *“To employ them, one must divide the left and right armies—let them be host while I am guest.”* The “left and right” refer to my own emptiness communicating with the emptiness of the Great Void. When emptiness reaches its extreme and stillness its utmost, one merges with the Great Void—no self, no other, no thing, no dharma—utterly as before the division of the primordial chaos. Within this profound and indistinct state, there arises a speck of true yang, of the same kind as the primordial nature of Qian.

Just as that guest comes from without to stand before me, the alchemical classics speak of *“when lead arrives, the medicine is born,”* *“true yang comes from the midst of emptiness,”* *“the nine-three man comes to lodge,”* *“within it there is an image, within it there is essence, its essence most true.”* At this moment, if I am neither startled nor afraid, neither awed nor alarmed, but rest in my emptiness and guard my stillness, unknowing and unperceiving, following its spontaneity—then in an instant, the Qian metal buried within Kan, hidden in the great abyss, deep and unfathomable, obscured and hard to perceive—the primordial nature of the life-receiving qi of Qian—suddenly surges forth by the principle of like attracting like, like a magnet drawing iron. It unites with the true yang that has come from without. The alchemical classics speak of *“mercury welcoming the medicine,”* *“gathering the medicine and returning to the furnace,”* *“marrying the Golden Lord as an old husband,”* *“the maiden of sixteen going to prepare the bed,”* *“leading the White Tiger home to raise,”* *“within it there is a sign.”* All refer to this.

This is the welcoming of the guest from without, making it the master of my Central Palace. My teacher, Master Dongting, said: "After I received the oral instructions, whenever I practiced, I would see Grandfather Sun come before me. I would quickly respond, saying: ‘I willingly step down as guest, for you are my old master. I beg you to come to my home as the new host, so together we may set up the furnace and cauldron to transform this mortal frame.’"

Yet when the primordial true yang arises, it flashes like lightning and is gone in an instant. If one is startled or attached to appearances, or if the mind stirs with thoughts, or if one cannot maintain emptiness and stillness, then it cannot be gathered. In a moment, it transforms into the postnatal and can no longer be used—this is called "missing it face to face." From ancient times to the present, how many have missed it in this way? Every person has a moment when yang arises, but those who do not recognize it invariably miss it and let it transform naturally.

For though the lead arrives, if the mercury does not welcome it, awareness is not keen, and the heart is not empty. Among those who cultivate the Dao, many miss it face to face—some fail to recognize Grandfather Sun, some become attached to the images in their minds or the visions before their eyes, and some, upon this awakening, are at a loss for what to do. All these are signs that the work of refining the self is not yet ripe; thus, the work of refining the self is endless.

If this true yang is made the master within, then the original spirit gradually returns while the conscious spirit gradually fades. As this shift occurs little by little, the abdomen becomes full. When the abdomen is full, the heart dies and the spirit lives—what worry is there then that the conscious spirit will cause harm? *"Drawing from Kan to fill Li,"* *"accumulating metal to fill the abdomen"*—all refer to this. *"Returning to my original nature,"* *"restoring my original spirit"*—these also refer to this.

Kan is without; Li is within. One is inner, one is outer; one is spirit, one is qi; one is emptiness, one is fullness. Li is empty within and full without, with spirit residing in qi—this spirit is what I inherently possess. Kan is full within and empty without, with yin dwelling outside yang—this qi comes from without, and what comes from without is fullness, the wondrous existence within true emptiness. What is within is emptiness, the true emptiness within wondrous existence.

Observe the diagram below to understand: In the primordial state, Kan and Li reside in the west and east; in the postnatal state, Li and Kan transform into Zhen and Dui. As the *Wuzhen Pian* says: *"The dragon of Zhen mercury emerges from the land of Li; the tiger of Dui metal is born in the region of Kan."* Zhen and Dui are Li and Kan. The union of Kan and Li, water and fire, is the same as the pairing of Zhen and Dui, metal and wood.

The dragon emerges from fire, for east (three) and south (two) share the same body. The tiger is born from water, for north (one) and west (four) share the same body. East and west are thus south and north; metal and wood are water and fire; the Five Phases are the Four Symbols; the Four Symbols are the Three Families; the Three Families are inner and outer; inner and outer are one family. All unions of water and fire, all pairings of metal and wood, occur within the Yellow Court of the Central Palace, guided by true intention.

The Yellow Court is earth; intention is also earth. Transformations come and go, returning to earth. Earth is the One; the One is emptiness, the Supreme Ultimate. The practitioner’s work lies in the inner spirit and outer qi—spirit is the self. In relation to others, the self is one, while others are two. The common worldly reference to "I" often points to the nose, which holds deep meaning, for the physical form is governed by the nose. This "I" refers to the emotions and perceptions of the intermediate state.

When a person is born, they receive the primordial qi of the One, without knowledge or perception. By the tenth month, they are stirred by the external intermediate state, which becomes their master, and only then do they gain awareness. It is through this awareness that the sense of self arises—this is what is meant by "I," and this "I" is the conscious spirit. Yet the conscious spirit contains the roots of karmic obstructions, compounded by habitual tendencies, making it the false self.

The "true self" is the primordial Qian nature received at birth. In Confucian terms, this qi is the principle within the Supreme Ultimate; in heaven, it is fate; in humans, it is nature, the foundation of life, complete and without lack. At death, the conscious spirit, burdened with karmic roots, falls into hell and suffers retribution, while the primordial Qian nature returns to the great void, neither born nor destroyed. Only this unborn and undestroyed nature is the true self.

The cultivator must merge the conscious spirit into nature, transforming the false self into the true self, thereby cutting off the roots of karma and entering the state of *nirvana* without remainder. The true self, in its original state, possesses boundless potential, but the false self usurps its mastery and drives it into the nine abysses, where it becomes utterly obscured. The false self, having become the host in place of the guest, entangles itself with the roots of sensory experience and thus falls into degradation.

If one fails to distinguish clearly, mistaking the conscious spirit for the true self and taking it as the basis for cultivation, Buddhist texts call this "mistaking the thief for one’s child." One must dissolve this conscious spirit, transforming the false into the true. The scriptures say: *"Push emotion to unite with nature,"* meaning to push the conscious spirit of the false self to merge with the true self.

Now, speaking of nature and emotion, the *Canticle* says: *"Nature presides within, emotion governs without."* Nature is the quiescent, unmoving empty body, still and unmoving. Emotion, however, is fond of movement, engaging with the roots of sensory experience and daily racing outward. Emotion refers to the seven emotions, and their arising all stem from consciousness. For they are only stirred after being provoked by external influences—there is no joy, anger, sorrow, or delight that arises without external provocation. Thus, one is always moved by what is without, and emotion stirs within.

Emotion stirs within and manifests without. The six consciousnesses excite it, and only then do the seven emotions respond—emotion arises from consciousness, and consciousness is revealed through emotion. As emotion and consciousness mutually arise, the six roots never return to their source. The lord of heavenly nature is thus obscured, losing its truth. As Immortal Cao Xian Gu’s *Ode to Qian Gold* says: *"In former years, clouds and mist deeply veiled me; today, we meet with the Dao-eye opened."*

Moreover, the Valley Spirit is called the primal qi born of the Great Ultimate, of the same kind as Heaven and the same body as the Great Void, hence the saying: *"That which Heaven commands is called nature."* Nature is fundamentally unmoving, thus *"presiding within"*; emotion, however, is ever-moving without, thus *"governing without."* What does *"governing"* mean? It means governing the six consciousnesses—defending against the six external thieves to prevent their intrusion, so that the six consciousnesses do not arise. If the six consciousnesses do not arise, the seven emotions remain unstirred; even if occasionally provoked, all responses remain measured. When measured, harmony is achieved.

What is harmony? It is the harmony of nature, the unity of inner and outer—this unity is the *"central"* state before arousal, which is nature itself. The centrality of nature and the harmony of emotion are the Dao’s *"central"* and *"one"*—the all-pervading Way. By governing emotion externally, nature naturally rests within. When emotion governs the external consciousness and consciousness dissolves, emotion returns to the primordial emotion of pre-heaven, the false merges into the true, and Qian Gold is restored.

The alchemical texts speak of primordial essence and primordial emotion, dissolving the false self and pushing it outward to summon the true self, forming the Golden Duke—this is the meaning of *"reversal."* To push emotion to unite with nature, one must first build dikes to defend against external provocations. If external provocations do not enter, the false self naturally dissolves, and the true self gradually returns.

Yet to summon the true self, one must first expel the false self from the spirit-terrace and sweep it clean—this is the essential work. Thus, one must begin within, though its function lies without, as the scriptures say: *"dual cultivation of inner and outer."* This is the origin of the work of return.

The *Canticle* says: *"Within, nurture the self, tranquil and void. The original root, hidden yet luminous, inwardly illumines the body. Seal the senses, fortify the spiritual root. The Three Lights sink into obscurity, warming and nourishing the child-pearl. Though unseen, it is near and easily sought."* This speaks of the initial work of nurturing nature, which is the dike of defense against externals.

*"Nurturing the self within"* refers to refining the self—cultivating the self within one’s small void, first achieving *"tranquility and emptiness"* to connect with the great void beyond, thereby merging into chaos and summoning the true self. The true self is the Qian nature, originally wondrously luminous, utterly empty yet utterly real. *"Hidden"* refers to its quiescence; when veiled by the false self’s conscious spirit, it becomes concealed in secrecy, unseen and ungraspable.

Yet its numinosity still inwardly illumines the body—how can this be known? Consider a person in deep sleep, devoid of knowledge, the conscious spirit dissolved, utterly still—yet spiritual awareness emerges. If someone calls from the side, they awaken; this awakening is the awareness of spiritual luminosity, not the conscious spirit’s awareness. Or when one encounters calamity and perceives omens beforehand, this too is the numinosity within nature. For the cultivator, if one can achieve tranquility and emptiness, one can restore this luminosity.

For nature is the fundamental substance, and emptiness is what nature itself generates. When emptiness reaches its extreme and stillness is profound, the heart naturally brightens and nature naturally appears. The key lies in *"sealing the senses."* The *"senses"* (dui) refer not to the mouth that eats and drinks, but to the gateways of the six roots. Blocking the six roots prevents inner thoughts from leaking out and outer thoughts from entering—withdrawing sight and reversing hearing, so they do not engage with externals. This is the Way of *"restraining the self and returning to propriety."* When seeing, hearing, speaking, and acting do not transgress propriety, one naturally restrains the self and returns to propriety, and *"all under heaven returns to benevolence."* Benevolence is nature.

The *"spiritual root"* is the primordial qi of pre-heaven—this is benevolence, *"that which ceaselessly generates is called benevolence."* Benevolence is the spiritual root of the myriad things. Fortifying it is *"holding fast to the center."* It is like building a wall—tending and strengthening it until it becomes firm. This is the work of laying the foundation.

The Three Lights in heaven are the sun, moon, and stars; in man, the Three Lights are the ears, eyes, and mouth. This "mouth" does not refer merely to speech but to all gates of ingress and egress. Only when they are sunk into the abyss can one "warmly nurture the child-pearl." The child-pearl is the pearl of nature—when tranquil and empty, nature is restored, hence it is called "child." The adept refines the Dharma-body through the physical form, returning from the postnatal to the prenatal, thus regarding the postnatal as the mother and the prenatal it generates as the child. "Pearl" signifies roundness and luminosity, denoting its original radiance.

First, one must submerge the Three Lights: the eyes do not see yet spirit congeals, the ears do not hear yet essence solidifies, the mouth and nose remain unmoving and qi gathers. The outward leaks of the Three Lights are gathered and sunk into the great abyss, so that the lost pearl may be returned to Hepu. Though this pearl is "invisible when sought," it is in truth "near and easily attained"—what is meant by "the Dao is before one’s eyes." As *The Awakening to Reality* states: "Once true gold returns to the golden house, a single luminous pearl will never depart." This is that pearl.

For those cultivating the Dao who seek the Scarlet Water’s Mystic Pearl, let "Wangxiang" retrieve it. Wangxiang means "unknowing and unrecognizing." Once, the Yellow Emperor lost his pearl and sent Liming to search for it, but to no avail; Wangxiang, however, chanced upon it. This signifies that only without the intent to seek the pearl can one obtain it.

Daoists call this pearl the "Spirit Pearl" or "Treasure Pearl"; Buddhists name it the "Muni Treasure Pearl" or "śarīra." Daoists also refer to it as the "Elixir Head," the prenatal Qian-metal of the human body. When practice reaches the state of extreme emptiness and profound stillness, this pearl appears and disappears, gradually becoming visible and bright. Its image manifests before the eyes—at times, it is exceedingly luminous. Daoists also call it the "star" or "Dipper’s bowl." The bowl, originally the Wen star of wood, is the eastern constellation, symbolizing the numinosity of nature and corresponding to "East Three" in the *River Chart*. It is also said, "Seizing the pearl beneath the black dragon’s chin"—this too refers to the pearl’s hidden, unmanifest nature.

When practice attains true stillness, a pearl-like image often appears before the eyes, for this is the initial revelation of nature’s light. Yet if stillness is not profound, it cannot be seen. Those who paint Buddhist images place a circle between the brows to depict this phenomenon, as the sutra says: "The Tathāgata emits white light from between his brows."

This pearl, though "invisible when sought," is "near and easily attained," concealed within the North Pole’s great abyss and born in the Dragon Palace of the Eastern Sea—hence the Buddhist tale of the "dragon maiden offering the pearl." "Warmly nurturing the child-pearl" means that before the pearl is obtained, one must remain tranquil and empty, submerging the Three Lights to seek it. Before nature’s light is restored, one must not search for it; once seen, its image appears before the eyes, its root fundamentally residing in body and mind—truly near.

All such descriptions are figurative. "Figure" means likeness—likening the matter or the state. Terms like "pearl of nature" or "moon of nature" are the forms of dharma-reality, virtual images that arise in the course of Daoist practice. Yet these images are also illusory; one must not cling to them, for if one does, the image will dissolve. As the virtual image before the eyes transforms, the mind too can no longer remain tranquil and empty.

When this image first appears, it resembles a tiny star, gradually accumulating and expanding until it brightens into a pearl. Its light dazzles the eyes, visible whether they are open or closed—hence the name "night-shining pearl." This describes the pearl of nature. In the dual cultivation of nature and life, the image sometimes manifests as the moon, and Daoist texts abound with such metaphors.

What is seen externally is called "image," while what the body experiences is called "scene." In Daoist practice, every step has its image, every step its scene; yet one must not fixate on images in the heart or cling to scenes in the body. Images arise from the union of yin and yang qi, so they appear in moments of vagueness and indistinctness—like earth’s vapor rising and, illuminated by heaven’s yang qi, forming colorful clouds that shift endlessly. When the yin qi of the human body harmonizes with yang qi, virtual images likewise arise. Yang possesses light, yin possesses color; when light and color intermingle, all manner of images naturally emerge.

As the *Diamond Sutra* states: "All conditioned phenomena are like dreams, illusions, bubbles, shadows." Daoist texts warn: "Do not cling to images." Both caution practitioners against falling into error here.

The terms "warming and nurturing" encompass the work of fire and timing. "Warming" means neither cold nor parched; "nurturing" means containing and fostering. If one falls into dull emptiness, the water grows cold; if thoughts do not cease, the fire turns parched. Cold water and parched fire prevent the formation of the pearl of the child. One must preserve the insubstantial while guarding the substantial, neither aiding nor forgetting, to warm it; and nurture it with unbroken continuity, using it without strain—only then can the hidden nature gradually become luminous, and the primordial spiritual root gradually firm. This is what is called "cultivating and nurturing the bud."

**Fifth Lesson: Utmost Emptiness and Profound Stillness—Merging Sentiment with Nature**
**The Two Apertures Method Naturally Connects with Spirit Illumination**

The *Canticle* states: "Gradually the Yellow Center becomes permeable with principle, moistening and nourishing the skin and flesh. Beginning with rectitude ensures completion; standing firm at the outset allows no holding back. The One veils and conceals—none in the world know it." This speaks of the efficacy of refining the self: the spiritual root is restored, and resonance brings immediate communion.

Once one attains tranquility and emptiness to nurture the root of one’s nature, the original countenance can gradually be recovered. By inwardly illuminating the body, closing the aperture of the mouth, and turning the light back to reflect, the spiritual root gradually grows, forming the luminous pearl that restores one’s primordial hun-tun spirit—this is the internal process. Yet the path of internal nurturing hinges on the word "tranquility," which arises from striving toward effortless ease. When first undertaking self-refinement, the six senses scatter outward, making stillness impossible. Through persistent practice of concentration—reducing and further reducing until reaching non-action—over time, body and mind attain great stability. Naturally, non-action prevails: effort is not applied, yet its function operates spontaneously; thoughts are not forcibly suppressed, yet they subside of their own accord. Only then is "tranquility, emptiness, and non-being" achieved. Within the empty chamber, whiteness arises, and from the void emerges a single point of true yang—this is the originally hidden luminous nature, forming the mysterious aperture of the central palace. As the *Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* states: "The medicine is born in the mysterious aperture."

This point of true yang is the great medicine. Initially born within the void, it has yet to be felt in the body, so one must concentrate the spirit into the qi cavity to warm and nurture this pearl of the child—the pearl being the generated true yang. "The three lights sinking" refers to concentrating the spirit into the qi cavity, which is also the "mysterious aperture." The medicine is the "wondrous aperture," and also fire—different names for the same thing, known to few.

Though this medicine and this aperture are invisible to the eye, they are near and easily sought. When spirit and qi converge, the three palaces fill with qi; the mechanism stirs, and the flute sounds—then the Yellow Center becomes permeable with principle. The phrase "the Yellow Center becomes permeable with principle" comes from the *Book of Changes*, *Kun* hexagram: "The Yellow Center permeates with principle, rightly positioned within the body. Beauty resides within and flows to the four limbs." This describes the principle of external resonance and internal response.

Here, "principle" refers to the principle of the Supreme Ultimate’s qi; it also means "interior" and "ritual," as the ancient characters were interchangeable, signifying the movement from external to internal. Thus, beauty resides within and manifests in the four limbs. Only when the Yellow Center becomes permeable with principle can its effects extend to the body—this is what the *Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* calls "the fire timing activates the yang furnace."

When stripping reaches its extreme, it returns—*Fu* (Return) is the initial line of *Qian* (Creative). By the fifth line of *Kun* (Receptive), emptiness has reached its utmost, and stillness has become profound. The fourth line, "the sack is tied," marks the moment when the myriad things return to their root; the fifth line, bearing "the yellow skirt, supremely auspicious," signifies the rise of the primordial at the end of integrity—precisely the time when the myriad things recover their mandate. Transforming into *Fire over Earth* (*Jin*), "the noble person illuminates his virtue." "Illuminating one’s virtue" means that virtue is the principle and qi of the primordial Supreme Ultimate—"illuminating" also means "clarifying," thus clarifying one’s own luminous virtue.

"The Yellow Center becomes permeable with principle" is the resonance of principle and qi, through which true yang arrives. The four limbs are the first to respond, experiencing a sense of ease and expansion. The *Xian* (Influence) hexagram of the *Book of Changes* takes this as its image: the first line says, "Influence in the big toe," followed by "influence in the calf" and "thigh," culminating in "cheeks and tongue." The arrival of external yang is first felt in the hands and feet; through sustained resonance, it gradually extends to the entire body, progressing through *Fu* (Return), *Lin* (Approach), *Tai* (Peace), *Da Zhuang* (Great Strength), to *Guai* (Breakthrough) and *Qian* (Creative). When hardness and softness first unite, clouds drift and rain falls, and the myriad things all flourish. When a single yin meets (in *Gou*), the fluids beneath the tongue are generated. Thus, the imagery of the *Xian* hexagram culminates at the cheeks and tongue.

Shao Yong’s poem, "Thirty-six palaces all are spring," describes the arrival of true yang at the palace, filling the entire body with supreme harmony. The formless method is nurtured by yin and permeated by yang—yin is emptiness, yang is wondrous being. The physical body of form is moistened by yin and permeated by yang—yang is metal essence, yin is wood fluid. As Lü Dongbin’s poem says, "I know how to arrange the four yin and yang"—this is the meaning.

"Yellow skirt" refers to the Yellow Center; "primordial" refers to the initially permeating yang. Because it permeates principle, it is called "auspicious"—this is what the *Mirror of Ingesting the Medicine* calls "receiving qi is auspicious." In the *Kun* hexagram, from "eternal integrity" to the fifth line, integrity ends and the primordial arises, giving birth to yang. The top line marks the union of yin and yang, where "dragons contend."

The *Canticle* further states: "Golden sand enters the five viscera, dispersing like mist and rain."

The steaming warmth reaches the four limbs, and the complexion becomes joyful and radiant. For this single spark of true yang is born within emptiness—precisely the image of "one yang returning" beneath the heavy yin. Thus Laozi says, "Stillness is called returning to destiny."

At the yang’s first return, it congeals within the Mysterious Aperture, which is none other than the Yellow Center. From the Yellow Center, it inwardly pervades the entire body. Though its numinous root remains within the Mysterious Aperture, its qi already fills the four limbs, penetrating above and below, warming the skin, and moistening it. As the *Clarifying the Obscure in the Kinship of the Three* states: "Nurture the child-pearl, neither forgetting nor aiding. Over time, spirit and clarity arise of themselves, gradually permeating all directions. The nine orifices, hundred vessels, three hundred sixty bones and joints, and eighty-four thousand pores of the body all become unobstructed, naturally luminous, moist, and harmonious—stimulated, they respond and penetrate." The phrase "beauty lies within" refers to the intercourse of yin and yang, which still occurs within the Mysterious Aperture. This is the meaning behind *Journey to the West*’s Sun Wukong entering the Water Curtain Cave and being named "Handsome Monkey King." The monkey represents *shen* (申), and within the character *shen* lies the center of the center—an exquisitely crafted symbol. This center is the natural endowment of heaven and earth, yet few in the world awaken to its emptiness.

The lines "First rectify, then cultivate; establish the stem before grasping the branch" clarify the necessity of nurturing and the gradual nature of practice. For the cultivator, thoughts must be upright—only the thought of no-thought is true thought, capable of engendering transformation. Otherwise, clinging to existence or nonexistence alike will fail to accord with the matter. Thus it is said: "First rectify, then cultivate; establish the stem before grasping the branch. The One veils and conceals—none in the world know it." The One, as the scriptures say, is "attaining the One, and all affairs are complete." The One is the single qi of the Supreme Ultimate. Independent and spirit-like, it exists before the division of yin and yang, thus able to generate the myriad things, to be spirit-like and transformative. In humans, it is the primordial nature of the *Qian* origin, the precelestial original spirit. Alas, people invariably obscure it with the acquired spirit, unaware of its existence and thus seeking it, drifting ever farther from the Dao.

From the perspective of destiny: when the single qi of the Supreme Ultimate has not yet divided into yin and yang, it is the precelestial true yang, the root of life for the myriad things. Humans receive it to form their bodies, hence it is called the "treasure of destiny." After the body is broken, it sinks into the Gate of Destiny, descending into the Yin Heel vessel, obscure and difficult to perceive. Though used daily, its calamities go unnoticed. Nature and destiny are in truth one thing. At the moment of receiving qi, nature and destiny are undivided; once divided, obscuration follows. People use what follows naturally and forget their origin. The *Song of Tapping the Trigrams* says, "Whispered close to the ear, the wondrous secret—ascend to Penglai’s first peak," referring to the primordial qi sunken in the *Kan* palace, which the cultivator must draw upward to the *Li* palace. "Whispered close to the ear" means one must abandon the cleverness of ears and eyes, withdraw vision and return hearing, seal the mouth and close the orifices. *Kan* is the ear, *Dui* is the mouth; speech issues from the mouth, sound enters the ear, and mouth and ear communicate through the single aperture of emptiness. By preserving nonbeing and guarding being, one draws forth true yang from the Great Abyss and returns it to Penglai Peak, concealing it within the ear.

In *Journey to the West*, Sun Wukong obtains the golden-hooped staff from beneath the Iron Plate Bridge, entering the Dragon Palace of the Eastern Sea to retrieve it from the sea’s treasury. The ones who inspired this were the Four Marshal Generals: the Red-Buttocked Horse Monkey and the Long-Armed Ape Monkey. The Crystal Palace symbolizes the Yin Heel cavity; the red buttocks represent the *Xun* trigram between the thighs, and the long arms signify the *Gen* trigram of the two hands holding the Gate. The golden-hooped staff symbolizes the primordial qi, able to grow vast or shrink small, to support heaven and stand upon earth, and to follow the heart’s desire. When transformed into something minute, like an embroidery needle, and concealed within the ear, it is because the Gate of Destiny and the ear both belong to the *Kan* trigram, and the primordial qi naturally flows between them. The analogy is exquisitely crafted and worth contemplating.

The *Kinship of the Three* further states: "The three treasures—ears, eyes, and mouth—close and seal, do not let them open and communicate." This is the meaning of withdrawing vision and returning hearing. "The true person lurks in the deep abyss, floating and roaming while guarding the compass’s center"—the compass’s center is the single aperture of emptiness. "Floating and roaming" refers to the firing times of neither forgetting nor aiding. The "three treasures" are the three luminants mentioned earlier. "Luminants" describes them from the perspective of concentrated spirit; "treasures" describes them as essence, qi, and spirit. "Turning and bending to see and hear" means focusing vision and hearing inward. "Bending" refers to partiality, through which one may advance to utmost sincerity. "Opening and closing all accord as one" means that with each inhalation and exhalation, yin contracts and yang expands, neither hurried nor delayed, matching their rhythms. From this line, one may understand that the "mouth" does not refer to the mouth of speech.

This is the foremost requirement, hence it is said: "It is the pivot and linchpin of the self, inexhaustible in movement and stillness"—that is, unbroken and continuous, deeply rooted and firmly established. The true breath comes and goes in endless circulation. Only then does the *Li* qi "absorb the constructive and defensive," referring to the aforementioned wood fluid, which from beneath the tongue descends the Tower of Nourishment to return to the constructive and defensive, nourishing the body. Daoists call this "refining the form with jade liquid." "The *Kan* no longer uses its hearing" means that when the *Li* qi returns to *Kun*, the *Kan* yang ascends back to *Qian*, the metal is sufficient, and *Kan* naturally ceases to leak. Mentioning *Kan* alone carries deeper meaning, to be detailed later in the practice.

The *Dui* union refrains from speech, uttering few words to accord with the primordial chaos, preventing the spirit and qi from scattering outward. The word "union" has a double meaning—it is not merely closing the mouth. "The three are thus interlinked": these three refer to the aforementioned ears, eyes, and mouth. "Relax the body in an empty chamber" also signifies the submersion of the three luminaries. "Entrust the will to emptiness and non-thought as the constant" means maintaining utmost sincerity, profound stillness, and emptiness. Since it is called "entrusting the will," it is evident this is not dull emptiness—dull emptiness is not the entrusting of the will. The use of the word "will" signifies emptiness that is not empty, for the true intention is inwardly guarded. "The mind is focused, neither scattered nor straying" requires not overstepping the square inch—ten characters form a vertical and a horizontal line, their intersection being the center; if one strays beyond, they have already passed this aperture. "In sleep, spirit and qi embrace" means the spirit embraces the qi in unbroken continuity. "Upon waking, await the presence or absence" refers to recognizing the "living midnight" and "living noon"—when the time arrives, the spirit knows. Thereupon, the complexion becomes radiant, the bones and joints grow firm, all yin in the body retreats, and yang qi returns. "Practice without ceasing, and the qi will move like clouds and rain." When yang qi arises in the body, it flows like clouds; when cool fluid descends, it showers like rain. Like spring’s moisture or melting ice, it flows downward from above and ascends from below, circulating endlessly between the brain and the bubbling spring. Within the boundless cavern of the Wuji, in the vast and small voids, it moves and flows ceaselessly. Due to the blockages in the postnatal physical form and its bones and joints, only this single void aperture remains interconnected.
