Though it moves externally, it is everywhere linked with the eight vessels and nine meridians of the entire body.

Master Zhang Ziyang’s *Treatise on the Eight Vessels* states: "The Yin Qiao vessel is a single aperture around which the eight vessels and nine meridians of the human body all revolve. When the Yin Qiao moves, all eight vessels and nine meridians move with it." The void aperture communicates with the eight vessels and nine meridians for this very reason. These two chapters share similar meanings, but the former focuses solely on refining the self, while this one encompasses both nature and life, the firing times, and all phenomena—here, the essential principles of cultivation are fully revealed.

The alchemical classics often say: "Borrow the false to cultivate the true." Borrowing the false to cultivate the true means using the false self to refine the true self. The physical body is the false self, while the Dharma body is the true self—this is spoken from the perspective of the body. The original spirit is the true self, while the acquired spirit is the false self—this is spoken from the perspective of the mind. The deluded intention is the false self, while the true intention is the true self—this is spoken from the perspective of intention. In sum, one truth encompasses all truth, and one falsity pervades all falsity. The postnatal essence, qi, and spirit are all false; only the prenatal original essence, original qi, and original spirit are called true. When these three return to their origin, it is called "perfect truth."

Yet for those whose bodies are broken by the postnatal, all three are false. Now, in seeking to cultivate them, from where can one summon "perfect truth" to replace the false? The true self is the Dharma body; before the Dharma body is formed and the true self appears, one should not concern themselves with true or false selves—both true and false are the self. It is by means of the false self that the true self is summoned, which the Daoist texts call "cultivating the true through the false." By removing the false, the true countenance naturally emerges. For instance, the acquired spirit is false; to summon the true self, one begins with the acquired spirit. At the outset, one still relies on the acquired spirit to practice, with utmost sincerity, focusing the mind and rectifying intention, merging the six senses of the false self into one. The Buddhist scriptures speak of "unifying the three minds and eliminating the four marks." The three minds are the past, future, and present; unifying the three minds means removing the minds of the past and future and returning to the single mind of the present. The *Book of Changes*’ *Gen* hexagram says, "The noble person’s thoughts do not go beyond their position"—this is the present mind that responds to things as they come. The "four marks" are the self, others, sentient beings, and lifespan, as explained in detail in the *Diamond Sutra*. Unify the six into one, and firmly guard this one. Then, return this guarded one to emptiness, and the original spirit will emerge.

However, at the beginning of practice, the great emptiness is not yet suitable. In cultivating the elixir, one must first employ existence—this is the teaching of the "land immortals who dwell in the void." At the start of entering the chamber, one cannot yet achieve pure stillness and non-action, for non-action is the work of "vigilance in solitude." If the body is inactive while the mind remains unsettled, what use is it? First, one must have sincere intention to attain true stillness; with true stillness, one naturally achieves non-action without striving for it. "True intention" is sincere intention—the six senses merge into this single, focused intention, which is neither present nor absent, neither held nor forgotten. Inner thoughts do not arise, and outer thoughts do not enter. With sufficient refinement, the mind naturally becomes still, and the intention becomes sincere. At this time, the mind is in a state of primordial chaos, unaware and unknowing. Sights, sounds, scents, tastes, touches, and phenomena cannot enter, and within the emptiness, there vaguely appears a solitary, luminous point of awareness—yet in truth, there is nothing perceived. Like an infant yielding to natural innocence, it is neither thought nor non-thought. The Confucians call this "sincere intention," the Daoists call it "true intention," and the Buddhists call it "right awakening." At this moment, when the true intention returns to the state of chaos, the original spirit emerges.

The practitioner of the Dao often becomes attached to either existence or non-existence—how, then, may one avoid such attachment? This is what must be deeply realized. Let us test this through practice, as in "sitting with gentle warmth." When not a single thought arises and not a single intention scatters, if the work is done correctly, within the mysterious aperture there naturally arises a formless mass of qi, warm and subtle. This qi is like breath yet not breath, truly the coalescence of spirit and qi, with true intention inwardly enfolded—this is the scene that appears. This warmth is not easily retained; the moment the mind stirs, it dissipates. With this subtle warmth as the crucible, the medicinal substance is precisely this. Without it, one is but "boiling emptiness in a cauldron," a state of "solitary yin in silent sitting."

The *Canticle* compares this to spring thaw melting ice, both containing the character for "warmth," drawing on the meaning of gentle warmth and harmonious fusion. Fire fears scorching, water fears freezing; overheating is scorching, lack of warmth is cold—neither is proper. Only with gentle warmth is the firing process correct. Once yang essence is born and the lead crucible established, the consciousness of perception dissolves, and the primordial spirit congeals. The *Canticle* speaks of "congealing spirit into form," which is the congealing of the primordial spirit to form the dharmakāya. For the primordial spirit is the master of the dharmakāya, just as the consciousness of perception is the master of the physical body—both are not apart from this self, yet the self is divided into true and false. The true self is fundamentally without birth or death; the false self perishes when its use is exhausted.

When a fetus is in the womb, the primordial spirit congeals into a spherical form—this is the fetus’s dharmakāya. Once the physical body is fully formed, the primordial spirit retreats, and the consciousness of perception attaches to it. At the beginning of fetal formation, there first exists the fetal origin—what is this? It is the nature and life stored within, enveloped by essence and blood in a spherical form. The inner emptiness is the origin of the dharmakāya; the outer solidity is the origin of the physical body. After a man and woman unite, after seven days, a pea-sized object appears below the navel, which, in stillness, can be felt to pulse, descending to the womb and ascending to the heart-mind—this is it. With this, conception occurs, and women accustomed to pregnancy know this well.

The fetal origin in the womb naturally moves at all times, but when a person is active, the consciousness of perception dominates, so it is not felt. In stillness, when the spirit’s light turns inward, it is perceived. The seventh day is the time of the first yang’s return; the Old Testament’s God creating the world in seven days also draws on the meaning of the seventh day’s return. The practitioner of the Dao must also cultivate until this appears—it is called the "elixir origin" or "elixir head," its form no different from the fetal origin. The elixir origin is the inner mysterious pass. The mysterious pass has inner and outer, upper and lower divisions, which will be detailed later.

The fetal origin in the mother’s womb constantly leaps and pulses, and when the elixir origin first forms, it also ascends to the heart-mind and descends to the Gate of Life—its form is identical, hence it is called "structuring the embryo." As the fetal origin gradually accumulates, it generates channels, viscera, bones, joints, skin, and flesh, forming the physical body. The reason the physical form grows is that spirit and qi operate within while essence and blood expand without.

After seven months, when the fetus is fully formed, spirit and qi, though formless, still coalesce into a mass below the fetus’s navel, pulsing like a turning wheel. Thus, the mother’s breath can pass through the umbilical cord, forming an inhalation and exhalation that turns without a single moment’s pause, nor does the breath ever cease—this is called "fetal breathing." Fetal breathing, from below the navel, ascends to connect with the Mud Pill Palace and descends to the Bubbling Spring, permeating every pore of the body. This is how the fetus constantly steals the primordial qi of Heaven and Earth to become human.

The cultivator must also refine their practice to achieve fetal breathing, where mind and breath depend on each other, moving together when in motion and resting together when at rest, congealing into the true golden crucible. As Liao Chanhui said, "In front, it faces the navel wheel; behind, it faces the kidneys—between them lies the true golden crucible." Li Hanxu said, "When stopping, it stops before the kidney hall and behind the navel wheel, bound within the compass and square." The Buddhist scriptures speak of the "Dharma Wheel constantly turning," referring to this.

Advancing further from fetal breathing to true breathing, the six pulses cease, qi returns to its root, and the entire body’s pores naturally breathe, no longer through the mouth or nose—placing a goose feather at the nostrils, it will not stir. A mass of precelestial ancestral qi moves invisibly in ascent and descent. The Buddhist scriptures speak of "turning the great Dharma Wheel," and Bai Yuchan said, "The spontaneous river chariot makes hundreds of circuits."

For those whose bodies have been broken by the postnatal, the breath only reaches the "Central Cavity" and cannot, with every breath, reach below the navel. The breath of the perfected person returns to its root with every breath, deeply reaching the heels, ascending and descending through the Conception and Governor Vessels, then dispersing through the eight extraordinary vessels and nine meridians. As *Zhuangzi* said, "The breath of the perfected person reaches the heels; the breath of ordinary people reaches the throat" (*Zhuangzi·Great Master*). Common sayings like "standing firm," "feet planted on solid ground," and "true practice and realization" all refer to this.

Thus, the practice of the Dao begins with the *Lǚ* hexagram. Confucius said, "*Lǚ* is the foundation of virtue" (*Book of Changes·Appended Judgments*). Virtue is attainment—attaining the correctness of principle and qi. Heaven’s virtue is ceaseless generation, and ceaseless generation arises from the flowing of the vast qi. Human virtue lies in the Five Constants and Four Virtues, and the Four Virtues and Five Constants are also born of the vast qi.

The *Book of Changes* states, "The great person’s virtue harmonizes with Heaven and Earth" (*Book of Changes·Words of the Text on Qian*). This means aligning with the correct *qi* of Heaven and Earth’s yin and yang. Borrowing the false to cultivate the true is to refine the Dharma-body from within the physical form. Buddhist texts speak of "removing ore to leave gold," while Daoist texts say "washing away sand to reveal gold"—once impurities are removed, pure gold emerges. The false is like ore or sand; when it is discarded, true gold appears of itself.

Alchemical texts compare the original spirit (*yuanshen*) to "cinnabar," also likened to "mercury" or "vermilion mercury," born within the *Zhen* trigram’s wood. They further call it the mysterious *qi* of the Green Dragon, which belongs to wood, and state that within the *Li* trigram’s fire lies this "wood-fluid" as mercury. Because the consciousness-spirit (*shishen*) is an impurity—like ore or sand—one must extract the wood-fluid from within it to obtain true mercury. Now, fire is born of wood, so why must wood emerge from fire? Because the consciousness-spirit obscures it: the consciousness-spirit is like fire, and when fire rages, wood is consumed; when fire is extinguished, wood fully appears. Reversing this to draw wood from fire is the method and gateway of practice.

Vermilion mercury is true mercury. To generate mercury within the cauldron, one must first place silver in the pool (the cauldron being the great and small emptiness). As *Awakening to Reality* says, "To retain vermilion mercury in the golden cauldron, first place silver in the jade pool." The method is to first introduce mercury, and then vermilion mercury can be extracted. This metaphor is most ingenious: mercury is vermilion mercury, and cinnabar arises from mercury. Cinnabar and mercury are originally one substance from the same ore; only with mercury can cinnabar be extracted, and cinnabar can also be refined into mercury. The meaning is that one must first unify the consciousness-spirit and enter it into the cauldron, after which the original spirit—cinnabar—emerges. Since the consciousness-spirit is inherently numinous by clinging to form, when it is placed in emptiness with no attachment, it can transform consciousness into wisdom, and the original spirit is restored.

Mr. Huang Yi once served as an official in Yunnan and Guangxi, where he witnessed the mutual refinement of mercury and cinnabar, recording it in his notes with great accuracy. The passage in *Awakening to Reality* refers precisely to the practice of "borrowing the false to cultivate the true."

The distinctions and transformations among the original spirit, consciousness-spirit, and intention are detailed as follows:

Initially, there is Non-Ultimate (*wuji*); from Non-Ultimate arises the Supreme Ultimate (*taiji*). The Supreme Ultimate originally contains nothing, yet possesses the impulse to move. When it is about to move but has not yet moved, it remains emptiness. The Supreme Ultimate moves and generates yang; the single point within the diagram represents the primordial principle-*qi*. When movement reaches its extreme, principle-*qi* fills the Supreme Ultimate. At the extreme of movement, stillness must follow. Stillness generates the One, which divides into Two, becoming tangible *qi*—yin *qi*. Yin and yang each occupy a position, forming the Two Modes (*liangyi*). The Two Modes further divide into the Four Images.

The first diagram illustrates the principle of Heaven and Earth’s creation; the second, the principle of human generation, which is identical to the great creation of Heaven and Earth. The third diagram shows that emptiness is non-nature, and the original must be rooted in true emptiness. The fourth diagram, contrasting nature with emotion, represents the Two Modes of nature and life already divided—this is the original nature of humans.

The so-called original nature is the substance of nature, bestowed by Heaven upon humans at the moment of receiving *qi* and forming the embryo, before the physical body exists. This must be clearly distinguished, without ambiguity. It is "the nameless beginning of Heaven and Earth," which is also the Dao.

The fifth diagram’s original spirit refers to the movement of this original nature: when undifferentiated, it is intention; when clear, it is awareness. The sixth diagram’s innate knowledge represents a further progression. Awareness has not yet become knowledge; from awareness arises knowledge, and from knowledge comes ability—this is "innate ability." Knowledge originates within, while ability manifests externally.

The seventh diagram’s consciousness-spirit is the spirit of postnatal thought, hence its division into thought and consciousness: thought arises internally, self-generated; consciousness forms externally, reactive. The eighth diagram’s original emotion is the fundamental emotion, not the postnatal Seven Emotions. It is the movement of nature when stirred by external stimuli—this meaning must be carefully examined.

Nature resides within, while emotion governs externally. Nature rules the heart, and emotion resides in the body. Nature is fundamentally quiescent and unmoving; when stimulated, it responds and emits numinous awareness. Emotion is inherently restless and dynamic; when bound to external objects, it becomes discriminating desire.

Thus, Buddhist scriptures distinguish between contact (*sparśa*) and dharmas: what is felt through the body is called contact, belonging to emotion; what is contained in the mind is called dharmas, belonging to nature. When the substance of nature is empty, dharmas also become empty. When nature is beyond description, dharmas are also beyond description. The mind is dharmas, and dharmas are the mind—this is why it is called "mind-dharmas." All people share this mind, and this mind shares this principle, hence the saying, "Hearts resonate with hearts, and a single word awakens enlightenment."

Emotion, however, is rooted in external conditions; it arises only after contact with the Six Dusts. When stirred within, it must disturb one’s essence—this is what is meant by "internal leakage following external dissipation." Thus, emotion has internal and external aspects, while nature has no internal or external. That which has no internal or external is called the "Center"; that which has internal and external yet arises from innate truth is called "Harmony." The Center is the root, and Harmony is the branch. Returning the branch to the root and aligning the root with the branch is the path of "dual cultivation."

Original emotion is fundamentally good but changes through habituation. Nature is an empty substance, without so-called change. The unchanging is without birth or destruction, while the changing is subject to birth and destruction. What causes change is solely the consciousness, hence it is also called "emotion-consciousness."

Intention arises when the original heart is about to move, appearing as a faint impulse yet not fully manifest—this is true intention, which the cultivator of the Dao must refine. Awareness represents a slight advance beyond intention, where movement gives rise to clarity. The distinction between awareness and intention lies in the difference between discernment and undifferentiated wholeness. One is "seemingly empty yet not empty," while the other is "form that is simultaneously emptiness." From awareness arises innate knowledge, a spontaneous alignment with the correctness of Heavenly Principle, responding to Heaven without the mind of right and wrong, without the heart of preference or aversion. This further develops into innate capacity. Innate knowledge and innate capacity are one—knowing is acting, without a mind of transformation; acting is immediately emptying, which is what is meant by "responding naturally to things as they come."

Innate knowledge is "the Qian hexagram’s ease of knowing," while innate capacity is "the Kun hexagram’s simplicity of functioning." The Great Dao is simple and easy, arising from innate knowledge and capacity—knowing is acting, acting is emptying, and this is the Great Dao itself. Moreover, knowledge and capacity must be united; if they are not, they are not true innate knowledge or capacity. There is no room for a shifting thought in this. Liu Wuyuan’s commentary on the *Yinfu Jing* calls them "spiritual knowing" and "true knowing." Spiritual knowing is luminous awareness, while true knowing is true intention. From intention arises aspiration, and from true intention arises true aspiration.

A single point of true intention, pure and unchanging, is aspiration. Neither attached nor detached, it is sincerity; neither shifting in thought nor generating deliberation, maintaining its undifferentiated state—this is sincerity. Aspiration carries greater weight. For when one moves a single intention without wavering, holding to it from beginning to end, this is called aspiration. As Mencius said, "Hold fast to your aspiration and do not harm your qi" (*Mencius·Gongsun Chou I*).

Thought, however, arises from the consciousness-spirit and differs from intention. Intention is about to move yet has not moved, remaining undifferentiated; thought is clear and distinct, involving specific matters and objects. Once intention becomes fixed, it becomes thought. Thus, in Daoist texts, thought is the spirit of deliberation: the past is recollection, the future is anticipation, and the present is reflection. Yet within thought, there is only the past and future, not the present. Present reflection contains awareness, though slightly more substantial—this is called "right thought." The *Book of Changes* states, "The noble person’s thoughts do not go beyond their proper place" (*Yi·Gen Xiang*), meaning they do not extend into the past or future.

Right thought is close to the Dao, which is why Buddhist scriptures speak of "merging the three minds"—eliminating recollection and anticipation, uniting them into the present mind. This is the aim of refining thought. Past, future, and present constitute the three realms, also known as the three minds. Merging past and future into the present transforms thought into awareness.

Thought arises upon contact, while intention moves spontaneously from the original heart. Consciousness and thought both arise from contact; consciousness is knowledge, giving rise to right and wrong, preference and aversion. Thought has not yet manifested externally, but consciousness is fully revealed. Nature encompasses all that moves spontaneously within, while emotion encompasses all that arises from external contact, generating the heart-mind.

External contact manifesting internally is thought; external contact manifesting externally is consciousness. Though they interact, they are fundamentally different. Intention, when shifted, becomes thought; awareness, when shifted, becomes consciousness. Good turns to evil, emptiness to existence—humanity’s fall stems from this. Consider the distinctions carefully:

Intention is sometimes called the "wish-fulfilling pearl," for true intention is like a pearl—perfectly round and pure, vanishing in an instant. When intention turns into thought, thoughts follow one another endlessly, usurping the true thought. The Buddhist use of "prayer beads" aims to transform deluded thoughts into the "wish-fulfilling pearl." Only the "sword of wisdom" can sever such thoughts—the "sword of wisdom" being the original spirit of true intention.

Within the original spirit, innate knowledge, innate capacity, and luminous awareness are all governed by the root of wisdom, the spiritual root. Only this sword can sever wayward thoughts, for only the true self can dispel the false self. The root of intellect comes from the intermediate state (bardo), which is the soul, having served as master countless times since beginningless kalpas. From remote antiquity through the Three Dynasties, Han, Tang, Six Dynasties, Five Dynasties, Song, Yuan, Ming, and Qing—passing through countless rebirths—it is called "wandering in the cycle of birth and death," like waves in a river, each pushing the next, ceaselessly rolling.

The intermediate state carries knowledge from past lives, which is the "root of intellect." For example, someone who recalls past-life events at birth, or writes as if they had prepared beforehand, demonstrates the functioning of this intellectual root. The root of wisdom is the spiritual root of Heaven and Earth, while the root of intellect is the spiritual root of past kalpas. There is also the root of karma, arising from the good and evil of past actions.

Daoist cultivation is generally called "developing wisdom." When intellect and wisdom unite, it is called "penetration," enabling knowledge of the three realms and ten directions, past and future, through these two roots. The root of wisdom is also the primordial spiritual mechanism. What can move is called "mechanism," hence Daoist texts say, "the mechanism is about to move," or "there is already a moving mechanism."

Before movement, it is fundamentally empty; once the mechanism moves, existence arises—this is "true emptiness does not obstruct wondrous existence." When the mechanism ceases, it returns to emptiness—this is "wondrous existence does not obstruct true emptiness." The "fire timing" in Daoist cultivation is like "the moon’s fullness, dependent on oral transmission."

The timely moment, the wondrousness of mind-to-mind transmission—all are mechanisms. True intent is the mechanism of life; the thoughts generated by the consciousness-spirit are called "mechanisms of destruction." The single character *ji* (mechanism) in the *Yinfu Jing* ("Heaven, Earth, and humanity all possess mechanisms of destruction") refers to the generative transformations of the forward-flowing Dao. The mechanisms of life and destruction are the initial stirrings of intent and thought. "Within grace lies harm": when true intent shifts into thought, thought becomes harm. "Within harm lies grace": when thought ceases and intent forms, intent becomes grace. The *Wuzhen Pian* states, "If you comprehend the mechanisms of destruction and their reversals, you will know that harm gives rise to grace." The two intertransform, and when intent stirs without cessation, it turns into thought. When discriminating thought halts, consciousness halts, and awareness arises. This subtle interplay, with thoughts following one after another, is called "afflictions" in Buddhism. The *Yuanjue Jing* states, "Afflictions are the seeds of samsara." If one follows and drifts with them, one enters the cycle of rebirth. The mind of right and wrong, of liking and disliking, is collectively called "entanglements." When the mind arises in response to phenomena, it becomes entangled, and where there is entanglement, there is birth and death. Only when the mind is free of entanglements can it attain purity.

These two—afflictions and entanglements—are the karmic obstructions of human life. Yet in the mutual dependence of yin and yang, intent, awareness, thought, and consciousness interact. When afflictions cease, they become intent; when entanglements cease, they become awareness. Both arise from emptiness. From nonbeing, being is born; from inherent nature, intent and awareness arise; from emotion, thought and consciousness arise. That which does not move is either true emptiness or inert emptiness. Inert emptiness gives rise to thought and consciousness; true emptiness gives rise to intent and awareness—this is their distinction.

Buddhism speaks of "transforming consciousness into wisdom" and "turning consciousness into wisdom," as elaborated in the *Vijñaptimātratā-siddhi-śāstra*. Consciousness has eight types, the seventh being *manas-vijñāna*, from which one reaches the primordial spirit of the eighth consciousness. The seventh consciousness is the "transmitting consciousness," and transmission is awareness. The principle of turning consciousness into wisdom is no different in Daoism. The *Vajracchedikā Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra* states, "One should give rise to the mind without abiding in anything." This mind is also true intent. It further says, "Empty the four marks, relinquish the three minds." Though Daoist and Buddhist schools use different terms for the functions of mind and nature, appearing slightly distinct, their principle is the same. The sutras say, "It cannot be spoken," and "All phenomena are illusory." "Only when one departs from all marks can the true mind be seen."

What Buddhism calls *anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi* (unsurpassed perfect enlightenment) is also wisdom, the numinous awareness of original nature. However, the mechanism of life arises from the root of wisdom, while the mechanism of destruction arises from the root of intellect—this is what Buddhist teachings do not elaborate. In the work of refining the false to reveal the true, one must cultivate both nature and life. Those of great spiritual capacity may cultivate only the nature aspect, which Buddhism calls the "sudden and complete method." Cultivating both nature and life is the "gradual method," yet sudden and gradual are mutually the root and function of each other. After gradual cultivation, sudden realization is still required to achieve thorough penetration. Those who attain sudden realization must also practice gradually to achieve perfection.

The sudden method does not require the cultivation of life; it returns to emptiness in a single step, fully connecting one’s own emptiness with the emptiness of heaven and earth. Upon completion, one naturally attains a thousand, ten thousand, or countless emanation bodies, with nature perfected and life encompassed within it. This method is only for those of great spiritual capacity, which is why the Perfected Zhang Ziyang taught the dual cultivation of sudden and gradual methods.

Among the nine grades of elixir methods, the highest vehicle is the most true and wondrous Dao—this is what is now being discussed. The great method of dual cultivation is this single vehicle; there is no other. Since Wang Chongyang, it has been expounded with great precision. Previously, Daoist texts spoke in veiled terms, but now there are direct explanations: taking the great void as the crucible and the Supreme Ultimate as the furnace.

The *Cantong Qi* states, "The superior virtue does nothing and does not seek through observation; the inferior virtue acts, and its function never ceases"—this is the distinction between sudden and gradual. The superior virtue does nothing; the inferior virtue acts. The superior virtue focuses solely on cultivating nature; the inferior virtue cultivates both nature and life. The Perfected Liu Huayang’s *Huiming Jing* says, "The superior virtue is the body of the eternal youth. It should be cultivated on the 'day of the five thousand four hundred auspicious births,' when true yang is abundant, enabling sudden enlightenment and attainment of the Dao. Its method is non-doing, yet nothing is left undone. The inferior virtue belongs to those whose bodies have already been broken and must employ effortful practices. The superior virtue is a body of pure yang, requiring no foundation-building; the inferior virtue, having already broken the body and lost true yang, must first build the foundation."

"Superior virtue does nothing and does not seek through observation": *observation* here means awareness, and one need not be aware of the firing times. By merging awareness with qi, returning fully to emptiness is sufficient. "Inferior virtue acts, and its function never ceases": every step has its firing times, and everywhere requires the work of observation. In *Journey to the West*, the bodhisattva Guanyin is the deity of numinous awareness and observation, constantly rescuing from difficulties—this means constantly examining the firing times, ensuring the fire is neither too hot nor the water too cold, striving to maintain a gentle warmth and preserving true intent at every moment. This is what is meant by "a single stroke opens heaven, just uniting yin and yang, the firing time gently warm."

The *Wuzhen Pian* states, "Fire is born from wood, its beacon originally hidden; without drilling and refining, do not forcefully attack." If one uses the consciousness-spirit as the primordial spirit, one will either fall into solitary yang or isolated yin, harming the body—thus one must be cautious.

To practice the false as the true is to begin precisely at the "center" within the great and small voids—neither inside nor outside, dissolving the consciousness-spirit while subtly employing awareness to guard the center and embrace the One. The center is the gateway through which spirit and qi exit and enter; the One is the root of essence and qi. When heart and breath interdepend, the lead cauldron remains gently warm, and desire is first subdued before it arises.

If awareness is overused, it becomes mere knowledge, no longer of the primordial but of the acquired, rendering it entirely useless. One must first seek true intent, uniting awareness with qi. Awareness inherently belongs to the consciousness-spirit, hence the saying "to practice the false as the true." This awareness is ordinary cognition, not innate knowledge. With prolonged practice, something arises from nothing: the Supreme Ultimate contains the Three, and true intent is born. True intent governs the entire body—what is called the "Yellow Dame" or the "earths of Wu and Ji" refers precisely to this.

With this true intent, over time the consciousness-spirit recedes while the primordial spirit advances. True intent exists only in the state of chaos, hence it is called the "chaotic primordial spirit." Yin and yang intermingle to form chaos: spirit is the formless yang, while the breath of mouth and nose is the tangible yin. Yin and yang unite to create chaos. The breath of mouth and nose, along with the consciousness-spirit, are all acquired phenomena. When the acquired returns to chaos, the acquired ceases to exist, and there is no longer any distinction between primordial and acquired—only utter nothingness. From nothing, something arises, and the primordial begins to emerge. One must return to utter emptiness and nothingness to stir the primordial qi into coming from the void.

Emptiness and nothingness correspond to the image of the *Kun* hexagram, pure yin. Only with this pure yin can one stir pure yang, summoning the primordial and drawing it forth. From the impure yin and yang of the acquired, one merges into chaos to return to the pure yin of nothingness, and from this pure yin, pure yang is born. This is what is meant by "the acquired returning to the primordial."

The *Cantong Qi* further states: "Closing above is called *having*; closing below is called *nothing*." Here, "above" and "below" refer to *Kan* and *Li*, not the virtues of above and below. "Closing above" and "closing below" describe the transformation of *Qian* and *Kun* into *Li* and *Kan*, arising from "their function never resting." Because of this closing above and below, *Qian* and *Kun* become *Li* and *Kan*: *Li* ascends above, forming the body of central emptiness, from which thoughts and intellectual deliberations arise, hence it is called *having*. "Closing below" means yang sinks and appears as nothing, hence it is called *nothing*. *Li* above is *having within nothing*; *Kan* below is *nothing within having*. Only by applying the work of closing above and below can one return. Drawing *nothing within having* upward is "offering nothing above," "taking from *Kan* to fill *Li*," after which the primordial spirit can manifest. This is the method of cultivating the lower virtue, refining the self, and laying the foundation.

This method is the "Two Apertures, One Cavity" technique (two apertures forming one cavity). The central yellow cavity contains golden qi (the two apertures are the Gate of the Mysterious Female, that is, the center). This golden qi is of the same kind as the sunken *Kan* below, and only with it can one summon and draw forth. The saying "the golden qi also mutually depends" means the upper gold summons the lower gold, relying entirely on resonance between like kinds, employing the Two Apertures, One Cavity method. Golden qi is white; to know white, one must first guard black, hence the saying "know white, guard black." Black is *Kun*, the body of utter emptiness and pure *Kun*—"guarding black" is returning to emptiness. Following this path, spirit and illumination will naturally come.

Spirit and illumination are the "white" of *Qian*-gold. Only by guarding the body of *Kun* can one stir *Qian*-gold, allowing the true self to arrive, becoming one’s master, and thus one becomes spiritually illuminated. This passage should be studied alongside the *Cantong Chan You*.

**Lecture Six: Knowing White and Guarding Black—First Returning to Chaos**
**Inverting Kan and Li—Dual Cultivation of Nature and Life**

"Know white, guard black, and spirit will illuminate itself."
"White is the essence of metal," the single qi of *Qian*-yang.
"Black is the foundation of water," the dual qi of *Kun*-yin.

At conception, one receives four elements: the nature of *Qian*, the life of *Kun*, the essence of *Li*, and the blood of *Kan*. The nature of *Qian* is the single qi of *Qian*-yang, the ancestral nature of emptiness. The life of *Kun* is the dual qi of *Kun*-yin, the primordial emotion of activity. To clarify with a diagram: parents give birth through desire, and the intermediate yin is drawn forth to unite with primordial emotion (the intermediate yin contains food and lust). Primordial emotion inherently contains the nature of material desires—food and lust—this is the root nature received from parents, but in truth, it is not nature but primordial emotion. Thus, *Journey to the West* depicts Zhu Bajie as the nature of food and lust, the consciousness-spirit. In heaven, he was Marshal Tianpeng, overseeing the Heavenly River, but for harassing Chang’e, he was demoted to the mortal realm. His true nature, a single spirit, mistakenly entered a pig’s womb, and throughout his life, he was lustful and gluttonous.

Primordial emotion is originally transformed from true nature, but because it carries acquired desires for food and lust, it strays outward. The term "entering the womb" is likened to the intermediate yin; calling it a "pig" refers to its straying into material desires, food, and lust, losing sight of the root—hence the analogy of mistakenly entering a pig’s womb. Throughout *Journey to the West*, Tang Sanzang encounters demons because he heeds Bajie’s words, showing how deeply the consciousness-spirit harms. Only at Two-Boundary Mountain does Tang Sanzang awaken the Daoist heart; at Eagle Sorrow Stream, he obtains the dragon-horse. Day after day, he remains earnest and sincere, utterly focused, and later at Gao Village, he subdues Bajie’s conscious emotions. Even after conscious emotions are subdued, they still often stir and cause demonic encounters. Only by the ninety-eighth chapter does consciousness cease to move—the difficulty of refining nature can thus be known.

Here follows the explanation of the precelestial Five Phases as depicted below:

The above diagram represents the precelestial Five Phases: True Emptiness is the Wuji; the Primordial Ancestral Qi is the single qi within the Taiji. East, West, South, and North constitute the Four Symbols. Heaven occupies the North, the sun rises in the East and the moon sets in the West, while the void between them is the fundamental body of the Taiji.

In the *River Chart*, the East holds three and eight, the West four and nine, the South two and seven, and the North one and six. The center, with five and ten, forms the Five Phases, which together comprise three families: the East’s three and the South’s two combine to make five, while the East’s eight and the South’s seven combine to make fifteen; the North’s one and the West’s four combine to make five, and the North’s six and the West’s nine combine to make fifteen. The center stands alone as five, and with the fifteen generative and completive numbers, precisely matches three fives, totaling sixty, though in reality only fifty-five.

Virtue possesses the power of ceaseless generation, thus it is aligned with the East and attributed to Wood. Spirit possesses the power of wondrous clarity, thus it is aligned with the South and attributed to Fire. Virtue and spirit—one still, one moving—stillness being the True Emptiness of virtue, movement the wondrous existence of spirit. Together, they form the number five, constituting one treasury. Emotion possesses the power of compliance, thus it is aligned with the West and attributed to Metal. Essence possesses the power of moistening and storing, thus it is aligned with the North and attributed to Water. Emotion and essence—one moving, one still—stillness being the inner storage of essence, movement the outward expression of emotion. Together, they form the number five, likewise constituting one treasury. The central palace of Wu and Ji Earth is five, standing alone as the number five, forming a single treasury. In the Dao, this is True Intention and True Breath, truly governing the movement and stillness of the Four Symbols. Thus, it is represented by "Primordial Qi," for without qi, virtue, emotion, essence, and spirit cannot move and manifest.

In *Journey to the West*, Sun Wukong corresponds to the North’s one and the West’s four, forming one five; Zhu Wuneng corresponds to the East’s three and the South’s two, forming one five; Sha Wujing stands alone as one five. These three fives together form one Tang Sanzang, while the dragon-horse, ceaselessly revolving in the Dao, signifies the meaning of "never to be parted even for an instant."

The precelestial Five Phases thus transform into three families, all being the functioning of the Primordial Ancestral Qi. Therefore, if one gathers the Ancestral Qi, the five can return to three, and the three can revert to one; otherwise, this cannot be achieved. The so-called three families are: the East’s three and the South’s two, yin and yang paired as one family; the North’s one and the West’s four, yin and yang paired as one family; and the central Wu and Ji Earth, also yin and yang paired as one family. The term "family" signifies harmony within the household. In Daoist texts, whenever the word "family" is used, it carries this meaning, which must not be overlooked.

From the false to cultivate the true, returning the Five Phases to the three families, is to seek the unmoving within movement and the non-still within stillness. Only then can the three fives return to one—this pertains to the precelestial. The postcelestial, however, consists of the five: spirit, essence, soul, corporeal soul, and intention.

Daoist texts state: "There is the precelestial nature and the postcelestial nature." The postcelestial nature is in fact emotion, which governs outward dispersion and constitutes the postcelestial nature—namely, the nature of qi-quality, food, and lust. Mencius said: "Food and sex are nature" (see *Mencius·Gaozi I*). He also said: "Infants know to love their parents. When they grow up, if they have wives and children, they love their wives and children; if they know the beauty of women, they love young women" (see *Mencius·Jinxin I* and *Wanzhang I*). The ancients regarded a child’s affection for parents as the natural bond of heavenly nature, and between father and child, their natures and emotions often resemble each other. This is because the root-nature carried at the time of conception and receiving qi is such. At the moment of receiving qi, when the two emotions of the parents unite and their two qi coalesce, this already carries the parents’ lifelong food, lust, and qi-quality within it. For the parents, this is postcelestial; for the fetus, it is precelestial. Thus, food, lust, and qi-quality are also called nature, but in reality, they are merely emotion—not the heavenly-endowed nature, but inherited emotion.

When the intermediate yin also carries the karmic roots of past afflictions, these karmic roots likewise constitute the nature of food, lust, and qi-quality. If an infant’s emotions do not resemble those of its parents, it is because the intermediate yin carries more of these influences.

Therefore, Daoist texts distinguish the word "nature": that which is mandated by heaven is precelestial, while that received from parents and the intermediate yin is postcelestial. Mencius’s "nature is good" refers to the precelestial ancestral nature; Xunzi’s assertion of "nature is evil" refers to the postcelestial nature of food, lust, and qi-quality. Gaozi’s view that "nature is neither good nor evil, but becomes so through habituation," sharing the same purport as Mozi’s "lament for the silk," judges nature by human conduct, thus also taking a postcelestial stance.

After the breaking of the body, the ancestral nature’s Qian Metal middle line sinks into Kun, and Kun is in fact Kan. Thus, nature falls into the palace of emotion. The primordial emotion of Kun Earth rises into Qian, and Qian’s emptiness becomes Li. Thus, emotion ascends into the palace of nature. Nature and emotion are inverted and intermixed, no longer pure. The unmoving primordial spirit then retreats into concealment, while the restless consciousness-spirit assumes central governance.

The middle line of the Kan trigram is Qian Metal, hence it is called "Metal within Water." The middle line of the Li trigram is Kun Earth, hence it is called "Earth within Fire." Yet Water is originally born from Heaven’s one, and Fire from Earth’s two. That which is born from Heaven has the nature of moistening and descending.

That which is born from Earth has the nature of flaming upward—such is the principle of yin, yang, and the Five Phases. Yet the Five Phases mutually conceal one another: when Kan and Li achieve harmony, Master Zhou said, “The essence of the two and the five mutually conceal their abodes.” This means that within Kan Water lies the true yang fire of the primordial Heaven, while within Li Fire lies the true yin water of the primordial Earth. Thus, the mutual concealment of Water and Fire arises from the intermingling of Qian and Kun.

Qian is originally Metal, yet within it resides yang fire; Kun is originally Earth, yet within it resides yin water. At their origin, they are Water and Fire; in their ultimate state, they become Metal and Earth. The Water of the One is the Earth of the Five; the Fire of the Two is the Metal of the Four—such is the cyclic imagery of the *River Chart*, from which these principles may be deduced.

Qian transforms into Li, which is Fire. Earth conceals Water, and Water flows beneath the ground—for all beneath the Earth is Water. Fire flames upward, Water moistens downward; that which flames upward tends toward the heights, while that which moistens downward tends toward the depths. Water is born of Heaven, Fire is born of Earth, and beneath the Earth lies Fire, as seen in volcanoes and fire wells. Fire wells are used to boil salt—this is the proof.

Metal generates Water, a principle commonly known. Yet Water flows beneath the Earth and is born in the Heavens: “The waters of the Yellow River come from Heaven,” like rainfall descending from the sky, flowing into valleys. All waters originate in Heaven—such are the waters of rivers, lakes, and seas. The waters of the Yellow River arise from the snows atop Kunlun Peak, melting into streams that feed the river—this is the proof.

The Five Phases mutually conceal one another, giving rise to generation, conquest, restraint, and transformation—its principles are most wondrous. Earth conquers Water, yet within Kun Earth lies Water. In ceaseless cycles of waxing and waning, Kan and Li mutually conceal one another, which is the mutual transformation of Water and Fire.

Let us first examine Kan: when Qian and Kun intermingle, one yang line of Qian descends into Kun, forming Kan—the image of Water flowing beneath the Earth. Its middle line is Metal within Water, which is Fire, the true Fire of the Great Yang. Speaking of it singly, it is Fire; viewed within Kan, it is Metal within Water. Kun’s yin naturally tends downward, yet its upward-tending line descends into the center, so its nature constantly seeks to ascend. Fire’s nature is to flame upward, hence it is likened to true Fire—this is the meaning of the hexagram’s image.

From the Five Phases perspective, the Metal within Kan belongs to the *si-you-chou* Metal triad. *Chou* Earth generates Metal, while *ji* Fire conquers Earth—this is a configuration of mutual generation and conquest. Now let us examine Li: one line of Kun ascends into Qian, forming Li. Its upper and lower lines are Qian Metal, while its center is Kun Earth. Kun Earth is *ji* Earth, heavy and turbid, unlike the soaring *wu* Earth. Li is Fire, Earth containing Fire—outwardly yang yet inwardly yin, the image of civil brilliance. Outwardly it flames upward, yet its center is empty; only when there is substance can it burn with radiant light. Thus, Li is the image of Fire, the essence of the Great Yang.

The Great Yang is also outwardly yang yet inwardly yin, containing the Golden Crow. The crow is the rooster, belonging to *you* Metal, yet its nature is yin—this is the image of Fire containing Metal. Within Fire also lies the Golden Lotus: a lotus planted in fire, the lotus belonging to Wood. Fire does not conquer Wood but instead generates it. Within Li also lies mercury, the Wood-fluid within Fire. In Sichuan, there is “fire-washed cloth,” woven from a grass that grows in fire, a plant of volcanic regions—this is Heaven and Earth’s vestige of Fire generating Wood. The Wood born of Fire differs from the grasses and trees of the world; its nature is exceedingly firm and hard, hence it is likened to true mercury.

Li is formed from Kun’s line, and Kun is Earth. Water flows beneath the Earth, so where there is Earth, there is Water—thus Li conceals true Water. From the Five Phases perspective, Li contains the *hai-mao-wei* Wood triad: *wei* is Kun Earth, *mao* is Wood within Fire, and *hai* is true Water. Both *hai* and *zi* are Water: *zi* is Earth-Water, while *hai* is Heaven-Water. Zhu Bajie is Wood within Fire, and Wood’s life begins in *hai*, hence it contains the *hai-mao-wei* Wood triad.

Further examining *wu* and *ji*: Li’s middle line is yin (--), *ji* Earth; Kan’s middle line is yang (—), *wu* Earth—together, the two Earths form a jade tablet. Moreover, the center is Earth, which has no fixed position but flourishes in all four seasons. Thus, the *Cantong Qi* says, “It weaves and binds from beginning to end.” When the Great Ultimate moves, it generates yang—yang Earth; when it is still, it generates yin—yin Earth. Earth is the source of the myriad things: *wu* Earth is pure, while *ji* Earth is turbid. Water and Earth are the root of all generation and transformation, hence Li embraces *ji* Earth, while Kan embraces *wu* Earth.

Now let us discuss the *Na Jia* system: *Na Jia* derives from Heaven’s One and Earth’s Two. *Jia* is the beginning of the primordial *qi*; yang begins with One and ends with Nine, while yin begins with Two and ends with Ten. Thus, Qian embraces *jia* and *ren* (One and Nine), while Kun embraces *yi* and *gui* (Two and Ten). Qian’s origin provides the beginning, Kun’s origin provides generation. All things are born of Water, which is the first of the Five Phases. Water generates Wood, which belongs to the East and possesses the *qi* of ceaseless generation. All transformations arise from Qian and Kun, and Water is the fundamental substance from beginning to end. Thus, *ren* Water is at the beginning, *gui* Water at the end: Qian embraces *jia* and *ren*, while Kun embraces *yi* and *gui*.

Gen and *bing* share the same nature, being yang. Gen is Lesser Yang, *bing* is yang Fire, hence Gen embraces *bing*. Dui is Metal, and within Metal lies Fire; *ding* is yin Fire, hence Dui embraces *ding*.

Kan is Water and yang; Li is Fire and yin. Water and Fire are the beginning of all transformations, while Earth is the root of the Five Phases, flourishing in the four seasons. Thus, Kan embraces yang Earth’s *wu*, and Li embraces yin Earth’s *ji*. Zhen and Dui share the same principle in embracing *ding*: Zhen is yang Wood, *geng* is yang Metal, hence Zhen embraces *geng*. Yang Wood is hard in nature, yin Wood is soft—hard like sandalwood, soft like willow. Zhen and *geng* are both yang and hard, while Xun and *xin* are both yin and soft. *Xin* is soft Metal, hence Xun embraces *xin*. *Geng* Metal is like silver, *xin* Metal like gold—one hard, one soft—thus, like natures attract, and *geng* is embraced by Zhen, *xin* by Xun.

The waxing and waning of the moon relate to the *na jia* system, while in the Dao of cinnabar, the moon’s growth and decline symbolize the firing process. The newly born moon emerges as *geng*; at the first quarter, it resides in the *ding* direction, its image facing upward. Dui is Metal, yet its *qi* differs from Qian’s full Metal, hence Dui embraces *ding*. At the last quarter, it resides in the *bing* direction, its image facing downward—*bing* being the domain of Lesser Yang. Earth generates Metal, yet also buries it; by the last quarter, half is buried, hence this is the direction of *bing* and Gen. By the fifteenth day, it is the full moon, and Qian’s Metal is complete.

The phrase *"liu wu jiu ji"* ("flowing *wu* toward *ji*") refers to the two Earths, *wu* and *ji*, uniting as true intent. In truth, this is the interdependence of heart and breath, spirit and *qi*, forming the union of the two Earths into the *gui* tablet. Within one’s body, Kan and Li are harmoniously aligned; at the Gate of the Mysterious Female, being and non-being are not fixed, but interdependent and embracing.

*The Orthodox Theory of Celestial Immortals* states: *"When moving, move together; when abiding, abide together—through the subtle intent of mutual knowing, they move and abide as one."* *The Scripture of the Wisdom-Life* says: *"Harmonious following is the intent of one heart, one breath, one spirit, one qi—embracing and interdependent."*

To undertake this practice, one must first understand acquired nature and emotions. What one receives from Heaven is the ancestral nature; what one receives from parents is emotion (the prenatal within the postnatal *qi*). When Qian and Kun unite, Qian’s line enters Kun to form Kan—thus, nature transforms into emotion. The primordial ancestral *qi* is trapped in the Gate of Life, becoming the true fire of the Gate of Life, and prenatal nature turns into emotion. When Kun’s line enters Qian to form Li, emotion transforms into nature—namely, the emotion containing temperament, food, and desire enters Qian to become postnatal nature. Because it bears temperament, food, and desire, it becomes governed by the consciousness-spirit, and postnatal emotion turns into nature.

Prenatal nature enters Kun to form Kan, sinking into the Gate of Life. When still, it is nature; when active, it is the primordial spirit, also the true fire of the Gate of Life, also a single yang of Qian. The phrase *"qu kan tian li"* ("taking from Kan to fill Li") means extracting the primordial spirit sunk in Kun and returning it to Li to restore its original state. Before extraction and return, the primordial spirit falls into Kan, hidden and unused—this is the image of yang *qi* lying latent, the "hidden dragon not yet in use." The dragon lies hidden in the abyss, yang *qi* sunken, further obscured by temperament, food, and desire from without (the prenatal within the postnatal *qi* contains temperament, food, and desire). When Kun’s line enters Qian, emotion transforms into nature, now entirely governed by temperament, food, and desire. Though the outer framework is Qian’s line, it has no function; all function lies in the central line, which obscures Qian’s yang nature, leaving only yin without yang. This is the unchecked rampancy of the consciousness-spirit, the nature of the postnatal as such.

Moreover, Kan, with the primordial spirit residing within, is "being within non-being"; Li, with primordial emotion within, is "non-being within being"—the physical body is thus formed. When the consciousness-spirit dominates, the primordial spirit hides itself; nature is lost, and emotion runs wild. The practitioner must harmonize nature and emotion, likened to the union of sun and moon. Li is the sunlight, containing the Golden Crow—namely, the Golden Rooster. Metal is the killing *qi* of *you*; outwardly yang, inwardly yin. Kan is the moonlight, containing the Jade Hare. The hare is the generative *qi* of *mao*; outwardly yin, inwardly yang. This is the mutual concealment of Kan and Li, the essence of the two and five mutually residing in each other’s abode.

*The Book of Changes* says: *"Three and five share the same palace but differ in position."* (See *Xici* II) The rooster functions in inhalation, taking exit as entry; the hare functions in exhalation, taking entry as exit. The hare is a creature of exhalation, without male or female, reproducing through exhaled *qi* from the mouth. The rooster has male and female; their union is also through *qi* resonance. The rooster is most sensitive to yang *qi*—even without a male, it can lay eggs and hatch chicks, all due to the influence of yang *qi*. Yet *"when a hen lays eggs on its own, the chicks are incomplete,"* for it receives only formless *qi*, not tangible essence. The metaphors in the cinnabar texts are wondrously apt.

Li is the human heart—outwardly solid, inwardly empty, hollow within, allowing the seven emotions and six desires to invade. Once filled, it constantly overflows outward, daily drawn to external things. Kan is the celestial heart—outwardly empty, inwardly solid. Its framework is non-being, and non-being generates being. Thus, the celestial heart arises at the moment of returning yang, having no framework to admit wayward thoughts. Moreover, the nature of yang fire constantly seeks to ascend. The phrase *"liu wu jiu ji"* is also *"qu kan tian li"*—raising *wu* toward *ji* to fill the Li palace. *Ji* Earth is Earth’s ten, *wu* Earth is Heaven’s five; five resides within ten, and ten inherently contains the two and five.

Removing two from the ten of *ji* Earth leaves five; combined with the five of *wu* Earth, the two fives resonate to form a *gui*-jade. When the two Earths unite, yin and yang harmonize, and true intent becomes the master—its image like refining pure gold from ore. In numerology, this means discarding the generative numbers to retain the substantive numbers, for five is the substantive number, hence called yang Earth.

The phrase *"withdrawing as entering"* refers to the sun; *"entering as withdrawing"* refers to the moon. The golden rooster governs inhalation, the jade hare governs exhalation. Why does the rooster *"withdraw as entering"*? To crow and produce sound, it must first draw in a breath—thus *"entering as withdrawing."* Why does the hare *"enter as withdrawing"*? The hare, in exhaling to interact, absorbs lunar essence to conceive; only by drawing in lunar essence can it generate—thus *"entering as withdrawing."* The rooster crows at the hour of *yang*’s birth, for when *yang* *qi* stirs, the rooster senses it and cries first. When the moon rises, the hare absorbs its yin *qi* to conceive—both are responses of *qi*-transformation.

How can the rooster first receive the earth’s *yang* *qi*? Because its body contains *yang* *qi*, and like attracts like. The hare follows the same principle. *Entering* is reception, *exiting* is activation—*The Kinship of the Three* uses the rooster and hare’s functions to illustrate the principle of inversion. One exhalation, one inhalation; one *kan*, one *li*—the dual functions of *kan* and *li*. *Function* refers to their operation: *"withdrawing as entering"* means outward exit and inward entry; *"entering as withdrawing"* means outward entry and inward exit. Though there is inner and outer, the operation lies within, and their distinction arises from the difference between precelestial and postcelestial.

The precelestial is the true breath’s opening and closing, inversely proportional to the postcelestial ordinary breath’s exit and entry—*The True Principle of Celestial Immortality* explains this in detail. Externally, the sun and moon move back and forth; internally, the sun and moon invert and reverse. The internal sun and moon are the heart and the body, and the body is the *mingmen*. The external sun and moon are breath. The infant’s abdomen contains true breath, though unaware; those who break the body lose it.

The cultivator must first return to the child’s body, seeking its natural opening and closing. Discovering this opening and closing, one obtains true breath. True breath resides below the navel—one opening, one closing. With each opening, *yang* *qi* ascends, exhaling to connect with the celestial root; with each inhalation, *yin* *qi* descends, inhaling to connect with the earthly root. Opening and closing are ascent and descent. The breath of mouth and nose operates inversely: exhaling opens the orifices, inhaling closes them; one comes (inhales) to open, one goes (exhales) to close, and the spirit’s exit and entry follow accordingly.

Heart and breath depend on each other, but the dependence differs. The primordial spirit relies on true breath, the consciousness spirit relies on ordinary breath. Joy and anger arise within and manifest externally—anger sends *qi* outward, joy draws *qi* inward—this is evidence of the consciousness spirit’s dependence on ordinary breath. The cultivator must seek the primordial spirit’s reliance on true breath.

The postcelestial person lacks primordial spirit and true breath; one must refine the four limbs to extract primordial spirit and true breath, still seeking them within the bodily consciousness spirit. First, one must establish nothingness, placing oneself in chaos—otherwise, not clinging to form is clinging to emptiness. Clinging to form leads to the mundane, clinging to emptiness loses all—both are misguided.

To seek true breath, one must first enter chaos; to seek primordial spirit, one must dissolve the consciousness spirit. *Li* is the consciousness spirit—its emotions scatter outward, restless and wandering. *The Kinship* says, *"The radiant pearl of the great yang constantly seeks to leave the person. Only when obtaining golden blossoms does it turn and remain"*—this refers to the consciousness spirit’s tendency to scatter outward. To retain it, one must first obtain golden blossoms; only when golden blossoms restrain the radiant pearl can heart and breath depend on each other.

*"Nine returns"* is the method of obtaining golden blossoms; *"seven reversions"* is the method of restraining the radiant pearl. Seven reversions occur within the body; nine returns must come from without, yet if one grasps the method, it is not difficult. The *yang* golden blossom of *kan* constantly seeks to ascend; above, if there is a substance of the same kind, the radiant pearl can descend to it. Below, if there is something to resonate with, it will respond and harmonize.

True water and true fire—*Journey to the West* illustrates this most aptly and clearly. Red Boy’s fire is true fire, which the waters of the four dragon kings cannot extinguish; only Guanyin’s willow-branch water can quench it. The willow is a yielding wood, and Guanyin dwells in the Southern Sea—this is true water descending to extinguish true fire. Withered Pine Ravine is *kan*, the Southern Sea is *li*; above *li*, true water and true fire interact at the central palace—this is the image of *kan* and *li* mutually containing and intermingling.

The *yang* of *kan* ascending becomes warm *qi*; the *yin* of *li* descending becomes cool fluid—this is the verification of true water and true fire. The cultivator must not neglect this. The true *yang* fire within *kan* is the golden blossom, also called white gold. *"Knowing the white"* means knowing this alone; *"guarding the black"* means guarding *kun*, the boundless, empty, mysterious aperture.

Without clinging to the four phases, all things are empty—this is the image of pure *kun*. Within pure *kun*, there begins a single *qi* flowing freely above and below. This is the one *yang* born beneath heavy *yin*, like the winter solstice’s transition from *kun* to *fu*. Once this *yang* arises, it is called *"the numinous comes of itself."* If one cannot reach this state of emptiness and stillness, how can true *yang* be born?

Thus, understanding this principle, one must guard *kun*. Sanfeng said: *"To know the white, one must guard the black; to guard the black, one must know the white. Only by knowing the white can one guard the black; guarding the black is precisely knowing the white."* These words are intimate and profound—all the work of *"emptying the heart and filling the belly"* lies herein.

*Heaven and Earth unite in virtue; sun and moon unite in brightness.*
*"To gain once is to gain forever"*—this is called the Great Person.
All things arise from earth; the Five Phases emerge through earth.
In the *Hetu*, one and six: one is the celestial water of the primordial, the water of prior heaven; six belongs to posterior heaven, where earth’s six combines with heaven’s one, one added to five to form six, for the central palace’s earth-five is added to it. All posterior Five Phases follow this pattern, as illustrated below:
South’s earth-two is fire, its generating number two, its completing number seven—two plus five makes seven.
East’s heaven-three is wood, earth-eight completes it—three plus five makes eight.
West’s earth-four generates metal, heaven-nine completes it—four plus five makes nine.
One, two, three, four, five are generating numbers; six, seven, eight, nine, ten are completing numbers.
Generating numbers plus five become completing numbers.
Five is emptiness, at the center as earth; one, two, three, four all arise from emptiness.
The posterior Five Phases cannot depart from emptiness, thus they are completed by adding the five of central emptiness.
The five of central emptiness is central earth, which is why earth flourishes in all four seasons, beginning and ending all things.
The Five Phases are born from the Supreme Ultimate; the Supreme Ultimate is fundamentally empty—emptiness is earth. The Five Phases arise from earth and are completed by earth.
Among the Five Phases, earth is the most essential, hence its comparison to names such as *"central yellow,"* *"spirit chamber,"* *"center,"* and *"yellow court."*
For the root of creation and the primordial foundation of the myriad things is this earth.
North’s one is *kan*; south’s two is *li*. *Kan* receives *wu*—*wu* is the earth within *kan*.
The water of north’s one arises from emptiness, thus water contains earth.
Earth’s two is *yin*, is *kun*—all *yin* of *kun* is earth. Its nature is heavy, turbid, and congeals downward to form earth, which is soil.
*Li* is born of earth’s two, thus it receives *ji* earth.
*Wu* and *ji* are the earth of the central palace, received into *kan* and *li* through matching with *jia*.
Yang earth *wu* is true emptiness within wondrous existence;
yin earth *ji* is wondrous existence within true emptiness—this is the meaning of *yin* and *yang* mutually containing one another.
Five is *wu* earth; ten is *ji* earth. Daoists say five is *wu*, and *ji* is also five, not calling it ten because two fives make ten—this is the meaning of two earths forming a *gui*.
Two fives form the character *"ten,"* which contains five within. When heaven and earth were yet unopened, a vertical stroke | was *yang*, a horizontal stroke — was *yin*. One *yin* and one *yang*, one vertical and one horizontal, form ten.
The center of ten is the point where *yin* and *yang* intersect, hence it is called the *"central palace,"* called *"five dwelling within ten,"* which is the principle of three, five, and one.
The central palace is empty and void. The *Cantongqi* says: *"The heart is focused, neither vertical nor horizontal"*—neither vertical nor horizontal means being at the center of the cross; if vertical or horizontal, it is partial *yin* or partial *yang*.
Later expressions such as *"crossroads,"* *"cross,"* *"seen by ten eyes, pointed at by ten hands,"* and *"the buddhas of the ten directions and three times"* all derive from this one vertical and one horizontal stroke.

The above discussion of the *shen* of consciousness—its thoughts, reflections, and considerations—remains incomplete; here it is supplemented:
Thought, reflection, and consideration are one. Consideration pertains to the future, while thought pertains to the past. Examining the ancient glosses on the character *si* (thought), in terms of meaning, *xiang* (imagination) and *si* (thought) differ: *xiang* also pertains to the past, while *si* is the present.
When a single thought arises and does not yet shift, this is *si*.
The character *si* is composed of *tian* (field) above and *xin* (heart) below—the thought that arises from the heart-field is the moment the intent just stirs, hence it is called *"the present."*
Yet in a single instant it passes, becoming a *"thought"*—the head of thought.
The head of thought is purely *si*; with the slightest shift, it becomes consideration, imagination, or thought. Thus, it can be divided into three: *si* (thought), *lü* (consideration), and *xiang* (imagination).
Confucius said: *"The noble person’s thoughts do not transgress their position"* (see *Analects*, *"Xian Wen"*). The heart-field is this *"position."*
The moment it stirs is *si*; transgressing the position is *xiang* or *lü*.
The *Yijing* says: *"Fluctuating to and fro, friends follow your thoughts"* (see *Yijing*, *"Xian"*). This describes *si*—coming and going incessantly, in great multitude.
This *"si"* is the same as in *"the noble person’s thoughts do not transgress their position,"* and does not enter the realm of *xiang* or *lü*.
It also says: *"What thought? What consideration?"* (see *Yijing*, *"Xici Xia"*).
From the perspective of the *shen* of consciousness, all employ *si* at the outset.
The moment a thought arises is called *si*, *sixiang* (thought), or *silü* (thought and consideration). The instant it arises is *si*; in a moment it becomes *xiang* or *lü*.
*"The Three Hundred Poems can be summed up in a single phrase: thoughts without deviance"* (see *Analects*, *"Wei Zheng"*).
At the moment of stirring, there is no deviance; with the slightest shift, deviance arises.
*"Thoughts without deviance"* is also the meaning of *"thoughts not transgressing their position."*
The *Analects* says: *"The noble person has nine thoughts: in seeing, think clarity; in hearing, think acuity; in countenance, think warmth; in demeanor, think reverence..."* (see *Analects*, *"Li Ren"*).
Observing this, the meaning becomes even clearer.

Why is the character "thinking" (*si*) placed above "clarity," "acuity," "warmth," and "reverence"? Precisely because when intention first stirs within, the eyes can immediately see clearly, the ears can immediately hear keenly, the countenance can immediately become warm, and the demeanor can immediately become reverent. There is a governing force at work here—*si* is the master of these nine, revealing that the moment the spirit of perception stirs, it is *si*. The noble person thus possesses nine forms of *si*, while the petty person, though also possessing *si*, allows it to shift and become perception (*shi*). When the root and dust intermingle, the six perceptions (*liushi*) take form. Only *si* is easy to withdraw and does not flow outward, which is why the Confucians regard *si* as correct and say, "The noble person has nine forms of *si*."

In *Journey to the West*, when Tang Sanzang encounters the nine-headed lion, it is the steed of the Great Compassionate Heavenly Worthy of Salvation. The nine lions are called the "Nine Numinous Primordial Sages," analogous to the noble person’s nine forms of *si*. *Si* is the lion, and *ling* (numinous) is the numinosity of *si*. Numinous *si* is the resource for a person to attain sagehood, but with the slightest shift, it descends into craft and depravity. The Nine Numinous Primordial Sages, originally aligned with the correct Way, transform into true intention (*zhenyi*), yet when they privately descend into the mundane world, they become demons and monsters. This arises from *si* turning into selfishness (*si*), and the nine forms of *si* becoming ordinary selfishness, where the Dao-heart fades and deluded intention arises. Without selfishness, one may become a sage or a worthy; the inscription reads, "Master and Lion Receive and Transmit Together as One." *Si* is the master, the lion is selfishness, and "receiving and transmitting" is the longevity of the beast. With selfishness, one is a beast; without *si* and returning to true intention and primordial spirit (*yuanshen*), one attains the longevity of heaven and becomes a sage. This saying is most profound. The nine forms of *si* returning to one is the return to sagehood and authenticity.

Buddhist texts often use characters in ways that do not align with their ancient meanings. For example, "Do not think of past events; do not conceive of future ones." *Xiang* (conceiving) refers to retrospective thought, belonging to what has already passed, so one cannot speak of "conceiving the future." Even *yi* (recollection) implies remembrance of the past, and to apply it to the future is clearly inconsistent.

Before joy, anger, sorrow, and happiness are expressed, this is called "the center" (*zhong*), the primordial spirit (*yuanshen*). When expressed in moderation, it is called "harmony" (*he*), the primordial emotion (*yuanqing*). If not harmonious and allowed to flow into delusion, it becomes the seven emotions (*qiqing*). In this moment, a single shift occurs: the center is the primordial spirit, harmony is the primordial emotion, and disharmony is delusion and emotion. Deluded emotion also arises from the deluded expression of primordial emotion, and when deluded emotion is expressed, it becomes the seven emotions.

As the saying goes: "Filial piety, fraternal duty, loyalty, and trust are rooted in heavenly nature." Nature (*xing*) is fundamentally empty; what is called "heavenly nature" is the acquired nature (*houtian zhi xing*), which is primordial emotion (*yuanqing*). Thus, it is called "emotion" (*qing*), arising from primordial nature (*yuanxing*) and involving duality.

Here, I illustrate this with a diagram (incorporating integrity and shame under the character for "moderation," without further elaboration):

What is called "refining the self to establish the foundation" refers to the self (*ji*) as the *ji* of *wuji* (戊己), the earth of the self. Within *li* (☲, fire), it is the spirit of perception (*shishen*), which at the first stir tends to rush outward. Though it soars to *wu* earth (戊土), this signifies a light and upward tendency, not an outward rush. Refining the self is refining the spirit of perception, like forging metal into a vessel. However, forging requires *wu* earth; when *wu* is added to *ji*, the firm overcomes the yielding, and with one yin and one yang, it can congeal and become motionless.

*Wu* earth is the primordial spirit, sunken in *kan* (☵, water). When nature transforms into emotion, it is drawn from this sinking and added to *ji*, so that the self can stabilize, and the two earths form a *gui* (圭, jade tablet). The self is the ego; *ji* derives from the heavenly stems, borrowed to refer to the self. The meaning of the character originally comes from phonetic loan, also originating from Daoist texts. *Ji* earth is the acquired earth, used to name the self, and thus refining the self may also be called "refining one’s own self." Taking *wu* earth to refine *ji* and build the foundation is the meaning of "flowing *wu* to *ji*." Only *wu* earth can build the foundation of *ji* earth, hence it is called "refining the self to establish the foundation."

*Wu* is primordial, *ji* is acquired; *wu* resides in *kan*, as one line of *qian* (☰, heaven). *Qian* is heaven, and when *qian* and *kun* (☷, earth) open and unfold, first there is yin and yang, and only afterward are heaven and earth divided. *Wu* earth generates *qian* metal, being the root of *qian*, existing before *qian* to give rise to it. *Ji* earth is *kun* earth; only with heaven does earth exist, and the earth within it is acquired, born of *kun*. *Li* generates earth, which is *ji* earth; *kan* generates metal, which is *wu* earth. That which gives birth to me is primordial; that which I give birth to is acquired. Among the five phases, that which gives birth to me is nature, and that which I give birth to is the spirit of perception. That which gives birth to me is *wu* earth, and that which I give birth to is *ji* earth—this is their distinction.

*Li* and *kan* internally contain the fire trigram of *yin*, *wu*, and *xu* (寅午戌), and the water trigram of *shen*, *zi*, and *chen* (申子辰). Their mutual generation and restriction are as described in the diagram. The wood trigram of *hai*, *mao*, and *wei* (亥卯未) and the metal trigram of *si*, *you*, and *chou* (巳酉丑) belong to *qian* and *kun*. Taking *wu* from *kan* to refine *ji* in *li* is the meaning of "flowing *wu* to *ji* for use," where "use" means function. Taking *kan* to fill *li* is the substance, where "substance" is the original body, the original body of *kan* and *li*, which is the function of *wu* and *ji*.

Commonly, this is called "returning essence to replenish the brain," which speaks in terms of imagery—transforming essence into *qi*, ascending from below. It is also called "accumulating essence and amassing *qi*," or "refining essence into *qi*," which speaks in terms of practice. Essence differs: there is primordial essence and acquired essence. Acquired essence is lewd essence, yin essence, derived from the essence of the five grains and food processed by the intestines and stomach. With this essence, the complexion is radiant and the skin is lustrous—these are the functions of yin essence; otherwise, one becomes withered. Essence nourishes the body, harmonizes the constructive and defensive *qi*, and circulates through the blood, which in turn moves by means of *qi*. Only then can the four limbs be lustrous and the body preserved, distinct from lewd essence.

Lewd essence is the essence of *qi*-transformation, originally formless; yin essence, however, arises from form and becomes formed essence. Lewd essence, as formless *qi*, transforms into formed essence—both are acquired essence, distinct from the formless primordial essence. Refining essence does not mean refining these two—lewd essence and yin essence—but refining the formless primordial essence-*qi* to transform it into *qi*.

We all possess broken bodies; if we wish to practice the work of refining essence into *qi*, we must proceed step by step. First, one must return to emptiness. Refining essence into *qi* is the work of "nine returns and seven reversions," but before this, one must first employ the work of returning to emptiness, which is the sudden method of nature-cultivation.

If we possess great spiritual capacity, returning to emptiness alone is sufficient, and no other work is needed. Whether one can follow this Way depends entirely on our virtue and spiritual capacity. In the end, the work of returning to emptiness is precisely this.

For the cultivator of the Dao, the beginning and end lie in this single matter of returning to emptiness. Returning to emptiness is the work that completes both beginning and end. If one knows what comes first, one draws near to the Dao.

Confucius said: *"Exhaust principle and fulfill nature, to reach destiny."* (See *Yi Jing*, "Shuo Gua") What we now explain is the exhaustion of principle. Only by exhausting principle can one fulfill nature; principle is the principle of creation and transformation. The generation of heaven and earth, the birth of humans and things, the unfolding of the cosmos, the conception of human life, and the ceaseless transformation and generation of the myriad things—all follow this principle of beginning and completion.
