Humans and things are no exception: one must first exhaust this principle to know the Supreme Ultimate, to know that all things arise from the single *qi* of the Supreme Ultimate. The Supreme Ultimate is born of the Ultimateless; one knows that *qi* arises from emptiness, and from *qi* all things are born. Exhausting this principle allows one to fulfill nature, for nature is emptiness. The generation and transformation of creation arise from emptiness, for from emptiness arises being, and from the void comes fullness.

Exhausting principle, one knows this empty nature and the generation of the myriad kinds. When a person dies, their nature returns to the great emptiness. Confucius said: *"Without knowing life, how can one know death?"* (See *Analects*, "Xian Jin") To know life is to know death; before birth and after death, it is the same. Before birth, nature abides in the great emptiness; after death, it likewise returns to emptiness—this is called *"complete reception, complete return."* "Complete return" means fully returning to emptiness.

The intermediate state also transforms and unites with nature, returning to heaven—only then can one speak of "complete return." If one has karmic roots, one cannot return to heaven but may enter the wheel of rebirth or fall into the six paths to receive retribution. Only when one unites with nature and returns to heaven can it be called *"complete reception, complete return."* This is indeed the ultimate aim of the cultivator of the Dao.

Having exhausted principle and fulfilled nature, one knows what nature is like before birth and after death. That which is neither born nor destroyed is emptiness. When nature and the spirit of consciousness unite and return, they are neither born nor destroyed but together return to emptiness.

*"To reach destiny"* means that by fulfilling nature, one attains destiny, and destiny is contained within it. Primordial nature and destiny were originally united, separating only after entering the acquired state. After the body is broken, nature transforms into emotion.

*"To reach"* means the union of nature and destiny, both returning to emptiness. By fulfilling nature, destiny follows. The Buddha speaks of the sudden method, beginning with returning to emptiness and carrying it to its utmost, where from nothing arises being, and nature and destiny are reborn, uniting with heaven in body. Thus, the supremely wise suddenly transcend and awaken fully, their spiritual nature communicating with heaven and earth, and their destiny likewise flowing freely—this is the method of *"once attained, eternally attained."*

Once one attains it, one attains it forever. However, only the supremely wise can achieve this; otherwise, one may fail to capture the tiger and end up worse than those who proceed step by step. Ordinarily, one must follow the sequence of the Golden Elixir, the Great Elixir, exiting spirit and returning to emptiness.

The sudden method, as the Buddha says, is *"a single morning’s sudden awakening, penetrating earth and heaven"*—this is *"sudden liberation,"* which is also the principle of *"hearing the Dao in the morning, one may die in the evening without regret."* (See *Analects*, "Li Ren") Such work is not easy; it requires long cultivation to achieve.

When emptiness is perfected, all methods are complete—this is what the *Wuzhen Pian* means by *"within a single hour, the elixir is formed."* Master Zhu said *"in an instant,"* and Daoist texts speak of *"instantaneous ascension,"* calling it the *"spiritual elixir."* The spirit is one’s own primordial spirit, which, being numinous, ascends—this is called *"elixir formation."* Chen Niuwan said: *"All comes down to a moment’s work, bringing eternal ease and joy."* This is precisely it.

Returning to emptiness is returning to the void, which is also returning to *Kun*. *Kun* is the body of emptiness. Yet circulating through the six voids, *Kun* resides in the southwest—*"In the southwest, one finds companions"* (see *Yijing*, *Kun* hexagram), which signifies the meaning of *"something arising from nothing."* *"Something arising from nothing"* is true emptiness not obstructing wondrous existence, hence *"In the southwest, one finds companions."* *Companions* are of the same kind, as the *Yijing* says: *"Like sounds resonate, like qi seek one another. Water flows toward the wet, fire tends toward the dry. Clouds follow the dragon, wind follows the tiger. When the sage acts, all things behold."* (See *Yijing*, *Qian* *Wenyan*) *"In the southwest, one finds companions"* is the image of resonance among the like. Utmost emptiness and profound stillness are the function of *Kun*; when stillness reaches its extreme and moves, the empty chamber generates brightness—this is the resonance of *Kun*. *Gen* and *Kun* are opposites, hence *"In the northeast, one loses companions."* (See *Yijing*, *Kun* hexagram) For *Gen* is the mountain of form, called the *"mountain of shape,"* which Daoist texts compare to the *"colored body,"* differing from *Kun* as the earth of formlessness. In the northeast, it cannot contain, hence *"losing companions."* The southwest is emptiness; only emptiness can contain, giving birth to the myriad things, from which all things derive their sustenance. Thus the *Wuzhen Pian* says: *"The medicine is born in the southwest, its native land."* *Kun* is the southwest, its body purely yin, which is also the great void. Those who cultivate the Dao begin here, where all six lines are empty—this is precisely the image of returning to emptiness. Proceeding step by step, one reverts to *Qian*: one yang arises as *Fuxi* (Earth over Thunder), two yangs arise as *Lin* (Earth over Marsh), three yangs as *Tai* (Heaven over Earth), four yangs as *Dazhuang* (Thunder over Heaven), five yangs as *Guai* (Marsh over Heaven), and six yangs as *Qian*. For *Kun* is the hexagram of emptiness; from emptiness, one generates one, two, three, four, five, and six, reaching *Qian*—thus restoring the body of pure yang.

Cultivation of the Dao also begins with the *Lü* hexagram: *"Lü is the foundation of virtue."* *Guai* (Marsh over Heaven) is *"the virtue of treading,"* the qi of generating life. *Virtue* is *attainment*; from this, one attains ceaseless transformation. The *"virtue"* by which the myriad things receive the qi of life is the *"primordial qi of Kun that nurtures life,"* distinct from the *"primordial qi of Qian that initiates existence."* This qi is the precelestial true yang qi, the primordial qi of the Great Ultimate. Its body is fundamentally empty and nameless; by force, it is called the *Dao*. *Virtue* is the common qi of life, the postcelestial qi; the true yang qi is *illuminating virtue*. *Illuminating virtue* is the qi of yin and yang conjoined in virtue, hardness and softness in balance. *Virtue* is spoken of in relation to the Dao. *"The Dao that can be spoken is not the eternal Dao"*—it is nameless, formless, imageless, the true emptiness. When named, it is called the *Dao*; the *Dao* is *guidance*, leading the myriad things along their proper paths, enabling them to fully receive and fully return. It is *"the nameless origin of heaven and earth,"* the Dao before the division of heaven and earth. *Virtue* has a name: *"The named is the mother of the myriad things."* Yet *illuminating virtue* encompasses both.

Let us explain in detail: The *Great Learning* says: *"The Way of the Great Learning lies in illuminating the luminous virtue."* The first *"illuminating"* is a verb, the second *"luminous"* an adjective. The Way of the *Great Learning* must first clarify *"illuminating virtue,"* that is, understanding the precelestial one qi, the primordial true qi containing the original qi, the ancestral qi of the origin. *"Great Learning"* is the learning of the great person. Daoists call this the *"Perfected Person,"* Buddhists the *"Bodhisattva,"* and Confucians the *"Great Person."* The *Great Learning* is the learning of the great person. The *Yijing* says: *"What is meant by the ‘Great Person’ is one whose virtue accords with heaven and earth, whose brightness accords with the sun and moon."* (See *Yijing*, *Qian* *Wenyan*) The *"illuminating virtue"* of the *Great Learning* derives from these two phrases. It further says: *"Whose order accords with the four seasons, whose auspiciousness and inauspiciousness accord with ghosts and spirits. Preceding heaven, heaven does not oppose; following heaven, one complies with the seasons."* (Ibid.) Accord with heaven and earth in virtue, accord with the sun and moon in brightness—this is the one qi of yin and yang conjoined. Heaven and earth, sun and moon, are all yin and yang. Yin and yang conjoined form the Great Ultimate; the Great Ultimate is emptiness, which is also the One. The One is spirit. As explained in the first and second lectures, *"illuminating virtue"* pairs with the sun and moon as *"change,"* hardness and softness in balance. By the same principle, *"illuminating"* also derives from the sun and moon, taking the meaning of yin and yang conjoined. Yin and yang are the Two Modes; conjoined as One, they generate the qi of heaven, earth, and humanity. In Liu Zhitang’s commentary on the *Great Learning*, this qi is called *"the principle-qi within the Great Ultimate."* In sum, it is the ancestral qi without beginning that generates the myriad things. The Great Ultimate is fundamentally empty; when it moves, yang arises, and this qi is born—thus heaven and earth are established. Therefore, the sage takes the qi of life as *virtue* and the ancestral qi of life as *"illuminating virtue."* The *Great Person* is one who possesses this *"illuminating virtue."* *"Preceding heaven, heaven does not oppose; following heaven, one complies with the seasons"*—this qi generates heaven, being precelestial; yet after heaven and earth divide, this qi also generates the myriad things. When the time comes, it generates; all resonances are answered. Hence it is said *"does not oppose"* and *"complies with the seasons."*

These two lines have already delineated *"illuminating virtue"* with utmost clarity. Yet the Song Confucians, lacking a proper foundation and unaware that *"illuminating virtue"* possesses substantive meaning, merely glossed its surface characters—thus obscuring the Great Dao.

To study the Way of the *Great Person*, one must first comprehend the principles of yin-yang transformation: formless, imageless, soundless, odorless, supremely vast, supremely firm, supremely empty, supremely real—true emptiness and wondrous being, undivided in its response to things, unfathomable in its generation of life. This *"illuminating virtue"* is the great sovereign of the cosmos. To realize this is to unify the myriad transformations, returning from the expansive to the essential, restoring one’s empty numinosity—this very emptiness is the fundamental substance of *"illuminating virtue."* This is what it means to exhaust the principles of reason.

The phrase *"In making bright the illuminating virtue"* pertains to *knowing*; the next, *"In renewing the people,"* pertains to *ability*; and the third, *"In resting in perfect goodness,"* represents the union of *knowing* and *ability*—the foundational work of the Great Dao. These three lines alone encompass the supreme vehicle of the Way in its entirety, leaving nothing unaddressed. The transmission of the Dao within the Confucian school is nothing more than this—it is the all-pervading Way.

Now, the character *"亲"* in *"亲民"* means precisely *"to be intimate with,"* and should not be interpreted as *"to renew."* In ancient times, the Way of cultivating the self and governing the state was one and the same: *"In obscurity, one perfects oneself alone"*—this is the Way of the *Great Person*; *"In prominence, one benefits all"*—this is the Way of the sagely ruler. Thus, the sages of the Three Teachings all took the *heart-mind* as sovereign and the *body* as the people. The *Daodejing* and the *Su Shu* (the *Su Shu* being Confucius’ work, namely the *Analects*) both convey this meaning. The so-called *"being intimate with the people"* is none other than the *heart-mind sovereign* descending to be intimate with the *body-people.*

The *sovereign* is the sovereign fire, the spirit of the heart-mind; the *people* are the people’s fire, the qi of the body. Beyond these, there is the *"ministerial fire,"* also called the *"minister fire,"* the fire of the Triple Burner. In the practice of the Dao, one matches spirit with *li* (☲), forming *qian* (☰)—noble, honored, great, superior, sovereign, husband, sun, crow, dragon; and matches qi with *kan* (☵), forming *kun* (☷)—humble, base, small, inferior, people, wife, moon, hare, tiger.

*"In being intimate with the people"* means the noble descending to the base, the superior engaging the inferior, the sun uniting with the moon, the dragon inhaling the tiger, the crow seeking the hare, the male descending to the female—this is the Way of uniting spirit with qi. Spirit is the master of the human body, inherently most noble; qi, sunken in *kan*, is inherently most base. Thus, the character *"亲"* is employed to reveal the work: the entire meaning lies in the union of spirit and qi, the interdependence of heart-mind and breath.

The later interpretation of *"新民"* refers to the daily replenishment of primordial qi, as when the body of a child is newly born—this meaning differs from the original. Zhu Xi, misled by the *"新"* in the subsequent text, erroneously assumed this character should also be *"新."* This is a mistake. This line is itself a secret formula for cultivation: *"The original Great Dao is not a complicated affair—it lies solely in concentrating the spirit into the qi-cavern."* Concentrating spirit to unite with qi, nurturing it day by day, one naturally becomes a *Great Person* of *"illuminating virtue."*

The *people’s fire* is the true yang fire within the Gate of Life. Originally, it is the precelestial nature, of the same substance as spirit; postcelestially, it transforms into emotion and is called the *people’s fire.* Daoist texts speak of *"the same origin with different names, known by few."* Laozi said: *"These two emerge together but differ in name; together they are called the Mysterious. The Mysterious of the Mysterious—this is the gateway to all wonders."* The sovereign fire is one Mystery; the people’s fire is another. When the two Mysteries unite, all wonders arise—this is what is meant by *"the gateway to all wonders."*

In antiquity, the Dao was the unifying principle: those among the people who possessed the Dao were honored as sovereigns. The sovereign fire and the people’s fire share a single source; yet within the postcelestial physical body, distinctions arise between sovereign and people, superior and inferior. The physical body regards the people’s fire as the treasure of life. *"The people are the root of the state; when the root is firm, the state is at peace."* Phrases such as *"The sovereign is light; the people are heavy"* (*Mencius·Jinxin II*) all convey the meaning of *"being intimate with the people"*—this is the learning that *"extends to destiny."*

The phrase *"In resting in perfect goodness"* is the foundational work of cultivating the Dao. *"Resting"* is the *zhi* of *zhi-guan* (cessation and contemplation); the character *"止"* is of utmost importance, while *"perfect goodness"* is the *"wondrous"* aspect of the Dao. The interdependence of heart-mind and breath is *"perfect goodness."* Goodness and evil arise in mutual opposition; *perfect goodness* transcends both good and evil, beyond all duality. This state is most difficult to describe: Daoists call it *"wondrous,"* Confucians call it *"perfect goodness,"* and Buddhists call it *"ineffable."* It is the heart-mind beyond the seven emotions, the consciousness beyond the six senses—heart-mind without heart-mind, intention without intention. The *Zhong Mei Xin Fa* describes this state within the ineffable, using analogies to clarify it most aptly.

*"Resting in perfect goodness"* refers to the heart-mind and breath, spirit and qi, uniting in the realm of perfect goodness, and to the cessation of thoughts and preservation of spirit in the state of perfect goodness. But where is this realm? What is *perfect goodness?* The *Doctrine of the Mean* states: *"Yan Hui’s character was such that upon grasping a single goodness, he would hold it fast in his bosom and never lose it."* *"A single goodness"* also conveys the meaning of *"perfect goodness"*—having attained it, one holds it fast and never loses it. The phrase *"holds fast in his bosom"* is even more dynamic than *"preserve."* *"Bosom"* refers to the *xuanying* (mysterious breast).

Where one clings tenaciously and holds fast in the bosom—that is the realm of perfect goodness, and it is also the Gate of the Mysterious Female. Thus, the heart-mind becomes still as water. The Buddhist tradition speaks of "cessation-and-contemplation" and "dual cultivation"; the Confucian calls it "knowing where to rest"; the Daoist terms it "contemplating the wondrous," "contemplating the aperture," or "contemplating the wondrous aperture"—all signify the meaning of "knowing where to rest." The practice of "cessation-and-contemplation in dual cultivation" is no different from the practice of "returning to emptiness." The *Great Learning* explains: *"Know where to rest, and thereafter there is stability; with stability comes tranquility; with tranquility comes serenity; with serenity comes reflection; with reflection comes attainment."* All that is described here pertains to the firing process. The realm of perfect goodness is the Gate of the Mysterious Female, and it is also the Gate of Dao and *yi*. Dao is emptiness, the nameless beginning of Heaven and Earth; *yi* is qi—the vast, overflowing qi that arises from the accumulation of *yi*, which is the qi of utmost yang. The Gate of Dao and *yi* is the meaning of the mysterious yang. In Daoist texts, this is "gold within water," born of the accumulation of *yi*—thus, the two are one. The Gate of the Myriad Wonders is the realm of perfect goodness (note that "realm" and "realm/place" differ). *"To rest in perfect goodness"* means to still the heart-mind, to still the heart-mind in the realm of perfect goodness, where the realm is the state of the heart-mind. It is the manifestation of qi in resting upon perfect goodness, which is also the stilling of the heart-mind in emptiness. Within true emptiness there is wondrous existence, not dead emptiness—this is the realm of perfect goodness. The Buddha teaches: *"True emptiness does not obstruct wondrous existence; wondrous existence does not obstruct true emptiness."* This is the explanation of the realm of perfect goodness. When the mind is undistracted, a mass of misty qi coalesces without dispersing, yet not a single thought arises—this is the realm of perfect goodness, the moment when wondrous existence and true emptiness do not obstruct each other. When the heart-mind reaches this realm, it becomes the Gate of the Myriad Wonders, which cannot be attained at the initial stage of practice. Only when one has returned to emptiness and arrived at true intention can this be achieved. Not a single thought arises, yet the mind remains undistracted. If it is undistracted, how can no thought arise? When the *huozi* hour arrives before the aperture, the upright qi does not scatter, and the heart-mind remains empty of all things, yet in a state of primal chaos—seemingly existent, seemingly non-existent, between intention and non-intention. It is purely a mass of true intention, fully aware of the firing process yet knowing nothing else. When the *huowu* hour arrives, one naturally knows it, while all else becomes unknown. The four great elements are all empty, yet one easily falls into dead emptiness; if there is even a trace of intention, one becomes attached to forms as in ordinary days. Only by neither clinging to forms nor falling into emptiness can one attain the state where not a single thought arises and the mind remains undistracted—this is the Gate of the Myriad Wonders, the realm of perfect goodness. The realm of perfect goodness is the Gate of the Mysterious Female. The mysterious yang and the female yin—yang is wondrous existence, yin is true emptiness. From true emptiness arises wondrous existence; when yang reaches its extreme, it becomes true emptiness, and when yin reaches its extreme, it becomes wondrous existence. Yet wondrous existence is true emptiness—all are the meaning of the Mysterious Female. "Mysterious" means unfathomably wondrous; "female" means boundlessly hollow. Only in boundless hollowness can the unfathomably wondrous transformations be born. Bai Yuchan said: *"Boundless hollowness is the mysterious aperture; knowing yet not clinging is the practice."* This is the practice of the elixir being born in the mysterious aperture. The great emptiness is boundlessly hollow, yet it cannot be clung to. The Classic states: *"The valley spirit does not die; it is called the Mysterious Female. The gate of the Mysterious Female is called the root of Heaven and Earth."* Only the spirit of the empty valley does not die. The valley spirit is the wondrous emptiness; the wondrousness within emptiness is the valley spirit. Within the great and small voids, it is the gate through which one connects with emptiness. Every gate is the aperture in the middle, the gate of the valley spirit. Only this gate can gather the gates of the six senses, hence it is called the "root of Heaven and Earth." Exhaling connects with the root of Heaven; inhaling connects with the root of Earth. The breath of exhalation and inhalation enters and exits through the mouth and nose. Normally, this qi is not easily seen, but in winter, one can see the breath at the mouth. The two breaths of mouth and nose—one exhaling, one inhaling—are like Heaven and Earth. The mouth exhales, the nose inhales, one exiting, one entering, and their intersection is the crossroads, the root of Heaven and Earth. The natural Gate of the Mysterious Female lies within this. When heart-mind and breath interdepend, the two breaths of exhalation and inhalation naturally intersect here. The *Canticle* says: *"Square and round, a inch in diameter, blending and restraining each other."* It speaks of their intersection within the space of a square inch. To nurture the heart-mind with the realm of perfect goodness is to store this heart-mind at the Gate of the Mysterious Female, thereby resting in perfect goodness. This can be divided into two layers: first, emptying the heart-mind and resting it in the realm of perfect goodness, where knowing is the state of the heart-mind and resting is the realm. Empty the heart-mind and rest it in the realm of perfect goodness, and only then can there be stability. Stability means stabilizing in the One—the One being "a single goodness." Having attained it, one does not lose it, and thus follows the firing process of tranquility, serenity, reflection, and attainment. All occurs naturally, without the slightest contrivance. Eighth Lecture.

In the cultivation of the *Great Learning*, the workings of the *Doctrine of the Mean*, and the principles of human nature and destiny, the firing process is detailed through reflection, tranquility, serenity, and cessation. The previous lecture discussed the principle of the Five Phases, wherein the heavenly stems and earthly branches are all assigned to the Five Phases, which mutually give rise to and complete one another. In the prenatal Five Phases, they pertain to the transformation of *qi*, each possessing an independent nature; in the postnatal Five Phases, they pertain to the transformation of form, where the *qi* of the Five Phases is already complete, thus forming shapes. Therefore, within a single phase, the functions of all Five Phases are encompassed, a principle also adopted in the Dao of the Elixir.

Now, observing the earthly branches through the lens of the "Receiving Stems" (*na jia*), the Five Phases are truly one phase, and one phase is the Five Phases. Below is a diagram:

Wood is gestated in *you* (Metal) and attains growth in *hai* (Water). The *Zhen* trigram, both inner and outer, is Wood, which is gestated in Metal, hence *Zhen* receives *geng*, and *Xun* receives *xin*. Metal overcomes Wood, yet Wood uses Metal as its gestation—thus overcoming is also generating. Metal generates Water, and Water is Wood’s growth, where growth is gestation. Metal is gestated in *mao* and attains growth in *si*; the golden lotus within Fire is precisely this. *Ding* Fire and *ji* Earth combine to generate Metal, and the fifteenth day of the eighth month is when Metal is at its peak. Metal perishes in *yin* yet is gestated in *mao*, revealing that within Metal are Wood, Fire, and Earth, and within Wood are Metal, Water, and Earth.

The *Qian* trigram (Metal) receives *jia* and *ren*, where *jia* is *mao* Wood. The *Dui* trigram (Metal) receives *ding*, for Metal is born in *si*, which contains *ding* Fire and *ji* Earth. Earth comprises the four branches *chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei*; Earth is *Kun* and *Gen*, nurturing the myriad things. The *Yin Yi* (*Gui Cang*) begins with *Kun*, while the *Zhou* *Yi* of King Wen of Zhou begins with *Qian* and *Kun*. The *Lian Shan* begins with *Gen*, embodying the principle of "knowing cessation"; the *Gui Cang* begins with *Kun*, also taking *Kun*’s meaning of "completion at beginning and end," no different from beginning with *Qian* and *Kun*. *Chen* and *chou* mark the beginning, while *xu* and *wei* mark the end; only Earth does not perish, containing the Five Phases without exhaustion, serving as the primal *Kun* of the myriad things, receiving *yi* and *gui*. *Yi* is Wood, possessing the *qi* of ceaseless generation, embodying the principle of nurturing; *gui* is the end of the heavenly stems, embodying the principle of storage. Thus, *Kun* receives *yi* and *gui*, its meaning drawn from this.

Clearly, the Five Phases circulate without interruption. Now, considering *Kan* and *Li*: *Li* is Fire, gestated in *zi*, attaining growth in *yin*, flourishing in *wu*, and stored in *xu*. Within *yin* is *ji* Earth, and within *xu* is also *ji* Earth, hence *Li* receives *ji*. *Kan* is Water, gestated in *wu*, attaining growth in *shen*, flourishing in *zi*, and stored in *chen*. *Chen* Earth is *wu* Earth, and within *shen* is also *wu* Earth, hence *Kan* receives *wu*. The *ji* of *Li* and the *wu* of *Kan* arise from this.

*Li ji* is the radiance of the sun, wherein resides the Golden Crow, which is *you* Metal. *You* Metal is gestated in *mao* Wood, and *mao* Wood is Fire’s bathing, where Wood conceals Metal as Fire’s source. Thus, the Golden Crow dwells within the sun, taking *mao* as its bathing time. *Kan wu* is the essence of the moon, wherein resides the Jade Hare, which is *mao* Wood. *Mao* Wood is gestated in *you* Metal, and *you* Metal is Water’s bathing, where Metal conceals Wood as Water’s source. Thus, the Jade Hare dwells within the moon, taking *you* as its bathing time.

Observing Water, Fire, Wood, and Metal: Wood is gestated in *you* yet perishes in *shen*; Water is gestated in *wu* yet perishes in *si*; Metal is gestated in *mao* yet perishes in *yin*; Water is gestated in *zi* yet perishes in *hai*. Birth here is death here, revealing the principle of circulation. Only Earth does not perish; Earth is emptiness, and emptiness is neither born nor destroyed. From this, one may understand that the heavenly stems and earthly branches are mutually interconnected: within *zi* Water is *gui* Water, within *wu* Fire is *bing* Fire, within *si* Fire is *ding* Fire, and within *ji* Earth and *hai* Water are *ren* Water and *wu* Earth. The earthly branches arise from the heavenly stems, and the Five Phases of the earthly branches conceal the Five Phases of the heavenly stems—thus, one may be seen through the other. The principle of "Receiving Stems" cannot be easily understood without observing the heavenly stems and earthly branches together.

Within the Five Phases, Water begins and Metal ends, yet Metal generates Water, ending and beginning anew—this is the forward sequence. The central fifth is Earth, and five is one; thus, within Water is Metal, within Metal is Fire, within Fire is Wood, and within Wood is Water—this is the reverse creation. The forward sequence is the prenatal transforming into the postnatal; the reverse sequence is the postnatal returning to the prenatal. In terms of forward and reverse, the prenatal pertains to yang, and the postnatal pertains to yin. In terms of differentiation, the Five Phases each possess yin and yang, and each phase also has its own yin and yang, as in the *River Chart*, where even numbers are yin and odd numbers are yang.

Though the Dao of the Elixir involves the Five Phases, it is purely symbolic; what is employed is merely one yin and one yang, as the *Book of Changes* states: "One yin and one yang is called the Dao." In truth, yin and yang are also symbolic representations; in reality, they are but emptiness, the Supreme Ultimate. As previously stated, this is the Three, the Five, and the One. The so-called "Mysterious Female" (*xuan pin*) is the mysterious Heaven and the receptive Earth; the mysterious yang and the receptive yin; the mysterious "having" and the receptive "not-having." Receiving the mandate of Heaven, the nature of *Qian* Metal is the mysterious; the prenatal within the postnatal, the mandate of *Kun* Earth is the receptive. Separated, they are Heaven and Earth, *Qian* and *Kun*; united, they are the undifferentiated.

The Gate of the Mysterious Female (*xuan pin*) is the Gate of Primordial Chaos (*hundun*). At the threshold where *Qian* and *Kun* unite, where yin and yang intersect, only then does Primordial Chaos form. This locus of Primordial Chaos is the Gate of Primordial Chaos, which is also the Gate of the Mysterious Female—it may further be called the "Realm of Supreme Good." The initial step lies in "knowing where to halt" (*zhi zhi*); "knowing" is awakening, and within it lies the work of *huohou* (timing and effort). With a mind of purity, one halts at this locus—only then is it the mind of "knowing where to halt." This "halt" is but a faint awakening, free from attachment to the forms of "having" or "not-having."

As *Zhuangzi* states: *"Let the mind wander in simplicity, blend the qi with vastness."* Simplicity (*dan*) is the realm of detachment, merging with emptiness. The character *dan* (淡) combines water on the left and fire on the right—only where water and fire meet can they intersect, the small emptiness uniting with the great emptiness. Both *dan* (淡) and *mo* (漠) denote boundlessness; one should let the mind wander therein and blend the qi therewith. Indeed, Zhuangzi’s words express the principle of "returning to emptiness" (*huan xu*), which is also the meaning of "knowing where to halt." The "Supreme Good" and "detachment" share the same essence.

The passage in the *Great Learning*—*"Know where to halt, and there will be stability; with stability, there will be tranquility; with tranquility, there will be peace; with peace, there will be contemplation; with contemplation, there will be attainment"*—is the *huohou* work of "halting at the Supreme Good," the esoteric transmission of the Sage’s gate, nothing more. This passage meticulously describes the stages of *huohou*; the entire process of Dao cultivation is encapsulated in these few lines. The so-called "knowing where to halt" is to halt at the Supreme Good through the divine awareness of innate knowledge (*liangzhi*). Innate knowledge arises naturally from awakening; for those in the postnatal state, the slightest stir of awareness becomes innate knowledge. Initially, there is no "divine awareness," only ordinary perception. Divine awareness is the awakening of non-awakening—spontaneous, untouched by the slightest intention, vague and indistinct, without a clear mind, quiescent yet responsive, utterly empty and numinous, perfectly round and luminous, arising without knowing why.

If it is mere perception, then awareness stirs and knowledge arises. For those in the postnatal state, the *shen* (discerning spirit) dominates, and the mind’s discriminating power is active—thus awareness is not numinous, and movement immediately flows into knowledge. Comparing the two, divine awareness is exceedingly light, while perception is slightly heavier; divine awareness arises purely from the celestial harmony, whereas perception is tainted by intellect. At the outset of entering the Dao, when the mind is not yet clear and the nature not yet manifest, those who nurture and employ it well may preserve the faint awakening of innate knowledge, preventing it from flowing into the realm of intellect—thus achieving sincerity. If one remains as restless as a monkey or horse, the moment awareness stirs, intellect intrudes, and even the lightest, faintest perception becomes unattainable—how much less can innate knowledge be preserved!

Only through practice does one draw near. The two characters "refining the self" (*lian ji*) are of utmost importance. Through diligent refinement, one naturally sheds the power of intellect and restores innate knowledge. Herein lies the work of "knowing where to halt"! To "know where to halt" is to halt at the realm of innate knowledge; and with this innate knowledge, to halt at the Realm of Supreme Good. The so-called "halt" is to halt the mind from flowing into thoughts, to halt knowledge from flowing into intellect. Only this thought-free, intellect-free innate knowledge is the pure mind—unattached to the four forms, unabiding in form, sound, scent, taste, touch, or dharma—this is what is meant by *"letting the mind wander in simplicity."*

With this innate knowledge, *"blend the qi with vastness"*—vastness (*mo*) is emptiness, the Realm of Supreme Good, the Gate of the Mysterious Female. To halt innate knowledge here is to unite spirit and qi, to interdepend mind and breath, to guard the center and embrace the One, to refine the self and return to emptiness—this is the initial work of practice.

As the *Book of Changes* states: *"To preserve and sustain the nature is the gate of Dao and virtue."* *"Letting the mind wander in simplicity"* is the Dao; *"blending the qi with vastness"* is virtue. *Mencius* says: *"That which is born of accumulated virtue is so great and firm that it fills the space between Heaven and Earth."* Is not "the space between Heaven and Earth" the Dao? Is not "that which is born of accumulated virtue" virtue? Thus, the gate of Dao and virtue is the Gate of the Mysterious Female. The "nature" (*xing*) here is the already-manifest nature; to preserve this nature, one must preserve it at the "gate of Dao and virtue." This is the moment when innate knowledge returns to divine awareness.

To "know where to halt at the Supreme Good" is merely to know where to halt, to receive the Sage’s esoteric transmission—yet the subtleties of *huohou* are not yet detailed. Proceeding from this point, following the mind’s method, one engages in the work of refining the self and returning to emptiness. When spirit and qi interdepend, this is called "stability" (*ding*). Stability is stability in the One—the One is the Realm of Supreme Good, the Gate of the Mysterious Female; the One is non-duality in things, the unfathomable source of life; the One is the Supreme Ultimate, the center, true emptiness. To stabilize within true emptiness is the first step in practice. The Dao calls it "cultivating stability" (*xiu ding*); Buddhism calls it "entering stability" (*ru ding*)—both are the same.

Thus it is said: *"Know where to halt, and there will be stability."* Yet stability, whether for a moment or an instant, is still called "stability." Stability may be momentary but not enduring, singular but not expansive—it still cannot grasp the transformative power. One must progress from stability to tranquility.

Tranquility is the initial stage of refining the fire of returning to emptiness, wherein body and mind are both purified. Compared to "stability," it endures longer and can embrace vastness, possessing a capacity for gradual expansion. To sit upright in non-action is called "tranquility"; when the heart-mind of the sovereign is at peace, this is called "tranquility." To forget the self, forget others, forget form, forget things—this is called "tranquility." To be without deliberation, without insistence, without fixation, without self—this is called "tranquility." When the four great elements remain unmoved, the body is tranquil; when the six gates do not open, the mind is tranquil; when the seven emotions do not arise, intention is tranquil. When the mind does not move, spirit is tranquil; when the body does not move, qi is tranquil; when intention does not move, essence is tranquil. Tranquility is like still water without ripples, like a mirror that clarifies itself.

The secret says: When body and mind are pure and tranquil, spirit and qi harmonize, merging into chaos, profound and obscure—unaware of heaven as a canopy or earth as a chariot; unaware of the self possessing a body, or the body possessing the Dao. This is called "utter emptiness and profound tranquility." When practice reaches this point, the first yang is about to transform. Yet for a beginner progressing from stability to tranquility, this must initially involve effort, and the spontaneous awakening of spirit cannot yet be attained. Thus, the four characters "utter emptiness and profound tranquility" are difficult to achieve. Compared to the initial stage of stability, however, the duration is longer, and distracting thoughts may cease for a time. If during stability one can remain thought-free for a quarter-hour, during tranquility one may do so for an hour. In stability, body and mind cannot yet be forgotten, but in tranquility, one enters the state of having no mind—hence it is said, "from stability one can attain tranquility."

What is called "from tranquility one can attain ease" arises from the pure and tranquil body and mind, which awaken the natural spiritual awareness. This natural spiritual awareness, as previously mentioned, is the inherent luminous clarity of the original nature. This nature is the primordial spirit (*yuan shen*) emanating from the ancestral nature, outwardly chaotic yet inwardly luminous and empty, first true emptiness and then wondrous existence. Buddhism calls it "right awakening Buddha-nature" or "perfectly awakened wondrous mind"; Confucianism calls it "the Dao-mind of the Supreme Ultimate" or "the mind of heaven and earth"; Daoism calls it "the awakened primordial spirit" or "the luminous true nature." The sages of the Three Teachings all describe it with the words "center" and "One." Daoist texts say, "Within the center there is the One; within the One there is the center." Zisi called it "attaining the mean and harmony" (*Zhongyong*): when quiescent and unmoving, it is the "mean"; when stimulated, it penetrates—this is "harmony." If the mean is attained before expression, expression will be harmonious; if the mean is attained during expression, harmony will follow. Without this mean, expression cannot be harmonious. This is a natural matter, not governed by the human mind, yet this spiritual awareness naturally governs. Invisibly, it suddenly becomes aware.

In the work of refining the fire, only this spiritual awareness can spontaneously operate, spontaneously regulate, moving when it should move and halting when it should halt. Not only this—even the movement and stillness of primordial qi follow this spiritual awareness; the generation and transformation of primordial essence are also governed by it. Naturally interdependent, naturally harmonious, naturally congealing, naturally gathering—no effort is required to sustain it. Thus *Laozi* says, "The Dao models itself on spontaneity," and *Wuzhen pian* says, "There is a natural true fire, needing no kindling or fanning."

Yet how does spiritual awareness arise? Only by completely eliminating the human mind, eradicating worldly desires, not generating a single thought, and maintaining utmost sincerity and focus—pure true emptiness, without the appearances of self, others, things, or form—will spiritual awareness spontaneously arise. Daoist texts also call this "true intention," meaning "a single undistracted intention." When true intention activates, it is wondrous existence; when it does not move, it is true emptiness, identical in substance to the great void and identical in function to the Supreme Ultimate—hence it is called "prior to heaven." At the initial stirring of spiritual awareness, it is in the state of prior-heaven, chaotic and undifferentiated, without the slightest impurity—thus it is also called "chaotic primordial spirit," "right and equal awakening," or "prior to heaven, yet heaven does not oppose it." Once activated, it governs essence and qi, regulates the fire, forgets form and body, and naturally aligns with the Dao—without the slightest involvement of the discriminating mind. Thus it is also called "perfectly awakened wondrous mind," "luminous spirit," or "posterior to heaven, yet following heaven's timing." At this stage, it is called "ease."

Ease is being at peace and at liberty, as in Emperor Yao’s "peaceful peace." Confucius said: "Some act from ease, some from advantage, some from effort" (*Zhongyong*), and also: "Those who know from birth are the highest; those who know through learning are next; those who learn through difficulty are lower still" (*Analects*). When one begins practice, letting go of all concerns and eliminating the discriminating mind, one stabilizes the mind in a state of purity to seek the innate knowing within the original nature. Using this innate knowing to halt at the gate of the Dao is firm indeed. Yet initially, this halting is largely forced. Forcing stability within emptiness lasts but a moment—an instant later, the discriminating mind returns, the mind is impure, and innate knowing vanishes. The body is constrained, qi is not tranquil, and true emptiness is lost—hence it is "momentary but not enduring."

Daily, one must forcefully persist in practice, learning through struggle. Gradually, progress accumulates—first stabilizing for a single minute, then after several days, two minutes, then five, then ten. Through months and years of accumulation, one reaches the state where both body and mind are tranquil, and this is "acting from advantage," "knowing through learning," and "stabilizing before attaining stillness." Yet at this stage, though the body is still, it has not yet reached steadfastness; though the mind is empty, it has not yet reached its utmost. Though one may attain stillness for a time, this stillness still relies on the effort of halting thoughts. It is merely that the discriminating mind is unified and deluded intentions are sincere—one has not yet arrived at the realm of utmost sincerity, and the natural spiritual awareness has not returned.

One must persist day and night, in movement and stillness, walking, standing, sitting, and lying down, refining the mind as before, so that body and mind merge into great stability and profound stillness, forgetting both inner and outer. Mind and form are transformed together; things come and one responds naturally, affairs pass and one ceases. The spirit is not pulled by intellect, qi is not driven by emotions, and intention is not bound by dharmas. Through long practice, when this becomes thoroughly familiar, innate knowing and innate ability are present at every moment. This is the process of "reducing again and again," dissolving the discriminating mind until the original spirit takes charge.

Yet when the original spirit first returns, the mind is like that of an infant—entering chaos but not yet luminous, possessing natural truth but unable to wield it. This may be called "acting from smoothness," but not yet "acting from ease." *Mencius* says: "The great person is one who does not lose the heart of an infant." *The Announcement of Kang* states: "As in protecting an infant, the heart sincerely seeks it." For once attained, it must not be lost. One must preserve it all day, never parting from it for even a moment. Confucius said of Yan Hui: "In grasping one good, he held it fast in his breast and never lost it." This single good is "the One that is spirit." In constant quiescent illumination, profoundly clear and bright, through long accumulation and deep familiarity, the original spirit expands, evolving from chaos to luminous clarity. Naturally, it governs the firing times, giving rise to transformation—no need to refine the mind, for the mind refines itself; no need to concentrate the spirit, for the spirit concentrates itself; no need to regulate the breath, for the breath regulates itself, and qi moves of its own accord. Moreover, advancing and retreating are in perfect measure; when the time comes, the spirit knows. As the living *zi* hour arrives, the spirit is already outwardly stabilized; as the living *wu* hour approaches, qi has already inwardly returned. Body and mind are in non-doing, yet spirit and qi naturally act. As *Laozi* says: "In non-doing, nothing is left undone." The pure yang stage, "simply resting the spirit and breath, entrusting to nature," is the same. This is called "acting from ease," requiring no effort of one’s own, yet in the unseen, spiritual awareness naturally adjusts the firing times—akin to "knowing from birth." This is "those who know from birth are the highest."

Yet where in the world are those who "know from birth"? Even the sagely Confucius sought the Dao from Laozi; even the enlightened Śākyamuni received instruction from Dipamkara. Even if there are those born able to speak or write, once they enter the *sahā* world, intellect awakens and the original spirit recedes, desires encroach and innate knowing fades—how could they practice the Dao without learning? One must proceed from knowing where to halt, to stabilizing, to stilling, and only then reach "ease within ease." When the mind is clear and nature is revealed, spiritual awareness presides over the work of self-cultivation, requiring no physical effort or acquired intellect. Only when suddenly penetrating and connecting all does one become a "sage who knows from birth." The Dao of self-cultivation, the learning of the great person, moves from effort to ease, from subtlety to transformation.

Thus, "ease" means that spiritual awareness presides over all. Therefore, in gathering, sealing, and refining the firing times, all arises naturally—when stability is due, one stabilizes; when forgetting is due, one forgets. Within chaos and emptiness, infinite transformations emerge. This "ease" is the true efficacy of refining the self. Yet without the return of the original spirit, there can be no ease. Only after attaining ease does one "sleep and wake with the spirit embraced, ever aware of its presence or absence," unbroken and continuous, deeply rooted and firmly established. Natural transformation then arises. At the extreme of emptiness, being arises from non-being; at the utmost of stillness, the yang returns. External stimuli and internal responses, medicine is produced and the spirit knows. Gathering with the gathering that is not gathering, neither assisting nor forgetting; taking with the taking that is not taking, naturally withdrawing and replenishing. In the state of not knowing or perceiving, lead and mercury are already harmonized, the great medicine returns to the furnace, yang fire advances of itself, and yin tally naturally appears.

When practice reaches the stage of ease, the practitioner must carefully observe and truly embody it, never growing negligent—hence it is said, "only after stilling can one attain ease." As for "only after attaining ease can one deliberate," this concerns guarding against danger and considering risks. This "deliberation" is rarely understood through the ages. The Song Confucians, not knowing the Dao, need not be discussed; even Daoists seldom offer a proper interpretation of this phrase. For when practice reaches "acting from ease," natural spiritual awareness has already awakened, the light of nature shines forth, the mind of Dao emerges, and the great medicine can be generated, gathered, sealed, and refined—what need is there for deliberation? Moreover, deliberation pertains to the mind of the future, belonging to the discriminating mind. At this stage, the original spirit is in charge—how could there be a "mind of the future"? The success or failure of the Dao, the attainment or loss of the elixir, hinges entirely on this word.

The *Book of Songs* states: "Only this King Wen, cautious and reverent" (see *Records of Rites·Biao Ji*). Confucius said: "As if treading on thin ice, as if standing by a deep abyss" (see *Analects·Tai Bo*). *Awakening to Reality* warns: "In drawing and adding, guard against danger," and further: "A deviation of a hair’s breadth fails to form the elixir."

When transitioning from stillness to ease, the true medicine arises in the heart, and the body likewise generates transformation. The natural fire-phase requires no human effort; the four limbs relax, soft as cotton. At first encountering this scene, scholars cannot but rejoice exceedingly. Yet this very joy may allow the discriminating spirit to seize the moment and invade, with worldly desires returning—thus the natural spiritual awareness, once gained, may be lost again. This is the first concern.

Moreover, even if spiritual awareness is regained and the great medicine produced, with wondrous scenes before one, sudden terror may arise, allowing the discriminating spirit to invade once more, transmuting the primordial into the conditioned. This is the second concern.

Further, when the primordial spirit has awakened and the gathering is complete, one may grow complacent, believing it sufficient. Then, indulging body and mind, unable to forget emotions amid circumstances—or practicing for days and deeming it mastery—one slackens in vigilance, inviting the discriminating spirit’s will. The moment it stirs, true intention and primordial spirit are lost, the effect immediate, the thought already awry. This is the third concern. The ancients called this "lead flying, mercury fleeing," "the cauldron breaking, the furnace collapsing." All these are dangers at the moment of gathering the medicine, yet they remain relatively easy to correct.

When natural spiritual awareness has returned, attained within quiescent stability, and one proceeds calmly with the fire-phase—gathering, sealing, obtaining the medicine, forming the elixir, completing the celestial cycle—when the inner elixir has coalesced and the fetal immortal may dance, the practitioner has reached this stage with cultivation refined and foundation firmly laid. Yet even here, in the work of gentle nurturing and bathing, one must guard against danger and consider risks. Otherwise, all prior efforts may be undone, or madness and illness may arise—this "deliberation" demands even greater emphasis.

Why? During gentle nurturing, success depends entirely on harmonizing water and fire to achieve the "already crossed" effect, morning in *Tun*, evening in *Meng*, taking the two hexagrams as images. The classics state: "At dawn, guard against dryness, use water to augment the lead; at dusk, guard against cold, use fire to replenish the mercury." At all times, one must contain light in silence, as if present yet absent, like a dragon nurturing its pearl, a hen brooding her egg, wholly returning to the state of the Great Ultimate. Should the measure deviate even slightly, regret and misfortune arise. The *Yi Jing*’s *Qian* hexagram, line nine-three—"All day long, *qian qian*, reverting and repeating the Dao"—depicts this very image. Without such meticulous sincerity, one cannot attain the image of "the flying dragon in the heavens, meeting the noble person."

The alchemical classics speak of "a year’s bathing, guarding against danger," and *The Mirror of Ingredient Entry* states: "Only with utmost sincerity does the method follow nature." Should there be even a single day of unnaturalness, a single moment of insincerity, the elixir is destroyed. Moreover, external worldly desires and internal dharmic appearances, should delusive thoughts arise, give rise to demonic obstacles. Though the discriminating spirit has been subdued, its roots remain unsevered. Not until completing the seven-day celestial cycle can the nature of food, sex, temperament, the six senses, and seven emotions be fully extinguished. During this period of gentle nurturing, karmic roots from past kalpas and lifelong worldly desires surge against the heart-mind like tides. Only by concentrating the spirit, stabilizing the will, forgetting self and others, practicing solitary vigilance, and preserving the natural fire-phase of spiritual awareness with utmost care can one ensure safety.

Moreover, at every step of this process, scenes arise and images appear. One must face scenes without attachment, perceive images without clinging to forms—only then can water and fire achieve balance. Thus this "deliberation" is not the deliberation of thought but a crucial principle within the fire-phase. Only by deliberating beforehand can one attain ease afterward; only by deliberating thoroughly can one achieve perfect ease.

Some may question whether "deliberation" should precede "ease," or whether it should persist throughout as a constant vigil. Yet if the work does not aspire to ease, there is nothing to deliberate. To deliberate is to guard against forgetting danger in ease, to guard against failing in sincerity, to guard against inability to calm the spirit and rest in naturalness, to guard against using wisdom when it arises rather than containing and expanding it. All these pertain to the stage after the primordial spirit has taken charge and governs the fire-phase. Before the discriminating spirit is transformed, there is no Dao to cultivate, no elixir to nurture, no wisdom to arise, no naturalness to follow.

Only after first restoring the heart of the infant does spiritual awareness take form; with spiritual awareness formed, the natural fire-phase emerges. Following this naturalness is called utmost sincerity, enabling one to reach the state of unceasing breath and fully recover the original face before father and mother were born. This is the primordial Great Dao, the supreme vehicle, purely a scene of the primordial. Only then can the cosmos settle in tranquility and wisdom’s light arise; with wisdom’s light arisen, sagehood and wisdom are complete.

Yet the distinction between primordial and conditioned lies in the subtlety between being and non-being. When being and non-being are not established, it is the primordial; otherwise, one falls into the conditioned. Thus, should a single thought stir in the heart or the slightest form attach to the body, the restored primordial true yang instantly transforms into conditioned turbid yin, becoming unusable. Before spiritual awareness is attained, this primordial state does not exist, and the fire-phase cannot proceed. While the discriminating spirit remains unexhausted, wisdom’s light does not arise.

When practice reaches a state of serene ease, the numinous clarity within one’s nature gradually begins to manifest—such as divine perception, knowledge of others’ minds, recollection of past lives, celestial vision, celestial hearing, and the like—all arising during the nurturing and nursing phase of cultivation. If wisdom is employed as soon as it first emerges, it transforms into discriminating consciousness; only by not employing wisdom does one succeed in transmuting consciousness into true wisdom. Moreover, the initial appearance of spiritual awareness is inherently natural; following this naturalness, awareness becomes ever more numinous, but if naturalness is lost, spiritual awareness departs. This is why I name it “natural spiritual awareness.” This subtlety is so fine that a hair’s breadth of deviation leads to a thousand *li* of error. The *Book of Changes* states: “To know the incipient is to be divine!” And again: “The divine is that which subtly pervades all things.” The mechanism of naturalness is the celestial mechanism, the workings of creation. The wise perceive its incipient signs and follow them—hence the saying, “Only when at peace can one deliberate.” As for “deliberation leading to attainment,” it is said: “All day long, ceaselessly vigilant, as if facing peril at dusk—then there will be no blame.” Thus one may attain the elixir and accomplish the Dao.

Attainment is an attainment that endures forever; if it is not everlasting, it cannot be called “attainment”—it is what Buddhism terms the “cessation of all defilements” or “nirvana without remainder.” Only when the discriminating spirit is utterly extinguished, the hun and po dissolved and transformed, the Dharma-body pervading the three realms, and one’s original nature filling the void—only then, free from rebirth and coeval with heaven, indestructible even when heaven and earth crumble—can it truly be called “attainment.” Buddhism calls this “completion,” Daoism calls it “accomplishment.” It is not the attainment of some transient gain, nor the recovery of the original spirit, nor the attainment achieved through stillness, concentration, or tranquility. One must guard against danger, deliberate over peril, exhaust principle and fulfill one’s nature to the point where no danger remains to guard against, no loss to deliberate over. The *Jade Emperor’s Heart Seal Scripture* says: “Once attained, it is attained forever, and the body naturally becomes light. Supreme harmony overflows, and the bones scatter like cold jade.” The *Scripture of Purity* states: “This is called attaining the Dao.” Chen Nidan says: “It takes but a moment’s effort to enjoy endless ease forever.” All these serve as proofs of the word “attainment.” The Buddhist sutras speak of “turning the mechanism in an instant to return to one’s original attainment,” referring to those who awaken suddenly and fully—akin to the meaning of “Hearing the Dao in the morning, one may die content in the evening.”

Earlier, “illuminating virtue” was identified as the Dao, the elixir, the medicine; “renewing the people” as the practice; and “utmost goodness” as the wondrous aperture. In this passage, through the successive practices of settling, stillness, tranquility, and deliberation, the Dao is assuredly attained. Bai Yuchan said: “Since receiving the oral instructions from my teacher, I have known that this elixir can certainly be attained and this Dao can certainly be accomplished. Though the various fire-phases may seem intricate and difficult to observe, in practice they are exceedingly simple.” Throughout, it is all a matter of naturalness, with sincerity as the unbroken thread. Cultivating this to the point of utmost sincerity without cease, one gradually gains numinous penetrations—hence the saying, “Deliberation leads to attainment.”

In my view, the opening passage on the Dao of the *Great Learning* seems to be the words of Confucius, while this passage detailing the practice is likely the teaching of Zengzi. These two sections alone have exhaustively expounded the great Dao of the golden elixir and the supreme vehicle of Chan. The Song Confucians, not understanding the Dao, merely provided exegetical glosses and thus missed the mark by a wide margin.

The following four phrases—“Things have their roots and branches”—are especially profound and flavorful. They mean that for those who cultivate the Dao, knowing the roots and branches, beginnings and ends, prior and posterior—these six aspects—brings one close to the Dao. All things have roots before branches; the Dao is the root. Knowing the root of the primordial qi, one naturally understands how the Dao constitutes the Dao. Cultivating the body by seeking the beginning from the end is reversal; worldly affairs proceed from root to branch in sequence. Seeking the Dao through reversal, knowing that nothing exists after death, one also knows that emptiness preceded birth. Through reverse cultivation and return, the Dao is found within. The use of “end” before “beginning” carries deep significance. “Knowing what comes first and what follows” is to know the source of the primordial and the conditioned. The *Book of Changes* says: “Preceding heaven, do not oppose it; following heaven, comply with its timing.” The primordial is the source of the Dao; the conditioned is the beginning of transformation. The common people, unaware, follow the conditioned forms of change in their daily lives; the sages investigate things and exhaust principles to explore the primordial origin. “Knowing what comes first and what follows” is the principle of “illuminating luminous virtue” and “resting in utmost goodness.”

The Dao of the great person begins with returning to emptiness and ends with returning to emptiness; end and beginning are one and the same. To know beginning and end is to know that both are matters of returning to emptiness—thus one knows the Dao. The Dao of the *Great Learning* is the Dao of the great person, and this “great person” is the “great person” of the *Book of Changes*. Later generations, not understanding, misinterpreted this—hence the obscurity of Confucianism and Daoism.

“Those in ancient times who wished to illuminate luminous virtue” first “ordered their states”—the state being the body, as the ancients always used the body as a metaphor for the state. To order the state, one must first regulate the family, for the family is a small state. To regulate the family, one must first cultivate the body. The essence of cultivating the body lies in the heart. Only when the original spirit presides over affairs is the heart rectified. Rectifying the heart begins with sincerity of intention, and sincerity of intention is true intention. With this true intention, one can wield its subtlety, and natural spiritual awareness gradually becomes numinous. Only this spiritual awareness can discern the fire-phases of the living zi and living wu, and employ the practices of ninefold return and sevenfold reversal.

Thus, in cultivating the Dao, one must first restore true intention. To restore true intention, one must first extract awareness from the acquired spirit and discard its knowledge, hence the saying, “To make intention sincere, first extend one’s knowledge.” The word “extend” (*zhi*) carries profound meaning—it signifies “to reach,” “to employ,” and also conveys the sense of singular focus. Only what can be reached is called “extended,” and what is inherently so is called “extended.” Concentrating the mind with unwavering resolve marks the beginning of sincerity; utmost sincerity and single-minded density mark its culmination. Thus, sincerity is also called “one-pointed sincerity,” and sincere intention is synonymous with utmost sincerity. When the mind is undistracted, dwelling solely on this single point, sincerity arises over time. Sincerity is the genuine, singular, and unwavering thought that arises from intention. Human intention is inherently true and thus inherently sincere, but it is disturbed by knowledge and invaded by material desires, giving rise to deluded thoughts and wayward impulses. All these stem from the acquired spirit governing affairs, as external temptations enter through the six gates and directly engage the six senses, activating the six consciousnesses. This further stirs wayward fire and arouses the seven emotions, creating an unbroken stream of thoughts that leaves the tranquil heart-monarch restless and the primal spirit deeply concealed and unseen. It is like clouds and mist obscuring the sky, hiding the sun and moon—yet the sun and moon remain inherently present. The acquired spirit wreaks havoc, veiling the primal spirit; deluded intentions run rampant, while true intention lies hidden, though not absent.

To “extend one’s knowledge” is to return to the innate good knowledge within the primal spirit; to “make intention sincere” is to awaken the true intention within the heart’s nature. The words “sincere” and “extend” both refer to what is inherently possessed, requiring only refinement—dispelling clouds and mist to reveal the sun and moon. The phrase “extending knowledge lies in investigating things” further clarifies that the awakening of good knowledge depends on the word “investigate” (*ge*). To investigate is to eliminate what is not the heart. As *Mencius* states, “Only a great person can rectify the ruler’s heart’s errors” (*Mencius·Li Lou I*). “Things” refer to entities with form; separating from all forms, the heart investigates itself—not merely targeting material desires, but akin in meaning to “overcoming the self and returning to propriety” and “the world returning to benevolence” (*Analects·Yan Yuan*). For the Dao-heart is subtle, while the human heart is perilous. The human heart is the heart attached to the four forms and six consciousnesses, driven by desire for existence. The Dao-heart is the heart’s original substance, empty yet luminous, simultaneously form and emptiness. People generate deluded thoughts through emotions and consciousness, and from deluded thoughts arise cravings. Thus, external objects become obstructions within the heart. Unaware that the physical body is a superfluous growth, they daily scheme to nourish it, allowing the acquired nature of food, sex, and temperament to usurp the primal spirit’s position as master of body and mind. Not only is good knowledge submerged and irretrievable, but even the formless awareness, when active, carries the roots of consciousness, instantly slipping into the realm of knowledge. This is not a sudden occurrence but a gradual accumulation.

The path of self-cultivation must begin here, gradually habituating oneself to eliminate knowledge and restore the heart’s originally spontaneous awareness. From this awareness, good knowledge accumulates, making its attainment certain—hence the saying, “Extending knowledge lies in investigating things.” The word “lies” (*zai*) is heavily emphasized, marking an essential stage for scholars. The term “investigating things” has broad implications, encompassing the exploration of yin-yang principles and the origins of creation. Why do all things in the universe come into being? Why do they perish? Each is investigated according to its natural nature. Knowing that the principles of generation and transformation in all things each possess their own inherent pattern, none deviating from the natural norm, the meaning of “illuminating luminous virtue” becomes fully manifest in the world. The ancients took the Dao as their unifying principle; the heart-to-heart transmission of Yao and Shun was this lineage, fundamentally embracing the world as public. Thus, to “illuminate luminous virtue throughout the world,” one must begin with investigating things to restore good knowledge. Only when good knowledge is returned does the hope of sudden penetration arise. Then, extending it to the six directions or withdrawing it into formlessness, participating in heaven and earth to nurture all things—none but the good knowledge sharing the same substance with “luminous virtue” can achieve this.

The following passage, “Only after things are investigated is knowledge extended,” reiterates the preceding meaning. “From the Son of Heaven to the common people, all must take self-cultivation as their foundation”—self-cultivation is the learning of the great person. Only after this learning is complete can one govern. The weight of the Dao’s lineage throughout history is evident here. The phrase “when the root is disordered, the branches cannot be ordered” means that scholars must start from the root. Though the branches may number in the thousands or tens of thousands, their root ultimately converges in one. Understanding the function of “illuminating luminous virtue” reveals that all things emerge from this. By grasping the essential to encompass the vast, governing the world becomes as effortless as pointing to one’s palm. If one abandons the root to chase the branches, busying oneself daily with tangible things, one only stirs the acquired spirit’s disturbances—yielding no benefit and causing harm.

“Thickness” and “thinness” relate to “investigating things.” If one is thick toward material desires, the primal spirit becomes thin and vanishes. Thinness means detachment; “letting the mind wander in detachment” expresses this idea. By thinning material desires—from thick to thin to complete elimination—the work of investigating things is accomplished.

"Daily renewal, ever renewed" expresses the meaning of "daily renewing one’s virtue." Human life, long accustomed to the acquired spirit, may discard it to employ the primal spirit—this is shedding the old to embrace the new. The *Book of Changes* speaks of "abolishing the old and establishing the new" (see *Zagua*), meaning to discard the old and open the new cauldron, setting up the furnace and establishing the tripod—the tripod being the primal spirit. Only by eliminating material desires and maintaining unceasing sincerity can the tripod be formed and the primal spirit return. "Daily" signifies layer upon layer of renewal.

"Brilliantly manifesting the lofty virtue": *lofty* means stern, elevated, majestic and profound, akin to the Daoist notion of "supremely great and deep," "abyssal and unfathomable," also termed "supremely great and firm"—hence *lofty*. "The noble person leaves nothing unexhausted in their pursuit": only when the work of investigating things reaches its utmost does true knowledge arise; only when knowledge is fully realized does the primal spirit return. Moreover, extreme movement gives rise to stillness, and extreme stillness gives rise to movement—only at the extreme can creation and transformation be generated, like the cyclic movement and stillness of the Supreme Ultimate.

Human life’s fortunes and misfortunes never depart from the Supreme Ultimate; cultivating the Dao aims to reverse the flow and return to the Supreme Ultimate. Benevolence, reverence, filial piety, compassion, and trust constitute the Five Phases, which are human nature. Aligning them with the Five Phases, benevolence corresponds to Wood, reverence to Fire, filial piety to Metal, compassion to Water, and trust to Earth. Those who study the Dao must "reduce and further reduce," first diminishing before gaining benefit—hence the saying "like cutting, like filing." For the acquired spirit must be investigated and discarded, reduced and further reduced. When the acquired spirit departs, the primal spirit returns; first diminishing, then gaining benefit—this also requires "like carving, like polishing," like sculpting jade into an implement, gradually refining it to completion.

"Sincerity" and "dignified bearing" manifest externally. The noble person studying the Dao must outwardly cultivate dignified bearing while inwardly maintaining vigilance and caution. The "modest noble person," guarding themselves with trembling caution, only then attains "abundant virtue and utmost goodness," so that the people turn to them and "cannot forget." "Abundant virtue" is "luminous virtue"; *luminous* and *abundant* both denote the finest.

Every human being should cultivate the Dao. The fundamental golden nature is universally possessed; with prolonged effort, one can naturally return to it—hence the saying "not forgotten even in death." "What is meant by making one’s intentions sincere is not deceiving oneself." The seven emotions and six desires all constitute self-deception. The original substance of the human mind is quiescent and unmoving, yet material desires entice it, constantly drawing it outward. Movement induced by external enticement is called "self-deception." Sincerity lies in not deceiving oneself; when deceitful thoughts depart, material desires can be eliminated. "Not deceiving oneself" begins with "vigilance in solitude"—*solitude* means standing alone. Being unwavering in one’s devotion to things is to be solitary and unified—unity is the true intention of the primal spirit. Only by being unwavering in devotion to things can one generate the unfathomable; if it is the acquired spirit, it is manifold and incorrect.

The "noble person" is the noble person who studies the Dao; only upon achieving the Dao does one become a great noble person. In guarding solitude for oneself, *solitude* is the "One" in "One and numinous"—*One* is emptiness. Vigilance in solitude preserves the primal spirit and strictly prevents the intrusion of material desires, hence Master Zeng said: "Watched by ten eyes, pointed at by ten fingers." For vigilance is paramount. "Ten" refers to "eyes watching," "ten" to "fingers pointing"—the word "ten" should mark the pause. "Watching" is stillness, while "pointing" is movement. Only when movement and stillness do not depart from the state of "ten" can the mind be expansive—*expansive* means vast and empty. Only with an "expansive mind" can one attain "embodied ease," manifesting as a radiant countenance and comfortable limbs. What the *Book of Changes* calls "beauty within" is the "expansive mind"; "extending to the four limbs" is "embodied ease." Not being harmed by material desires, due to discarding the acquired spirit and maintaining vigilance in solitude, sincerity within naturally manifests without—this is inevitable.

Yet to what degree must material desires be eliminated? *Mencius* said: "The great person does not lose their childlike heart." A *child* refers to a small child under three years old; above three is a *child*, under one is an *infant*. A heart like a child’s is free from material desires and empty of things—this is the great person. To not lose the childlike heart, to have a heart like a child’s, is to have already made one’s intentions sincere. The *Kang Gao* states: "Cherish the childlike heart, seek it with sincerity." The child’s heart is single-minded, without duplicity; seeking it with sincerity, one attains it.

The function of "sincerity" is vast—ascending to become a Buddha or an immortal, all rely on sincerity. The *Doctrine of the Mean* discusses "sincerity" most extensively: "Sincerity is enlightenment, and enlightenment is sincerity." "From sincerity to enlightenment is called nature; from enlightenment to sincerity is called teaching." Cultivating the Dao with a childlike body is "from sincerity to enlightenment"—*enlightenment* is seeing one’s nature. "From enlightenment to sincerity is called teaching": one must first investigate things to extend knowledge, exhaust principle and fulfill nature, first eliminating the acquired spirit and returning to the primal spirit—hence it is called "teaching." The childlike body’s heart is sincere; as long as one gathers and restrains the sincere heart, not allowing it to drift outward, one becomes enlightened. Enlightenment is attaining wisdom and the six penetrations. Those who have broken their bodies must first exhaust principle to attain sincerity and enlightenment—hence it is called "from enlightenment to sincerity."

Moreover, it states: *"Sincerity is the Dao of Heaven; cultivating sincerity is the Dao of humanity."* (See *The Doctrine of the Mean* in the *Book of Rites*.) The Dao of Heaven is the Dao of nature; nature and Heaven share virtue, for the nature endowed by Heaven’s mandate is preserved as the Dao—this is supreme virtue. *"Cultivating sincerity"* refers to those in the postnatal state, already burdened by material desires, who must devote effort to the work of "sincerity," which is precisely *"investigating things to extend knowledge."* Thus, it further states: *"Only the utmost sincerity under Heaven can fully realize one’s nature."* This means that only when the great person has attained the *"childlike heart"* and generated wisdom can they fully realize and perceive their nature—nature being one’s own nature. Once one perceives their own nature, they can then perceive the nature of others, which is the penetration of past lives; further, they attain the penetration of others’ minds. *"To fully realize the nature of others is to fully realize the nature of things"*—this is the divine penetration of the *"six penetrations."* At this stage, there is nothing between Heaven and Earth that is not penetrated. The sequence proceeds from sincerity to utmost sincerity, thereby attaining the penetrations of past lives, others’ minds, and divine realms. From utmost sincerity, the six penetrations are obtained, forming the *"six-penetration immortal,"* who can then assist in Heaven and Earth’s transformative nurturing. For their nature returns to emptiness, becoming one with Heaven and Earth, and thus they can aid in transformation and nurturing, *"participating in Heaven and pairing with Earth."*

*"Participating"* means three; *"participating in Heaven and pairing with Earth"* constitutes the five phases. Heaven and Earth possess the power of origination and generation; the great person, sharing the same empty and numinous essence as Heaven and Earth, also possesses the virtue of origination and generation, thereby participating in and aiding their transformative nurturing. Thus, they can unite with Heaven and Earth to form the *"three talents."* As Dong Zhongshu said, *"The Confucian is one who connects Heaven, Earth, and humanity."*

From *"sincere intention"* to *"utmost sincerity,"* cultivation is easier for the childlike body but difficult for those who have broken their bodies. Thus, another path of aspiration is opened, as *The Doctrine of the Mean* states: *"Next is to extend the partial."* Those of lesser virtue, unable to attain sincerity, must first *"extend the partial."* *"Partial"* means one-sided—focusing the mind singularly on one thing. By concentrating the mind to investigate things and extend knowledge, with wholehearted devotion, one restores sincerity. Through unwavering purity, this becomes *"self-sincerity."* The so-called *"utmost sincerity is singular and dense"*—the words *"singular and dense"* convey the meaning of *"extending the partial."* For the human heart is heavy with desires; if one does not first eliminate distracting thoughts and focus singularly, gathering its dispersion and concealing it densely, how can one attain utmost sincerity? In the *"learning of the great person,"* investigating things to their utmost naturally engenders sincerity, hence it is said *"the partial can attain sincerity."*

*"Sincerity takes form, form becomes manifest, manifest becomes luminous, luminous becomes dynamic."* *"Form"* means *"sincerity within takes form without."* At the state of primordial chaos, the primal spirit naturally reemerges, and a mass of misty primal *qi* returns to the crucible—is this not *"form becomes manifest"*? *"Form"* refers to the three marks of the Dharma-body, which serve as verification of one’s practice. *"Manifest becomes luminous"* signifies further progress beyond form and manifestation: the yin of the body is exhausted, the mind is illuminated to perceive nature, and the six penetrations are attained. Once the six penetrations are obtained, one can transform on a grand scale, and countless emanations may instantly appear—hence it is said *"luminous becomes dynamic."*
