*"The Dao of utmost sincerity enables foreknowledge"*—this is the great function of immortals and Buddhas. *"Sincerity is self-sincerity,"* *"and the Dao is self-Dao"*—it does not come from without but arises solely from one’s own heart. By investigating things and exhausting desires, sincerity becomes self-sincere. The Dao is the Dao of one’s own body; it is not sought externally. Rectifying the mind and sincerity of intention reside within oneself, so the Dao is within the self. As the Daoist texts say: *"The original true heart is possessed by all, complete in each,"* and as *The Heart Sutra* states: *"neither arising nor ceasing, neither defiled nor pure, neither increasing nor decreasing."*

*"Sincerity is the beginning and end of things"*—for things obscure and prevent sincerity. When material desires are exhausted, sincerity naturally arises. Before thoughts arise, there is originally no lack of sincerity; when the mind is set down and not a single thing remains, there is also no lack of sincerity. Where was nature before parents were born? Where does nature go after death? From this, one knows the emptiness of nature. Thus, it is said: *"Sincerity is the beginning and end of things; without sincerity, there are no things."* When things are exhausted and return to emptiness, emptiness is the beginning, and sincerity is the end. Emptiness is the heart; without sincerity, there is no heart, and without heart, there are no things—neither true emptiness nor the myriad things. Now that sincerity is attained, one can not only perfect oneself but also perfect things, benefiting oneself and universally ferrying others across.

*"Perfecting oneself is benevolence; perfecting things is wisdom."* These are the three virtues inherent in nature. *"Uniting the Dao of inner and outer"*—merging inner and outer into the center—is also the inner and outer of the elixir path. To unite the great and small emptiness of inner and outer is the Dao, is sincerity. The Dao of union lies in *"timely application."* *"Timely"* refers to the *"living zi"* and *"living wu."* Confucius was called the *"sage of timely mean"*—acting appropriately in each moment, not only mastering the advance of fire and retreat of the tally but also progressing in step with the times, ultimately returning to emptiness without error of excess or deficiency. This is why he is called the *"timely sage."*

*"Timely"* also means guarding against danger and considering peril. As the Daoist texts say: *"A deviation of a hair’s breadth in fire timing fails to congeal the elixir,"* and *"The timing of the zi hour is wondrous in mind-to-mind transmission."*

The moon’s fullness depends on oral instructions.” Thus, even Confucius taught the timing of fire, hence his emphasis on “acting appropriately in each moment.” “Excess and deficiency both deviate from the Mean.” It is also said: “The noble person acts appropriately in each moment.” To achieve “utmost sincerity” is to “have no cessation”—that is, to sever the breath of mouth and nose. Only when the breath of mouth and nose is cut off can the Dao be attained. If even a trace of yin qi remains, one does not become an immortal. The Buddhists call this the “extinction of all perceptions”; extinction means the absence of breath—this is the great samādhi. The Daoists say that when the six pulses have ceased and qi returns to its root, true breath is established, equivalent to an infant in the womb. This work is undertaken during the refinement of qi into spirit. *The Orthodox Theory of Celestial Immortals* states that there must be a method of verification: placing a goose feather before the mouth and nose without it moving—only then is true breathlessness achieved. Only when breathlessness is attained is there utmost sincerity, and only then can one avoid death. Thus it is said: “Without cessation, there is duration; with duration, there is verification.” Verification means external manifestation—this is the emergence of spirit. “At first, with effort, none perceive; when effortless, all come to know.” (See *Awakening to Reality*.) In the beginning, the practice is secret, unseen by others; but when spirit emerges, all witness it. “Verification leads to boundlessness; boundlessness to vastness; vastness to brilliance.” Boundlessness is emptiness. In heaven and earth, only emptiness endures longest. The great person who unites virtue with heaven and earth shares the vastness of earth, the brilliance of heaven, and the boundlessness of emptiness—able to generate and transform the myriad things without dying, enduring for eons without end, all through “utmost sincerity.” This depends on number. By deducing from numerical principles, it can be understood. The Dao gives birth to one, one to two, two to three—these are numbers. Three, five, one—these too are numbers. By gathering the five phases into three, and the three families meeting, then returning from three to one, and one to emptiness, one attains “boundless endurance.” Number derives from “three heavens and two earths.” The great person is “earnest in benevolence, profound as an abyss, vast as heaven.” “Profound as an abyss” means depth—deeply hidden yet empty, communing with the qi of heaven and earth, supremely great and unyielding, filling heaven and earth. Without “clarity, wisdom, sagacity, and comprehension of heaven,” one cannot “illumine the bright virtue.”

The following section concerns the work of sincerity. Its practice proceeds from small to large, from subtle to manifest, from a single point to full accumulation. Initially, no one perceives it; but when the six penetrations are achieved, all see and hear, and “its brilliance becomes daily more evident.” Through daily accumulation and monthly progress, success is attained. *The Seal of the Unity of the Three* says: “Prolonged obscurity leads to clarity.” At first, one must obscure the discriminating mind to enter primordial chaos; over time, the original spirit emerges, and clarity dawns. Chen Nidan said: “At the beginning, one seeks obscurity but cannot attain it; later, one seeks clarity but cannot attain it.” When first practicing, the spirit must first become obscure to extinguish the discriminating mind, uniting knowledge with spirit, free from attachment, and the heart becomes still—entering a drowsy state. If one clings to appearances, sleep does not come easily, and the spirit does not easily become obscure. After prolonged obscurity, one seeks clarity, which must be pure and cool—this work is also not easily attained. To seek obscurity, one must first reach extreme emptiness and profound stillness; to seek clarity is to generate movement from extreme stillness. Without extreme stillness, movement cannot arise. One should first dwell hidden in the depths, then reveal light through the aperture. Thus it is said: “Though hidden, it shines through the aperture.” (From *The Book of Songs·Xiaoya·Zhengyue*.) First, one returns to pure Kun to become empty; then, one regains light. “I cherish the bright virtue, not with loud voice or stern countenance.” (From *The Book of Songs·Daya·Huangyi*.) “Virtue is as light as a feather” (From *The Book of Songs·Daya·Zhengmin*), “yet feathers still have form. The workings of heaven above are without sound or scent.” (From *The Book of Songs·Daya·Wenwang*.) Only then, through utmost sincerity, does one become a celestial immortal—without sound, form, scent, or taste—ascending beyond the thirty-three heavens to the realm beyond thought and non-thought, becoming a Buddha. The Daoists call this “shattering emptiness, where the eighty-four thousand pores of the body each become a world.”

At the outset of life, all are deluded by false thoughts. By returning from delusion to sincerity, one attains utmost sincerity. The Dao lies in obscuring the discriminating mind, making it muddled and chaotic. When a person sleeps, they enter chaos. As the saying goes: “Rise at sunrise, rest at sunset.” At dusk, when one retires to rest, this is when the discriminating mind rests. The spirit dwells in the eyes by day and lodges in the liver by night. The liver, associated with wood, is fond of movement, and its orifice is the eyes. When the eyes move, they generate brightness, drawing forth the seven emotions and six desires, and even at night, they can produce dreams. The original spirit differs from the discriminating mind: though it also dwells in the eyes by day, at night it lodges in the kidneys. In the body of a child, the original spirit lodges in the kidneys at night. The kidneys, associated with water, govern stillness, which is why children’s spirits are still. By returning to the state of a child, one also lodges the spirit in the kidneys at night. The kidneys connect to the ears. As practice advances, the spirit lodges in the ears at night; with further progress, it lodges in the ears day and night. Seeing form as formless, facing circumstances without attachment, being without thought in all matters, and lodging in the ears day and night—only then is the Dao attained. In *Journey to the West*, Sun Wukong’s golden cudgel is stored in his ear—this is the meaning: only by entering the ear is the Dao achieved.

Guanyin Bodhisattva resides in the Purple Bamboo Grove by the Southern Sea, at the Tide-Sound Cave. The tide is the kidneys; sound is the ears. The Purple Bamboo Grove is the forest of individual trees, synonymous with the "Twin Forests." Wood represents the eyes, also the mind, and belongs to *Zhen* (Thunder), for *Zhen* is thunder, and the original spirit is also thunder—mercury within fire-wood. Thus it is called "Twin Forests" and also "Thunder Sound," both containing golden mercury within.

The third of the East generates wood; the third of the East is nature, likening wood to nature. The Lord emerges from *Zhen*, and the Lord is the Great One, also human nature. The nature of human life is one’s own Lord. All Dao cultivators are governed by the Lord of Eastern Florescence; the Eastern Lord governs nature, and this is the meaning.

The third of the East is nature; the second of the South is spirit, combining to form the One Five. The first of the North is emotion; the fourth of the West is essence, combining to form the One Five. The central Five is intent, forming its own One Five. Together they make the Three Fives, which is the Dao of the Three Fives.

Returning to emptiness means returning to the void. The six lines of *Kun* hexagram are all empty; Dao cultivators should take this as their model and circulate through the six voids. "The first six: Treading frost, solid ice arrives"—Dao cultivators should begin with investigating things, as when "treading frost, solid ice" comes. Ice follows the exhaustion of yang; frost is the beginning of *Bo*. "Treading frost, solid ice" means "yin begins to congeal," so one should eliminate desires to complete the *Kun* body. Frost is autumn qi, metal qi, killing qi—all seven emotions and six desires must be slain, like "treading a tiger’s tail," yet the tiger "does not bite," seeking grace from harm.

"The six in the second place" is the second line of *Kun*, where yin grows heavier. "Upright, square, and great—without practice, nothing is unfavorable"—this refers to guarding the aperture. "Upright" is |; "square" is 口. The characters "upright" and "square" contain the meaning of "aperture." "Great" is the void, connecting to the great void outside, possessing the image of greatness—this is the Gate of the Mysterious Female. "Without practice" means knowing but not clinging, thus "nothing is unfavorable," for clinging instead brings disadvantage. "The movement of the six in the second place: upright becomes square. Without practice, nothing is unfavorable—this is the radiance of the earthly way." "Without practice" means emptying this earth, so the Dao shines. The ancient forms of "upright" and "nose" are as follows: 直, 鼻. 匕 is comparison, the two eyes compared and descending straight; 艹 is the gate of vast qi. Above is the middle field of the eyes, below is the gate of qi—this is the image of the characters "upright" and "nose."

The explanation of "Awakening to Reality" in *Awakening to Reality*: "Awakening" is our mind; in the character "real," the top is comparison, the bottom is the gate of vast qi. Comparing the eyes means withdrawing vision, and by withdrawing vision, one attains the gate of vast qi—this is the meaning of "awakening to reality." The ancient form of "square" is 口; "upright" is |. 二, 二 are the characters for "above" and "below." "Vast" contains the meaning of "heaven"; the *Book of Changes* relies on images to speak. To abandon images and speak of principle is why Song Confucians often misinterpret.

"The six in the third place"—the work should advance, thus it says, "Containing brilliance, it may be correct." "Containing" means obscurity; "containing brilliance, it may be correct" means "obscure yet daily brilliant." "Correct" means enduring. One should abide at the Gate of the Mysterious Female, nurturing with true intent, which can then activate and be cultivated to completion. If not yet complete, one must guard the aperture and know when to stop, eventually achieving success. "Royal affairs" contain the meaning of advancing yang and retreating yin. The heart is likened to the ruler; with yang within, true intent has motive, waiting for the right time to act. Using true intent in affairs can make one "radiant and great." "Without completion, there is an end" also contains the meaning of caution, introspection, and cultivation.

"The six in the fourth place" is the first line of the outer trigram. Though *Kun* is not yet pure, it gradually approaches completion, thus it says, "A sack tied shut—no blame." Like an empty sack, neither good nor bad; inside is empty, but outside is not yet empty. The sack’s mouth must be tightly closed to guard against harm, only then can there be no fault.

"The six in the fifth place"—the work of returning to emptiness is nearly complete, forming a pure *Kun* body, thus it says, "Yellow skirts—primally auspicious." "Yellow" is earth; "skirts" are lower garments. "Primal" is virtuous power, with pattern within—true intent is already within. Thus "yellow center connects to principle," "beauty is within and reaches the four limbs"—true intent is in the central palace, and the whole body is at ease.

"The top six: Dragons battle in the wild, their blood dark yellow." Extreme yin generates yang; yang is the dragon. "Battling in the wild" means at the time of pure yin, yin and yang meet but do not unite, thus there is the image of "battle." For one yin and one yang, or three yins and three yangs, yin and yang can unite; but now six yins and yang is about to arise, yang qi is very weak and cannot compare, thus it is called "battle," with the image of "wild battle." Whenever six yins or six yangs are about to generate yang or yin, yin and yang cannot be balanced, so there is the image of battle, battle with bloodshed—the image of yang unable to overcome yin. "Using the six" means using the meaning of the six lines; "favorable to perpetually correct" means it is fitting to be enduringly correct and auspicious.

To seek enduring correctness and auspiciousness, one must hold emptiness and void as the heart. If one employs the six lines of *Qian*, it implies the meaning of advancing fire and retreating talisman, which differs from this and will be discussed later.

**Lecture Nine: Returning to Emptiness, Guarding Stillness, Abiding in Nature, and Reverting Qi**
**Nine Returns, Seven Reversions—All in a Moment**

In the *Kun* hexagram, qi circulates through the six voids; "returning to emptiness" means returning to *Kun*. Those who cultivate the Dao must ultimately return to emptiness and should always take *Kun* as their model. Yet how can one achieve the pure body of *Kun*? The classic states: *"Cast the body aside and the body is cultivated; forget the form and the form endures."* This is the principle of turning inward to achieve sincerity.

"Casting the body aside" means forgetting the body, treating it as external and letting it go. The corporeal form is but a shell; Buddhists call it a "foul skin-bag," composed of the essence and qi of Heaven and Earth and the essence and blood of one’s parents. It is like a house in which the spirit temporarily resides. Without this house, the primordial spirit would have no place to attach itself. Yet the body is the body, and the spirit is the spirit—at death, the primordial spirit returns to the Great Void, while the body decays. The intermediate state (*bardo*) also treats the body as a dwelling; when the dwelling is destroyed, the intermediate state departs. If one has karmic offenses, one enters hell to receive punishment; if not, one drifts aimlessly until encountering a womb and entering it, like changing dwellings.

The primordial spirit within the body is most numinous. In the postnatal state, the *hun* soul is considered numinous, acting as the master of the self, with the body as its dwelling. Since it is like a dwelling, one should regard it as insignificant and cast it aside to cultivate the Dao of "returning to emptiness." When one achieves the pure *Kun* and the body is emptied, one has returned to emptiness.

In the Buddhist *Sutra of the Sixteen Contemplations*, there is the "Contemplation of White Bones," where one visualizes one’s own body, seeing the five viscera and six bowels, imagining it as already dead, infested with worms, foul and putrid beyond approach. Practicing this for a long time, one transforms the body into white bones and contemplates them—this is called the "Contemplation of White Bones." By maintaining this contemplation, one naturally forgets the body, and for those attached to bodily form, this method is highly beneficial.

"Turning inward to achieve sincerity" requires reverting to the Dharma-body to attain sincerity. Only by casting the body aside can one cultivate the Dharma-body. Mencius said: *"I am good at nourishing my vast and flowing qi."* (See *Mencius*, "Gongsun Chou I") The two instances of "I" are emphatic: the first "I" refers to the physical self, the false self; the second "I" refers to the Dharma-body, the true self. Cultivating the false to refine the true, one nourishes the vast and flowing qi that belongs to the self. If one does not distinguish between the physical body and the Dharma-body, the first "I" could be omitted. The ancients used words with profound meaning.

The "vast and flowing qi" arises from the primordial spirit and Dharma-body; "nourishing my vast and flowing qi" means the primordial spirit and true intention unite. *"Forget the form and the form endures"* means casting the body aside, forgetting form and relinquishing the heart—for form is the heart, and the human heart is the spirit of consciousness, which sees form and enslaves it. Only by forgetting the form can one have a heart; forgetting form is relative to forgetting the heart.

All that the heart contains has form, and what is formless is deemed nonexistent. The myriad forms seen by the eyes and heard by the ears are considered "having form," while the formless is considered "not having form." Yet who knows that all forms arise from the formless? Only by seeing as if not seeing does one accord with the Dao.

The *Sutra of Purity* states: *"Inwardly contemplate the heart, and the heart is without heart; outwardly contemplate the form, and the form is without form; distantly contemplate things, and things are without things."* Forgetting form means forgetting things; inwardly contemplating without heart and outwardly contemplating without form is the meaning of forgetting heart and forgetting form, referring to oneself.

As the saying goes: *"What has form will eventually decay; what is formless is true."* Thus, to perceive all things with equanimity, forget the heart and the heart is cultivated; forget the form and the form endures. By forgetting the body’s outer form, one can empty all things and return to emptiness.

Human life consists of three aspects: body, heart, and intention. In cultivating the Dao, one must refine these three. Forget the body as body, empty the heart of heart, and when the body is forgotten and the heart is empty, intention can become true. If one cannot empty the heart, one is attached to the appearance of heart; if one cannot forget the body, one is attached to the appearance of form—for form is one of the six dusts. When the roots and dusts interact, consciousness arises, and consciousness is the heart. If one remains within the appearance of form, one cannot advance in the Dao. To transcend the appearance of form, one must forget the body and empty the heart.

The heart is the appearance of self, the body is the appearance of person, things are the appearance of sentient beings, and the desire for longevity is the appearance of life—the *Diamond Sutra* enumerates these four. Only by emptying the four appearances can one eliminate the three poisons. Greed, anger, and delusion are the three poisons, born of the seven emotions and six desires. Fame, profit, and official rank are greed; pride and competitiveness are anger; lust and infatuation are delusion. Only by emptying the four appearances can one dissolve the three poisons. If one clings to the appearances of self and others, one cannot dissolve the three poisons.

Attachment to appearances is certainly inadmissible, yet attachment to emptiness is also forbidden in Daoist cultivation. This so-called "emptiness" is dead emptiness, not true emptiness. What, then, is the difference between true emptiness and dead emptiness? True emptiness is yang and can generate wondrous existence; dead emptiness cannot. It generates thoughts of heart, mind, and emptiness, producing the appearance of emptiness—this is dead emptiness. Vast and undifferentiated, empty yet without the appearance of emptiness, is true emptiness. To forcibly empty what is not empty, to forcibly stop thoughts when they arise—this stopping mind itself is a thought, and whether the thought stops or not, it is all dead emptiness.

True emptiness arises naturally, without knowledge or perception; in a state of indistinct obscurity, profound and unfathomable. Empty yet not empty, it contains wondrous existence; existent yet not existent, it remains true emptiness. Those who cultivate the Dao must discern this carefully.

Yet in this world, all that one encounters is the seven emotions and six desires. To return to the state of emptiness, one must attain stillness of body and purity of mind. The *Kun* hexagram comprises inner and outer trigrams, corresponding to body and mind. Only when both inner and outer are empty can one circulate through the six directions of emptiness and become true emptiness. When body and mind are both still—body encompassing qi—both achieve stillness and purity, forming this ○○ phase. Only upon attaining dual emptiness does this phase manifest. When the two merge, true intention arises.

Stillness of body and mind is the essential task of Daoist cultivation. The *Analects* states: "One who can for a single day master the self and restore the rites will have all under heaven return to benevolence." (See *Analects*, "Yan Yuan") One must begin with "mastering the self and restoring the rites" (to master is to subdue thoughts; only by mastering the self can one restore the rites, for rites and principle are one) to restore this "heavenly principle." Daoists call this the "Dao-mind," like the moon on the third day of the lunar cycle. When the Dao-mind emerges, a single yang returns, generating the principle and qi of the Great Ultimate, manifesting as the crescent moon.

(The character *ren* 仁 is composed of two *ren* 人, as is *yuan* 元, signifying the mutual completion of yin and yang. The character *tian* 天 also contains two *ren* 人, representing the undifferentiated chaos of yin and yang, which coalesces into the Great Ultimate.) "All under heaven returning to benevolence" means stillness of body and mind, hence the saying: "Look not at what is contrary to ritual; listen not to what is contrary to ritual; speak not of what is contrary to ritual; move not in ways contrary to ritual." (See *Analects*, "Yan Yuan") These four actions are governed by the principle of the Great Ultimate, thereby forming the Dharma-body.

In Daoist terms: "Contain the light of the eyes, condense the resonance of the ears, seal the breath of the tongue, and regulate the breath of the nose. In seeing, hearing, speaking, and moving, do not act contrary to ritual—this is how it is achieved." Only by mastering these four practices can one attain stillness of body and mind, merging into undifferentiated chaos.

"Containing" means lowering the eyelids to seal the senses. The upper eyelid serves as the screen (whether half-lowered or fully closed is not the proper method; there is a natural degree of closure that feels effortless and comfortable. Lightly closing them, appearing shut yet not entirely so, as in sleep, is the natural way of containing the light. Each person differs in their natural endowment; it should be neither too loose nor too tight, without attachment to form, just right. The key is to avoid strain, aligning with nature as one’s innate endowment.)

The words "condense" and "resonance" carry profound meaning. That which arises from within is sound; that which enters from without is noise. The ear governs resonance; when one withdraws perception and returns to listening, external sounds vanish, and the ear naturally perceives a celestial resonance. The ancients spoke of "hearing the zither without strings"—this is that sound. To condense means to prevent external influences from entering and internal sounds from scattering, allowing the sound to coalesce within. The inner resonance arises from the even regulation of nasal breath, creating an auditory impression of resonance that is not external but originates from the breath. Ancient texts say that those deeply advanced in Daoist cultivation can hear the subtle and wondrous music of the celestial realm—this is precisely it.

"Regulating nasal breath" refers to the breath of inhalation and exhalation, which must employ the "unregulated regulation." If one deliberately regulates it, it will never be regulated. This "unregulated regulation" means that throughout the twelve hours of the day, one should subtly listen to the breath outside the nose—this is "returning to listening," whereby the ear generates resonance derived from the regulated breath. If it resembles the chirping of insects or the rumble of thunder, it is unregulated. Regulation must produce resonance, as the two are interconnected. *The Later Journey to the West* states: "The shadow of the staff above the head (the qi of the prior heavens has form, like a streak of white light), the shout enters the ear." These are the secrets of breath regulation. The shadow appears when the breath is regulated, and the shout enters the ear as the sound of inhalation and exhalation—this is the way of immortals.

The tortoise lives a thousand years, breathing through its ears, known as "tortoise breathing." There is also the saying: "The crane’s embryo and the tortoise’s breath endure a thousand years," and "The continuous awareness of the tortoise’s breath"—these are practices for those who study the Dao. By listening with the ears, one can regulate the breath.

*Zhuangzi* says: "Listening with the ears is not as good as listening with the mind; listening with the mind is not as good as listening with spirit; listening with spirit is not as good as listening with qi; listening with qi is not as good as listening with emptiness." (See *Zhuangzi*, "The Human World") "Listening with spirit" refers to awareness; "not as good as listening with qi" means qi resides in emptiness.

There is a secret to regulating breath through the ears: the "Six True Words," which are *Om, Ma, Ni, Pad, Me, Hum*. From *Om* to *Me* is inhalation; *Hum* is exhalation—these are Sanskrit sounds. From *Om* to *Me*, the breath enters and descends below the navel; *Hum* returns from below upward to the throat. It is said: "The breath should be drawn in deeply and released lightly." These six Sanskrit sounds are the natural phenomena of breathing. When the breath is regulated, one naturally attains these six sounds. If the mind is not still and sleep is elusive, recite these six words. With stillness and regulated breath, one will sleep peacefully.

The six sounds of the five notes may be recited silently without vocalization. Daoists also have seven words: *Xi, Xu, He, Xi, Xi, Chui, Hu*. From *Xi* to *He* is inhalation, while *Xi* and *Chui* are exhalation—like the six sounds above, these too are breath-regulating incantations. "Sealing the tongue" means "closing the mouth," not permanent silence but merely speaking less. In sleep or waking life, many leave their mouths agape; these must be closed. Yet forcing them shut with effort strains and clings to form, which is also unsuitable—one must close them naturally. These four—ear, eye, and mouth—are the "three treasures" of which the *Canon* speaks: "Close and seal them, do not let them flow freely. The mouth remains shut, not speaking; few words accord with the primal chaos." When this is achieved, one naturally "dwells deep as a true person, floating and abiding within the rule," and true intent emerges, allowing return to emptiness.

Initially, in returning to emptiness, there is a provisional method: "sleeping practice." The former four are "classical methods"; this is a "provisional method." Since in our bodies yin is excessive and yang scarce, sleep stills both body and mind. Over time, yang arises and yin retreats in sleep, making the mind easier to quiet—thus sleep may be used first. If one can fall asleep the moment the head touches the pillow with a pure mind, that is best. If not, one may first draw a circle ○ and gaze at it with intent yet without fixation, naturally falling into deep sleep and stilling both body and mind. Only when both are stilled does sleep accord with the method. Such tranquil sleep, however, lasts but a moment before waking; if there are matters to attend to, one may address them; if not, one may sleep again. This is the finest method for generating yang in stillness.

When spirit gathers in the eyes, gazing with intent yet without fixation forgets the body and enters emptiness. One must govern this with a formless method, exercising careful judgment at the proper time.

The *Mencius* chapter on nurturing qi is also a breath-regulating practice: "This qi is most vast and most firm; nurture it with uprightness and do not harm it." (See *Gongsun Chou I*) "Neither forget nor force" is "upright nurturing"; without it, harm arises. "That which arises from accumulating rightness" is metal qi—Daoists speak of "metal," which shares the meaning of "rightness." "Upright, square, and great. Reverence straightens the inner; rightness squares the outer." These are the words of the *Kun* hexagram. Thus qi comes from without, a teaching shared by Daoists and Confucians. Yet nurturing qi is most difficult: broadly, it is nurturing qi; narrowly, it is regulating breath—neither is easy.

The *Laozi* says, "Continuous as if existing." "Continuous" means unbroken; "existing" means generating being from non-being. Will is the commander of qi, its master. Only with will can one lead qi, and will arises from intent. The *Laozi’s Scripture of Emptiness and Non-Being* states: "That from which intent arises is called will." Thus intent commands qi, and qi flows continuously. "Continuous as if existing" means relying on breath, with will as its master. Only thus can one return to the root and restore life. "Existing" means abiding in the root. Thus the *Book of Transformation’s* "Pearls and Jade" chapter says: "Attaining the gate of vast qi, one returns to the root; knowing the vessel of original spirit, one conceals its light." This is the union of spirit and qi.

When spirit and qi unite, they restore the primordial Taiji, after which extreme stillness gives rise to movement, and extreme movement to stillness. Movement is the mechanism of stillness; stillness, the mechanism of movement—thus creation arises. The work of returning to emptiness culminates in great stillness, generating being from non-being. Heaven and humanity merge in activation, establishing the foundation of all transformations—its function lies in movement and stillness. Movement and stillness are the firing times.

When body and mind are both stilled, one embodies the pure *Kun* hexagram, empty within and without, and creation begins. In utter chaos, yin and yang unite; extreme stillness generates movement, and yang rises. Yang is the external kidney; its rising is movement and arousal. At this moment, one may employ the nine-return practice—movement is the "living midnight." Upon movement, one must awaken and advance the work; without awareness, neither nine returns nor seven reversions can begin. Since human bodies are yin-heavy, most cannot move without intent, and even when moving, may remain unaware—thus one must first return to emptiness.

Returning to emptiness seeks the extreme of stillness and its spontaneous movement, the moment when the water’s source is utterly clear. At the outset of practice, when body and mind first grow still but have not yet reached the extreme, there may be "living midnight" and "living noon," though faint and subtle. Externally present, they fail to elicit an internal response; yang may not rise, or if it does, one may not perceive it first—only the seven-reversion practice is possible, while nine returns must wait. As yang qi gradually permeates the body, at first it moves faintly, but over time, the entire body becomes supple and tingling, metal qi fills it, and yang naturally rises. Then nine returns and seven reversions proceed simultaneously, practiced together—no sequence can be distinguished.

Initially, when cultivating, body and mind may not both be stilled, so no creation occurs. Only upon achieving inner and outer emptiness, attaining the *Kun* hexagram’s firing time, do body and mind become truly still.

Hereafter, the movement begins, gradually returning and reversing. At first, it remains sluggish. With practice and familiarity, however, the moment one engages in the work, body and mind settle into stillness. In stillness, yang rises; at the first stir, spirit perceives. Nine returns and seven reversions proceed with such swiftness that no interval can be discerned.

Yet if the arriving yang qi cannot be retained within the body, it cannot accumulate to form the elixir—thus the need for "gathering and harvesting." When yang resides within, the heart becomes alert and aware; upon reaching the *living midnight*, one senses it and becomes enlivened. Why speak of *living midnight* and *living noon*? When yang qi first arrives in the body, it is the *living midnight*; when one clearly perceives its arrival, it is the *living noon*. Midnight resides in *Kan*, noon in *Li*; yet northern midnight is southern noon, and southern noon is northern midnight. Midnight and noon share the same function: when qi arrives at midnight, awareness arises at noon. Awareness of its movement is noon; the movement itself is midnight. Midnight, from *Chou* to *Si*, completes its ascent to noon—six yang hours; noon, from *Wei* to *Hai*, descends to midnight—six yin hours. In theory, yang qi travels from emptiness to the body, from midnight to noon-awareness, spanning six hours. Yet in truth, it is exceedingly swift—movement and perception occur almost simultaneously, hence "midnight and noon share the same function." As *Wuzhen Pian* states, "Seek the true seed where lightning flashes"—though likened to lightning, its speed is no exaggeration. Movement and awareness are nearly concurrent.

Stillness is the *Wuji*; from *Wuji* arises *Taiji*; *Taiji* moves and generates yang—existing in emptiness, it is called yang qi. It emerges from nothingness, truly born of non-being. Yet when one’s body attains utmost emptiness and profound stillness, becoming true emptiness and a body of pure *Kun*, pure yin, the qi generated in emptiness is pure yang. Pure yin and pure yang, being of the same kind, resonate—like a magnet attracting iron, they communicate across barriers. Thus, from non-being arises being, drawing pure yang qi into the body. The *Kun* hexagram’s words state: *"Yellow center penetrates principle"*—from external to internal; *"Beauty resides within, extending to the four limbs."* The four limbs include hands and feet; when yang qi first stirs the body, it begins at the extremities, gradually suffusing the entire body until qi is abundant, evoking the sense of *"chang"* (unobstructed flow). The character *chang* (畅) derives from *shen* (申) with an added stroke—*shen* is metal, metal is *Qian*; when metal qi fills the body, one feels unbounded ease.

Finally, when true yang qi reaches the kidneys, yang rises, and the entire body tingles with a soft numbness—not the numbness of deadened sensation, but the gentle, reviving softness of cotton. The whole body grows warm and supple, yet not lax; at this moment, the body remains as steadfast as a mountain. This is the arrival of prenatal true yang, which must be retained and gathered. Yet the truest gathering is a gathering without gathering—one must forget the body entirely. If the body is not forgotten, yang qi may escape; if forgotten, one can draw it from one yang to six yangs, accumulating it to fullness. When fullness is reached, though one may wish to forget the body, it becomes impossible—one must cease the fire, halt the practice, and either walk or attend to tasks. If one hesitates and continues, yang qi transforms into postnatal energy and dissipates. Heat leading to sweat is the sign of postnatal transformation and loss—this must not be overlooked.

*The Red Writ of the Ancient Grotto Classic* expounds the principles of returning to emptiness in movement and stillness; Lü Zu’s *Hundred-Character Stele* and other works reveal the complete work of the elixir path, which will be discussed later. Here, we interpret *The Supreme Red Writ of the Ancient Grotto Classic* as follows. This scripture comprises three chapters.

**● The Chapter of True Conduct (Part One)**
Movement in movement arises from non-movement; action in action arises from non-action. Heaven and earth found their basis in chaos; the sage establishes the ultimate through non-action. The beginning of heaven and earth is utter stillness. Through movement, the Two Modes arise; from one comes two, from two comes three, from three come the myriad things—and the myriad things manifest. The sage abides in profound stillness and non-action, the unaroused center. When stirred, all responses are timely—this is the utmost of being. Matching heaven and earth, nurturing the myriad things, the human ultimate is established. Heaven and earth’s movement arises from non-movement; the sage’s action arises from non-action.

In non-action, spirit returns; when spirit returns, the myriad things are still. In non-movement, qi dissolves; when qi dissolves, the myriad things are born. Only by guarding the center and embracing the One, preserving spirit in silence, can one hibernate and preserve the true, awaiting its arousal. Only by attaining profound tranquility and unity, gathering qi in the abyss, can one move with the seasons, flourishing and growing. Observe the progression of the four seasons: in winter, the myriad things retreat into the earth, deeply preserving their roots; when spring arrives with the scattering of ashes, yin exhausts and yang returns, and all things sprout and grow. Only by storing deeply can one flourish abundantly—such is the principle.

Spirit guards spirit; things rely on things—their root returns to the origin. Between heaven and earth, the principles of resonance are two: first, resonance between like kinds; second, completion through opposites. Water flows toward moisture, fire toward dryness; clouds follow the dragon, wind the tiger—this is the principle of resonance between like kinds. Only pure yin can receive pure yang; only utmost stillness can give rise to utmost movement—just as extreme cold must yield to extreme heat—this is the principle of completion through opposites.

Spirit returns to the quiescence of things—this is the mutual guarding of spirit, belonging to the first category. Qi dissolves and things arise—this is the mutual reliance of things, belonging to the second category. When spirit returns to great stillness, all things become quiescent; this is the principle of spirit guarding spirit, like water nourishing water. When essence and qi are inwardly gathered, all things rely on them to come into being; this is the principle of things relying on things, like stillness giving rise to movement. Yet the sage’s attainment of emptiness and preservation of quiescence, cultivation of qi and storage of spirit, aim at nothing but returning to the root and origin.

Thus, "In learning, one daily accumulates; in the Dao, one daily diminishes"—its fundamental purport is to seek the return to the root and restoration of life, achieving longevity and enduring vision; there is no other path. Contemplate and realize it, and I myself awaken to it. The "it" refers to the great Dao of returning to the root and restoring life. In its lesser aspect, it is the principle of resonance between like kinds and completion through opposites; in its greater aspect, it is the cosmogony of the universe. From one comes two, from two comes three; following this sequence, there is death and life. From five return to three, from three return to one; reversing this process in cultivation, one attains the Dao of becoming an immortal or a buddha—all must be realized and awakened to.

The "I" is the supreme self. Entering the realm of no-gap, neither dying nor living, one becomes one with heaven and earth. "No-gap" means without separation; the great transformation is inherently without division. Entering the great transformation without gap, neither dying nor living, one transforms the mortal body into the dharma-body, perfects merit and virtue, returns to truth and becomes an immortal, sharing the same substance as the great transformation—hence it is said, "one with heaven and earth."

**Entering the Sage Chapter**
Forgetting the eyes, light overflows without limit; dissolving the ears, the mind’s perception remains profoundly deep. The reason our ears and eyes cannot be keen and bright is that improper seeing and hearing are obscured by external things. As the *Daodejing* says: "The five colors blind the eyes; the five tones deafen the ears." This is the principle. Now, having forgotten seeing and hearing, the obstructions to clarity and brightness are removed, enabling the return of one’s natural luminosity—hence it is said, "light overflows without limit" and "the mind’s perception remains profoundly deep." "Light overflows without limit" means seeing all without obstruction, the utmost clarity. "Deep" means profound; "the mind’s perception remains profoundly deep" means penetrating all depths, the utmost keenness.

When both faculties are utterly forgotten and severed, it is the gate of all wonders. The ears and eyes are the organs of seeing and hearing, hence called "the two faculties." Cultivating the Dao also requires transcending the consciousness of the ears and eyes, employing the primordial spirit—yet many forget but cannot sever. Only by forgetting and severing completely, without the slightest residue, can the transformation of creation arise. The word "sever" must be noted. Our original nature is inherently luminous and complete, but the six senses interact with external dust, mistaking the false for the constant and clinging to it, thus generating delusion. Now, awakening to the illusory nature of external realms, setting aside the two roots of ears and eyes, utterly forgetting and severing them, one root returns to its source, and the six senses attain liberation. Entering the stream and forgetting existence, one achieves true enlightenment, giving rise to the most wondrous transformation. For example, the six senses can function interchangeably—seeing with the ears and hearing with the eyes, this is one; perceiving without relying on the senses, this is two; attaining the six supernatural powers, such as the divine eye and divine ear, this is three. The wonders are manifold, hence called "all wonders." It is called a "gate" because only through this can one attain all wonders, like a doorway.

Pure and complete, in harmony with the great square; boundless and vast, in harmony with the measureless. This describes the sage who has attained the Dao: the great square has no corners, the measureless is absolute; pure and complete without localization, boundless and vast beyond duality. Only the sage who has attained the Dao can achieve this.

The vastness of heaven and earth is what I sustain; the multitude of the ten thousand things is what I uphold. How can there be an end to speak of its exhaustion? Assisting heaven and earth in transforming and nourishing, the cosmos is in one’s hands, the myriad transformations arise from one’s body; heaven and earth rely on this to endure, and the ten thousand things thereby flourish. It all depends on the sage to uphold them. Confucius said: "Great was Yao as a ruler! Only heaven is great, and only Yao emulated it." (*Analects*, "Tai Bo") The *Book of Changes* says: "When the sage arises, the ten thousand eyes behold." (*Qian* hexagram, "Wenyan") Lai Zhide’s commentary states: "Rising above the myriad things, all states are at peace, and all things are intimate." Heaven and earth, the ten thousand things—because of the sage, they do not come to an end. How great is such merit and virtue!

**Abiding in the World Chapter (Part Two)**
Nurture the formless, and form endures; preserve the bodiless, and the body remains whole. When wholeness and truth mutually support each other, one can endure long. The form of the formless is the true form; the body of the bodiless is the true body. Only by attaining the formless and bodiless can one’s form and body achieve longevity and enduring vision. The so-called formless and bodiless entity is the true reality of the limitless, the primordial qi of the Great One that contains truth—the root of becoming an immortal or a buddha.

Heaven attains its truth and thus endures; earth attains its truth and thus lasts; humans attain their truth and thus live long. Heaven and earth, by attaining this limitless truth and the primordial qi, can endure long; humans, by attaining this limitless truth and the primordial qi, can also achieve longevity and enduring vision. The reason people cannot endure long is that they lose the formless, scatter the bodiless, and cannot make the hundred bones and nine orifices coexist with the true body—hence they die.

People perish prematurely because they injure and lose their formless and bodiless truth. The primordial qi of the Great One, within the human body, is the original qi—also called yang qi—innate from birth. What the common folk "use daily yet remain unaware of" is precisely this original qi. Human life is dissipated by external temptations, ensnared by the seven emotions and six desires, and the true yang and original qi are squandered. Day by day, month by month, the injury accumulates until the loss is complete; the hundred bones and nine orifices become hollow vessels, and the true essence dissipates, leading to death. As the saying goes: *"With a trace of yin qi, one cannot become an immortal; with a trace of yang qi, one does not die."* When yang is extinguished, death is inevitable. The purpose of Daoist practice lies in preserving what has not yet been lost and replenishing what has. From the void, one draws the primordial qi to the body to restore what has been lost; further accumulating essence and amassing qi, one fills the true yang to ascend as an immortal. The so-called "Small Celestial Cycle" and "Great Celestial Cycle" are both methods for refining this true yang. Lü Zu’s *Hundred-Character Stele* is the most concise among alchemical classics, yet it exhaustively expounds the Dao of inner alchemy—practitioners should study it deeply. Below is its explication:

**Nurture qi, forget speech, and guard.**
Nurturing qi is one matter, yet it requires forgetting speech and forgetting guarding. "Forgetting speech" means sealing the mouth; "forgetting guarding" is the guarding of non-guarding—knowing yet not clinging. If the word "guard" is taken too literally, truly guarding the orifices or qi, it becomes incorrect, hence "forget guarding." Only when both speech and guarding are forgotten can qi be nurtured. Speech forgotten, one remains unmoved; guarding forgotten, one attains purity—this is the meaning of "neither forgetting nor forcing." Qi and spirit arise in mutual dependence, so there is no need to speak of guarding with spirit. Returning to the orifices *is* guarding; no separate guarding is necessary.

The dwelling places of spirit are three: the Celestial Valley, the Responsive Valley, and the Spirit Valley. The Celestial Valley is the Mud Pill Palace; the Responsive Valley is the Crimson Palace, which responds to stimuli—hence its name; the Spirit Valley is the Valley Passage, also known as the yin heel orifice. Returning to the Celestial Valley, one remains still and unmoving; returning to the Responsive Valley, one may extend outward; returning to the Spirit Valley, one conceals and hides. When the original spirit returns to the Celestial Valley and the consciousness spirit retreats to the Spirit Valley, they are concealed and return to nothingness. The Responsive Valley is where inner and outer correspond, uniting with qi—this is the Crimson Palace. Daoist practitioners should begin their work here.

**Subdue the heart to act without acting.**
"Subduing the heart" means taming one’s mind. Dissolving the consciousness spirit is subduing the heart. "Acting without acting" means taking non-action as action, operating in the space between intention and non-intention—acting yet not acting—to subdue the heart. For mere action is attachment to existence, and non-action is falling into emptiness. Acting without acting means the mind is undivided, no thoughts arise—this is the meaning of "continuous yet subtle," "neither forgetting nor forcing." When the heart is subdued to this point, dynamic and static practices naturally emerge.

**In movement and stillness, know the Ancestor and the Origin.**
The "Ancestor" is the primordial qi of Qian, which in humans is nature; the "Origin" is the original qi of Kun, which in humans is life. The Ancestor is the original spirit; the Origin is the original qi—two names for the same source, divided into lead and mercury, though mercury truly resides within qi. The Ancestor is the principle-qi of the primordial Great Ultimate; the Origin is the qi born from the union of parental essence and spirit. In the postnatal body, the Ancestor is the original spirit, while the Origin is the root of sentient life—also the externally derived qi. The Ancestor and Origin are distinguished in movement and stillness: in movement, one knows the Origin; in stillness, one knows the Ancestor—this is the time of the "living zi" and "living wu." When body and mind are both still, the original spirit manifests—this is nature, the Ancestor. When stillness reaches its extreme and movement arises, yang is born from emptiness and returns to the body—this is life, the Origin. When movement reaches its extreme and stillness returns, stillness again gives rise to movement, cycling endlessly. Stillness is spirit; movement is qi. Only when spirit and qi unite can one know movement and stillness. When stillness reaches its extreme and movement arises, yang returns with the original qi—this is the time to advance the yang fire. When movement reaches its extreme and stillness returns, yin approaches with cooling fluid—this is the time to retreat the yin tally. Knowing movement and stillness is knowing the Origin and the Ancestor. With these two, one completes the human Dao and may progress to the immortal Dao. The path of progress lies within this "knowing," for it contains the principles of ceaselessly returning to the root and the interdependence of heart and breath.

**Why seek further when there is nothing more to do?**
These three lines alone exhaustively expound the essentials of Daoist practice. Following this method, one can transform the mundane into the sacred without seeking elsewhere. If one deems it insufficient and seeks other paths, that is "external cultivation."

Nurturing qi with the guarding of non-guarding, nurturing the heart with the action of non-action. Movement is qi; stillness is spirit. Only by nurturing qi and nurturing the heart can one know that movement and stillness have an Ancestor and an Origin. The details of fire timing and the great Dao are simple—there is nothing else to it.

**The true constant must respond to things; in responding, one must not be deluded.**
This Dao is the "true constant." By nurturing the heart and nurturing qi, one attains the true constant and preserves the original spirit and original qi. Yet one must still respond to things—remaining impartial and magnanimous, responding to things as they come, aligning with their principles—this is "responding to things without delusion."

Within the realm of phenomena lie the six roots and six dusts—internally the six roots, externally the six dusts. When root and dust meet, one must respond by entrusting things to things themselves, without being deluded by them. For instance, when encountering wealth or feminine beauty, one must handle them with the Dao, without being seduced. This is how one preserves the original spirit and original qi without loss—this is the Dao of the true constant.

When not deluded by phenomena, the nature abides of itself; when the nature abides, qi returns of itself. Not being deluded by phenomena, the nature naturally abides. When the nature abides in the Heavenly Valley, it becomes the primordial spirit of the Heavenly Valley, and one can thereby "illumine the heart and see the nature." Once the primordial spirit abides, true breathing returns of itself. The character "return" (回) consists of two mouths, signifying mouth-to-mouth and aperture-to-aperture alignment.

Nature and life are originally inseparable. If the practitioner can cultivate nature to firmness, the primordial spirit becomes its sovereign and attains natural spiritual awareness, then the work of life will naturally advance alongside the work of nature. Ordinary breathing then becomes true breathing, and the Mysterious Pass takes form. For the primordial spirit and true breathing depend upon each other—when spirit settles, qi abides, and each breath returns to the root. As *Zhuangzi* says: "The true person breathes from the heels; their breath is deep and subtle, as if present, as if absent." This describes the state when nature abides and qi returns.

When qi returns, the elixir forms of itself; within the crucible, Kan and Li unite. When nature abides and qi returns, the one spirit and one qi naturally coalesce to form the elixir. For the primordial spirit and primordial qi, the true nature and true breathing, embrace and depend upon each other. When spirit scatters outward, qi also scatters outward; when nature returns to the root, qi naturally returns to the root—hence the saying, "exhausting nature to reach life." Buddhist texts focus solely on the principles of nature without addressing life, yet when the work of nature is accomplished, the work of life follows as a natural consequence, an inevitable attainment.

The three instances of "of itself" in the preceding and current lines reveal the subtle dynamics at play—practitioners may thus discern where to apply their effort. "Within the crucible" describes the aperture resembling Penglai’s gourd, which is also the meaning of the elixir furnace. With water and fire in harmony, Kan and Li unite—this is the manifestation of elixir formation. Once nature abides and qi returns, the elixir naturally forms within the furnace.

Kan is the moon; Li is the sun. Their union forms the character for "elixir" (丹). Yin and yang undergo reversal and transformation, universally transforming in a single thunderclap. The union of Kan and Li is the process of extracting from Kan to fill Li: taking the one yang within Kan and returning it to Li to restore its pure Qian vigor; descending the one yin within Li and returning it to Kan to restore its pure Kun image—this is the reversal of yin and yang.

The practice of the Dao lies entirely in the reversal of yin and yang. From birth after the breaking of the body, yin daily waxes while yang daily wanes, and when yang is exhausted, death follows. This is the natural course—life and death unfold in sequence. The Daoist practitioner reverses this process, from returning to emptiness and laying the foundation to refining the self, progressing through the Microcosmic and Macrocosmic Orbits. The entire practice consists of gathering the true yang from the void and returning it to the body to refine and transmute the body’s turbid yin—this is what is meant by "reversing the course to cultivate immortality and Buddhahood."

Thus, the phrase "reversal of yin and yang" encompasses the entire great function of elixir formation without omission. However, to embark on this path, one must await the moment of "earth thunder’s return." The *Yijing*’s *Fu* hexagram states: "Return—is this not the manifestation of Heaven and Earth’s heart?" When the heart of Heaven appears and the living midnight arrives, one should gather fire to carry metal, set the River Chariot in motion, and circulate the orbit. From the Microcosmic to the Macrocosmic Orbit, the principle is the same—one must await the "return of the one yang," the arrival of the midnight hour, before commencing the work of reversal and refinement.

As Ancestor Lü said: "When earth thunder shakes the mountain’s peak, rain awaits to wash the yellow sprout as it emerges from the soil." At this time, yang fire and yin tally circulate above and below, generating being from non-being, returning from being to non-being, ascending and descending, advancing and retreating, until yin is exhausted and yang is pure, transmuting into a single qi of yang. This is the moment when the yellow sprout emerges from the soil. If the practitioner obtains the method and cultivates accordingly, even a rustic fool may transcend the ordinary and enter sagehood, shedding the mortal coil to become an immortal.

The *Yijing* says: "The Supreme emerges from *Zhen*." *Zhen* Wood is the ancestral nature of the eastern trigram, "prior to Heaven yet Heaven does not oppose it"—hence the phrase "universally transforming in a single thunderclap."

White clouds ascend to the crown; sweet dew sprinkles Mount Sumeru. "White clouds ascend to the crown" describes the maturation of the great medicine, the return of the one yang, as yang qi ascends along the Governor Vessel to the Qian crown. This is what the *Awakening to Reality* means by "relying on Kun’s position to generate the body, planting it in Qian’s palace of mutual resonance." "Sweet dew sprinkles Mount Sumeru" describes yang qi reaching the crown, where the one yin arrives to mate, transmuting into a cool liquid that descends from the crown like a drop of sweet dew, passing through the multi-storied tower to return to the Yellow Court. This is the process of ingesting and obtaining the elixir—the work of the Macrocosmic Orbit.

One drinks the wine of immortality—who in their carefree wandering could know? The sweet dew enters the mouth with an extraordinary fragrance; by ingesting and obtaining the elixir, one attains longevity and enduring vision—hence it is called the "wine of immortality." As the *Awakening to Reality* says: "The eldest son suddenly drinks the wine of the west," and the *Rootless Tree* says: "The wine of ghee, the nectar of returning souls, is the Medicine King that revives the dead"—this is precisely it. This is the wine of immortality brewed and drunk by oneself, not to be shared with others—"like one who drinks water, knowing its warmth or coolness for oneself."

When the sweet dew is ingested, a pervasive coolness suffuses the body, and joy fills the heart—joy beyond compare. The Confucians call this "true joy," as in Yanzi’s "unaltered" state. This joy is known only to oneself, for only one’s own inherently empty and luminous nature, one with the Great Void, can roam freely and at ease—truly beyond the reach of words to describe.

Silently listen to the stringless melody,
Stealthily penetrate the mechanism of creation.

These two lines summarize the whole. At the outset of practice, one begins by returning to emptiness, playing the holeless flute and hearing the stringless melody, so that "nature abides of itself," "qi returns of itself," and "the elixir coalesces of itself." The three "of itself"s are precisely the "stealthy penetration." In the final stage of "returning to emptiness," one’s own emptiness merges with the emptiness of heaven and earth. With prolonged practice, one naturally shatters the void. Others see only my non-action, unaware that I have already assisted in heaven and earth’s transformative nurturance—hence "stealthily penetrate the mechanism of creation."

All told, these twenty lines form the very ladder to heaven. Though this chapter comprises only twenty lines, it exhaustively describes the work of Daoist cultivation. Follow this practice, and the ladder to heaven stands ready, allowing one to ascend step by step.

After composing the *Awakening to Reality*, the Perfected Zhang Ziyang wrote the *Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* and transmitted it to Bai Yuchan. He knew that Bai Yuchan could not fully grasp the profound subtleties of the *Awakening to Reality*, leading to the error of "midnight wind and thunder." The *Four Hundred Words* is simpler and clearer, serving as an explication of the *Awakening*’s imagery. Countless commentaries on the *Four Hundred Words* exist, but Peng Haogu’s is the finest. Min Yide selected Peng’s commentary and added his own annotations, making it especially lucid. Since the annotations are already exceedingly clear, a careful study of their meaning will reveal what lies beyond words. Below, we present the *Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* by the Perfected Zhang Ziyang, with Peng’s commentary and Min’s annotations, and expound upon them:

True earth seizes true lead,
True lead restrains true mercury.
Lead and mercury return to true earth,
Body and mind still and unmoving.

*(Peng’s Commentary)*
At the beginning of human life, Heaven’s One generates Kan Water as the kidneys, and kidney water, heavy and dense, resembles lead. The kidneys generate qi, and within qi lies a single essence of True One—this is true lead. Earth’s Two generates Li Fire as the heart, and heart fire, volatile and soaring, resembles mercury. The heart generates fluid, and within fluid lies a single spark of right yang qi—this is true mercury. Within Kan resides Wu Earth, and within Li resides Ji Earth. Water is fundamentally yin, yet within yin lies true yang; fire is fundamentally yang, yet within yang lies true yin—these are true earth. Yin intermingles with yang, and yang with yin, all depending on true earth. True lead is the qi within the body, true mercury is the spirit within the heart, and true earth is the intent within the body. If one can mobilize one’s true intent to ensure mercury always welcomes lead and lead always restrains mercury, so that lead and mercury return to true earth, then spirit and qi merge, nature and emotions unite, and body and mind become still and unmoving. Though called unmoving, within this thusness there is clarity, and within this darkness there is illumination. Illusory thoughts are extinguished, yet the illuminating mind remains undimmed; the physical body seems dead, yet the Dharma-body endures—this is not a fall into inert emptiness or withered desiccation.

*(Min’s Annotation)*
By directing intent to dwell in the body and gather the mind, one achieves the clustering of the Five Phases and the harmony of the Four Images. From this, the Five Qi converge at the origin, and gradually the Three Flowers gather at the crown. How direct and unencumbered—it is simply a matter of sincerity leading the way. True earth is true intent, which is also the natural spiritual awareness; true lead is the precelestial True Yi’s qi; true mercury is the restless spirit within the heart. By using true intent to seize the precelestial True Yi’s qi, one employs precelestial qi to subdue the restless human mind. When lead and mercury both return to true earth, the Three Families meet at the Central Palace, and body and mind become still and unmoving.

From emptiness, white snow is born,
From stillness, the yellow sprout emerges.
The jade crucible’s fire glows gently,
The golden tripod emits purple radiance.

*(Peng’s Commentary)*
Emptiness and stillness are the initial efforts in gathering the medicine; white snow and the yellow sprout are the signs of the medicine’s arising within the body. Yet associating "white snow" with "emptiness" and the "yellow sprout" with "stillness" also has its distinctions. White snow arises from the void, while the yellow sprout requires the nurturing of fire, for fire can generate earth. When the time corresponds to the *Gou* hexagram, heaven conjoins with earth, and from emptiness, white snow naturally arises; when the time corresponds to the *Fu* hexagram, earth conjoins with heaven, and from stillness, the yellow sprout naturally emerges. When yin reaches its extreme, yang arises, and the yellow sprout grows from the earth. The "jade crucible" is the Yellow Court, and "fire" is spirit. Using spirit to gather qi, the fire resides within the jade crucible, and the gentle warmth is the sign of firing. The "tripod" is the Qian position, the original palace of spirit. Black transforming into red becomes purple, and fire ascending becomes radiance—radiance and fire are not two things. "The jade crucible’s fire glows gently" means the spirit of the Qian Palace illuminates within the Kun Palace. When the tripod emits purple radiance, the spirit, receiving the fire of Qian, manifests beyond the Kun Palace, revealing the image of ascent.

*(Min’s Annotation)*
The heart dwelling in the body for a long time marks the initial effect of the body being governed by the mind. White snow is the root of jade fluid, and the yellow sprout is the origin of golden fluid. One arises from nothingness into form, the other emerges from extreme stillness into movement.

"The jade crucible" and "golden tripod" are the very places where white snow and yellow sprout attain longevity. Only within the jade crucible does the natural firing process occur—its gentle warmth nurturing until purple radiance ascends into the golden tripod, where spirit guides qi to accomplish the Dao. When true intent remains undispersed, the fire naturally glows gently.

The lotus of the Flowery Pond blooms,
The spirit-water’s golden waves grow still (or: pure).
Deep in the night when the moon shines bright,
Heaven and earth become a single mirror.

*(Peng’s Annotation)*
Master Ziyang’s *Preface* states: "Using lead to refine mercury is called the Flowery Pond; infusing mercury into lead is called spirit-water." From this perspective, the Flowery Pond is the stem of life, while spirit-water is the root of nature. To merge nature and life, first cast mercury into lead, then use lead to govern mercury. The true essence of the Kan Palace, bearing the upright yang qi, ascends—its appearance like the blooming of a lotus. The upright yang qi of the Li Palace, following the true essence, descends—its tranquility like the stillness of golden waves. When the hour approaches midnight, water clears and metal whitens, and nature and life, spirit and qi, merge into one. Thus it is said, "Heaven and earth become a single mirror." This is simply the union of nature with life—concentrating spirit into the qi cavern.

The ancients said, "There is no other method in elixir refinement; merely guide spirit into the Flowery Pond, and all matters are complete." This is precisely the meaning.

*(Min’s Annotation)*
When heart and body unite, purely following Heaven’s principle, the natural workings begin, and the time to commence arrives. The line "The lotus of the Flowery Pond blooms" signifies spirit entering the qi cavern; "The spirit-water’s golden waves grow still" signifies extending nature to harmonize with emotion. Buddhist scriptures take the blooming of the lotus as the manifestation of wisdom-light. Within the fire of Li resides true water—this is spirit-water, an analogy for true mercury, which is also the heart. When the heart-ground is luminous, emotional consciousness quiets, and golden waves naturally still—golden waves being a metaphor for emotional consciousness. "Deep in the night when the moon shines bright" is the radiance of nature’s light, the image of the heart-moon’s full brilliance. Midnight is the time when external realms are most serene. At this moment, all scenes are clear, a single wheel shines alone, the furnace fire turns azure, and body and mind remain unmoving. This passage describes the time when inner cultivation matures and the light of nature manifests.

Cinnabar refines yang qi,
Quicksilver cooks metal essence.
Metal essence and yang qi—
Cinnabar and quicksilver.

*(Peng’s Annotation)*
Cinnabar is the upright yang qi within liquid; quicksilver is the true essence within water. To replenish the yin of the Li Palace, extract the true essence from the liquid. "Metal essence and yang qi" when refined with yang fire become like cinnabar; when nurtured with yin tally, they become like quicksilver—yet they are not two things.

*(Min’s Annotation)*
When the time to commence arrives, refine and cook intensely, yet still govern the body with the heart and nurture the heart with the body—there is no other method. Cinnabar, when refined, is yang qi; quicksilver, when cooked, is metal essence. Yang qi and metal essence reside within cinnabar and quicksilver; without fierce firing and cooking, their true nature does not emerge. This is an analogy for original spirit and the heart of Dao, which reside within the spirit of consciousness and the human heart. By eliminating the spirit of consciousness and the human heart, one can return to the original spirit and the heart of Dao, attaining true mastery. Even cinnabar and quicksilver are one thing—different names for the same essence, united to form the Dao. This speaks of preserving the original spirit, whereby the heart of Dao naturally appears. As the *Wuzhen Pian* says: "It is originally a single taste of quicksilver, circulating through all the celestial stems." This is the meaning, and the analogy is most apt and fitting.

The sun’s soul is the jade hare’s marrow,
The moon’s spirit is the golden crow’s pith.
Gather them into the tripod’s interior,
And they transform into a pool of water.

*(Peng’s Annotation)*
The sun’s wood-fire is the sun’s soul, which stores spirit, and within yang resides yin—yin being the moon’s spirit. The sun cannot generate its light without the moon, hence "the jade hare’s marrow," marrow deriving from essence. The moon’s metal-water is the moon’s spirit, which stores essence, and within yin resides yang—yang being the sun’s soul. The moon cannot complete its form without the sun, hence "the golden crow’s pith," pith deriving from spirit. Just as cinnabar is the essence within the sun, which, when touched by the moon’s radiance, generates true qi; quicksilver is the radiance within the moon, which, when touched by the sun’s essence, generates true lead. Great cultivators and superior adepts transform spirit into qi, and qi into essence; middling adepts retain essence to preserve qi, and qi to preserve spirit. When spirit and essence merge and harmonize within the precious tripod, sweet dew descends, forming a pool of water.

*(Min’s Annotation)*
Through mutual nurturing and fusion, body and mind begin to transform, and only then can one observe the aperture.

The true mercury and true lead are the medicinal substances used to complete the elixir. These medicinal substances contain yin and yang within each other: true lead, a yang substance, is the sun’s soul, yet it is stored within water as the central line of Kan; true mercury, a yin substance, is the moon’s spirit, yet it is stored within fire as the central line of Li. The cultivator gathers these medicinal substances into the tripod, where the four great elements steam and fuse, yang qi ascends to the summit of Kunlun, unites with true yin, transforms into liquid, and returns to the tripod—this is the returning elixir. This passage clarifies the mutual containment of yin and yang, instructing the cultivator to invert yin and yang, returning them to the tripod.

Medicinal substances arise in the mysterious aperture,
Firing processes ignite the yang furnace.
When dragon and tiger meet,
The precious tripod gathers the mysterious pearl.

*(Peng’s Annotation)*
"The mysterious aperture" is the mysterious female—mysterious yang and female yin—together called the mysterious aperture.

The lower elixir field of the human body is the furnace, where the spirit resides, and fire arises from here—hence it is called the "yang furnace." Above the furnace is the tripod. When stillness reaches its extreme, movement arises, and the medicinal substances naturally emerge—this is the time for their gathering. After obtaining the medicine, if there is no firing process to refine it, the medicine will scatter again, and how could the mysterious pearl be produced? One must "turn the pivot at the crown gate and seize the pearl beneath the black dragon’s jaw." The medicine is fire, and fire is medicine; when medicine and fire unite, dragon and tiger meet, the embryo hangs in the precious tripod, and the millet-like mysterious pearl is naturally born. Yet only when the mysterious aperture produces the medicine can the yang furnace emit fire. If the moment has not arrived but one forces growth prematurely, external fire may act, but the internal tally will not respond—this will only burn one’s own body. Generally, when spirit and qi harmonize, the mysterious aperture appears of itself. Once the mysterious aperture appears, the firing process is naturally known; once the firing process is known, dragon and tiger naturally merge. When dragon and tiger merge, the mysterious pearl is naturally formed—its subtlety lies in the interplay of movement and stillness, not deviating by even a hair’s breadth.

*(Min’s Annotation)*
When body and mind are purely unified without admixture, the symbolic transformation becomes suddenly clear. Yet one must maintain single-minded focus, for vigilance in solitude is of utmost importance. The preceding five sections emphasize only the word "stillness," but here the word "reverence" is added as the guiding principle. The work of "maintaining reverence and preserving sincerity" lies precisely here!

Observe: the medicinal substances are true lead and true mercury, which are also spirit and qi. Only after filling the abdomen with metal can the mysterious pass take form; when the abdomen contains medicine, the mysterious aperture arises. The yang furnace is the Supreme Ultimate furnace; spirit and qi are both the medicinal substances and the fire. Once the mysterious pass takes form, fire naturally arises from the yang furnace; one dragon and one tiger meet in the central palace, giving birth to the mysterious pearl. The mysterious pearl is another name for the golden elixir. The dragon pertains to wood and is called wood mercury; the tiger pertains to metal and is called metal lead. The meeting of dragon and tiger is also the merging of metal and wood. When the mysterious pearl takes form, yang is born—this truly stems from the precious tripod refining dragon and tiger. This chapter speaks of the merging of metal and wood to congeal the elixir.

This aperture is no ordinary aperture,
For it is jointly formed by Qian and Kun.
Called the cavity of spirit and qi,
Within it lies the essence of Kan and Li.

*(Peng’s Annotation)*
"This aperture" refers to the aforementioned mysterious aperture. As it belongs neither to being nor non-being, transcends form and location, and exists beyond body and mind, emerging in the realm of the indistinct, it is called "no ordinary aperture." In the human body, Qian is above and Kun below; Qian’s central line seeks Kun to form Kan, which occupies Kun’s position while qi resides within it. Kun’s central line seeks Qian to form Li, which lodges in Qian’s position while spirit resides within it. Spirit is the mercury within the heart; qi is the lead within the body. The heart is the chamber of spirit, and the body is the tally of qi—hence it is called "the cavity of spirit and qi." Within the body lies a point of true yang qi, which pertains to Li; within the heart lies a point of true yin essence, which pertains to Kan—hence it is said, "within it lies the essence of Kan and Li."

*(Min’s Annotation)*
When body and mind merge into oneness, and stillness reveals the aperture, one is no longer a mortal body of flesh. Thereafter, one must guard body and mind with caution, never straying nor releasing them. The essential principle lies solely in sincerity of intention and strict mastery of desires.

Observe: the mysterious pass is the most profound and wondrous mechanism. Master Zhang’s preface to this chapter explains it thus: "To know this aperture is to know where the winter solstice occurs, where the medicinal substances reside, where the firing process takes place, where bathing occurs, where the embryo forms, and where the embryo is shed. It has no boundaries, no inside or outside—it is the root of spirit and qi, the valley of emptiness." His words are truly incisive and illuminating.

This aperture is formed by the principle and qi of the Supreme Ultimate, the root of human nature and life—hence it is said to be "jointly formed by Qian and Kun." In our postnatal bodies, it is hidden and invisible; only when medicinal substances enter the body and the elixir’s origin is congealed does this aperture manifest—hence it is called "the cavity of spirit and qi, within which lies the essence of Kan and Li." The essence of Kan and Li is the medicinal substance. As the Immortal Ge’s *Xuanxuan Poem* says:
*"Where Qian and Kun unite is the true center,
The center lies in emptiness, vast and boundless.
Gather dragon and tiger within the aperture,
And the pivot of creation rests in your grasp."*
This perfectly annotates these two chapters. The nature and function of the mysterious pass have been fully expounded. Yet people still seek the mysterious pass in the physical body—how foolish!

A speck of wood mercury, crimson red,
Four pounds of metal lead, pitch black.
Lead and mercury congeal cinnabar,
Glowing with the hue of purple gold.

*(Peng’s Annotation)*
The point of true yin essence within the heart is called "wood mercury." Wood corresponds to the number three, containing one point of true yang qi—hence it is called "a speck." Wood generates fire, and fire is red—hence it is called "a speck of red." The point of true yang qi within the body is called "metal lead."
