Metal corresponds to the number four—hence it is called "four pounds." Lead is black, and metal and water share the same palace—hence it is called "four pounds of black." First, red enters black; then, black is refined into red. Red and black merge, congealing a millet-like pearl in the precious tripod, which shines with a purple glow. Red and black combine to form purple—hence it is called "the hue of purple gold." Yet lead and mercury are not fixed substances, red and black have fixed colors, and "a speck" and "four pounds" are not fixed quantities. Those who grasp the meaning will understand for themselves.

(Min’s Exegesis) This remains the work of merging body and mind, of maintaining reverence and preserving sincerity. Through true accumulation over time, when the great Dao congeals to its fullest, then—and only then—will the heart be fully satisfied, and one may employ the practice of pure spontaneity.

Note: This chapter again speaks of the intermingling of metal and wood, culminating in the formation of the Great Elixir. Mercury is fire, and "wood mercury" is the liquid of fire within wood, its color still red; east (three) and south (two) together make five. Lead is water, and "metal lead" is the metal within water, its color still black; north (one) and west (four) together make five. Through the matchmaking of the Yellow Dame of the Center, the Golden Liquor Returned Elixir is formed—hence the saying, "Lead and mercury congeal cinnabar, shining with the hue of purple gold."

Further note: Peng’s commentary records "four pounds of black metal lead," while the version cited by the lecturer reads "three pounds of black metal lead." The characters "three" and "four" do not align. Peng’s commentary retains the original text to preserve its authenticity, while the main text follows the lecturer’s version, using "three." This discrepancy is duly noted here for scholars to consider.

The homestead’s scenery is fair,
Wind and rain deepen spring’s care.
Plowing (or tilling) costs no toil,
All the earth turns to gold.

(Peng’s Commentary) "Homestead" refers to the true earth within the body; "scenery" refers to the manifestations of the medicinal substances within. When the time of the return of the first yang arrives, simply stir the wind of Xun to blow upon the earth of Kun, thereby drawing the divine water to moisten the Flowery Pond. By the time the three yangs intermingle in harmony, one must be cautious not to expend effort in gathering, but rather neither forget nor force—then the yellow sprout of the earth will naturally burst forth. It is called "gold" because it coalesces into the Golden Elixir.

(Min’s Exegesis) The qi generated by the accumulation of righteousness is vast and unyielding; it is merely nurtured directly without harm. Note: "Homestead" here is the "treasure possessed by every household" mentioned earlier. "The homestead’s scenery is fair" signifies that one’s innate medicine is already sufficient. "Wind and rain deepen spring’s care" indicates that the firing process has reached the stage of "pure azure in the crucible." At this time, one should remain at ease, neither forgetting nor forcing—hence "plowing costs no toil." Any exertion would instead harm the Elixir. When the spiritual embryo is delivered and transformed, mountains, rivers, and the great earth all become the Seven Treasures, and one’s body is enveloped in golden light—hence "all the earth turns to gold." This chapter speaks of the work of nurturing the Great Elixir. As *Awakening to Reality* states: "To know the method of refining and nurturing the Returned Elixir, / One must plant the seed in one’s own homestead. / No need for blowing or forcing, / The fruit will naturally ripen, forming the spiritual embryo." This is the explanation of the present chapter.

True lead is born in Kan,
Its function lies in Li.
From black it transforms into red,
A dense cloud of qi fills the crucible.

(Peng’s Commentary) The kidneys generate qi, and the true water of the One within qi is true lead. This true water of the One is the correct yang qi of the Li Palace. When qi and liquid merge, the true water of the One is gathered from within the qi—not the correct yang qi extracted from the liquid—so that mother and child meet and cherish each other. Then metal is concealed within water, and there is no way for it to ascend. For the spirit to guide qi is the essential oral transmission of the ages. Hence it is said, "Its function lies in Li," for its substance is in Kan while its function is in Li. Once lead emerges through fire, water transforms from black to red. The medicine is fire itself. From the path of the Three Passes, it rises to the Ninefold Heaven, surging forth, ascending lightly, gathering and steaming, condensing like mist—its qi as dense as clouds, ascending into the crucible.

(Min’s Exegesis) This continues the previous section, emphasizing that the work of merging body and mind depends entirely on the governance of the body by the mind. It is the power of stillness. Note: True lead is born in Kan, which is fundamentally water, yet this true lead is the true fire within water—hence "its function lies in Li." The true fire of the Gate of Life surges upward into the heart’s palace, where it meets the true water of the Li Palace. Initially, it is black. Black is the color of water, but through its union with fire, black transforms into red, and the crucible generates cloud-like qi. The second chapter of the original text says, "Purple mist flies above the crucible"—this is the same dense cloud-like qi. This chapter clarifies the function of true lead.

True mercury is produced in Li,
Its function, however, lies in Kan.
The maiden passes through the southern garden,
Holding a jade olive in her hand.

(Peng’s Commentary) Though mercury is produced in the Li Palace, without the correct qi of the north to subdue it, it constantly seeks to leave the body and cannot be congealed—hence "its function lies in Kan." The maiden is mercury; the southern garden is Li. When the mercury in Li encounters the lead in Kan, lead and mercury unite, abandoning the Li Palace to descend together into the Kan position—hence "passes through the southern garden." Once it passes through the southern garden, the mercury water becomes white snow. "Jade" symbolizes its white color; "olive" symbolizes its shape, neither square nor round.

(Min’s Exegesis) This again emphasizes that the work of merging body and mind depends entirely on the nurturing of the mind by the body. It is the utmost of reverence.

Now, true mercury is born within Li, and Li is fundamentally fire. Yet this true mercury is in fact the true water within fire—hence "its function lies in Kan." When true yang ascends to the Li Palace, it immediately combines with the true water within Li to form an essence, which then descends into the Kan Palace. There, true water and true fire merge—hence "its function lies in Kan." "The maiden passes through the southern garden" means she comes from Li. It speaks of true mercury traveling from southern Li to the Kan mansion, where it unites with true fire to congeal into the jade liquid returning elixir—hence "hand holding a jade olive." The returning elixir is the fruit attained by immortals; this is the jade liquid returning elixir, thus symbolized by the "jade olive." This passage clarifies the function of true mercury.

Zhen and Dui are not east and west; Kan and Li are not south and north.
The Dipper’s handle revolves through the celestial cycle—one must grasp its gathering and clustering.

(Peng’s Commentary) Zhen is east, Dui is west, Li is south, and Kan is north—these are the directional positions of the eight trigrams. The Dao of Heaven operates without ceasing, and the Dipper’s handle shifts month by month, pointing in turn to each direction. Yin and yang, cold and heat, never miss their appointed time. The human body is a microcosm of heaven and earth: from the Mysterious Valley it ascends to the Mud Pill Palace, then descends past the Double Towers to return to the Northern Sea—this is called the "celestial cycle." The mechanism of its revolution likewise depends on the Dipper’s handle. A year has twelve months, a month thirty days, totaling three hundred and sixty days. A day has one hundred ke, so a year amounts to thirty-six thousand ke. A single day’s work can seize the qi of thirty-six thousand ke. It speaks of the cultivator of the elixir, who circulates the celestial cycle’s fire times: within one day, one day replaces a year, and the twelve hours replace the twelve months. Years are gathered into days, months into hours—only by following the Dipper’s handle in its revolutions, and east, west, south, and north need not be mentioned. If the Dipper’s handle errs even slightly, the seasons will fall into disorder. Be cautious, be cautious indeed.

Now, the ingredients for congealing the elixir, from beginning to end, are two things: true mercury and true lead. True mercury is born from Li, and Li resides in the south; true lead is born from Kan, and Kan resides in the north—hence "southern Li and northern Kan." Yet true mercury is also called the wood essence within Li, and wood resides in the east, belonging to Zhen; true lead is also the metal within water, and metal resides in the west, belonging to Dui—hence "eastern Zhen and western Dui." In truth, the two substances of mercury and lead have no fixed positions in east, west, south, or north. For the cultivator who refines lead and mercury to form the elixir, everything depends on the true intention at the center, harmonizing spirit and qi, firmly grasping the pivotal mechanism of the Dipper’s handle, gathering and clustering the qi of a full year, circulating the celestial cycle, and revolving in accord with the Dipper’s handle—hence "the Dipper’s handle revolves through the celestial cycle—one must grasp its gathering and clustering."

Fire times do not rely on hours; the winter solstice is not confined to *zi*.
When it is time for bathing (or "the method"), *mao* and *you* are also (or "at times") empty comparisons.

(Peng’s Commentary) Fire, as a thing, if too great becomes scorching, if too small becomes cold—its key lies in harmonious moderation, hence fire must be called "times." A day has six times, and these times are the nodal points of our body. What hour is there? When our body reaches the extreme of stillness and begins to move, in the month this is the winter solstice, in the day this is the *zi* hour. Seizing this moment and moving in unison with it, then the fire times are here. Generally, *zi* is when yang ascends, *wu* is when yang descends, *mao* is when yang ascends and yin descends, and *you* is when yin ascends and yang descends. The saying "do not divide *zi* and *wu* by the hour" does not mean one must take the winter solstice as *zi* and the summer solstice as *wu*, or the months of the hare and rooster as *mao* and *you*. For external elixirs, adding fire is *zi*, withdrawing fire is *wu*, neither increasing nor decreasing fire is bathing—it is not bound to *zi* and *wu*, nor to *mao* and *you*.

(Min’s Exegesis) These two passages together speak of the work of merging body and mind into one, relying entirely on the true intention to preside over it—this is the power of stillness, the utmost of reverence. In truth, it is the utmost of sincerity, without ceasing. The Perfected One again reveals these eighty characters not only to impart the handle of practice to the learner but also to sweep away all the eye-obscuring fixations of those who cling to texts and images.

Now, in elixir methods, *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you* are the four cardinal points. Advancing fire, retreating tally, warming and nurturing, and bathing all occur within them. The "winter solstice" refers to the return of the first yang, corresponding to the time for advancing fire. When it reaches *mao* and *you*, this corresponds to the time for bathing. In truth, these are the methods and measures for making the elixir, all spoken of by way of analogy. The learner must not rigidly adhere to images and texts, seeking them in the divisions of the sundial. *The Mirror for Entering the Medicine* says: "Within a single day, the twelve hours—wherever intention reaches, all can serve." Each day’s twelve hours, whenever the yang arises and the tally appears, can be the time to make the elixir—it need not be confined to the winter solstice of the year or the *zi* hour of the day. One can see this from the term "living *zi* hour." If *zi* and *wu* are like this, *mao* and *you* can be understood likewise. When learners circulate the celestial cycle, they must follow the Dipper’s handle to recognize the month, never carving the boat to seek the sword, lest they fail the earnest admonitions of Patriarch Zhang.

Crow’s liver and hare’s marrow,
Seize them and bring them to one place.
Grain upon grain,
From the subtle to the manifest.

(Peng’s Commentary) Humans receive the true primordial qi of heaven and earth, weighing three hundred and eighty-four *zhu*, totaling one *jin*. Eight *liang* are the essence of the sun, hence called "crow’s liver"—this is the wood essence. Eight *liang* are the essence of the moon, hence called "hare’s marrow"—this is the metal essence.

Between metal and wood lies separation; they borrow *wu* and *ji* as their matchmakers. Through the firing of the furnace, one grain is born each day, like a millet seed in size, weighing one *zhu* and eight *li*. From the subtle to the manifest, grains accumulate into *liang*; in thirty days, they weigh thirty-eight *zhu* and four *si*; in three hundred days, three hundred and eighty-four *zhu*, round and one *cun* in diameter, weighing one *jin*.

(Min’s Explanation) This plainly states that one need only unite body and mind—there is no other method of practice. Simply nurture it directly without harm, from the subtle to the manifest. This is to proceed from "vigilance in solitude" to "a heart expansive and a body at ease," until one’s *qi* fills heaven and earth.

Now, crow’s liver and hare’s marrow are the medicinal substances of true mercury and true lead. These medicinal substances are all formed through the accumulation of droplets. True lead is gathered from the void; true mercury is transformed within oneself. Refined with fire to form the elixir, hence it is said, "Grain upon grain, from the subtle to the manifest." The method involves two steps: "seizing" and "uniting." "Seizing" means gathering and sealing; "uniting" means refining and congealing. One employs one’s steadfast, unshaken true intention to accomplish this. This intention is also the natural spiritual awareness. Through prolonged practice, success may be achieved. The *Huangting Neijing Waijing Jing* states: "Immortals and Daoists are not divine; they accumulate essence and amass *qi* to attain the real." This is the explanation of this passage.

Chaos envelops the void; the void encompasses the three realms. Yet when one traces its root, it is but a grain the size of a millet seed.

(Peng’s Commentary) Where there is a world, there are the Three Powers; where there are the Three Powers, there is the void; where there is the void, there is chaos. Before the existence of heaven and earth, chaos was undifferentiated. Chaos envelops the void; the void encompasses the three realms. Yet when one traces the origin of the *qi* received at the beginning of one’s life, it is but a single grain, as large as a millet seed. This is the root of human life, which those who cultivate the elixir must know. Thus it is said: "If one seeks the secret of longevity, one must search for the root of creation."

(Min’s Explanation) Chaos is the body; the void is the mind; the One is sincerity. The body envelops the mind; the mind encompasses the body. The means by which this is achieved is the true intention. True intention in heaven and earth is the pivot of creation; in the human body, it is the master of generation and transformation. Though one may follow it, one need not remain ignorant of it. When one perceives the workings of a true adept, their grandeur is as vast as this.

Now, from the inception of creation, it proceeds from Non-Ultimate to Supreme Ultimate, to the Two Modes and Four Images, to the transformation and generation of the myriad things—this is "chaos envelops the void; the void encompasses the three realms." Yet when one returns to the origin and restores life, the Dao lies in proceeding from the myriad back to the Three, from the Three back to the One, and from the One back to the void. Thus one may say that life arises from chaos and returns to chaos. Those who cultivate the Dao follow this principle: they begin with returning to the void and accomplish it. In the end, they shatter the void, return to the Non-Ultimate, and the great work is complete. Yet from the beginning, it is truly formed through the accumulation of one mercury and one lead, droplet by droplet, no different from the arising of the tangible world from the One. Thus it is said, "Yet when one traces its root, it is but a grain the size of a millet seed."

The *Śūraṅgama Sūtra* states: "The void arises within great awakening, like a bubble in water. When it expands to the Flower Treasury World, a single tip of a hair contains three thousand great thousand worlds; three thousand great thousand worlds return to the tip of a single hair." From the perspective of differentiation, three thousand great thousand worlds are vast, while the tip of a hair is minute. Yet from the perspective of interpenetration, the tip of a hair is no different from three thousand great thousand worlds. The principle is profound; it invites deep and prolonged reflection.

The true fluids of heaven and earth intermingle;
The sun and moon contain true essence.
When one grasps the foundation of *kan* and *li*,
The three realms return to one body.

(Peng’s Commentary) The fluid of the heart descends; the *qi* of the kidneys ascends. Fluid and *qi* together are called "fluid"—this is "the true fluids of heaven and earth intermingle." The *hun* is the essence of the crow; the *po* is the marrow of the hare. Together they are called "essence"—this is "the sun and moon contain true essence." *Kan* pertains to yin; within *kan* is concealed *wu*, which is yang earth—this yang is the foundation of *kan*. *Li* pertains to yang; within *li* is concealed *ji*, which is yin earth—this yin is the foundation of *li*. When one grasps that the foundation of *kan* is *wu* earth, beginning with yang, and the foundation of *li* is *ji* earth, beginning with yin, then the heaven and earth of the body, the sun and moon within the body, are all operated by true earth, and the three realms return to one body.

(Min’s Explanation) *Kan* is the body; *li* is the mind; the foundation of *kan* and *li* is intention. In the prenatal state, *kan* resides in the west as *po*; in the postnatal state, it resides in the north as *po*, having flowed into essence. Now, combining prenatal and postnatal, merging essence and *po* into one, is the body. In the prenatal state, *li* resides in the east as *hun*; in the postnatal state, it resides in the south as *hun*, having manifested as spirit. Now, combining prenatal and postnatal, merging spirit and *hun* into one, is the mind. Only earth resides at the center, uniting the Four Images, and is truly the foundation of prenatal and postnatal *kan* and *li*. That which does not change with the prenatal or postnatal is intention. Only the superior adept employs intention to dwell in the body and gather the mind, causing body, mind, and intention to merge into one, unwavering and unceasing. Nurturing them to perfection, one attains such grandeur as to generate beings—is this not the myriad things being complete within oneself, the three realms returning to one body?

This is the ultimate work of the elixir Way, the consummate achievement of the sage. ○ The following four sections reaffirm the work of congealing essence and coalescing spirit—this is not a discourse on advancing to a further stage. Take care not to suspect the establishment of another crucible and furnace, lest you be led astray by heterodox teachings.

Now, *kan* and *li* are none other than true lead and true mercury. In other words, they are true yang and true yin, yang *qi* and yin essence. The union of heaven and earth, the radiant exchange of sun and moon, are all the workings of these two substances. Thus, they enable heaven and earth to endure eternally, and the sun and moon to preserve their luminosity forever. If one can obtain the essence of *kan* and *li* and ingest it, one may attain longevity and lasting vision, holding the cosmos in one’s hands and generating the myriad transformations within the body. Hence it is said, “the three realms return to one body.”

The three realms are the realm of desire, the realm of form, and the realm of formlessness—also corresponding to heaven, earth, and humanity, as well as essence, *qi*, and spirit.

The dragon comes from the Eastern Sea,
The tiger rises from the Western Mountain.
The two beasts wage a great battle,
Transforming into the marrow of heaven and earth.

*(Peng’s Annotation)* The central yang *qi* within the heart is the dragon. Wood generates fire, and *zhen* (☳) pertains to wood, hence the dragon comes from the east. The true unity of essence within the kidneys is the tiger. Metal generates water, and *dui* (☱) pertains to metal, hence the tiger rises from the western mountain. If the dragon’s roar summons clouds to descend and the tiger’s howl stirs winds to ascend, the two beasts meet and clash before the Yellow Chamber. Then the dragon swallows the tiger’s marrow, the tiger devours the dragon’s essence, wind and clouds converge in celebration, merging into one to become the marrow of heaven and earth.

*(Min’s Explanation)* This retroactively describes the initial moment of practice, when body and mind first achieve coalescence—there is no actual battle. The word “battle” signifies that in returning from the postnatal to the prenatal, one must engage in a struggle between heaven and humanity. If heaven prevails over humanity, the demons of passion are extinguished; if humanity prevails over heaven, the obstructions of principle dissolve. Only then do heaven and humanity merge, and body and mind achieve complete transformation.

○ According to the original *Preface* of Master Ziyang: “The heart’s wildness is like a dragon; the body’s ferocity is like a tiger.” This section speaks of “the dragon coming from the Eastern Sea,” which is the *hun* of the heart; “the tiger rising from the Western Mountain,” which is the *po* of the body. In truth, these are the two substances mentioned in the *Preface*: “a single point of true yang *qi* in the body, a single point of true yin essence in the heart.” These are the two beasts. The *Preface* further states: “The heart pertains to *qian* (☰); the body pertains to *kun* (☷).” The phrase “wage a great battle” here corresponds to Chen Niniwan’s words: “subduing body and mind is like waging a field battle.” The phrase “transforming into the marrow of heaven and earth” corresponds to “heaven and earth, sun and moon, soft as silk.” Master Peng, forgetting the original positions of prenatal *kan* and *li*, hastily sought images in the postnatal realm, thus introducing terms like heart, kidneys, *zhen*, and *dui*, which deviate from the original intent. The annotation should be revised according to the *Preface*.

Now, the dragon is mercury, the heart, and spirit. Because the human heart is easily agitated, soaring and difficult to restrain, it is called a dragon. The tiger is lead, the body, and *qi*. Because the human body is full of desires, wild and often devouring, it is named a tiger. The east is *zhen* and pertains to wood; the west is *dui* and pertains to metal. In the elixir method, one drives the tiger to follow the dragon and rides the dragon to subdue the tiger—this is the union of dragon and tiger. As they are the eastern dragon and western tiger, this also represents the merging of metal and wood. In the central palace of *wu* and *ji*, they fiercely contend. Soon, the eastern dragon and western tiger both die and return to the fertile earth, transforming into the true fluid of heaven and earth, the true essence of the sun and moon, thereby congealing the returning elixir. This is the image of the great medicine returning to the furnace, the refinement of golden fluid and the returning elixir.

*The Wuzhen Pian* says:
“The white tiger of the western mountain rages wildly,
The azure dragon of the eastern sea is uncontrollable.
Seize the two beasts and make them fight to the death,
Transforming into a sheet of purple golden frost.”

The “marrow of heaven and earth” is precisely this “purple golden frost,” the symbolic image of the returning elixir.

Golden blossoms open mercury leaves,
Jade buds grow lead branches.
*Kan* and *li* never rest,
How long have *qian* and *kun* endured?

*(Peng’s Annotation)* The golden blossom is true lead, which borrows mercury to form the embryo; the jade bud is true mercury, which borrows lead to take shape. In the human body, mercury is spirit, and lead is essence. “Golden blossoms open mercury leaves” refers to the indistinct and subtle, where spirit is born within water. “Jade buds grow lead branches” refers to the profound and obscure, where essence is generated within fire. Those who grasp the method sit until golden blossoms appear and jade buds sprout, at which point the branches and leaves of lead and mercury flourish. This is the proper moment to gather the medicine and advance the fire, causing *kan* and *li* to traverse east and west, *qian* and *kun* to revolve above and below, moving back and forth, ascending and descending in an endless cycle without the slightest pause. Before obtaining the medicine, one must recognize the opening of mercury leaves and the growth of lead branches. After obtaining the medicine, one must also understand the subtleties of circulation and the proper timing of ascent and descent to achieve perfection from beginning to end and complete the sacred embryo. If *qian*, *kun*, *kan*, and *li* do not circulate throughout the ten months, the crucible and medicine, once briefly obtained, will soon be lost. How can the golden blossoms avoid withering? How can the jade buds avoid wilting?

*(Min’s Explanation)* This section emphasizes that the key lies in the true intention presiding over the process, ensuring that body and mind merge ceaselessly without the slightest interruption. Only by embodying the great strength of the *qian* Dao with the heart and the great compliance of the *kun* Dao with the body can one naturally circulate without cease, ultimately restoring *kan* and *li* to *qian* and *kun*.

Nor is there need to elaborate on *zhen*, *dui*, *gen*, and *xun*, lest they obstruct the mind’s eye. The following section will expound upon the method of "filling and replenishing."

Consider how flowers, leaves, stems, and buds all arise from a single root. *"Golden blossoms bloom on mercury leaves"*—this is the interplay of one *kan* and one *li*, fire rising from water. *"Jade buds lengthen lead branches"*—this, too, is the interplay of one *kan* and one *li*, lotus planted in fire. Through long and steadfast cultivation, the adept first completes the *Small Celestial Cycle* of *Jade Fluid Returned Elixir*, forming the inner elixir; then proceeds to the *Great Celestial Cycle* of *Golden Fluid Returned Elixir*, forming the great elixir—yet all remains the operation of one *kan* and one *li*, one *qian* and one *kun*. Like flowers, leaves, stems, and buds growing from a single root, there is no second body.

Once the *Great Celestial Cycle* is completed and merit is perfected, one may attain longevity and lasting vision, sharing the lifespan of heaven and earth, the radiance of sun and moon. The fruits of *kan* and *li*’s operation may thus endure forever. Where there is *qian* and *kun*, there is *kan* and *li*. Moreover, within the six directions, from primordial antiquity to the present—whether the vast foundation of the cosmos or the ephemeral morning mushroom and evening earthworm—all are molded by yin and yang, and none transcend the operation of *kan* and *li*. The enlightened golden immortal represents their most wondrous application.

Thus, while *qian* and *kun* may at times dissolve into primordial chaos, the golden immortal transcends chaos and endures eternally. The Most High has already passed through seven epochs of chaos, surviving seven kalpas unscathed. The *qian* and *kun* of today are no longer those of the past. From the perspective of change and constancy, one might say that *qian* and *kun* must undergo kalpas in but a brief span, yet the operation of *kan* and *li* in the great sage’s attainment never ceases for even a moment.

The *Book of Changes* states: *"The Way of heaven moves with vigor; the noble person strengthens the self without ceasing."* (See *Zhouyi*, *Qian* hexagram.) To cease not is to endure.

Bathing guards against peril,
Withdrawal and addition require careful vigilance.
In all thirty thousand moments,
A hair’s deviation may cause loss.

*(Peng’s Annotation)*
A year has twelve months, totaling thirty-six thousand *ke*. These are gathered within a single day and the twelve months. For ten months, the fire timing is practiced: when yang arises, medicine is gathered from the *zi* palace and made to ascend in reverse—this is called "withdrawing lead"; when yin arises, fire is withdrawn at the *wu* position and made to descend in sequence—this is called "adding mercury." In the remaining two months, the third month nourishes water to stabilize metal, and the ninth month applies fire to restrain water. Only the water timing is practiced, not the fire timing—this is called "bathing." In the third month, life arises amid destruction; in the ninth month, destruction arises amid life. This guards against the peril of fire’s excessive dryness. Excluding the two months and six thousand *ke*, only ten months and thirty thousand *ke* remain, hence the saying, "In all thirty thousand moments."

Within these thirty thousand *ke* of effort, even a hair’s deviation may disrupt yin and yang. Only by guarding against peril and exercising careful vigilance, focusing the mind and intent, can one appear with movement and vanish with stillness in every cycle. After a full year, the infant may be nurtured, becoming an immortal of transcendence.

*(Min’s Explanation)*
Only through reverence and sincerity does the thing remain undivided. If one applies this method, a single month suffices—hence the saying, "In all thirty thousand moments." The fear of deviation lies solely in the need for absolute unity without interruption; within thirty thousand *ke*, there must be no pause for even a single breath. There is no other deviation to fear.

The *Book of Changes* states: *"If one does not constantly maintain virtue, disgrace may follow."* (See *Zhouyi*, *Heng* hexagram.) This is what is to be feared.

Here follows the translation:

---

Now, in the Dao of the Elixir, there are the methods of "drawing and adding at midnight and noon" and "bathing at dawn and dusk," whereby one advances the Yang fire to draw forth lead and retreats the Yin tally to add mercury—these must be undertaken with utmost care. When the time arrives at dawn and dusk, with the forces of punishment and virtue at the gate, Yin and Yang each occupying half, one must bathe to guard against danger and avoid error.

In the firing times of compounding the Elixir, there lies a most crucial secret. Whenever human emotions and perceptions take hold, and external visions appear before one, they inevitably stir emotional thoughts—either joy or fear. At the time of bathing, one should still contain one’s light and remain silent, free from emotional dust. Let the true intention at the center serve as one’s master, withdrawing the consciousness of the acquired mind to employ the primordial spirit, allowing it to follow its nature, unaware and unknowing, in accordance with the Emperor’s principle.

*The Great Learning* states: *"Only after attaining tranquility can one deliberate; only after deliberation can one achieve success—how strict this is!"* *The Mirror for Compounding the Medicine* says: *"Water fears drying, fire fears cold. The slightest deviation, and the Elixir will not form."* Throughout the thirty thousand *ke*, one must remain constantly vigilant to prevent any loss.

When husband and wife unite, the bridal chamber brings forth clouds and rain. After a year, a child is born—each one able to ride the crane.

*(Peng’s Annotation)*
Husband and wife refer to the true Yin and true Yang within the body. When true Yin and true Yang, with true Earth as their matchmaker, become husband and wife, they unite in the bridal chamber, clouds rising and rain descending. Within a year—ten months of gestation and two months of bathing—the infant’s qi is full, and the baby is born, riding the crane forth from the Mud-Pill Palace. Nine years produce nine children—hence "each one"—yet these are a thousand, ten thousand, or a hundred million transformations, not truly nine in number. The infant is called a *child* (孩), formed by the union of *hai* (亥) and *zi* (子)—this is the essential point for initiating the embryo’s formation.

*(Min’s Explanation)*
The husband is Qian, the wife is Kun, and the bridal chamber is the "extraordinary aperture." This elucidates the main purport of the seventh section, speaking generally of the union of body and mind through intention, their capture and control, and their blending into oneness to stand alongside Heaven and Earth—all through the single function of utmost sincerity. It may be likened to husband and wife, who, with a matchmaker to arrange their marriage, unite to bear a child. Yet this child is born from the "extraordinary aperture," and thus is no ordinary infant; it is only by reversing the flow that one becomes an immortal. This differs from the way of worldly husband and wife, who follow the natural course to produce a human being.

Let me further illustrate with an analogy: true Earth is intention, likened to the matchmaker; true lead is the body, likened to the husband; true mercury is the heart, likened to the wife. With the matchmaker uniting husband and wife to form a household, it is like employing intention to blend body and mind, thereby congealing the supreme Dao. Is this so? Or is it not?

I venture to submit this for your consideration, Master!

Now, in the worldly union of man and woman, the two and five essences conjoin, following the natural course to give birth to a human being—this is the way of the common. Yet the Dao of immortality bears a resemblance to this. One should take the radiant maiden of the Li Palace and pair her with the golden lord of the Kan Mansion, sending them into the Yellow Chamber of the central cavern, where they may grow intimate and affectionate, becoming clouds and rain. Thereby, the numinous medicine is matched, the sacred embryo congeals, and after ten months of nurturing, qi is perfected within and spirit is complete, giving birth to the sacred child and divine descendants. In days to come, they will ride cranes in carefree flight, entering earth and penetrating heaven—all the work of this sacred child and divine descendants.

The *Wuzhen Pian* states:
*"The golden lord is originally the son of the eastern house,
Raised in the western neighbor’s home, born of a borrowed body.
Recognize and summon him to return and be nurtured,
Pair him with the radiant maiden to forge marital bonds."*

It also says:
*"The radiant maiden wanders in all directions,
Her forward steps must be short, her backward steps long.
Returning, she enters the Yellow Woman’s abode,
Wed to the golden lord as his aged husband."*

This, too, conveys the same meaning. Patriarch Zhang used the natural course to illustrate the reverse cultivation, employed the way of humanity to clarify the Dao of immortality, making it easier for learners to comprehend—truly a case of "a Buddha’s mouth with a grandmother’s heart." Scholars should ponder deeply and reflect upon this repeatedly.

The *Four Hundred Characters* comprises twenty sections, unified throughout by the theme of "the essence of Kan and Li." This essence of Kan and Li arises within the mysterious aperture, which is none other than the medicinal substances of lead and mercury. It is born from emptiness and stillness, and only when body and mind are utterly serene and unmoving does the mysterious aperture first emerge. From beginning to end, all hinges upon these two lines—readers must not overlook them.

**Tenth Lecture: Yang Arises in Emptiness, Stillness Reaches Its Extremity and Reverses**
*Without Gathering, Yet Gathering—Knowing When to Stop Is Enough*

The liver stores the *hun*-soul, and the *hun* is the intermediate state of consciousness; it enters my central palace, the chamber of spirit, to become my master. At night, it is stored in the liver, and though the eyes are closed without leakage, the spirit still stirs—thus, what one ponders by day must manifest in dreams by night. For stillness belongs to the *hun*, while movement belongs to the spirit. The liver pertains to Wood, the generative *qi* of the East; the nature of Wood is movement, and only through movement can transformation arise. In nightly dreams, if one’s gall is timid, one dreams of encounters with peril; if Liver-Wood flames upward, one dreams of vexation and restless sleep—all caused by the disquiet of spirit and *hun*. To pacify them, nothing surpasses storing them not in the liver but in the kidneys. The kidneys pertain to Water and are Kan; within Kan originally resides the primordial spirit, and when the consciousness-spirit merges into it, dwelling in the deepest place, this is called “holding fast to the heart and filling the abyss.” Storing at night in the kidneys is equivalent to storing in the ears. The ears connect with the kidneys: the kidneys are the inner treasury of the ears, and the ears are the outer treasury of the kidneys. When Sun Wukong stores his golden-hooped staff, he transforms it into a needle and hides it in his ear—this is no different from storing it in the kidneys. If one can regulate the breath so that heart and breath interdepend, then storing the spirit in the ears means that inwardly it enters the ear, and outwardly it becomes true Earth. The ancients spoke of “listening to the stringless zither,” or “hearing the grand music of the heavens,” or “the wondrous sound of Avalokiteśvara,” or “the ocean-tide sound of Brahmā’s voice”—all are the wondrous sounds heard within the ear after harmonizing heart and breath. In beginning the work, this is most essential. Sun Wukong is the primordial spirit; the golden-hooped staff is primordial *qi*. When spirit and *qi* unite, they are stored in the ear. For the ears and eyes can be divided by movement and stillness: the eyes are movement, the ears are stillness. Physicians regard the area behind the ears as the “Gate of Destiny,” because the ears connect with the “Gate of Destiny”; the area before the eyes is the “Gate of Wind,” the gate of vast *qi*. When *qi* issues and enters through the ears, this is immortality—physiognomists have long held this view. The ear-gate is the “Gate of Wind,” and the nape of the neck is the “Mansion of Wind.” In Daoist practice, there is the matter of “wind arising behind the brain.” The “Mansion of Wind” is the treasury that assists *qi*; when the breath of exhalation and inhalation reaches this point, it is called the “Mansion of Wind.” In *Journey to the West*, Avalokiteśvara bestows upon Sun Wukong the power of “when he calls to Heaven, Heaven responds; when he calls to Earth, Earth answers,” and with three willow leaves transforms them into three “life-saving hairs,” placing them behind the traveler’s head. This “when he calls to Heaven, Heaven responds; when he calls to Earth, Earth answers” means exhaling to connect with the root of Heaven and inhaling to connect with the root of Earth. Willow is a yielding Wood, which is Xun-Wood; three is the *Qian* hexagram. The three hairs are primordial *qi*; placing them behind the head is taking from Kan to fill Li, transforming the *Li* hexagram into the *Qian* hexagram. This occurs after subduing the dragon-horse, for the dragon-horse is true breath and true intent. Only when the Tang Monk obtains the dragon-horse can he journey to the Western Heaven. “All day long, *Qian* acts with ceaseless vigilance”—this is the opening and closing of true breath. The three life-saving hairs are *Qian*, and also true breath; the dragon-horse is likewise true breath. When first undertaking the work, one should begin by directing the sound of the shout into the ear. Avalokiteśvara attained the Dao not through contemplating the mind but through contemplating sound. Not only Avalokiteśvara, but many buddhas have attained the Dao by this means. Thus Mañjuśrī said: “Were it not for Avalokiteśvara, I too would have realized it thus.” Why is Avalokiteśvara depicted in female form? The *Huangting Jing* says, “The ears are the enchanting maiden,” for the ears govern stillness. Now, *Li* is the middle daughter, and *Kan* is the middle son. The ears also pertain to *Kan*—why then are they female? It is not known that *Kan* pertains to Water and governs stillness, and stillness also has a feminine aspect; thus the ears are the delicate maiden, and Avalokiteśvara appears in female form. The *Qingjing Jing* says: “Heaven moves, Earth is still; the male moves, the female is still.” The male symbolizes Heaven, the female symbolizes Earth—both take stillness as their nature. When spirit and *hun* return to the ears, the heart naturally becomes still. There is another related matter that should be discussed: the *hun* is stored in the liver, and the liver connects with the gallbladder, which stores consciousness. Those with consciousness are bold; those without are timid. Both liver and gallbladder pertain to Wood. The *hun* is stored in the liver and meets the gallbladder’s consciousness, becoming the consciousness-spirit—liver and gallbladder are one, which is why they are called the “five viscera.” The gallbladder is both a *zang*-organ and a *fu*-organ. In the human body, the gallbladder resides in the precarious region of *Xu* and *Wei*. Lü Dongbin called it the “Xu-Wei cavity” and also said, “Advancing the fire in the work of Ox and Dipper.” This cavity is within the body below the gallbladder, and outside the body in the Yin Heel vessel. Both the Yin Heel and the gallbladder are *Xu-Wei*, and liver and gallbladder follow one another. Here, a brief diagram would clarify this. The *Cantong Qi* says: “Turning and bending to see and hear, opening and closing are all in harmony.”

Now, for those who cultivate the Dao, there is the "Union of One Society," which employs the term "uniting as one." The teachings of later heavens also hold this meaning of "uniting as one." The phrase "next, bend with subtlety" (from the *Record of Rites·Doctrine of the Mean*)—the character *qu* ("bend") here is the same *qu* as in "turning and bending to see and hear." When seeing and hearing are returned to unity, opening and closing naturally harmonize. This harmony arises from turning and bending, which is to withdraw vision and return hearing to the Gate of the Mysterious Female. To "turn" and to "bend" is to direct seeing and hearing here, governed by spirit—opening and closing occur precisely in this way.

"Separating qi to receive *ying* and *wei*" is the act of withdrawing vision. Not perceiving external things, one returns to *ying* and *wei*, and thus "the *kan* no longer employs hearing." *Qu* is the unity of the chariot; "turning and bending to see and hear" embodies the meaning of Avalokiteśvara. Only thus can one attain the true "harmony" of "opening and closing."

In the Daoist Canon, there is the *Scripture of Stabilized Contemplation of the Numinous Treasure of the Cavern Mystery*. "Numinous Treasure" is numinous in heaven and precious on earth. In humans, the numinous treasure is called "spirit," which is the numinous of heaven and the treasure of earth. "Stabilized contemplation" means that only through stabilization can one contemplate—this refers to contemplating the wondrous and observing the aperture.

Its opening lines state: "Those who wish to cultivate the Dao must first relinquish affairs." To "relinquish affairs" means "to sever all external matters"—"external matters" being the seven emotions and six desires. As for loyalty, filial piety, integrity, and righteousness, these manifest sincerity outwardly, rooted in heavenly nature, and are not external matters. "Sever all external matters, and nothing will oppose the mind. Then, sit in tranquility and inwardly observe the heart’s qi."

"Sitting in tranquility" does not mean the physical body assuming a seated posture. When the original spirit does not depart from the aperture, this is "sitting." To gather and settle vexations is "sitting in tranquility"—this is when the original heart does not stir. The original heart is the heavenly heart, the Dao-heart. When the original spirit and original heart remain unmoved, this is "sitting in tranquility." The character *zuo* ("to sit") is composed of "person" beside "earth"; in the ancient form, two mouths flank the earth—both signify one yin and one yang. Yin and yang arise within true earth, and harmonizing yin and yang with true earth is "sitting." Only when the original heart does not stir and vexations are dispelled can one engage in tranquil contemplation, striving for stillness.

In contemplation, one extinguishes only the moving mind, not the illuminating mind. The moving mind is the spirit of consciousness; the illuminating mind is the light of wisdom, and the original spirit is true emptiness. When the light of wisdom shines perpetually without arising thoughts, this is wielding the sword of wisdom to slay demons—demons being deviant thoughts and deluded intentions. One should congeal the empty mind, not fixate the mind; fixating the mind is attachment to form.

The *Diamond Sutra* states: "Thus should one subdue the mind." It also says: "One should give rise to the mind without abiding anywhere." When the mind does not attach, it abides nowhere—this is the Dao-heart. The words of the *Diamond Sutra* and the *Scripture of Stabilized Contemplation* share the same meaning, which is to eliminate the "four marks." When the four marks are extinguished, there is "no abiding"; the non-abiding mind is the Dao-heart, the true intention. The subsequent passages of the *Scripture of Stabilized Contemplation* all explicate this meaning, and one may refer to them.

The heart should take non-action as its true dwelling and action as its responsive trace—thus, it is constantly still and constantly responsive. Stillness is "quiescent and unmoving"; responsiveness is "moved and thus penetrating." Only after mastering the work of quiescence can one respond.

A common Buddhist saying goes: "The eye observes the nose; the nose observes the heart. The heart meets the intention; the intention meets the heart." Observing the nose is to halt at the aperture. Furthermore, in the *Śūraṅgama Sūtra*, the Buddha told the Venerable Sundarananda: "At the tip of your nose, there is whiteness—you should observe it." "Whiteness at the tip of the nose" signifies direct acceptance; "whiteness" is the whiteness born of the empty chamber. Only at the extreme of stillness does one see the white qi at the tip of the nose. Observing this whiteness for twenty-one days, one may attain the Dao. If no whiteness appears at the tip of the nose, one should first seek it. "The eye observes the nose" embodies the meaning of "you should observe it." The *guan* in "the nose observes the heart" should be read in the departing tone—*guan* means emptiness. "Nose observes" refers to the emptiness below the nose, which is the heart of the emptiness below the nose.

"Heart meets intention" means the heart meets the true intention—the true intention is the heavenly heart. Returning to emptiness begins with lowering the eyelids, congealing the ears, sealing the tongue, and regulating the breath—this is the meaning of the *Cantong Qi*'s "managing and nourishing the *yin* and *e*". "Managing" is truly congealing spirit into a body, which, from a single point accumulated over time, expands and thus enables the congealing of spirit to form a body.

The heart is the "Spiritual Mountain" and the "Spiritual Terrace." Below the Spiritual Terrace lies the "Spiritual Pond." The Spiritual Terrace is the "Crimson Palace" and the "Responsive Valley"; the Spiritual Pond is the "Gate of Life" between the two kidneys. The "Spiritual Terrace" and "Spiritual Pond" rejoice with the people—"people" here follows the meaning of "cherishing the people" in the *Great Learning*.

This chapter of the *Book of Songs* (from the *Greater Elegantiae·Spiritual Terrace*) contains the meaning of cultivating the true. The lines "When the Dipper is aligned, build the Chu Palace" also refer to guarding the aperture—"Chu Palace" is the south, the *li* direction, which is empty within. "Measuring it by the sun, build the Chu Chamber" (from the *Odes·Yong·When the Dipper is Aligned*)—observing this character *ri* ("sun") further clarifies the meaning. The Five Classics contain many allegories of Dao cultivation, which later generations failed to recognize.

The work of returning to emptiness is the matter of managing the "Spiritual Terrace" and constructing the "Spiritual Pond"—this is drawing from *kan* to fill *li*. The former is the nine returns; the latter is the seven reversions.

Yet the nine returns during the work of returning to emptiness differ slightly from the true Great Return. The Great Return is the intercourse of Qian and Kun, while the nine returns are the union of Metal and Wood. When yang qi fills the body, water and fire naturally merge within—this is the seven reversions. Water, fire, metal, and wood all depend on Earth; thus, one must begin with Earth and first attain true intent. Earth serves as the dike for water, and fire is also restrained by Earth, which likewise acts as its dike. Without Earth, water overflows; without Earth, fire rages—only with Earth can they be harmonized, allowing water and fire to merge. Metal is born of Earth and completed by it, while Wood arises from water and grows through Earth. Metal and Wood do not naturally unite; only through Earth’s mediation can they interact. Therefore, only when the two Earths form a *gui* does water and fire merge within, and Metal and Wood unite without—nine returns within and seven reversions without. Fire is spirit, water is qi; their union is thus the intercourse of spirit and qi. Only with Earth as the dike can the inner spirit and qi be stabilized, for Earth possesses the power to restrain water and fire.

Metal and Wood arise externally: Metal is the yang qi within emptiness, born from Earth’s true emptiness, the primordial qi returned from the postnatal to the prenatal. Wood is the original spirit of one’s nature, still at its utmost and then revived. Metal and Wood are established by external Earth, while water and fire are established by internal Earth. The union of Metal and Wood without and the intercourse of water and fire within are truly the work of internal and external Earths—internal and external emptiness. Externally, one forgets the body as Kun Earth, the outer Mysterious Female; internally, one forgets the heart, allowing not a single thought to arise, as Pure Earth, the inner Mysterious Female. As the ancients said, “Where the thought moves is the Mysterious Female”—this is the inner Mysterious Female, pure and thoughtless. Its image is like emptiness ○, the inner Mysterious Pass. Externally, forgetting the body to become pure Kun, its image is also like emptiness ○, the outer Mysterious Pass. With these two Mysterious Passes established, the work of seven reversions and nine returns may be accomplished.

Scholars who cannot yet attain the state of forgetting both body and heart, of cultivating both internally and externally, may first practice becoming pure Kun externally, then progress to the stage of internal purity. The verification of external pure Kun practice is the return of yang within emptiness: initially, a faint yang arrives in the body, causing the limbs to tingle and numb, gradually spreading until the whole body tingles and yang qi is abundant, and external yang stirs. Attaining internal purity is the return of one’s original spirit—yang stirs, and one can sense, gather, seal, and refine it, following the natural fire phases without artificial effort. Pure Earth is Sha Wujing in *Journey to the West*.

First, externally, the body becomes pure Kun, obtaining one Earth as Wu Earth; later, internally, the heart becomes pure, obtaining one Earth as Ji Earth. The two Earths combine to form a *gui*, like Sha Wujing and his treasure staff. *Journey to the West* first attains Wukong, then Wuneng, and finally Wujing—this is the meaning. In the work of returning to emptiness, within the nine returns, when yang qi arrives in the body and a subtle stirring is felt, this is the mechanism. To achieve the true nine returns, one must await the true yang’s response in the body, when yang qi is abundant.

Lü Zu said: “To refine the seven-reversion elixir, one must first refine the self and await the time.” The “time” refers to the “living zi” and “living wu.” The seven-reversion elixir is the meaning of seven reversions and nine returns; refining the self is refining the self to return to emptiness. Refining the self means refining the self’s Earth to obtain prenatal Wu Earth, drawing true yang from emptiness. Yet yang transforms in an instant, so one must have a method to retain it. Having summoned yang qi through returning to emptiness, one must have a method to gather this yang. This is the primordial Great Dao, which must first enter the state of chaos before it can be attained.

The primordial is the state before heaven and earth gave birth to beings; one must return from chaos to the Supreme Ultimate. At the final stage of pulverizing emptiness to return to emptiness, one goes even beyond chaos, to the state before heaven and earth were opened. The “before the image of the Emperor” (*Laozi*) is the primordial before the primordial. The initial primordial of returning to emptiness is the return from the Two Modes to the Supreme Ultimate; the final return to emptiness, the primordial before the primordial, is the return from the Supreme Ultimate to the Non-Ultimate—this is the “primordial Great Dao.” The primordial is the One. The union of spirit and qi is also the union of yin and yang, in utter chaos. If the One divides into two, one enters the postnatal from the prenatal.

The primordial Dao lies in uniting the three into one. Initially, this is the meeting of the three families: spirit is one, qi is two, and the gate of the Mysterious Female is three. Uniting the spirit in the heart with the qi in the body at the central palace of the Mysterious Female’s gate is the meeting of the three families, merging them into one, and chaos is achieved. The primordial Dao, from beginning to end, lies in uniting the three into one, the union of spirit and qi, the intercourse of Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li. Then transformation arises—this is when true Earth and true intent emerge, enabling the summoning of the primordial qi from without, the image of “a single stroke opening heaven.” Yet how are true intent and true Earth established? The Dao lies in not a single thought arising, not a single intent scattering—only then can true intent and true Earth be attained. This is what the *Diamond Sutra* means by “giving rise to the mind without abiding anywhere.”

Beginners may find this difficult to attain; one must first seek sincerity of intent. Even sincerity is not easily achieved, so there is the work of “proceeding from the indirect.”

To begin, focus lies in singular concentration, which naturally ensures that intent does not scatter. Yet this "intent not scattering" in the external differs from the "single thought not arising" within. Singular concentration means gazing here, listening here, regulating breath here—dwelling wholly upon one without division. Regulating breath is observing breath, embodying the meaning of *zhi* and *guan*. Within this, there is the "water clock" and "martial fire." The "water clock" refers to a count of drops, though not literally numbering the breath. It echoes the idea of "the shout entering the ear"—listening to the breath with the ear becomes the counting of drops, like the measured drip of a water clock, which must be heard with the ear. This is the method of Guanyin. Yet it merely borrows the concept, not the actual counting of breaths.

*The Wuzhen Pian* states: "The eye’s sight falls short of the ear’s; the mouth’s speech cannot match the nose’s!" Eyes, ears, mouth, and nose converge into one intent, then return to unity. "Turning and bending to see and hear"—the beginning and end are martial, the middle is civil. Initially, martial fire is employed, which means listening to the breath with the ear, implying a subtle intent. The so-called "singular concentration" and "proceeding from the indirect" are one and the same. It is not about guarding a single aperture, nor using the mouth, nor relying on the heart-mind. The spirit issues forth, and the ear listens to the breath. Naturally, heart and breath depend on each other, spirit and *qi* unite, and intent does not scatter.

As the saying goes, "Knowing without guarding is the work"—this refers to "embracing the One" rather than guarding it. "Embracing" means the two ears turning inward to hold each other. If intent is used to guard, it falls into visualization; visualization generates thoughts, and emptiness is lost. The idea of "the shout entering the ear" is most subtle—ponder it carefully.

*The Cantong Qi* says: "Turning and bending to see and hear, opening and closing all harmonize." "Harmonizing" means regulating, the meaning of one opening and one closing in even rhythm. "It is the pivot of self-cultivation." The *shu* is the door’s hinge; a cart has its linchpin, within which lies a pin, allowing it to turn without falling from the pivot—this is the meaning of not scattering. Only by listening to the breath with the ear can opening and closing harmonize, rhythm remain even, and scattering be prevented, like a well-oiled pivot. The five senses all enter the *qi*, and the discriminating spirit does not stray outward. In movement and stillness alike, this is so, and thus "exhaustion is avoided." Only then can one "leave *qi* and enter *ying* and *wei*," where *ying* and *wei* are blood and *qi*. When *qi* gathers and flows to the ear, external sounds may no longer be heard. "Closing the mouth without speech, few words follow the primordial chaos," and the spirit naturally does not scatter.

"In sleep, spirit and *qi* embrace"—in stillness, spirit and *qi* unite, with spirit governing *qi* as it moves. In movement and stillness alike, there is emptiness, returning to the primordial chaos. "Awakening to await life and death"—when the time comes, the spirit knows and awakens, recognizing the arrival of the "living midnight" yet refraining from gathering it. The "true person" within Kan "floats and drifts while guarding the center." At times rising, at times descending, at times exiting, at times entering, the original spirit moves up and down through the three valleys, with the Yellow Court’s path all being the mysterious pass. From the Gate of Mystery, to the aperture of Yin Qiao, all lie within the "center." Above and below, there are two "centers." Connecting the two centers into one embodies the meaning of "My Way is threaded by one" (*Analects*, "Li Ren"). Only with this undivided intent can a single thought not arise. If one intentionally guards the Gate of Mystery, thoughts will continue unceasingly. This cannot be achieved without the word "listen"—only by listening can a single thought not arise.

The character for "life" (*ming*) is composed of one, person, mouth, and ear. When mouth and ear unite, life is attained. Mouth and ear side by side form the image of listening to the breath with the ear. The great capital city, *dudu dayi*, is derived from the ear—Guanyin enters the Dao through the ear, which is the correct path. The importance of the ear is thus evident. The ear is Kan, the mouth is Dui. Dui is the youngest daughter, also metal. It is said, "Seek it in Dui," and "Having fallen into Kan, seek it in Kan"—both refer to seeking metal. The two trigrams Kan and Dui, with three yin and three yang lines, must transform into Qian and Kun to form the *Earth-Heaven Peace* hexagram. Taking the upper line of Dui as the middle line of Kan forms *Peace*. Peace means "the universe is settled and stable." Only by returning to emptiness in this way can one gain control.

At first, the arising yang is extremely subtle; whether one can awaken to it upon its arrival is uncertain. If one can perceive it upon arrival, that is awakening. The sixth line of Kun, "Dragons battle in the wild," marks the extreme of stillness and yin, where yin is at its peak and yang is about to arise—its mechanism is exceedingly subtle. When yang arises, it forms the *Return* hexagram, with yang in the earth, yet it cannot be gathered. Thus, the first line of Qian embodies the meaning of *Return*. Here, interpreting from the *Qian* hexagram: the first line, yang hidden below, is the time of "the hidden dragon does not act." Though yang has stirred, its mechanism is subtle, so it remains concealed and unseen—hence "does not act." The medicine is still tender. Yang returns from beneath the earth, thunder moving within the earth, at the time of the winter solstice. Thus, "the hidden dragon does not act," for yang lies below. The second line, "the dragon appears," is the *Earth-Marsh Approach* hexagram. The hidden yang *qi* emerges into the field—the field is the "cinnabar field."

Having arrived at the Gate of the Mysterious Female, one is about to feel the body. The virtue is universally bestowed—this refers to the *qi* of luminous virtue, like clouds moving and rain descending. At this point, one must summon and retain it within the body, employing the work of "fire forcing metal to move," allowing it to reach fullness. This corresponds to the third line of *Qian*. The third line states, "The noble one is ceaselessly active throughout the day"—*qianqian* is yang. "Ceaselessly active throughout the day, vigilant in the evening as if facing peril, without blame." Here, one must apply effort to gather and refine. The gathering is a gathering that does not gather, hence the meaning of "vigilant in the morning and evening." The opposite of *Qian* is *Kun*; one must circulate through the six voids, taking pure *Kun* as the body. The work lies in the two words: *stabilizing* and *forgetting*. *Stabilizing* means stabilizing in the One; *forgetting* means forgetting the self. When a speck of yang *qi* reaches the four limbs, one should stabilize in the One—this is the time of the second line, "in the field." Stabilizing at the Gate of the Mysterious Female requires a slight exertion of effort, yet one must never cling to the body. Only by stabilizing externally can one generate sufficient yang *qi*. The work of stabilizing and stillness is a constant practice for the cultivator. "Ceaselessly active throughout the day" is to magnify and expand—when yang is full, it becomes the fourth line. "Concealed in the abyss" refers to the yang of the Life Gate. The external yang joins with the yang of the Life Gate, uniting like with like to resonate. The use of the word "martial" here signifies that within the Life Gate, there originally exists primordial yang *qi*. When these unite as one, great function emerges—this is the matter of yang *qi* ascending to the cauldron and filling the *Li* palace. To fill *Li* is to obtain the *qi* of luminous virtue and gather true yang. Without stabilizing externally, the third line cannot generate yang; without forgetting the self, the fourth line cannot resonate with the yang of the Life Gate. If, while forgetting the self, even a slight thought arises—whether joy or fear—the yang *qi* generated may still dissipate. Only through complete forgetting can one reach the fifth line. Having reached the fifth line, the external yang *qi* is so abundant that it stimulates the two kidneys at the Life Gate, causing the external yang to rise. It further surges upward to the Heart Palace, bringing awareness. At this time, one gathers the medicine and returns it to the crucible; heaven and human unite in activation, hence the image of "the flying dragon in the sky." Heaven and *Qian* represent the crown—yang *qi* ascends, filling *Li* to complete *Qian*. One should still take *Kun* as the body and *Qian* as the function, gathering with an empty mind. Otherwise, upon reaching the ninth line, "the exalted dragon has regret." The yang of the fifth line is neither too old nor too tender—it should be gathered immediately. The so-called "gathering" is a gathering that does not gather, a taking that does not take. Only with an unmoving mind and the forgetting of both body and self can one achieve "beauty within and harmony throughout the four limbs." If even a single thought or intention arises, the yang *qi* will depart before arriving or transform upon arrival—transformation here means reverting to the postnatal. When yang *qi* reaches the body, it brings a gentle warmth like spring, warm yet not hot. When it transforms, it induces sweating and heat, becoming postnatal. Thus, in gathering, one fears its departure; in taking, one fears its transformation. When yang arrives, first the four limbs feel supple, numb, and soft; then the entire body becomes thoroughly supple. The initial arrival at the body is the *living zi* hour; once it arrives and the heart becomes aware, it is the *living wu* hour. *Living zi* pertains to *qi*; *living wu* pertains to spirit. The saying "when the time comes, the spirit knows" means that when *living zi* arrives, the heart-spirit becomes aware—this is already the *living wu* hour. The cultivator returns to emptiness and chaos, knowing the white yet guarding the black. Awareness of *living zi* and *living wu* is the birth of medicine and the knowing of spirit. Once the time is known, one must stabilize and forget externally, never clinging to body or mind—this is of utmost importance. Though the body feels comfortable when yang arrives, as if soft and supple, the four limbs remain upright like a mountain, steadfast and unmoving. When the body becomes thoroughly supple and numb, one wishes to forget yet cannot—directly generating function, making one unable to forget even if one wishes. At this moment, one must cease the fire. The initial period is the martial fire; upon stabilizing, there remains an intention of martial fire. When forgetting the self, it becomes the civil fire; when stopping the work, it is the cessation of fire. At this point, one round of work is complete—one may say the gathering and taking are done. One should then rise and take a walk, or softly recite poetry, or gently read a book. One must not continue the practice immediately—this is of utmost importance and must not be neglected. Ceasing the fire is sealing and securing; otherwise, the yang *qi* will become hot and dissipate. Heat and sweating indicate the medicine’s departure—this is most forbidden. If yang becomes excessive, it transforms into yin, hence "regret." Here, one may distinguish three kinds of work: the first, martial fire, is "gathering and taking"; the second, civil fire, is "warming and nourishing"; the third, cessation of fire, is "sealing and securing." These three methods have been most secret among the ancient perfected ones, never lightly discussed. I now reveal them—do not treat them lightly. Initially, one practices gathering, taking, and sealing to obtain subtle yang. Through daily accumulation and monthly refinement, the metal *qi* becomes full, true yang flourishes, and the external kidney rises. Then one should gather fire to carry metal, driving the river chariot, passing through the barriers to ascend Kunlun, and "benefiting from meeting the great person." This is the Small Celestial Cycle, differing in function from the Great Celestial Cycle, which shall be detailed later. In practicing the nine-return work, the water source must be utterly pure. Some attempt to practice the nine-return work upon waking from a dream in which yang has risen—this is truly inadvisable.

Why is this so? For between the moment of dreaming and waking, it cannot be known whether stray thoughts arise. Thus, there must be a method to ensure that both body and mind remain utterly still during sleep and dreams before it can be employed. If even a single stray thought intrudes, the water source becomes impure and cannot be used. Discerning the clarity or turbidity of the water source is the most critical matter for those who cultivate the Dao.

If there is any concern that the water may be turbid, it is better to abandon it and not use it at all. The details of this shall be explained later.

The Great Dao of Primordial Heaven ultimately resides in returning to emptiness. With emptiness responding to emptiness, the work is entirely within the realm of emptiness.

The methods of elixir refinement are divided into three grades:

**Upper Grade:**
No trigrams or hexagrams, no weights or measures, no fire phases.
There is gathering, but no fixed method.

**Middle Grade:**
No trigrams or hexagrams, but there are weights, measures, and fire phases.
There is gathering, but no fixed method.

**Lower Grade:**
There are trigrams and hexagrams, weights, measures, and fire phases.
There is gathering, and there are fixed methods.

In the Upper Grade, stability and forgetting constitute the entire beginning and end of the work, with no fire phases. Guarding the center and embracing the One—this is its fire phase. Returning to emptiness within emptiness, the resonance occurs naturally within and beyond the body, and the work does not fall into form or appearance.

The Middle Grade is half in form and half beyond it, already clinging to appearances compared to the Upper Grade. The Lower Grade is entirely bound to form and appearance, requiring adherence to fixed methods.

Thus, the Upper Grade may be practiced by superior cultivators, who can achieve success in a year. The Middle Grade may be practiced by average cultivators, who may succeed in three years. The Lower Grade may be practiced by inferior cultivators, but success is uncertain. Even if fortunate enough to succeed, it consumes vast time, and many spend their entire lives without achievement. This method is an "easeful practice," capable only of dispelling illness but not prolonging life.

The Middle Grade may still lead to the Dao, as its work combines pre- and post-heavenly practices, though results are not easily attained. The Lower Grade operates entirely on the post-heavenly physical body, even more limited than the Middle Grade.

Today, most discussions of elixir methods pertain to the Middle and Lower Grades, which cultivators must also understand. Yet the Great Dao of Primordial Heaven does not reside within these three grades of elixir methods, though it shares much with the Upper Grade.

Its work is exceedingly simple, attainable in a single moment. The subtlety of the fire phase lies in its ability to condense: condensing a year into a month, a month into a day, a day into an hour, so that a Celestial Cycle is completed in an instant. As long as one can maintain stability, forgetting, and cessation without error, success is assured.

The Middle Grade, however, is intermixed with the post-heavenly, employing intention and awareness—already clinging to form and partially bound to the body. Thus, returning to emptiness at the end is difficult, and refining the mind is arduous. Even if the work is performed flawlessly, the utmost achievement is merely an earthly immortal, for its work is largely upon the physical form.

The Lower Grade, at best, may produce a human immortal, mostly serving as a method to dispel illness and preserve the body, far removed from true immortality. Only the Upper Grade yields divine immortals.

What I now expound is the highest vehicle of Daoism, the supreme and most authentic Dao—the Great Dao of Primordial Heaven. It takes the Great Void as the crucible, the Supreme Ultimate as the furnace, purity as the foundation of the elixir, non-action as the mother of the elixir, nature and life as lead and mercury, and stability and wisdom as water and fire.

Stability is water; wisdom arises within stability, and fire emerges within wisdom. Restraining desires and curbing anger is the union of water and fire, for only through stability can wisdom arise. As the *Book of Changes* in the *Gen* hexagram states: *"The noble person curbs anger and restrains desires."*

The union of nature and emotions is the parallel of metal and wood. Washing the mind and cleansing thoughts is bathing—this is the great forgetting. Preserving sincerity and stabilizing intention is the seal. The Three Essentials—precepts, stability, and wisdom—are no different from Buddhism; the cultivator need only refine these three.

The center is the Mysterious Pass; illuminating the mind is the sign; perceiving nature is coagulation. The unity of the Three Origins—Heaven, Earth, and Human—is the sacred embryo. The fusion of nature and life is the completion of the elixir. A body beyond the body is the shedding of the embryo. Shattering the void is attainment.

This is the subtlety of the highest vehicle, which superior cultivators may practice. It is called *"simultaneous cultivation of sudden and gradual,"* *"fulfillment of merit and virtue,"* *"direct transcendence and sudden realization,"* *"perfection of form and spirit,"* and *"union with the true Dao."*

Our practice is gradual, yet what we cultivate is *"simultaneous sudden and gradual refinement,"* ultimately achieving sudden enlightenment and perfect penetration through gradual means.

The aforementioned Upper, Middle, and Lower Grades are all gradual methods—gradual methods are expedient teachings, adapted to individuals with varying transmissions. Age is a significant factor, for when one reaches sixty-four, the hexagram *qi* is exhausted. Once the hexagram *qi* is complete, no yang remains, and the highest Dao cannot be immediately practiced. One must first train in gradual methods to dispel illnesses of the physical body, prolong life, and gradually restore true yang before the highest vehicle of the Dao can be transmitted.

Among the three grades of gradual methods, only the Upper Grade can lead to the Dao. The Middle Grade dispels illness and prolongs life, while the Lower Grade merely removes illness. The Dao commonly taught today belongs to these three grades of gradual methods. Few know of the highest vehicle.

In the Upper Grade of gradual methods, Heaven and Earth serve as the crucible and furnace, yin and yang as the mechanism of transformation, lead, mercury, silver, cinnabar, and earth as the Five Phases, the sun and moon as water and fire, nature and emotions as dragon and tiger, and thought as the true seed. Since thought is taken as the true seed, there is intention—this is not the supreme and wondrous Dao, which is rooted in non-action.

With intention comes the method of visualization. First, one must visualize the "Mountain Root" (according to *Xingming Guizhi*), using the refinement of thought as the firing process, the cessation of thought as the nurturing of fire, the containment of light as the sealing of the crucible, and the subjugation of inner demons as field combat. Body, mind, and intention serve as the Three Essentials—here, one grasps their essence. The Heavenly Heart is the Mysterious Pass—here, one grasps its function. When emotions return to nature, the elixir is formed; when harmonious qi steams and permeates, it is the bath. This is the Upper Grade’s Way of prolonging life. Though similar to the Middle Grade, if a superior practitioner cultivates it with unwavering consistency, they may still attain the Dao—though it is not the Way of the Golden Immortal.

The Middle Grade differs from this, becoming even more attached to form: Heaven and Earth serve as the crucible and vessel, with Qian (Heaven) as the head and Kun (Earth) as the abdomen. Kan (Water) and Li (Fire) are the water and fire—namely, the heart and kidneys. The Black Crow and Jade Hare are the medicinal substances, where the qi of the heart and kidneys interact. Essence, spirit, ethereal soul, and corporeal soul are the Five Phases; body and mind are the dragon and tiger; qi is the true seed. A year’s cycle of cold and heat serves as the firing process, and the irrigation of Dharma-water is the bath. When inner visions do not leak outward and outer phenomena do not intrude, this is the sealing of the crucible. The Great Abyss, Crimson Palace, and Essence Chamber are the Three Essentials. The Mud Pill Palace is the Mysterious Pass (the point where the great and small intersect, like the outer edge of a dot)—this is the inner void within the human body, a tangible location. When spirit and essence merge, the elixir is formed. This is the Middle Grade’s method of nourishing life, differing only slightly from the Lower Grade. When the work is complete, one may become a Human Immortal, achieving longevity and enduring vision. This type of elixir method is the most commonly taught today. Aside from heterodox schools, those who gather to transmit the Dao mostly teach this method. The teachings of Master Sun of Tieling belong to the Upper Grade, as can be seen from his writings—his method of gathering the medicine involves intentional form and appearance.

There is also the Lower Grade, which is even harder to master. Here, one’s own body and mind serve as the crucible and furnace, and essence and qi as the medicinal substances—all entirely postnatal. The heart and kidneys are water and fire; the five viscera are the Five Phases; the liver and lungs are the dragon and tiger; essence is the true seed. The firing process follows the cycles of years, months, days, and hours. Swallowing saliva serves as the bath; the mouth and nose are the Three Essentials; the space between the kidneys and the navel is the Mysterious Pass. When the Five Phases merge, the elixir is formed. This is a method of ease and comfort, somewhat similar to the three higher grades but differing in application. Its flaw lies in taking the phrase "returning essence to replenish the brain" literally and putting it into practice. Some even employ dual cultivation with husband and wife, limiting themselves to their own spouse—though this is not as harmful as those who commit transgressions. In this method, the Mysterious Pass between the kidneys and the navel is where the work begins: one uses intention to draw breath, guiding qi to the space between the navel and the kidneys, causing it to circulate and condense there. This is its principle.

The most crucial aspect of the Lower Grade’s elixir method is forgetting emotion. If one can truly forget emotion, they may still nourish life.
