But if they cannot, not only will they fail to nourish life—they may even hasten its end. The Middle Grade has a method for gathering the medicine, taking qi as the true seed. When qi stirs and yang arises—at the moment of arousal—one gathers it. Since the Black Crow and Jade Hare are the medicinal substances, the left hand grasps the Golden Crow while the right hand seizes the Jade Hare. Alternatively, "the left hand seizes the Green Dragon’s head, the right hand grasps the White Tiger’s tail"—this, too, is a method of nourishing life. If practiced well, one may still achieve longevity and enduring vision.

Only the Upper Grade resembles the Supreme Vehicle, allowing one to become an immortal and merge with the Dao. Using Heaven and Earth as the crucible and furnace, one still abides in emptiness. Thought is the true seed, and refining thought into intention yields the medicine. First, one employs the practice of stopping thought, and when thought is truly gone, intention becomes the true seed. Refining thought is the firing process; ceasing thought is the nurturing. This does not contradict the Supreme Vehicle’s principle of "emptying the mind." However, refining and ceasing thought are methods of visualization—all who teach visualization proceed in this manner. By fixing the mind in one place and cultivating with unwavering focus, one may become an Earth Immortal. Yet if the source of water is not pure, becoming a Golden Immortal is difficult.

Now, let us distinguish the Mysterious Pass in the Upper, Middle, and Lower Grades as follows:

**Upper Grade:** The Mysterious Pass lies above (in the void aperture of the Mud Pill Palace or the Mountain Root) and in the Heavenly Heart—when yang stirs, it is the Heavenly Heart.

**Middle Grade:** The Mysterious Pass lies in the middle, one *cun* and three *fen* below the navel, between the navel and the kidneys.

**Lower Grade:** The Spiritual Pass lies above for cultivating nature, with the Mountain Root as the Spiritual Pass. The Mysterious Pass lies below for cultivating life, with the Gate of Life as the Mysterious Pass.

From the Buddhist perspective, this can lead to the fruition of Arhat, Śrāvaka, or Pratyekabuddha, for it takes thought as the true seed and has not yet extinguished all fixations. Even if one first attains Arhatship, then progresses to Bodhisattvahood, and finally to Buddhahood—or achieves "instant Buddhahood"—this is not the cultivation of the Supreme Vehicle and does not count as merit. Those who ascend through two stages to become Buddhas do so slowly because their original source has not returned to emptiness.

Today, in discussing the firing process, I have briefly explained the gradations of Daoist and Buddhist cultivation. In the future, when you encounter those who teach the Dao, listen to their words and examine their writings—you will then know to which grade and vehicle they belong, avoiding misguided paths. This will benefit those who seek to cultivate the Dao.

The Supreme Vehicle is the primordial Great Dao, the singular method shared by the Three Teachings, as the saying goes: "There is only this one vehicle; the other two are not true." All who have become sages, Buddhas, or Heavenly Immortals throughout history have entered through this path. Heaven has no second Way.

The firing process described in alchemical texts encompasses three grades. Thus, while expounding the primordial, they also cite postcelestial images, further elaborating with "image and word." On one hand, they speak of "gathering in emptiness"; on the other, they speak of "acting with purpose"—the distinction is stark, and it rests with the reader to read and apply wisely. This is why, regardless of grade, all who refine the elixir study the *Triple Concordance* and *Awakening to Reality*.

What I describe as "numbing softness" is termed "silken melting" by Patriarch Qiu. Master Shao said, "The thirty-six palaces are all spring," while Bai Ziqing said, "Bone and flesh harmonize, all unawares"—these, too, are scenes of silken melting. When returning to emptiness, the yang qi born in the void can reach one’s body, for there is a principle of resonance at work. Having attained utmost emptiness and profound stillness, one embodies the pure Kun state, so that inner and outer are both tranquil, boundlessly obscure. Not only is the body emptied, but the heart is emptied as well—not superficially, but to the utmost extreme. Yang qi enters wherever there is no emptiness; in a state of vague indistinctness, inner resonance and outer response bring it to the body—all is the function of arising from the obscure and indistinct.

The *Supreme Jade Emperor’s Heart Seal Wondrous Classic* explains this in great detail, and it shall be expounded fully later.

*Straightforward Explication of the Heavenly Mechanism in One Thread, Scroll Three*

**Lecture Eleven: The *Heart Seal Wondrous Classic* as the Ancestor of Alchemical Scriptures—Unadorned with Imagery, Its Words Reveal Profound Meaning**

Among alchemical texts, the *Yin Talisman*, *Dao and Virtue*, *Supreme Jade Emperor’s Heart Seal*, *Purity*, and *Contemplation* are regarded as the most ancient, distinguished by their brevity and clarity, with scarcely any symbolic language. Later works, however, abound in layered metaphors, giving rise to symbols within symbols and analogies within analogies, leaving scholars dazzled and bewildered, with no foothold for understanding. Moreover, the immortals, sages, and worthies of each era addressed the prevailing ills of their time, and thus their teachings often lean toward partiality. Readers must possess keen discernment to recognize their schools of thought and thereby grasp their true principles. Heterodox teachings and external paths all stem from misinterpretations of alchemical classics—such is the difficulty of reading these texts.

Here, the *Supreme Jade Emperor’s Heart Seal Wondrous Classic* shall be explicated in detail as follows:

**The Three Supreme Medicines: Spirit, Qi, and Essence**

Spirit is the One—one, yet spirit. Qi is the Two—two, yet transformative. Essence is the Three—spirit and qi unite to generate essence, forming the triad. Since these are called the "supreme medicines," they are not postnatal substances but undoubtedly the prenatal *yuan* spirit, *yuan* qi, and *yuan* essence.

Distinguishing them: the postnatal spirit, qi, and essence—namely, the spirit of consciousness, the spirit of thought, the qi of breath, and the essence of intercourse—differ from the prenatal triad. The *yuan* spirit is the primordial nature of Qian, its substance inherently empty. The *yuan* qi is the qi generated at the moment of parental union, when the two essences and two qi merge—this is the prenatal within the postnatal. The *yuan* essence is the primordial, most refined essence. When these three unite as one, Dao may be attained—such are the three supreme medicines.

In the postnatal realm, the *yuan* spirit generates the spirit of thought, which is a part of the spirit of consciousness. The spirit of consciousness arises from the *yuan* spirit and *yuan* essence. During the intermediate state before rebirth, in that state of undifferentiated chaos, though tainted by karmic consciousness, the *yuan* spirit remains, sufficient to restrain desire. Postnatally, it transforms into Kan and Li, where emotion supplants nature, usurping the place of the *yuan* spirit; nature sinks into emotion, merging with the realm of primordial emotion. Consciousness seizes the undifferentiated chaos of the *yuan* spirit, rendering it luminous—thus, the spirit of consciousness is the transformed body of the union of primordial emotion and *yuan* spirit. The spirit of thought is the internal movement of the spirit of consciousness, hence called "one part," yet it truly governs the postnatal human heart. Thus, in postnatal terms, it is referred to as the "spirit of thought."

The postnatal spirit of consciousness arises from the loss of the prenatal *yuan* spirit, so alchemical texts call the *yuan* spirit the mother and the spirit of consciousness (the spirit of thought) the child, establishing a mother-child relationship. Initially, humans possess only the *yuan* spirit; the spirit of consciousness comes later. It arrives bearing the roots of emotion and the stems of intellect, entering the central palace to become the master, coating and contaminating it with habitual defilements. All postnatal base qualities arise from the pull of this intellect. If the intellect remains unstained, the spirit of consciousness remains as empty as the *yuan* spirit. From this, it is evident that the spirit of consciousness is born from emptiness, taking emptiness as its substance and responding to the seven emotions and six desires to become the spirit of consciousness. Emptiness is the *yuan* spirit—hence, the *yuan* spirit is the mother, and the spirit of consciousness the child. Alchemical texts and Buddhist scriptures call it a thief, saying, "Do not mistake the thief for your child." It is named a "thief" due to its base qualities, though in truth, it is the child.

Alchemical texts refer to the *yuan* spirit and the spirit of consciousness as "the child concealed within the mother’s womb." If the spirit of consciousness returns to the *yuan* spirit, it ceases to move.

The qi of breath and the prenatal qi also share a mother-child relationship, though in reverse: the qi of breath is the mother qi, while the prenatal qi is the child qi. The prenatal qi is formless; the qi of breath is formed. Returning from form to formlessness entails extracting the prenatal, formless qi from the postnatal qi of breath. The Dao lies in the union of yin and yang, steaming and permeating the four limbs, thereby drawing forth the prenatal qi. It is like distilled water: subjected to fire and refinement, the purest vapor emerges. Thus, the qi of breath is the mother qi, and the prenatal qi the child qi. Alchemical texts say, "The mother is trapped in the child’s womb," meaning the prenatal qi resides within the womb of postnatal qi.

Only when spirit and qi share this mother-child relationship can the elixir be formed with a foundation. *Journey to the West* speaks of the "Mother-Child River," where Zhu Bajie, after drinking its water, becomes pregnant, only learning the cause from the locals. Once pregnant, no abortion is possible—except by traveling over a thousand *li* south to "Sun-Unbinding Mountain," where the "Miscarriage Spring" flows. Drinking its water cures the condition. This is the prenatal qi: mistaking the formed qi for the prenatal qi in elixir-making traps one in form, producing a phantom elixir—hence the analogy. The Mother-Child River is governed by the Women’s Country, representing the purely yin, formed qi.

While spirit and qi share a mother-child relationship, *yuan* essence and the essence of intercourse differ. Though called "essence," *yuan* essence is in truth qi—more precisely, emotion. The union of *yuan* spirit and *yuan* qi generates *yuan* essence—this is the prenatal. Postnatally, the essence of spirit and the qi of breath, stirred by emotion, transform into formed essence, the essence of intercourse—this is the postnatal.

The *shí* spirit is one, the breath of respiration two, and *yuan* essence three—combining these three to form four yields the essence of intercourse. It cannot generate or transform itself; it must arise from the union of the three. Thus, those who cultivate the Dao must first transmute the essence of intercourse into *yuan* essence—this is what is called "refining essence into qi," the first step in the work. The second step is "refining qi into spirit," and the third is "refining spirit to return to emptiness."

The essence of intercourse arises from movement and possesses a distinct root of lust, serving as the gateway of ingress and egress. Without severing this root, one cannot cut off lust. The cultivator transforms the formed into the formless, the non-empty into emptiness, and emotion into nature. To sever the root of lust, one must first transmute the essence of intercourse into *yuan* essence.

The *Śūraṅgama Sūtra* originates from lust: Ānanda fell into the chamber of demonic women, his root of lust unsevered, and thus failed to attain the "perfection of ending outflows." Unable to escape calamity, what leaked was merely the essence of lust, yet this essence arises from the intercourse of spirit and qi—thus, spirit and qi also leak with it. Only by attaining the "perfection of ending outflows" can one cease leakage and sever the root of lust.

Why did Avalokiteśvara attain the Dao through the ear? Because the ear and kidney both belong to *kǎn*, and when essence leaks from the kidney, it also leaks from the ear. Thus, one must regulate the breath, directing sound into the ear to conceal the spirit within it. To purify the source of water, one must first cleanse the ear, and the kidney’s water will naturally clear. The ear can hear in all eight directions, possessing one thousand two hundred merits; the eye can only see what lies ahead and not behind, possessing but eight hundred merits. The mouth and nose share the same eight hundred merits as the eye, while the mind, capable of knowing both near and far, also possesses one thousand two hundred merits, like the ear.

The Buddhist school first purifies the ear and mind, seeking the true mind to return to the ear—only then can one attain the Dao as Avalokiteśvara did. The one thousand two hundred merits can govern the eight hundred merits, and only after attaining the Dao as Avalokiteśvara can one sever the roots of lustful thoughts and desires.

The scriptures speak of three lusts in the human body: mind, body, and root. Severing only one, whether body or mind, cannot cut off lust or end outflows. Only by severing the root of lust can one achieve the "perfection of ending outflows" and witness the "concealment of the horse’s yin," the "yin retracting like a tortoise." When the minor celestial cycle is complete and the work of seven returns and nine refinements is finished, this sign appears, the "three lusts" are severed, and the body returns to the state of a virgin—only then can one advance to cultivate the "effortless Dao of supreme virtue," no different from a virgin cultivating the truth. This is the highest realm, the great Dao of non-action.

The gradual methods of the upper, middle, and lower grades differ. Even with deep cultivation, the root of lust remains difficult to sever entirely—it may merely retract and cease movement, yet it is not extinguished to restore the virgin body. In the middle and lower grades, the root of lust is even harder to sever. During cultivation, postnatal essence and qi are intermingled, allowing only the alleviation of illness and extension of life—immortality remains distant. To become an immortal, one must first sever the root of lust, as the saying goes: "There are no immortals in heaven who leak essence."

The methods of bliss often involve refining the postnatal, taking the *shí* spirit and breath as the cauldron and furnace of body and mind, and the hare and crow as medicinal substances, harvesting the essence of intercourse. Yet what use is this formed, substantial thing? It merely yields a temporary glow to the body and a flush to the face. All formed, substantial things will eventually decay.

There are those who, in sexual intercourse between men and women, do not emit essence but return it—this is heterodox and heretical. Among them, those who practice dual cultivation with husband and wife do no harm to personal virtue, but to use a female cauldron is to commit sin, not to cultivate the Dao.

The three grades of supreme medicine are inherently formless and prenatal; how can the postnatal be called "supreme"? As the *Book of Changes* states: "That which is above form is called the Dao" (*Xì Cí*, Part I). "Above form" is the prenatal. The prenatal spirit, qi, and essence are the great medicines for elixir-making, and water and fire are also within them.

The *yuan* spirit governs the heart, *yuan* qi governs the body, and *yuan* essence governs the mind. When the spirit moves, qi follows, and essence moves accordingly—thus, spirit is mentioned first, followed by qi and essence.

Bái Yùchán said:
*"This spirit is not the spirit of thought,
But the primordial *yuan* spirit of chaos;
This qi is not the breath of respiration,
But the mist of pristine simplicity;
This essence is not the essence of intercourse,
But the saliva in the Jade Emperor’s mouth."*
(Note: The original *Xīn Yì Rèn Dì Gē* by Bái Yùchán reads:
*"Its essence is not the essence of intercourse,
But the saliva in the Jade Emperor’s mouth;
Its qi is not the breath of respiration,
But the mist of pristine simplicity;
Its spirit is not the spirit of thought,
But one that can stand shoulder to shoulder with the beginningless."*)

The prenatal *yuan* spirit is the dynamic aspect of empty nature, the true emptiness that is wondrous existence. The prenatal *yuan* qi is the principle and qi within the Supreme Ultimate, the wondrous existence that is true emptiness. "These two emerge from the same source but differ in name; together they are called the mysterious, the mystery of mysteries, the gateway to all wonders" (*Laozi*). *Yuan* qi is the "mysterious," and *yuan* spirit is the "mystery of mysteries."

When spirit and qi unite to generate *yuan* essence, it is called "the essence of utmost yang." The Buddha said: "I possess a treasure, hidden within the mountain of form." The Dao says: "The single point of prenatal yang essence resides not in the heart or kidney, but in the single aperture of the *yuan* pass." The *yuan* pass is the mysterious aperture where spirit and qi unite as medicine, and from which medicine is born.

When this medicine is born, the "mountain of form" appears. When the mountain of form inverts, essence is entirely transformed into *qi*, and the passage of leakage is fully opened—this is what is meant by "overturning the flagstaff before the gate." Spirit resides in nature, *qi* resides in emotion; together they form the Five Phases of nature, emotion, spirit, *qi*, and essence. The Five converge into Three, called the "Three Grades," which are the supreme medicines for making the elixir.

The Confucians speak from the perspective of nature and emotion, thus distinguishing the "center" before arousal and "harmony" after arousal. "Attaining the center and harmony" means the Three Grades return to One. The *Book of Changes* says: "Qian and Kun are nature and emotion." It also says: "Refining essence to enter the spirit." And further: "Preserving the Great Harmony, each rectifies its nature and destiny." The principle and *qi* of the Supreme Ultimate reside in emotion, hence they are also called nature and destiny—this principle and *qi* are what Heaven bestows.

The supreme medicines are true lead and true mercury: true lead is the jade hare within the moon, true mercury is the golden crow within the sun. They are also called the White Tiger and the Green Dragon, the *qi* of the two crescents—this is spoken from the perspective of the medicines. The *yuan* spirit is fire, the *yuan* *qi* is medicine; the *yuan* spirit is the wood sap within fire, the *yuan* *qi* is the metal essence within water—this is spoken from the perspective of cultivation. These two are the true sun and moon within the human body. When sun and moon unite, they become "Change," the "elixir," and "illumination"—"the Dao is called the alternation of one yin and one yang."

The prenatal Three Grades are all without birth or extinction; Three is Two, and Two is One. Unlike the postnatal acquired spirit, the *qi* of breathing, and the essence of intercourse—roots of the human cycle of life and death that perish with the person—they are not the supreme medicines but rather harm nature and destiny.

*"Vaguely, indistinctly; dimly, obscurely—"*
These two lines describe the phenomena when the medicine is born and the work of initial practice. *Laozi* says: "Indistinct! Vague! Within it there is an image. Vague! Indistinct! Within it there is a thing. Dim! Obscure! Within it there is an essence. Its essence is utterly real; within it there is sincerity."

The indistinct and vague mark the moment when yang arrives; the dim and obscure mark the extreme of yin—there is a distinction between them. At the outset of practice, one returns to emptiness and guards stillness; when spirit and *qi* unite, one enters the state of primordial chaos. When chaos reaches its extreme, one enters the dim and obscure. The dim and obscure are the inner and outer purity, the four great emptinesses. At this time, knowledge is entirely absent; body and mind are both forgotten. The self no longer knows itself, and form has no form—like the profound and unfathomable, the distant and unattainable. Thus it is symbolized by the words "dim and obscure." When intensified, it becomes even more so—this is stillness taken to its utmost, what the elixir texts call "the meeting of *hai* and *zi*."

At this moment, Heaven and Earth unite, sun and moon converge; all phenomena are empty, yet wondrous existence is nurtured within. The prenatal Wuji is the foundation of creation. Thus it is said, "within it there is an essence." This essence is the primordial, undifferentiated supreme essence—the true essence that generates Heaven, Earth, humans, and all things, the *yuan* essence born from the union of *yuan* spirit and *yuan* *qi*.

At the juncture of chaos, yang emerges from the center, latent within this dim and obscure state. Though formless and imageless, Heaven and humanity have already begun to stir together. When *yuan* essence is born, "within it there is sincerity"—sincerity means the movement of ebb and flow, an unchanging truth. True essence arises from the utmost emptiness, and true sincerity appears at the extreme of stillness. When sincerity arrives, true essence reaches the palace. This "sincerity" is the movement of the living *zi* hour; when sincerity arrives, one moves from the dim and obscure to the vague and indistinct.

The vague and indistinct are the sudden awakening within chaos—yet also as if unawakened—body and mind empty and settled, as if ascending clouds and mist. At this time, the three palaces are full of *qi*, the mechanism stirs, and the flute sounds; one feels only the movement, unaware of anything else. It is clear yet not clear, like deep sleep yet not sleep—thus symbolized by the words "vague and indistinct." When intensified, it becomes even more so—this is the extreme of emptiness and stillness, what the elixir texts call the living *zi* and living *wu* hours.

At this juncture, true yang has been born; wondrous existence does not obstruct true emptiness, nor does true emptiness obstruct wondrous existence. Thus it is said, "within it there is an image"—the image is the arising of yang. In the dim and obscure, there is utter stillness; when being arises from non-being, one feels and thus connects—this is the "vague and indistinct." Yang has returned, and the medicine has been produced; emptiness has reached its extreme, and things are about to be fulfilled. The Three Grades, formless and blended, are concealed within this vague and indistinct state—seemingly with form and image, yet body and mind remain empty and still.

True sincerity has appeared; "within it there is a thing"—the thing is the medicine, the arrival of prenatal true yang in the body. True yang is stirred and born within the dim and obscure, while true yin responds internally at the vague and indistinct moment. With the return of one yang, the Heavenly Heart is immediately revealed—this is what is meant by "when the medicine is produced, the spirit knows." When the spirit knows, it is the vague and indistinct; within the vague and indistinct, one gathers it with the gathering that is no-gathering.

When the medicine is produced, it has an image; when gathered, it becomes a thing. Thus sincerity is the living *zi* hour; the image is the living *wu* hour; the thing is when the medicine is sufficient and ready to be gathered. The Daoists compare the elixir method to the moon, calling the initial stirring of one yang the "geng direction of the moon"—like the first crescent of the new moon on the third day, the moment when the Dao Heart begins to arise, which is also the time of the vague and indistinct.

These eight characters all carry profound meaning and must not be overlooked.

**"Preserve the nonexistent, guard the existent"**—in an instant, this is accomplished.
This phrase, continuing from the preceding text, refers to the work of gathering the medicine. From the perspective of cultivation, however, its scope is vast, encompassing the entire process from beginning to end within these four characters, "preserve the nonexistent, guard the existent." Here, I explain them in detail.

*Laozi* says: *"Attain utmost emptiness, guard deep stillness; the myriad things arise together, and I thereby observe their return."* "Attain" means knowing without clinging; "guard" means singular and unceasing. Emptiness cannot be guarded, but stillness can. Emptiness is nonexistence; when pushed to its extreme, existence arises. Stillness is existence; when deepened, it returns to nonexistence. Only when emptiness is utmost does the image of indistinctness appear; only when stillness is deep does one enter the time of profound obscurity.

The alchemical texts say: *"The boundless void is the Mysterious Aperture—knowing without clinging is the work."* This is preserving the nonexistent. *"Close the senses, lower the lids, and silently observe"*—this is guarding the existent. *"Rest the mind in the realm of utmost goodness"*—this is preserving the nonexistent. *"Know to rest at the gate of utmost goodness"*—this is guarding the existent.

"Preserve" is "attain"; "preserve the nonexistent" is thus profound obscurity, while "guard the existent" is indistinctness. Preserving the nonexistent is observing the wondrous with desirelessness; guarding the existent is observing the aperture with desire. At the outset of practice, empty the mind, forget the form, know without clinging, calm the spirit, and regulate the breath to return to primordial chaos—this is the work of preserving the nonexistent. When body and mind are both still, entering profound obscurity, one yang returns, the earthly thunder stirs, and one shifts into the realm of indistinctness. Then, concentrating the spirit into the qi cavity, fire urges metal to move, and when metal fills the moon to its fullness, the medicine becomes firm and substantial—this is the work of guarding the existent.

Gathering with the gathering that is no-gathering, taking with the taking that is no-taking; externalizing the body to refine it, forgetting the form to preserve it—this is again the work of preserving the nonexistent. Refining and tempering, gathering the medicine into the crucible; sealing and securing it, filling the abdomen with true gold to accumulate and form the elixir—this is again the work of guarding the existent. Once the returned elixir is obtained, acting without acting yet leaving nothing undone, one reaches the point where the primordial spirit transcends, and the void is pulverized—this is again the work of preserving the nonexistent.

Thus, these four characters encompass the entire process from beginning to end, and in a single moment, the elixir may be congealed. The Great Dao is simple and easy—there is no other method. *"Preserve the nonexistent, guard the existent"* is the work of the three grades of resonance; *"indistinctness and profound obscurity"* are the phenomena of the three grades merging.

Scholars should reflect on this carefully, and they will naturally grasp its subtlety.

Preserving the nonexistent is returning to emptiness; only by preserving the nonexistent can one reach the realm of primordial chaos. Hollow and vast, not a single thought arises, the discriminating spirit dissolved, inwardly forgetting the mind, outwardly forgetting the body—this is called the stillness of both body and mind, which is preserving the nonexistent. When spirit and qi unite, the Mysterious Aperture appears, and a wisp of true intention emerges, as if present, as if not. Once the Central Earth is established, guarding this true intention as the Yellow Dame, water and fire naturally merge, metal and wood naturally unite. Safeguarding this single point of true yang, one completes the returned elixir. In a fleeting moment, the qi of a full celestial cycle gathers—this is what is meant by *"the three families meet"* and *"the five phases harmonize,"* which is guarding the existent. This is spoken from the perspective of firing times.

Spirit is formless—yang; qi has form—yin. Yin and yang generate each other, just as existence and nonexistence complete each other. Yang in heaven forms images; yin on earth forms shapes. Thus, *"preserve the nonexistent, guard the existent"* is the harmonization of yin and yang. In postnatal humans, the primordial spirit in the heart has been eroded by the discriminating spirit, so one must first preserve the nonexistent. Preserving the nonexistent means dissolving the discriminating spirit, eliminating the four appearances, and returning to emptiness. The primordial qi in the body has sunk into the Kan trigram, so one must next guard the existent. Guarding the existent means that when one yang returns, one safeguards the Heavenly Heart to generate the medicine. This is spoken from the perspective of principle.

In all Daoist practice, at the outset, spirit and qi must unite, which is the interpenetration of existence and nonexistence. Then, spirit and qi resonate, and existence and nonexistence are no longer established. The numinous platform is pure and still, without existence or nonexistence. As the *Wuzhen Pian* says: *"Existence and nonexistence naturally interpenetrate from this—how could mere thinking bring it about?"* This refers to the state where neither existence nor nonexistence is established. Therefore, one must first preserve nonexistence to establish nonexistence before existence can be guarded.

Observing the wondrous with desirelessness, returning to my true emptiness—this is preserving the nonexistent. When existence arises from nonexistence, true essence emerges and the Mysterious Female is established—only then does one respond by guarding the existent. When there is desire, there is an aperture to observe. Thus, preserving the nonexistent is non-action; guarding the existent is action. Preserving the nonexistent is leaving nothing undone; guarding the existent is acting without acting. This is spoken from the perspective of practice.

The preface to the *Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* says: *"It cannot be guarded with intention, nor sought with non-intention."* The key is that intention must not scatter—this is guarding the existent. Guarding requires that not a single thought arises—this is preserving the nonexistent. Rely on the true intention that is neither intentional nor unintentional to embody existence and nonexistence. Guarding the existent does not mean guarding with intention; preserving the nonexistent does not mean seeking with non-intention.

In the initial application of effort, the practice lies in not allowing a single thought to arise while maintaining an unscattered intention. Yet to maintain an unscattered intention, one must first not allow a single thought to arise. Only by not allowing a single thought to arise can one maintain an unscattered intention. By sustaining an unscattered intention, one can naturally not allow a single thought to arise. The two mutually complete and mutually overcome each other, neither completing nor overcoming.

Scholars should first cultivate sincerity, obtaining true intention that constantly and silently illuminates. When there is existence to guard, thoughts naturally do not arise, and one thereby preserves the nonexistent.

First purify the six consciousnesses, and when the mind merges with emptiness and nothingness, intention naturally becomes sincere and returns to the real. This is the ability to preserve the nonexistent, and thus one can guard the non-existent. This is again spoken from the perspective of initial application of effort.

The four phrases "preserve the nonexistent, guard the existent" encompass such breadth that the entire process of learning the Dao never departs from them. From the perspective of refining essence to transform into qi, this is guarding the existent; refining qi to transform into spirit is preserving the nonexistent. In terms of refining the self, it is preserving the nonexistent; in foundation-building, it is guarding the existent. The seven returns and nine reversions, jade liquid and golden liquid, are all conceived within these four characters.

"Revolving wind blends and unites"—a hundred days’ work proves efficacious. These two lines continue the preceding, referring to the work of the microcosmic orbit, which is the task of foundation-building in a hundred days. Within the fire phases of the microcosmic orbit, "preserving the nonexistent and guarding the existent" constitute the work of producing and obtaining the medicine. Thus, when "revolving wind blends and unites," after a hundred days essence is transformed into qi.

The "wind" is the xun wind; in alchemical practice, the xun wind is true breath. The opening and closing of true breath is called "revolving wind." Revolving means one ascent and one descent, the circulation of the Conception and Governor Vessels. The Dharma Wheel turns along the central Yellow Path, while yang fire advances along the left Black Path, and yin tally retreats along the right Red Path, returning once more to kun—this constitutes one day, cycling without end. As the inner sun and moon move back and forth, the outer sun and moon invert and reverse; yang qi ascends counterflow, which is called "revolving wind." Entering the qian cauldron, yin and yang blend, and true liquid begins to descend to the Yellow Court, congealing into cinnabar.

"Blend" means yin and yang intermingle in chaos; "unite" means lead and mercury intermingle and congeal. The *Four Hundred Characters* states: "Heaven and earth exchange true liquid; sun and moon meet true essence." As such, Bai Yuchan said: "The river chariot ascends straight to Kunlun’s peak." Xiao Zixu said: "The self-so river chariot has turned several hundred times." Revolving wind is the river chariot. One yin and one yang circulate through the six voids, revolving like a wheel, akin to a windmill. The celestial orbit comprises three hundred and sixty degrees; three hundred celestial orbits complete the foundation. In a hundred days of foundation-building, an average of three revolutions occurs each day—this is an approximate figure. True breath is likened to the xun wind, taking its soft subtlety.

Opening and closing in unison, the nine reversions and seven returns restore the primordial, which is the effect of the revolving wind. The nine reversions of qian, kun, zi, and wu involve external blending and union. The seven returns of kan, li, south, and north involve internal blending and union as well. Flowing from wu to ji, the two earths form a jade tablet—this is blending and union. Extracting kan to fill li, returning li to kan—this too is blending and union. Internal and external unite, metal and wood, dragon and tiger intermingle—this too is blending and union. After blending comes union; after union comes blending. Spirit and qi unite in chaos; heart and breath interdepend in profound obscurity. Sun and moon conjoin to form the elixir; heaven and earth conjoin to form the great elixir—all are effects of blending and union.

Yet at the hundred-day threshold of refining the self and building the foundation, the revolving wind blends and unites true yin and yang, hence it is called "a hundred days’ work proves efficacious." "Efficacious" means mysterious and unfathomable, empty yet spiritually luminous. Through a hundred days of work, refining the self becomes consummate, the foundation solid, and one’s original spirit of empty luminosity is restored, while the errant consciousness of deluded thoughts dissipates. The primordial ancestral nature will thereby attain perfect clarity—this is called "foundation-building." "Building" means constructing by eliminating杂念 and nurturing sagely attainment. Through preserving the nonexistent and guarding the existent, the three families of body, heart, and intention meet, blend into one, and generate the primordial true yang qi, circulating ceaselessly to establish the foundation of the elixir.

Its efficacy is like an echo responding to a call or a shadow appearing when a pole is raised. Within a hundred days, achievements become manifest, and the spirit within one’s heart returns to its originally luminous essence—how can this not be called efficacious? However, "a hundred days" is symbolic; in practice, this varies according to the strength or weakness of one’s constitution, age, diligence, or laziness in practice. It may take as little as a month or extend indefinitely. Alchemical classics also state: foundation-building has limits because the microcosmic orbit comprises three hundred revolutions—even performing one per day, it can be completed in a year. Yet refining the self has no fixed term, for it must reach the point where the three grades blend and unite, and the medicine is obtained in an instant before the revolving wind’s celestial orbit can begin. Only with this ebb and flow of ascent and descent does the fire phase of foundation-building arise. After three hundred celestial orbits, the inner elixir naturally forms—this is limited when refining the self is consummate, but unlimited when it is not.

"Silently audience with the Supreme Emperor—a twelve-year ascension." These two lines continue the preceding, referring to the work of refining qi to transform into spirit, which is the year-long nurturing of the macrocosmic orbit. The "Supreme Emperor" is the Emperor of Supreme Purity, the primordial spirit of the Celestial Valley. "Silently audience" means non-action. Body and heart in non-action, spirit and qi unite and naturally return to the upper field. For the hundred-day foundation-building focuses on the lower field; after the seven-day celestial orbit, it shifts to the middle field; when the embryo forms in a year, it moves again from the middle field to reside in the upper field, whence the spirit emerges—hence it is called "a twelve-year ascension."

"A twelve-year cycle" refers to the stellar cycle of a full revolution of the Dipper’s handle, that is, one year. It does not mean one hundred and twelve years as a cycle, but rather the monthly cycle following the Dipper’s handle from zi to hai. This line is most crucial, for the two characters "silently audience" encompass the work of the fire phases.

The *Laozi* states: "Supreme virtue is non-action, not seeking through discernment." Supreme virtue is the body of pure yang and youthful perfection, the primordial ancestral qi that originally resides within. Only by dwelling in reverence and preserving sincerity can one illuminate the heart and see the nature—this is what is meant by "proceeding in tranquility" and "knowing from birth." With body and heart in non-action, spirit and qi achieve all without striving, which is what is meant by "naturally possessing the true fire phases, requiring neither kindling nor fanning."

"Silence" means "the three treasures of ear, eye, and mouth are closed and sealed, not allowing passage." "Audience" means "with my great emptiness, I connect to heaven and earth’s great emptiness; with my heart’s true void, I merge with heaven and earth’s true void." As it is said, "Heaven follows the Dao, the Dao follows spontaneity." In this state, there is nothing to be done—reducing and further reducing—yet the radiance of nature shines forth, extending to the six directions and penetrating heaven and earth. After foundation-building, the physical form has become a youthful body; the departed original spirit has returned, and the leaked original qi has been restored. This is already attaining the primordial within the postnatal; advancing further, one returns to the primordial within the primordial. It is the principle that when gathered, it forms shape, and when dispersed, it becomes qi—yin spirit transforms into yang spirit, filling the cosmos and uniting its virtue with the great void, its brightness with the sun and moon.

This stage is the work of a single word: "stillness." One need only empty the heart and forget the form, transcending self, others, sentient beings, and longevity. All appearances are illusory. Guarding one’s true self naturally brings about the great union of dragon and tiger; preserving one’s nothingness naturally leads to the liberation of the original spirit. Within a year, in profound obscurity, form and spirit transform themselves, and longevity aligns with heaven. This "year" consists of only ten months, excluding the two months of Mao and You for bathing, amounting to three hundred days. Each day completes one celestial orbit, totaling three hundred orbits. During these three hundred days, one need not employ intention or effort—all returns to emptiness, and naturally, spontaneously, one sees the nature, emerges the spirit, and ascends.

"Audience" means unceasing sincerity, storing the spirit in the celestial valley, ascending to the Upper Clarity Palace, where heart and nature commune with the divine. The emperor emerges from *Zhen*, embodying the image of "Qian’s nine-five, the flying dragon in heaven." When the golden light grows as large as a chariot wheel, the yang spirit departs the body and ascends. This is called "form and spirit both wondrous, uniting with the true Dao." The final step of returning to emptiness pertains solely to the Dharma-body’s path, unrelated to the cultivation of the ordinary body. Thus, the scripture says, "Herein it ceases," for once attained, it is eternally possessed, and the work of immortality is complete.

The wise grasp it easily; the deluded find it hard to practice. The above outlines the methods of Daoist cultivation, concluding with these two lines, which serve as the guiding principles of the entire scripture.

The three grades of superior medicine harmonize to form the elixir. With a hundred days of foundation-building and a year’s work, one may ascend to the Jade Palace—is this not the simplest and easiest of matters? Yet cultivating the Dao is not difficult; hearing the Dao is difficult. Hearing the Dao is not difficult; believing in the Dao is difficult. The sages of old transmitted it orally, heart to heart, without committing it to writing. Only the superior person, upon receiving the oral instructions, can believe and practice it; the inferior person will merely laugh. Thus, Confucius said, "If one hears the Dao in the morning, one may die in the evening."

"Knowing" is innate knowledge. When knowledge is realized, intention becomes sincere; when intention is sincere, the heart is rectified; when the heart is rectified, the body is cultivated. The cultivation, rectification, and sincerity of body, heart, and intention all rely on the functioning of innate knowledge—exercising innate capacity. Innate knowledge manifests as innate capacity, which is Qian’s "easy to know" and Kun’s "simple in capacity." This is the origin of the saying that "the great Dao is simple and easy." When innate knowledge is not obscured, the great Dao is utterly simple and easy—non-action yet all-accomplishing. With a single moment of awakening, heaven and earth are penetrated.

Innate knowledge is the center; innate capacity is harmony. Perfecting innate knowledge is achieving the mean and harmony. Positioning heaven and earth and nurturing the myriad things—this is the Dao. If innate knowledge is obscured and the acquired spirit dominates, essence, qi, and spirit leak away through the sense roots, becoming seeds of hell. How then can the Dao be practiced?

Innate knowledge is the original spirit’s true awareness. When still, it is profoundly quiescent, undifferentiated and unaware. When stimulated, it responds and communicates—a spark of true sincerity flows forth spontaneously, purely as the music of heaven, following the emperor’s principle. It responds to things without confusion, embodies things without omission, acts without duality, and engenders things beyond measure—this is innate capacity. Innate knowledge and innate capacity are fundamentally one. Innate knowledge is the heart resting in utmost goodness; innate capacity is the place resting in utmost goodness. When knowledge and capacity unite, it is the heart resting in utmost goodness while also resting in the place of utmost goodness.

The heart of utmost goodness is the ultimate in emptiness; the place of utmost goodness is the ultimate in voidness. Precisely because it is empty, the true numen remains unobscured, returning wholly to sincerity, free from the lure of external desires. The primordial true awareness manifests as innate knowledge. Precisely because it is void, it is unstained by a single speck of dust, radiant and reverent, free from the flaw of partiality. The natural spiritual awareness moves as innate capacity, and the Dao resides within.

When knowledge and capacity unite, clarity and brightness abide in the person. Will and qi become spirit-like, "solemn and unceasing, diligent day and night" (see *Shijing*, "Zhou Song," "Wei Tian zhi Ming"). This is why the great person values "knowing to stop."

"Obscuration" arises when the seven emotions and six desires obstruct the center, causing the body and mind to act solely under the sway of acquired consciousness. Even to seek great rest and cessation proves unattainable. This is the human heart in operation, while the Dao-heart is extinguished. Even if one hears the Dao, one may not necessarily believe and practice it—hence it is called "difficult."

From the beginning to this point forms one chapter, elucidating the stages of the great Dao and the medicinal substances and firing times. Below, the initial efforts are discussed in greater detail.

**Treading the celestial radiance, breathing to nurture purity.** This chapter directly describes the initial efforts. These two lines speak of the beginning of refining the self, which lies wholly in true treading and real practice—namely, the Dao of returning from the acquired to the primordial. The meaning is profoundly subtle; here is a detailed explication.

**Celestial radiance** refers to the yang *qi* within the Great Ultimate, what the alchemical texts call "primordial true yang." *Qian* is Heaven, corresponding to Metal in the Five Phases, firm and strong, upright and unceasing. The body of Heaven is utterly void yet utterly numinous, utterly bright, covering all without exception. Thus, yang *qi* is formless yet has image, accumulating to become the sun, illuminating all without exception. The *Yijing* states: "In Heaven, it forms images." These images are the images of light.

In refining the self, the Dao lies in "emptying the heart and filling the abdomen." *Wuzhen pian* says: "Emptying the heart and filling the abdomen both hold profound meaning; it is only to empty the heart that one must recognize the heart. Without knowing to first fill the abdomen with lead, one is taught to guard the hall full of gold." The heart resembles the middle line of *Li*, which is empty—originally the primordial *Qian*. Yet through its union with *Kun*, the middle line sinks into *Kun*, forming *Kan*, while *Qian* becomes empty and transforms into *Li*. Thus, *Li* is the mutation of *Qian*. This shows that the heart originally images Heaven. Since the body of Heaven is void yet spiritually luminous, the heart is no different. Only when the heart is empty can the abdomen be filled; only when the abdomen is filled can the heart be empty. Why? The Dao lies in obtaining the primordial *Qian*-Metal.

Primordial *Qian*-Metal is the correct *qi* that generates Heaven, also called the "original ancestral *qi*" or the "principle-*qi* within the Great Ultimate." This speck of ancestral *qi* originally transcends the Three Realms and Five Phases. Because it accumulates to generate Heaven, it is imaged as true gold and named "firm and strong," taking the meaning of indestructible metal. In those of acquired inferior virtue, the image has shifted from *Qian*-*Kun* to *Kan*-*Li*, with yin and yang mixed, nature and life separated. The nature of temperament dominates, while the heavenly Mandate’s nature lies hidden; the emotions of delusion vanish, while the emotions of desire rage unchecked. To learn to be a great person, one must first undertake the work of "restraining the self," investigating things to extend knowledge, rectifying the heart, and making the intentions sincere. Achieving "sincerity" is the work of emptying the heart. Before the heart can be emptied, one must naturally seek the Dao of restraining the heart—this is the filling of the abdomen with accumulated gold. Gold corresponds to righteousness among the Five Constants, which is "accumulating righteousness to generate the flood-like *qi*." This flood-like correct *qi* is called "true gold" by Daoists. This *qi* arises from the void, entirely dependent on resonance and attraction. With this filling of the abdomen, the guest evils in the heart naturally depart, and desires are spontaneously rectified.

Alchemical texts often employ symbolic language, giving this *qi* myriad names. This scripture, being most ancient, uses few symbolic terms, hence it directly calls it "celestial radiance."

**Treading** refers to *Tianze Lü* (*Yijing*: "Above *Qian*, below *Dui* is *Lü*"). Confucius said: "*Lü* is the foundation of virtue." In practicing the Dao, one begins with *Lü*. The *Lü* hexagram’s *Nine in the First Place* states: "Treading on a tiger’s tail, it does not bite the person." The tiger is the white tiger of *Dui*-Metal. *Wuzhen pian* says: "The white tiger’s first channel is the supreme treasure, the divine water of the Flowery Pond, true gold." Primordial true yang is precisely this "first channel of the white tiger." Obtaining it leads to accomplishment; failing to obtain it leads to ruin.

True gold is originally the primordial *Qian*-origin, formless and without substance, born from the midst of emptiness and nothingness, concealed in the profound and unfathomable depths. Thus, alchemical texts symbolically say it is born within *Kun*. *Kun* is utter emptiness; it is stored within *Dui*, and *Dui* is utter depth. Guangchengzi said: "Brilliant, it emerges from Heaven yet reaches that most yin of origins." This true yang emerges from above Heaven yet is actually born below Earth. *Kun* is Earth, the old yin; old yin does not nurture, so young yin takes *Kun*’s place in service. *Dui* is marsh, young yin. *Qian* pertains to Metal, and *Dui* also pertains to Metal; *Qian* is yang, *Dui* is yin; *Qian* moves, *Dui* is still. When stillness reaches its extreme, movement arises, and yang is born within yin; when movement reaches its extreme, stillness arises, and yin is born within yang. Hard and soft push and pull, cycling endlessly like a ring. Thus, primordial yang *qi* is born within *Kun* and *Dui*.

The first yang’s arrival is *Dilei Fu*, the initial stirring of the celestial heart. The second yang’s birth is *Dize Lin*, the celestial heart now revealed. This is precisely the time to apply fire, when one should tread and practice in reality. Both treading and practicing are spoken of in terms of Earth, while *Tianze* (*Lü*) is the image of inner yin summoning outer yang. Daoists say: "Enter the tiger’s den to seize the tiger’s cub." The tiger’s cub is water. In the primordial, water is the *qi* of Heaven’s One, the initial stirring of principle-*qi* within the Great Ultimate. This *qi* accumulates to generate Heaven, hence it is called "primordial." With the Five Phases inverted, water returns to generate Metal, becoming "Metal within water."

The "tiger’s tail" is the first line of *Dui*; the "white tiger’s true gold" belongs to the yang within *Qian*. Thus, when it appears in *Dui*, it is like a tiger’s tail.

Grasping this mechanism, the cycles of generation and destruction reverse. Treading upon the tiger’s tail incurs no misfortune, and it naturally ceases to bite. Moreover, *Qian* corresponds to the human head, while *Dui* corresponds to the mouth and tongue. The mouth is the gate of ingress and egress, containing five yang lines with one yin at its center—thus bearing the image of a gate. When scholars begin with the *Lü* hexagram and truly practice the *Kun* hexagram’s principle of "circulating through the six voids," they may attain the yang of "Heavenly Light," which is precisely what is meant by "the medicine." Some claim that "Heavenly Light" (*Tian guang*) is a corruption of "Heavenly Heart" (*Tian xin*), and this interpretation is also quite sound. Yet yang forms images, while yin forms shapes: the sun, moon, and stars, adorned in the heavens, all possess light; mountains, rivers, and seas, borne upon the earth, all possess form. The original countenance of *Qian*’s primordiality, singular and true, shines forth in solitary radiance—this is the advent of true yang, and it also presents the scene of "an empty chamber generating brightness," hence the term "Heavenly Light" is indeed fitting.

The following line connects to the preceding, speaking of the wondrous efficacy of true treading and practice, which is most crucial. These four characters encompass the entire work, serving as the secret transmission of the heart-mind. Breathing refers to the inhalation and exhalation through the mouth and nose, yet it distinguishes between the true person and the ordinary person. *Zhuangzi* states: "The true person breathes from the heels, while the ordinary person breathes from the throat." The heel signifies deep and profound respiration; true breathing is embryonic breathing, internal respiration, the inner sun and moon—an affair of the primordial. Mouth-and-nose breathing is external respiration, ordinary breath, the outer sun and moon—an affair of the acquired. The sun and moon alternate in their comings and goings, giving rise to the four seasons; breathing alternates in its comings and goings, giving rise to human longevity—both take their images from the *Kan* and *Li* hexagrams, which are the primordial *Qian* and *Kun*. *Qian* moves and closes, *Kun* is still and opens; one closing and one opening is called transformation, ceaseless coming and going is called penetration. Thus, *Qian* and *Kun* bear the image of a gate, and the space between heaven and earth resembles a bellows.

The Great Void uses heaven and earth as its embryo and the sun and moon as its breath, thereby achieving undying circulation. Humans use *qi* as their breath. With each inhalation, one steals the *qi* of heaven, earth, and the myriad things to make it their own; with each exhalation, heaven, earth, and the myriad things steal one’s *qi* to make it their own. *Qi* circulates throughout the bodily form, commanding the five senses, the hundred bones, the four limbs, and the nine orifices to accomplish movement. When *qi* is exhausted, the person dies, and breathing ceases. Thus, the bodily form’s existence depends on this *qi*, and this *qi*’s ceaseless coming and going depends on this breath. *Qi* originally comes from without, yet humans rely on it to live; with each inhalation and exhalation, it becomes the root of the four great elements and the body’s life—how weighty indeed!

External respiration governs tangible *qi*, which belongs to earth and is inherently heavy and turbid; internal respiration governs intangible *qi*, which issues from heaven and is inherently light and pure. At birth, humans first possess the embryonic breathing of internal respiration, then draw in tangible *qi* through the nostrils. Once internal respiration ceases, the acquired state begins, and the numinous clarity of the primordial becomes hidden and unseen. This external respiration, with its inhalations and exhalations, sustains the bodily form’s life; yet the true root of life still lies in internal respiration. The fetal breathing of the unborn child and the true breathing of the true person are both internal respiration. What is called "true breathing" resides an inch and three-tenths below the navel, where primordial *qi* congeals in formlessness, revolving like a wheel. When true breathing moves spontaneously, primordial *qi* reaches the four limbs, descending to the "Bubbling Spring" and ascending to the "Mud Pill," rising behind and descending in front, transforming into true fluid that falls into the "Yellow Court," accumulating to form the elixir—thus, true breathing is the foundation of Daoist cultivation.

Elixirists speak of "exhaling to connect with the Heavenly Root, inhaling to connect with the Earthly Root," which is the pathway of primordial *qi*’s circulation. In acquired humans, embryonic breathing has long ceased, leaving only mouth-and-nose respiration. The work of refining the self is the work of breathing primordial *qi* to seek immortality. The four characters "breathing to nurture purity" (*huxi yu qing*) also convey this meaning. "Nurture" (*yu*) means to gestate; "purity" (*qing*) refers to the primordial *qi* of true breathing, which is the true breath gestated from within external respiration. For tangible *qi* and intangible *qi* originally share a single source. When mouth-and-nose breathing ceases, true breathing arises spontaneously within the body; yet only when the mind’s deluded thoughts are entirely extinguished does mouth-and-nose breathing cease.

The principle of returning to emptiness is precisely the cessation of thoughts and breath, extending to the cessation of pulse and breathlessness. Chen Niniwa said: "After a year of practicing the work, the six pulses had already stopped, and *qi* returned to the root." Returning to the root means reverting from ordinary breath to true breath. The method lies in the embrace of spirit and *qi*, forging them into one, whereupon nasal breathing becomes faint and indistinct, primordial *qi* steams and rises, and the Dharma Wheel turns ceaselessly. Emerging from the mysterious and entering the female, as if absent, as if present—these two lines continue the preceding, describing the state of true breathing’s establishment, while the four characters "mysterious" and "female" (*xuan pin*) subtly encompass the meaning of the gate.

Viewing the wondrous and the aperture as one, neither existence nor non-existence is established—only then does the medicine arise in the mysterious aperture. The mysterious is yang, heaven, *Qian*; the female is yin, earth, *Kun*. The mysterious is true emptiness yet wondrous existence; the female is wondrous existence yet true emptiness. In elixir texts, the characters "mysterious" and "female" are of utmost importance.

Bai Yuchan said, "Where the thought stirs, there is the *xuan pin*." Zhang Ziyang said, "It is entirely upon the *xuan pin* that the foundation is established." He also said, "One must neither guard with intent nor seek with absence of mind." Lü Chunyang said:

"*Xuan pin*, *xuan pin*, true *xuan pin*—
Neither in the heart nor in the kidneys.
Speaking of the moment when one receives *qi* at birth,
Blame not the celestial mechanism for being wholly revealed."

Thus, the *xuan pin* is not an aperture within the human body but rather the moment when one receives *qi* at birth. The *xuan pin* is the foundation upon which the cultivator achieves the Dao. What is called "laying the foundation" (*zhu ji*) is but the laying of this; "refining the self" (*lian ji*) is but the refining of this; "obtaining the medicine" (*de yao*) is but the obtaining of this; "conceiving the embryo" (*jie tai*) is but the conceiving of this. The *Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* states, "The medicine and substances arise in the *xuan* aperture." The *xuan* aperture is the *xuan pin*; the *pin* is the aperture. It is also called the "mysterious pass" (*xuan guan*) or the "gate of *xuan pin*." However, the "mysterious pass" and the "gate of *xuan pin*" refer specifically to a single aperture, whereas the term "*xuan pin*" encompasses a broader meaning.

"Exiting the *xuan* and entering the *pin*" is exiting yang and entering yin, exiting existence and entering non-existence. Since it is spoken of as exiting and entering, there must be a gate, an inner and outer aspect. In general terms, exiting is from within to without, and entering is from without to within. Yet the elixir classics also speak of "inner *xuan pin*" and "outer *xuan pin*," while the *Yellow Court Classic* states, "Exiting the clear and entering the *xuan*, the two *qi* interchange"—this appears to differ. Why? The term *xuan pin* truly conveys the meaning of "generating existence from non-existence." For when things reach the utmost emptiness and mystery, there is the primordial true yang, which arises from the formless, unfathomably subtle—hence it is called "*xuan pin*."

"Exiting the *xuan*" is exhaling to connect with the root of heaven; "entering the *pin*" is inhaling to connect with the root of earth—both refer to the movement of true breathing and the ascent and descent of primordial *qi*. Because there is the semblance of exhalation and inhalation, it is metaphorically called exiting and entering. In truth, there is no exiting or entering, nor is there anything that does not exit or enter. With each exhalation, yang ascends—this is exiting the *xuan*; with each inhalation, yin descends—this is entering the *pin*. It is not called ascending and descending but exiting and entering because its ebb and flow are entirely within the gate, that is, the "gate of *xuan pin*." "Exiting the clear and entering the *xuan*" advances this meaning further. Entering the *pin* to generate the *xuan* is called "entering the *xuan*"; from the *xuan*, ascending to the first peak of Penglai is called "exiting the clear."

In sum, the *xuan pin* is true yin and true yang, the true breathing of exhalation and inhalation, circulating the microcosmic orbit within the formless: exhaling ascends from the gate of *xuan pin*, and inhaling descends into the gate of *xuan pin*. True yang meets true yin at the *xuan* gate; true yin unites with true yang at the *pin* aperture. Internally, *Kan* and *Li* intermingle; externally, the Dao and its power intermingle. Where the celestial heart is restored—there is the *xuan pin*. The foundation of Dao cultivation lies entirely in this.

The four characters "as if absent, as if present" (*ruo wang ruo cun*) pertain to the work of fire timing. Mencius’s "neither forget nor force," Confucius’s "neither obstinate, certain, willful, nor self-centered," as well as Yao’s "tranquil tranquility," King Wen’s "harmonious harmony," and the Duke of Zhou’s "ceasing ceasing"—all are methods of nurturing *qi* through fire timing. "Forgetting" and "preserving" are the same as "as if absent, as if present." Regulating postnatal breathing to seek true breathing lies entirely between intent and non-intent. For intent clings to form, and absence of mind falls into emptiness—neither suffices to generate the medicine or establish the *xuan* aperture. Even in "exiting the *xuan* and entering the *pin*," one must maintain "as if present, as if absent." For to preserve is to overdo, and to forget is to fall short—neither suffices to refine lead and draw forth mercury. Only these four characters deeply accord with the principle of natural spiritual breathing. Dissolving knowledge and purely following nature, the alchemical furnace will naturally possess the appropriate fire timing. As Ancestor Lü said:

"Rest not the river chariot of the elixir hearth,
For the crane’s embryo and the turtle’s breath flow ceaselessly."

"Ceaseless and unbroken, the root deepens and the stem firms"—these two lines closely follow "as if present, as if absent." For "ceaseless and unbroken" means intermittent yet continuous, as if present yet as if absent—this is precisely "as if present, as if absent." "Ceaseless" (*mianmian*) describes extreme length and fineness, connecting above to the root of nature and extending below to the stem of life. In the realm of profound obscurity, there is not a single breath that does not reach the heels. As Laozi said, "Ceaseless and unbroken, its use is never exhausted." The *Classic of Embryonic Breathing* states, "Exhale only subtly, inhale only ceaselessly." Both are methods of regulating the breath, which is the way of "exiting the *xuan* and entering the *pin*." Day after day, moment after moment, in movement and stillness, sitting and lying down, never departing for an instant—with long and persistent practice, the root naturally deepens and the stem naturally firms.

The root is the root of nature, the celestial root; the stem is life, the earthly root. At birth, one receives the ancestral *qi* of *Qian* as one’s nature and the primordial *qi* of *Kun* as one’s life. The root of nature is the abode of the valley spirit; the stem of life is the root of true breathing, also called the homeland of long-embryo abiding breath. Only through exiting the *xuan* and entering the *pin* do the root and stem daily deepen and firm, nature and life daily harmonize. When root and stem unite, the three revolutions become one, and the embryo is formed.

This is the second chapter, which comprehensively discusses the initial work of Dao cultivation. For the great Dao is simple and easy—there is no other method. Through this practice alone, one may ascend to the Jade Capital and roam freely in the heavens.

Each person possesses essence, and essence unites with spirit. This chapter first expounds the origins of the three treasures and the wondrous function of the primordial spirit, thereby revealing the mystery of "converging the three into one" in the text that follows—making this the most essential passage in this *Scripture*. Moreover, it encompasses the postnatal spirit, *qi*, and essence within the primordial spirit, *qi*, and essence, demonstrating to people the Dao of "borrowing the false to cultivate the true."

The phrase "each person possesses essence" speaks from the inception of the postnatal physical form. The *Book of Changes* states, "When male and female unite their essence, the myriad things are transformed and born" (see *Xici* II). The *Cantong Qi* says: "At the receiving of life, the body is originally non-being; primordial essence spreads like clouds, relying on *qi* to establish its beginning." When a person is first born, they receive *qi* from heaven as their nature and are endowed with *qi* from earth to initiate the formation of the embryo, where nature and life unite as one Supreme Ultimate. After ten months, primordial essence responds and enters, occupying the central palace of the spirit chamber to become one’s master, thus giving rise to the physical body. From then on, one can eat, move, speak, act with benevolence and righteousness, passing through infancy, youth, adulthood, and old age, until death returns one to emptiness. Over several decades, countless deeds are accomplished through this body. Yet tracing back to its very beginning, it arises simply from the union of the two passions of father and mother, where formless *qi* is stirred to form the physical body. The physical body of form is constituted by the union of tangible procreative essence, while the formless numinous *qi* is bestowed by the intangible principle-*qi*. Postnatal awareness is governed by primordial essence, which activates the senses and orifices. This body is truly formed of essence. Primordial essence, though formless, is also a thing of the prior heaven; in the postnatal realm, it is called "consciousness." This consciousness, when employed to serve the body and indulge worldly desires, may lead to endless descent; when turned inward to dissolve worldly affairs, it may enable one to become a sage or immortal. In the postnatal body, consciousness acts as the master, and whether one is wise or foolish, worthy or unworthy, all are the same in this regard. Each person has their own consciousness, and thus each has their own primordial essence—what Buddhism calls the "mind-made body."

"Essence unites with spirit" refers to the postnatal person, where the primordial spirit has deeply concealed itself in the realm of the unseen and unheard, while primordial essence enters the central palace of the spirit chamber to govern the movements of body and mind. The spirit of our heart has no outward manifestation; it is only through the movement of intention that it becomes apparent, hence it is also called the "spirit of consciousness." The primordial spirit is originally the substance of emptiness, undifferentiated and unaware. When primordial essence enters and stirs, awareness arises, and this unaware primordial spirit then sinks into consciousness, remaining unseen throughout one’s life. Only after death does the spirit return to its formless state, hence it is said to be "neither born nor destroyed." Those who cultivate the Dao must recover this primordial spirit, and thus we speak of it separately. In truth, the primordial spirit is formless and without substance, invisible and intangible; only when emptiness is utmost and stillness profound can its original face be restored. Therefore, the alchemical texts also say: "Stillness is the primordial spirit; movement is primordial essence." This phrase "essence unites with spirit" likewise conveys this meaning—it speaks of primordial essence entering the body and uniting with the spirit within.

Tracing back to the principle of the Supreme Ultimate, spirit is one, while essence is three. The one resides at the center of the Supreme Ultimate, while the three reside within the Two Modes. The position of the three is the position of the one; the position of the one is the position of the five. The *Cantong Qi* states: "The three, five, and one are the purest essence of heaven and earth. They may be transmitted orally but are difficult to convey in writing." For the one, three, and five all reside at the center of the Supreme Ultimate, the primordial *qi* from which the myriad things are born. In humans, primordial essence moves as intention, and intention pertains to the central earth—thus one becomes three, and three becomes five. Moreover, spirit and *qi* unite to generate essence, and when essence moves, spirit moves. For example, when male and female engage in intercourse, the spirit must first be stirred, after which *qi* arrives and essence is produced. Within the human body, there is yin essence that nourishes it. Yin essence is the refined essence transformed from the five grains and food. This essence in the body maintains the luster of the skin and flesh; when this essence is exhausted, the person becomes emaciated. Additionally, there is the essence of sexual union—procreative essence. This essence arises from sexual stimulation; at the moment of union, the heart-spirit is agitated, rushing into the Gate of Life, and the primordial *qi* follows, surging through the spinal marrow to transform into essence and be released. Thus, those who indulge in lust often suffer from aching backs and waists, for their primordial *qi* and spinal marrow are depleted. The existence of the body also depends on this essence, which overflows due to the stirring of the heart-spirit—thus the essence of sexual union is fundamentally united with the spirit of our heart.

Spirit unites with *qi*, and *qi* unites with the true body. The preceding sentence continues to describe the function of spirit and *qi*; the following sentence speaks of the marvel of returning from the postnatal to the prenatal. Together with the two preceding sentences, it clarifies the Dao of seeking the Dharma-body through the physical body—all is naturally operative, requiring no artificial cultivation. When essence unites with spirit, intention moves and *qi* moves; when *qi* moves, essence moves. Initially, movement begins with formless essence, and ultimately, it moves with tangible essence. The three treasures circulate and transform to generate the myriad things—such is the profound mystery of the Dao. Spirit is formless and belongs to yang; *qi* is tangible and belongs to yin; essence is born from the union of yin and yang.

The Dao gives birth to the One, and the One is spirit; the One gives birth to the Two, and the Two is *qi*; the Two gives birth to the Three, which is essence. The Three is formed by the union of the One and the Two, and thus essence may encompass both spirit and *qi*. Spirit, in the prenatal state, is the master of all transformations; in the postnatal state, it resides within primordial essence. The movement and stillness of *qi*, in turn, follow the activity and quiescence of spirit—when spirit moves, *qi* moves; when *qi* moves, essence is generated. The nature and life of humans consist of nothing but one spirit and one *qi*. When spirit and *qi* unite, they produce essence, which attaches to the physical body and governs it. In the prenatal state, primordial spirit is originally empty and true, primordial *qi* is subtly existent, while primordial essence stands neither in being nor non-being, yet interpenetrates both. This is why essence is spoken of first (possibly a redundant repetition). In the postnatal body, primordial spirit and primordial *qi* are deeply concealed and unseen; only primordial essence may still be sought in stillness. The initial practice begins with refining essence into *qi*, which is to return from essence to primordial *qi*. Once primordial *qi* is attained, one then seeks primordial spirit within it—this is called "refining *qi* into spirit," which is truly the method of uniting *qi* and merging the Two into the One. When the Three Treasures unite, the Two Modes return to the Supreme Ultimate, and from the Supreme Ultimate, one returns to the original face of prenatal true emptiness—this is called "refining spirit to return to emptiness," also known as "the utmost truth." The utmost truth is yang spirit: when quiescent and unmoving, it is emptiness; when responsive and freely flowing, it is subtle existence. Radiant, pure, and unfathomably transformative, Daoists commonly call it "the True," while Confucians name it "spirit," as in "what is so sagely as to be unknowable is called spirit." "Spirit is that by which the myriad things are wondrously spoken of." This formless body is called the "pure Dharma-body"; in terms of its transformations, it is called the "transformation body," which is truly the expansion and magnification of the heavenly-endowed nature, transcending the Three Realms to become one with the Great Void.

This passage establishes its discourse in four stages: when the adept refines essence into *qi*, refines *qi* into spirit, and refines spirit to return to emptiness, the Dharma-body is completed, becoming the body of the utmost truth. Yet this body of the utmost truth is in reality but the wondrous function of one spirit. Dissolving the postnatal consciousness of primordial essence to restore the primordial spirit originally endowed, and uniting it with primordial *qi* to transmute turbid yin into pure yang, the Dharma-body is fully perfected. This is the meeting of the Three Teachings, where primordial true *yi*-*qi* is gathered, congealed, and harmonized to obtain the yang elixir—hence it is said, "*qi* unites with the true body." The preceding three sentences must be interpreted in continuity to be readily understood; otherwise, the final phrase "true body" lacks foundation. Primordial essence and primordial *qi* are both encompassed within primordial spirit, fully manifesting the wondrous function of one spirit. Indeed, primordial spirit is originally pure yang *qi*, of the same kind as the principle-*qi* that first arises in the Supreme Ultimate and gives birth to heaven and earth. When vast and luminous, it unites in virtue with heaven and earth, and in brightness with the sun and moon.

The *Scripture* states: "Essence unites with spirit, spirit unites with *qi*." It firmly establishes spirit as the master that guides *qi* and refines essence, and the repeated use of "spirit" reveals its subtlety. Primordial spirit is undifferentiated, the pure embodiment of the prenatal state. In postnatal humans, primordial essence occupies the chamber of spirit, transforming emptiness into existence, stillness into movement, nature into emotion, obscuring true numinosity and giving rise to the six senses and seven emotions. Thus, one daily descends further, suffering the cycles of birth, aging, sickness, and death. If one can attain emptiness and preserve stillness, eliminating worldly desires to return to the undifferentiated state, innate knowledge may naturally be restored. Seizing this opportunity to nurture it, primordial spirit may be recovered. When primordial spirit returns, primordial *qi* naturally harmonizes, and the yin spirit of primordial essence is spontaneously transformed. How wondrous is the function of spirit!

Without attaining the true, all are but forced designations. These two sentences further explain that primordial essence, primordial *qi*, and primordial spirit are all prenatal entities; when united, they form the true, but without this union, essence, *qi*, and spirit are all mere illusions. Why is this so? In the postnatal physical body, the consciousness-spirit dominates, primordial *qi* lies concealed, and life depends on the breath of mouth and nose, the water and grains that generate turbid essence. Within the body, nothing but turbid yin is gathered; the prenatal formless Three Treasures of the supreme medicine lie hidden in obscurity, unseen. Though named essence, *qi*, and spirit, they lack the reality of essence, *qi*, and spirit. These are merely three tangible, visible things forcibly called essence, *qi*, and spirit, capable only of sustaining the body for several decades but unfit for use as the supreme medicine in elixir refinement—hence it is said, "without attaining the true."

Spirit can enter stone, spirit can make the body fly. It enters water without drowning, enters fire without burning.

The preceding four lines expound the marvels of the yang spirit, completing the emphasis on the word "spirit" in the previous section. The spirit, by nature formless, belongs to yang in its essence. In the postnatal realm, it becomes entangled with emotions and consciousness, obscured by turbid yin, and thus loses its function of emptiness and numinosity. In the prenatal state, the original spirit’s body is fundamentally true emptiness, giving rise to the ten thousand existences. It walks with the sun and moon without casting a shadow, enters metal and stone without form. Because it is empty, there is nowhere it cannot penetrate; because it is numinous, there is nowhere it does not succeed. Above, it reaches the nine heavens; below, it descends to the nine abysses. When released, it pervades the six directions; when gathered, it retreats into secrecy—all is the circulation of the primordial *qi*. The dharmakāya of the yang spirit, when concentrated, takes form like a human body; when dispersed, it becomes *qi*, returning to emptiness. Thus, tigers and rhinoceroses cannot harm it, blades and weapons cannot pierce it, water and fire cannot injure it.
