Moreover, the spirit is the master of the body, the root of spirit and essence. Essence and *qi* achieve their marvelous harmony through the spirit—hence the *Yijing* says, "Essence enters the spirit." Once the original spirit recovers its emptiness and numinosity, it follows the heart’s desires, responding with boundless subtlety. Thus, even the physical body can transform in accordance with the spirit: when the spirit rises, the body rises; when the spirit conceals itself, the body conceals itself. This is the origin of the ancient "art of invisibility." To live or die is as one wishes—Confucius said, "Hear the Dao in the morning, and you may die in the evening!" *The Awakening to Reality* says, "My life is governed by me, not by Heaven." The *Śūraṅgama Sūtra* states, "Pure thought can fly," and the *Sūtra* says, "The spirit can make the body fly." All these speak of the body’s marvelous transformation following the spirit.

The spirit’s wondrous functions can be observed in moments of human peril. When one encounters disasters of water or fire, even objects weighing a thousand pounds can be lifted; walls a thousand fathoms high can be leapt down from. This is the effect of single-minded focus, forgetting the body. Only by concentrating wholly on the original spirit does one forget the physical form, and thus this body may become deathless. Formerly, when Shenxiu sent someone to assassinate the Sixth Patriarch, the sword struck his head but could not harm him. When a lay monk set fire to burn Jigong, the temple burned to ashes, yet he remained unharmed. The *Complete Book of Anshi* records: "A monk obtained several pounds of Solomon’s seal, intending to consume it for longevity, though he did not know whether it would be effective. He first sent someone into a dry well, where the Solomon’s seal was stored, and as soon as the person entered, he covered the well with a millstone. Unable to escape, the person resigned himself to death. Suddenly, a fox in the millstone’s hole spoke: ‘The spirit can make the body fly. Simply focus your mind single-mindedly, gaze at the hole in the stone, and after seven days, you will naturally fly out. We who cultivate the Dao first refine the body. We dig into ancient tombs and lie down, gazing upward. Over time, both body and spirit transform.’ The person followed this advice, and on the eighth day, he indeed flew out through the stone’s hole. The monk, seeing this, rejoiced greatly, believing it to be the effect of the Solomon’s seal. He then leapt into the well, and his disciples covered it again with the millstone. After several days, he died."

The *Master of Supreme Roaming* records: "A certain adept could enter fire unharmed and enter water without drowning. The Master of Supreme Roaming summoned him and asked about this. The adept said, ‘I have no other marvel; I only know how to forget the body. Formerly, I received my teacher’s instructions: when practicing arts in water, regard the body as withered wood, the hands and feet as rotten branches; when entering fire, regard the body as mountain stone, the hands and feet as clumps of earth. With single-minded focus, water and fire naturally cannot harm you.’" These are all proofs of forgetting the body and employing the spirit. If an adept, through momentary concentration of spirit, can achieve such effects, how much more will a cultivator who maintains the spirit throughout attain ascension on rosy clouds!

The spirit depends on the body to arise; essence depends on *qi* for fullness. Unwithering and undecaying, the pine and cypress remain evergreen. These four lines complete the meaning of this chapter, expounding how essence, *qi*, and spirit become abundant. The spirit is fundamentally utterly empty, and only by attaching to form does it have agency. Before a person is born, there is nothing at all; once born, the spirit immediately depends on it. The body is the spirit’s dwelling; the spirit is the body’s master. The spirit in heaven is merely a point of principle-*qi* that ceaselessly generates life. When a person receives this to form an embryo, it becomes the mandate of heaven, endowed as one’s nature. Nature, in true emptiness, nurtures wondrous existence, governing from the center to enact its transformations, thereby preserving the body. Thus, speaking from its emptiness, it is called nature; speaking from its wondrousness, it is called spirit. That by which humans are more numinous than the myriad things is precisely this spirit.

In the prenatal state, the spirit is merged with the great void, without color or form, without substance or function—its numinosity cannot be perceived. Only when it attaches to form do true emptiness and wondrous existence mutually serve as substance and function. Form is not different from emptiness; emptiness is not different from form. Though its substance cannot be seen, its function manifests in the world. That which depends on form to arise is called spirit. Before birth, it is pure emptiness, without the name of spirit.

Essence is the congealing of the marvelous harmony of spirit and *qi*. The body of youthful purity possesses only original *qi*, without the essence of intercourse. Once emotions and consciousness arise, the spirit scatters and *qi* moves, generating the essence of intercourse. Thus, the Buddha speaks of a single point of yang essence, hidden in the mountain of form, and also says it is not in the heart or kidneys, but in the mysterious pass of the One Aperture. (Note: The Buddha says, "I have a treasure, hidden in the mountain of form." The Dao says, "The prenatal single point of yang essence is not in the heart or kidneys, but in the One Aperture of the mysterious pass.")

The One Aperture is that moment when "earth thunder" returns in *fu* (hexagram 24), where spirit and *qi* unite to generate the supreme medicine. Within this aperture lies the primordial undifferentiated essence—primal yang *qi*—which, when harmonized and congealed with the spirit of the heart, becomes the nurturing womb. The waxing and waning of yang essence depend entirely on the fullness of original *qi*; the movement and stillness of original *qi*, in turn, follow the activity and quiescence of original spirit. Original spirit dwells within form, and original *qi* adheres to it, accompanied by true essence. That spirit relies on form to manifest its numinosity is due to *qi*’s capacity to animate the body. Even postnatal emotions and consciousness—what are they if not the transmission of *qi* within emptiness? Original *qi* and spirit are like tallying tokens, sharing a single origin under different names, both called the mysterious. Thus, original spirit knows no birth or death, nor does original *qi*. When form decays, spirit returns to true emptiness, and *qi* reverts to the great void.

Moreover, at the inception of life, nature and destiny are one: spirit resides within *qi*, and *qi* circulates within spirit, with no distinction between them. Only after the postnatal body is ruptured do spirit and *qi* separate. The cultivator begins by emptying to the utmost and stilling profoundly, generating being from non-being to recover the prenatal spirit and *qi*, seeking the undifferentiated primordial essence. This method mirrors the principle by which, in the union of male and female, the primordial *qi* of the Supreme Ultimate is spontaneously engendered through effortless resonance. Spirit and *qi*, in truth, are without birth or death: when united, they form the yang spirit, merging with emptiness to become indestructible; when sundered, they return to the void, not abandoning the bodily form but reverting to it, only then called original spirit and original *qi*. Tracing their origin, they are fundamentally imperishable, like the pine and cypress that remain verdant year-round. If one nurtures spirit, preserves *qi*, and secures essence, this bodily form will likewise flourish like the pine and cypress, attaining longevity and enduring years. This is what is meant by "form and spirit both wondrous, merging with the Dao in truth."

From "each person possesses essence" onward constitutes a single chapter, comprehensively discussing the three grades of the great medicine and their relation to the human body, while also touching upon the wondrous principle of the three returning to one and the transcendence of the yang spirit. "Spirit depends on form to arise," hence "spirit can make form take flight"; "essence waxes with *qi*," hence "*qi* unites with the true body." Thus, though the Dharma-body is not the physical body, those who cultivate the Dharma-body cannot depart from the physical body. This is why yin spirits and ghostly immortals struggle to endure, and why the practice of Dao cultivation and Chan meditation must begin with the physical form. The subtleties herein are "easily grasped by the wise" but "difficult to practice for the benighted."

The three grades share a single principle, ineffable in its wonder. When gathered, they exist; when scattered, they dissipate. This is the teaching of the three grades uniting to form the elixir. The "three grades sharing a single principle" means that original spirit, original *qi*, and original essence merge into one, restoring the prenatal Supreme Ultimate’s original state: stillness is true emptiness, movement is wondrous being—wondrous being is the principle-*qi* within the Supreme Ultimate. Principle-*qi* transforms endlessly, giving rise to the ten thousand things, hence called "wondrous being." The principle-*qi* of the Supreme Ultimate, when differentiated, becomes the three grades of original spirit, original *qi*, and original essence; when united, they remain the Dao of generating the one. The cultivator cultivates nothing but this; the attainer attains nothing but this. The three grades all emerge from the emptiness of the Supreme Ultimate, originally a single *qi* in the prenatal state.

Thus, if the cultivator takes spirit as primary, they refine essence and *qi* to return to original spirit; if they take *qi* as primary, they refine spirit and essence to return to original *qi*; if they take essence as primary, they refine spirit and *qi* to return to original essence. Terms like "the three origins merging," "the three families meeting," "the three essences uniting in *qi*," "gathering the three natures in the original palace," and "flying the three elixirs to the purple court" all convey this meaning, their wonder beyond words. The ancients spoke of "the Dao of the three-in-one": when the three unite, they return to the one; when the three are forgotten, the one is known. *Laozi* said, "Attain the one, and all affairs are complete." The three grades of the supreme medicine are this one.

The work of fire times and effort is entirely a natural process, not contrived through artificial means. It has been transmitted orally from mouth to mouth, imprinted from heart to heart. That no discourse is established is because it truly cannot be spoken, having no means of expression. The Buddhist school says that in teaching the Dharma, there is in fact no Dharma to teach; the Confucian school says, "The nature and the Dao of Heaven cannot be heard." For the Dao is but a forced name: being generated from non-being, the three grades return to emptiness, and only then is the one attained. With the one attained, the three grades naturally reside within it. If one relies on written words, then ten thousand phrases and a thousand sayings cannot explain it. The sages of the three teachings resolve it with a single word: "wondrous." Indeed, the wondrous cannot be exhausted by speech.

The character *yan* (言) here may be interpreted as "to hear," but I follow another version in reading it as "to speak." When the three grades return to the one, they form the yang elixir, also called the spirit elixir or yang spirit—the primordial supremely yang body, eternally indestructible, unperishing even when heaven and earth collapse. The Buddhist canon states, "That which has form will ultimately decay; only the formless is true." The yang spirit moves in and out, penetrating earth and connecting with heaven, following the heart’s desire. When gathered, it takes form; when scattered, it becomes *qi*. In quiescent stillness, it reverts to the formless emptiness; when responsive and unobstructed, it assumes the true form of manifest presence: this is the wondrous function of the three grades uniting in a single principle.

If the three grades scatter and are lost, they enter into profound obscurity. They cannot be perceived in a lifetime, and one drifts through the cycles of birth and death, the bodily form withering away as a result.

The seven apertures interconnect, each aperture radiant with light. The sacred sun and sacred moon illuminate the Golden Court. This further expounds that when the Great Elixir is accomplished, golden light suffuses the body, and when one aperture opens, all apertures open. After the adept completes the Great Celestial Circuit, the yin demons within the body are entirely dispelled, transforming into the yang spirit. When the golden light at the crown of the head becomes as vast as a chariot wheel, this signals the moment of spirit emergence. The eyes, ears, mouth, and nose—these four roots together form the seven apertures. At this time, the yang spirit flows through the seven apertures, each becoming a gateway for its entry and exit. Thus, all seven apertures are luminous and unobstructed, all connecting with the Great Void. The reason for this lies in the "sacred sun and sacred moon" illuminating the Golden Court. The "Golden Court" is none other than the Yellow Court, which pertains to earth, and earth generates metal. At this stage, the body returns to pure yang, and the Yellow Court is filled with the yang *qi* of Qian metal, hence it is called the "Golden Court." The sacred sun and sacred moon are the true light of Kan and Li: the sacred sun is the original spirit, and the sacred moon is the original *qi*. When the original spirit and original *qi* harmonize and coalesce into the numinous elixir, their radiance illuminates the Yellow Court. Spirit inherently belongs to fire, and *qi* inherently belongs to metal; fire shines and metal gleams, and therein lies the Gate of the Mysterious Female. Thus it is said, "illuminating the Golden Court."

The Golden Court is the master gate of the seven apertures, and its light naturally extends into them. The spirit of the Yellow Court governs all spirits; when the Yellow Court is filled with golden *qi*, all spirits within the body transform their yin into yang, and the function of the yang elixir is vast indeed. Once attained, it is eternally possessed, and the body naturally becomes light. Supreme harmony overflows, and the bones turn to cold jade. This speaks of the wondrousness of attaining the elixir. The elixir is the sacred sun and sacred moon united as one. The ancient character for "elixir" (*dan*) is composed of the characters for "sun" and "moon," sharing the same meaning as the characters for "ease" (*yi*) and "brightness" (*ming*). Though this elixir is not easily obtained, once attained, it is eternally possessed without loss, and the true numen communes with heaven and earth. Thus, the *Xici* of the *Yijing* states: "The great person harmonizes their virtue with heaven and earth, their brightness with the sun and moon, and their auspices and misfortunes with ghosts and spirits. They precede heaven yet heaven does not oppose them; they follow heaven yet accord with its timing." For the elixir is the union of sun and moon, which is "the Dao of one yin and one yang." The union of yin and yang is the primordial Supreme Ultimate, wherein true emptiness and wondrous being both reside. The Supreme Ultimate is the source of heaven, earth, and all things; expanding its emptiness, one may advance to the Ultimateless. Though heaven and earth may perish, this remains transcendent and independent beyond the kalpas. Is it not fitting that it is called "eternally possessed"?

Supreme harmony is the primordial *qi*. The *Yijing* says: "Preserve and unite supreme harmony, and each rectifies its nature and destiny." That is, by safeguarding and harmonizing the primordial *qi* of supreme harmony, one naturally attains the rectification of nature and destiny. The *qi* of Qian yang is clear, light, and mild, hence it is called "supreme harmony." After attaining the elixir, the entire body becomes pure yang; the *qi* of supreme harmony flows from within to without, filling the entire body and overflowing beyond the pores and fine apertures. Thus, there appears the phenomenon of golden light enveloping the body, as the *Wuzhen Pian* says: "Lately, golden light manifests throughout the body; I do not speak of this principle to others." Only by obtaining the *qi* of supreme harmony do the sinews, bones, blood, and fluids shed their turbid yin. The white bones turn azure, the azure sinews turn crimson, and the red blood transforms into white marrow—this is the proof of yin cold returning to yang firmness, as the *Huangting Jing* says: "Bones azure, sinews crimson, marrow like frost." These two lines express the meaning that when the yang elixir enters the mouth, yin evil naturally disperses.

Supreme harmony is also what Mencius called the "flood-like *qi*." Attaining the elixir brings numinosity; failing to attain it brings ruin. The elixir within the body is neither white nor azure. The preceding line spoke of "once attained, eternally possessed"; this line speaks of the possibility of attainment or non-attainment, teaching the importance of careful attention to the firing process. For the formation of the elixir involves the firing process of "sealing and securing" and the work of "warm nurturing." If one does not guard against danger and consider risk, there is the peril of loss and leakage. In the past, Patriarch Lü failed three times to return the elixir; only after reading Cui Xiyi’s *Ruyao Jing* did he understand the subtle and wondrous function of the firing process and thus achieve transcendence. Patriarch Bai was careless in returning the elixir, resulting in the "midnight wind and thunder" loss. At that time, Zhang Ziyang was at Tiantai and sent him the *Jindan Sibai Zi*, enabling him to refine it again and succeed. Thus, though the elixir may have formed, errors in the firing process can lead to minor leakage or major calamity, even the ruin of one’s life. The alchemical texts warn: "The treasure of life should not be lightly toyed with." Yet once attained, the mortal bones can be exchanged, and numinosity is instantly achieved, as Chen Nianzu said: "In a moment’s effort, one eternally enjoys boundless ease."

Once attained, "the elixir within the body is neither white nor azure." White pertains to metal, and azure pertains to wood. The elixir arises from the intermingling of metal and wood, the union of the five phases, four images, three families, and two modes. It is formless and colorless, soundless and odorless, and cannot be named by color. This line likely cautions learners against mistaking it for a tangible object, hence it directly states that it cannot be sought through form or appearance—this is the meaning of the *Vajracchedikā Sūtra*: "Those who seek me through form or sound walk the wrong path and cannot see the Tathāgata." Recite and uphold it ten thousand times, and the wondrous principle will naturally become clear.

The closing couplet speaks of the profound principles and wondrous words of this *Sūtra*, which must be savored with care to grasp its meaning beyond the surface. Should one read hastily, its truths remain obscure—hence the analogy of "reciting and upholding it ten thousand times, whereupon the principle becomes clear of itself." The primordial spirit of humanity is inherently luminous and bright, yet veiled by worldly desires, losing its true awakening. If one focuses the mind single-pointedly, reflects deeply, and cultivates with utmost sincerity, resonance with the truth arises naturally. All Buddhist and Daoist scriptures that teach recitation and upholding share this intent. Yet the world fails to perceive this, mistaking the *Sūtra* itself for the Dao—how laughable indeed!

This chapter concludes the entire *Sūtra*, expounding in detail the principles of attaining the elixir, its meaning most lucid. Though this *Sūtra* comprises but three hundred characters, it encompasses without omission the workings of Daoist practice and the firing times, surpassing even the *Yinfu* and *Qingjing* scriptures in clarity. Truly, it is the "supreme seal of the heart-mind."

In its four chapters, it first speaks of medicinal substances and the dharma’s measure, then of the workings of practice and firing times, next of the wondrous functions of the three grades, and finally of the three natures returning to unity. The mysteries of attaining the elixir and exiting the spirit are laid bare in full, with no hidden meanings. Should one reflect deeply and read it thoroughly, the path of cultivation will present no difficulty.

**Twelfth Lecture: Heaven’s Theft, Human Theft, Vital Mechanisms, and Lethal Mechanisms**
The Dao resides in natural stillness—only those who are tranquil attain it.

Examining the *Yellow Emperor’s Classic of the Hidden Talisman* (*Yinfu Jing*), we find that earlier commentaries include those by the Grand Duke Jiang and five others, such as Zhang Liang; later, Li Quan and Zhang Guo provided annotations; still later, Lu Qianxu, Li Hanxu, and Liu Wuyuan offered their interpretations; and more recently, Yang Renshan has contributed his own. Each possesses unique insights. As for the Confucian commentaries by Wang Bi, Guo Xiang, and others, they fall far short of the mark.

This scripture encompasses a vast wealth of principles, serving as the foundational source for self-cultivation, family harmony, state governance, and military strategy. The arts of *Fengjiao* (wind divination) and *Qimen* (strange gates) likewise draw from its teachings. For all things arise from *mechanisms* (*ji*), and fortune, misfortune, regret, and shame all emerge from movement. The *Hidden Talisman* expounds the mechanisms of generation and destruction through inversion and reversal—its profundity is extreme.

Within the *Daoist Canon*, there are also the *Classic of the Hidden Talisman’s Celestial Mechanisms* (*Yinfu Tianji Jing*) and the *Classic of the Hidden Talisman’s Divination and Concealment* (*Yinfu Dunjia Jing*), which are referred to as the "outer chapters" of the *Hidden Talisman*. Upon examining their content and meaning, they appear not to be ancient works but may have been forged during the Song dynasty. Here, we shall directly expound the commonly circulated version of the *Hidden Talisman’s Inner Chapters*.

Now, we lecture on the *Classic of the Hidden Talisman*. What this scripture emphasizes lies in the single word *mechanism* (*ji*). In our cultivation of the Dao, the gathering of medicinal substances and the attainment of the elixir depend entirely on *firing times* (*huohou*)—entirely on the adept use of timing. Thus, *mechanism* is what must be most carefully examined.

All things and affairs in the cosmos arise from *mechanism*, and the path to sagehood or immortality particularly demands the urgent mastery of its workings. Why does it not speak of *yang fire* but instead of the *hidden talisman*? The Dao is the Dao of Heaven. *Yin* means hidden, and *fu* means alignment—hidden alignment with the Dao of Heaven. The Dao of Heaven is formless, neither seen nor heard, yet the Dao of the cosmos aligns perfectly with it. Observing the ceaseless transformations of great creation reveals the Dao of Heaven. Those who cultivate the Dao and seek it here will attain the Dao of Heaven.

In human life, there is birth and death, formation and decay. The Dao of Heaven has the *Peeling Away* of winter, which gives rise to the *Return* of spring. Without *Peeling Away*, there can be no *Return*—only with *yin* does *yang* emerge. Though the verdant pine and cypress do not wither in winter, they still sprout new leaves in spring, for they too wax and wane with the *mechanisms of qi*. The so-called *hidden talisman* is the alignment of *yin* with *yang*. The Dao of form aligns with the formless Dao. By observing the manifest, one may know the hidden; grasping one, one naturally returns to the three.

Here, we first provide a line-by-line explication of the *Classic of the Hidden Talisman*:

**"Observe the Dao of Heaven, grasp the movements of Heaven—this is perfection."**
These three lines serve as the guiding principles of the entire scripture and clarify the meaning of the *hidden talisman*. Heaven is *yang*; Earth is *yin*. The Dao of Heaven initiates but is invisible, while the Dao of Earth nurtures and is visible. *Qian* (Heaven) governs the Dao of Heaven and is strong, while *Kun* (Earth) compliantly bears Heaven and sustains all things. Cosmic transformation originates from Heaven but is manifested on Earth. The Dao of Earth aligns with the Dao of Heaven as if matching tally tokens—hence the term *hidden talisman*.

Heaven does not speak, yet the four seasons proceed and the myriad things are born. The alternation of cold and heat has never deviated from its order since antiquity—this is *grasping the movements of Heaven*. *Movements* refer to the actions of the Dao of Heaven. The Dao of Heaven, though formless, governs great transformation, and the myriad things ceaselessly generate, forming the world of form. Though the Dao of Heaven is invisible, its movements can be grasped and observed to understand that the Dao of Heaven is the principle of *yin-yang* interaction, by which one may trace the origin of the Supreme Ultimate. This is what is meant by *"The Dao is the alternation of one yin and one yang."*

*Yang* is formless, while *yin* has form. The formed arises from the formless; the formless governs, while the formed moves—yet all remains within the transformative nurturing of the single principle and *qi* of the Supreme Ultimate. By observing the principles of waxing and waning in all things, one may know that the root of the Dao is emptiness, which manifests as wondrous existence. From the One, the spirit emerges; nothing remains untransformed. All things decay, and fallen leaves return to their roots. Tracing beginnings to ends, one may know life through death; reckoning the past, one may know the future. By observing images, one may awaken to the Dao. The One disperses into the myriad, yet the myriad ultimately return to the One. By inferring creation from transformation and exhausting the Dao through its movements, the principles of Heaven and humanity are one—hence *"this is perfection."*

This aligns with *"exhausting principle and fulfilling nature,"* *"investigating things to extend knowledge,"* and *"the *Doctrine of the Mean*’s fulfillment of human nature to partake in the meaning of Heaven and Earth."* Li’s commentary, which sees these two lines as encompassing *Qian* and *Kun*, is correct.

*"Observe the Dao of Heaven"* refers to *Qian*, to Heaven. *"Grasp the movements of Heaven"* refers to *Kun*, to Earth. The cosmos’s generation and destruction occur on Earth, and the waxing and waning of the myriad things all emerge from within Earth. The Dao of Heaven is unknowable, but the Dao of Earth is knowable. Earth is rooted in Heaven and enacts its transformative work. The *Qian* element initiates—*initiation* refers to the *Great Beginning*. Before the Five Phases, there are the *Great Simplicity*, *Great Prime*, *Great Inception*, *Great Beginning*, and *Supreme Ultimate*. The *Great Beginning* has a root but cannot be seen. The *Qian* element’s initiation is the transformative work of *Qian* Heaven—this is the pure *yang* metal *qi*, from which all things originate. *Origination* is formless. The *Kun* element nurtures—this is the transformative work of *Kun* Earth, the *qi* of *Kun*’s element, from which all things are born. *Birth* is formed.

The *Qian* hexagram of the *Book of Changes* states: *"Qian: origin, prosperity, benefit, perseverance."* *Origin* is the chief of virtues; *prosperity* is the convergence of excellence; *benefit* is the harmony of righteousness; *perseverance* is the backbone of affairs. These are the four virtues of the *Qian* element.

Its virtue is vast yet invisible; only when the *Kun* element submits in compliance does it manifest in all things. Thus, *"to observe the Dao of Heaven"* lies in *"grasping the workings of Heaven"*—through the *Kun* element one knows the *Qian* element. One *Qian*, one *Kun*: this is one *yang*, one *yin*. *Yang* is great, *yin* is small; the small departs, the great arrives. By measuring the waxing and waning of *yin* and *yang* in the earthly Dao, one fathoms the soundless, scentless heavenly Dao—thus, nothing of the heavenly Dao remains concealed. These two phrases serve as the guiding principles of the entire classic. The following text then explains the mechanism of mutual generation and restraint, the subtlety of origination and birth, each discussed in turn.

Heaven has five thieves—those who perceive them prosper. This continues the above, elucidating the marvel of creation’s differentiation and the unity of Heaven and humanity. *Tai Gong* and *Zhang Guo* annotate the five thieves as the opportunities of time and things (interpreting them as *life*, *things*, *time*, *achievement*, and *spirit*); *Yang Renshan* annotates them as *form*, *sound*, *scent*, *taste*, and *touch*—neither interpretation is apt. The five thieves are the *qi* of the Five Phases, also the *yin* of the Five Aggregates. As seen in the later phrase *"fire is born of wood"* and *"the human heart is a mechanism,"* this is evident. I therefore explicate them in two layers.

From the perspective of *qi* transformation, the five thieves are the Five Phases: *metal*, *wood*, *water*, *fire*, and *earth*. From the perspective of mind and nature, the five thieves are the Five Aggregates: *form*, *sensation*, *perception*, *volition*, and *consciousness*. The Five Phases steal Heaven’s *qi*; the Five Aggregates steal Heaven’s nature.

Why is this so? At the inception of creation, emptiness held nothing—this is called *Wuji*. Then arose *Taiji*: a singular, undifferentiated *qi*, true emptiness unobstructed by wondrous existence, wondrous existence unobstructed by true emptiness. Without names for *yin-yang* or the Five Phases, without signs of arising or ceasing, growth or decline. Further progression brought movement and stillness, dividing *yin* and *yang*. Their interplay formed the four trigrams, joined with the central void to become the Five Phases—all transformations of a single principle-*qi* within *Taiji*.

Yet when one divides into five, there is fullness and depletion; when five unite as one, they do not perish. *Taiji* is the master of cosmic creation: mountains and rivers, flying and burrowing creatures, womb-born, egg-born, moisture-born, and transformation-born beings—those with thought, without thought, neither with nor without thought—all arise from *Taiji*’s principle-*qi*. In humans, at the moment of conception, there is but a single formless principle-*qi*. Upon forming the embryo, this *qi* generates the Five Phases. The One is emptiness; from emptiness comes fullness, becoming the physical form endowed with the Five Phases. Once the Five Phases are complete, arising and ceasing begin—thus, the five of *birth*, *aging*, *sickness*, *suffering*, and *death* all arrive in profusion.

For only emptiness in the world does not decay. Since fullness arises from emptiness, *qi* accumulates into form, and the Five Phases take shape, decay becomes inevitable—this can be stated with certainty.

Humans are a microcosm of Heaven and Earth. At the moment of receiving *qi*, there is but a single *qi*, chaotic and undifferentiated, neither knowing nor recognizing. Heaven endows it as *nature*; humans receive it as *destiny*. Heavenly nature is originally empty, neither arising nor ceasing. From *prior Heaven* it transforms into *posterior Heaven*, and the single *qi* generates the Five Phases. Thus, nature turns into emotion; the *qi* of the Five Phases transforms into *essence*, *spirit*, *hun*, *po*, and *intent*—these five correspond internally and externally with the physical form, perishing when the form perishes. Yet there are cases where the form has not perished, but these five have already vanished. These five steal one’s heavenly destiny, preventing what is received from Heaven from fully returning to Heaven. When the form perishes, these five perish as well, and may even harm the form. If these are not thieves, then what are? These are the five thieves of the body—external thieves.

As for the Five Aggregates—*form*, *sensation*, *perception*, *volition*, and *consciousness*—they are the five thieves of the *posterior Heaven* nature. *Form* steals one’s physical appearance, entering through the eyes and residing in the heart, so that objects arise in the heart. *Sensation* is reception, felt in the heart and stored there. *Perception* is thought, the two minds of past and future. *Volition* is action, responding to things. Only with *form* and *sensation* does *perception* arise; stillness of body and thought lead to movement and action—this is their natural order. *Consciousness* is the *shen* of awareness, *"fluctuating to and fro, companions to your thoughts"* (as in the *Xian* hexagram of the *Book of Changes*), ceaselessly galloping.

Heavenly nature is stolen by these five, giving rise to good and evil, life and death. When emptiness held nothing and preserved the celestial truth, there was no talk of arising or ceasing, and the luminous essence remained unlost. Now, ravaged by these five, one cannot fulfill their heavenly years, suffering illness, premature death, and the dulling of their luminous subtlety, becoming a heart of *yin* turbidity and dissipation. Heavenly nature is originally empty, yet the Five Aggregates solidify it; heavenly nature is originally luminous, yet the Five Aggregates obscure it. Over time, heavenly nature seems lost, and the *shen* of awareness takes control.

Immersed in the Five Aggregates, our original face is utterly obliterated. We commit all manner of evil deeds, planting calamitous roots for a thousand generations. The evil karma of beings persists through eons, emerging and submerging in the Six Paths, unable to extricate themselves for ten thousand kalpas. Only *karma* in the world is beyond comprehension. Tracing the root of calamity, it lies in the Five Aggregates obscuring our heavenly nature, stealing our primordial *shen*, transforming the pure, luminous, and perfect heart into one of cunning and dissipation. Thus, we sink to such depths—how lamentable!

If these are not thieves, then who else could be? These are the five thieves of the heart—thieves that dwell within. Thus, from the perspective of *qi*-transformation, the five thieves are the Five Phases: Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, and Earth. From the perspective of spirit-transformation (that is, the transformations of the heart-mind), the five thieves are the Five Aggregates: form, sensation, perception, volition, and consciousness.

The five thieves, whether internal or external, plunder body and mind. If we could but recognize them as thieves and cast them out, we would escape their poison and preserve our body and mind—an achievement no less than that of the sages. Yet ordinary people often mistake thieves for their own kin, regarding the five thieves as benefactors who gave them life, cherishing and embracing them, for they fail to see them as thieves.

If one can truly perceive these five thieves, allowing body and mind to return to the Great Void, employing the reverse course of creation to subdue them—Water overcoming Fire, Fire overcoming Earth, Earth overcoming Wood, Wood overcoming Metal, each in turn restraining the next, culminating in emptiness—preventing them from following the natural sequence of creation (Wood generating Fire, Fire generating Metal, Metal generating Water, Water generating Earth) and thus falling into the cycle of birth and death, then transforming delusion into constancy and refining thoughts into sageliness, attaining longevity and transcendence as an immortal, would be no difficult matter. Hence it is said, *"To see them is to flourish."*

To see is to delve into the source of yin and yang, to comprehend the principle of True Emptiness, to recognize the original face before father and mother were born, and thus to preserve the heavenly-endowed nature and life. One can then embrace and magnify them. Yet only sages—and those who later study to become sages—can achieve this.

The five thieves reside within humans but operate throughout Heaven. Where, then, do the five thieves ultimately lie? The answer is: *"The five thieves are in the heart, yet their operations extend to Heaven."*

The five of form, sensation, perception, volition, and consciousness are the Five Aggregates, born originally from emptiness. Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, and Earth are the Five *qi*, transformations of the primordial *qi*. When receiving life from Heaven, one is originally round and luminous, pure and unadorned, free from the slightest trace of karmic dust or worldly fruit. Yet as *qi* takes form and the embryo matures with full *qi*, the primordial *qi* divides into the Five *qi*, the spirit-chamber harbors the intermediate *yin*, the body embodies the Five Phases, and the heart contains the Five Aggregates.

Once the Five Phases exist, birth and death arise; once the Five Aggregates exist, rebirth begins. This is the primordial work of creation, concealed within body and mind, inescapable. If one wishes to remove them, the Way lies in *"seeing."* Recognizing them as thieves and preserving the original face of emptiness, one engages in the path of restraint and is no longer afflicted by them.

The five thieves within body and mind operate according to the natural principles of Heaven and Earth’s waxing and waning: One gives rise to two, two to three, three to five—all rooted in the natural order, ordained by Heaven, shared by all without distinction between sage and commoner. Only by overcoming them does one become a sage.

This passage clarifies that the five thieves originate from Heaven, not from man. The following text will expound on the efficacy of restraining the five thieves, proceeding in an orderly and unconfused manner.

*"The cosmos is in the hand; the myriad transformations arise from the body."*

Above and below, the four directions are called *"space"* (宇); past and future, ancient and modern, are called *"time"* (宙). The cosmos is in my hand, and the myriad transformations arise from my body—only those who perceive the five thieves can achieve this. Why? Because one who recognizes these five as thieves must also understand the principles of creation: the cosmos and its myriad transformations all arise from the primordial *qi*. Knowing this, one can return to the root and revert to the source, ultimately merging into emptiness, naturally becoming a great person of primordial spirit and creation, with the cosmos and its myriad transformations at one’s command.

The *Book of Changes* states: *"The great person harmonizes their virtue with Heaven and Earth, their brightness with the sun and moon, their order with the four seasons, and their auspices and omens with ghosts and spirits. They precede Heaven, yet Heaven does not oppose them; they follow Heaven, yet accord with its timing."* (See *Xici* in the *Book of Changes*.) The *Doctrine of the Mean* says: *"Only the utmost sincerity under Heaven can fully realize one’s nature. Fully realizing one’s nature, one can fully realize the nature of others. Fully realizing the nature of others, one can fully realize the nature of things. Fully realizing the nature of things, one can assist in Heaven and Earth’s transformative nurturing. Assisting in Heaven and Earth’s transformative nurturing, one can form a trinity with Heaven and Earth."*

This is the annotation for *"the cosmos is in the hand; the myriad transformations arise from the body."* Indeed, our body and mind originally form a Supreme Ultimate, identical to that of Heaven and Earth. Through reverse cultivation and return, pushing to the utmost, one transcends the ordinary and enters sageliness, standing alongside Heaven and Earth to form the Three Powers. Grasping yin and yang, silently participating in the great transformation, the sage’s virtue is equal in measure to Heaven and Earth, beyond all conception.

Moreover, the hand is the gate of total control, the place of mastery. Yan Hui, upon gaining one good quality, *"clasped it to his breast with reverence"* (both from the *Doctrine of the Mean*). Confucius *"governed the world as if viewing it in his palm."* In the hexagrams, this corresponds to *Gen* (艮), which symbolizes restraining anger and suppressing desire. The youngest son represents the hand, embodying the dynamic of yang extending and yin yielding. Zengzi said: *"When ten hands point at one, how severe it is!"* (See the *Doctrine of the Mean*.) The alchemical texts say: *"The two hands split open the primordial chaos."* There is also the saying: *"The left hand subdues the dragon; the right hand tames the tiger."*

The hand, in relation to body and mind, is linked to both movement and stillness. The arising of true yang first responds in the hand. Thus, in the *Xian* (咸) hexagram, the first six begins with self-restraint and resonance. Joy, anger, sorrow, and delight stir within, and the hands dance and point, trembling in response.

The heart of the infant is ever one, without duality; the hands of the infant, too, are ever clenched. *Laozi* says: *"The infant’s bones are soft, its sinews supple, yet its grip is firm."* *Mencius* says: *"The great man is one who does not lose the heart of the infant."* (*Li Lou II*) While the fetus lies in the womb, its two hands grasp at the gate of creation; when it first stirs, the hands part, and soon it is born. If one can guard the unperturbed heart and hold fast with firmness, one may progress from sincerity to clarity, from clarity to duration, and from duration to manifestation. The Buddha asks: *"The four directions—east, south, west, north—above, below, and the four intermediate quarters, together with the empty space between: can these be conceived? No."* Between the two hands, too, lies emptiness, vast and boundless. From ancient times to the present, all life unfolds within this emptiness; and every movement of the hands, whether flexing or extending, likewise operates within it. *"The great Dao is clearly before the eyes"*—and what is before the eyes is the inconceivable emptiness. If one can empty the heart and fill its capacity, merging with the great void, then the transformative nurturing of heaven and earth becomes one’s own nurturing; the space between the hands encompasses the vastness of heaven and earth. Thus it is said: *"The cosmos lies within the hands."* It is also called the *"gate of total holding,"* or *"guarding the center and embracing the One,"* or *"pointing to the palm."*

As the human body is a microcosm of heaven and earth, heaven has the sun and moon, while man has the ears and eyes; earth has water and fire, while man has spirit and qi. Heaven and earth bring forth creation through the alternation of cold and heat, their mechanism residing in the solstices of winter and summer; the human body, too, undergoes the waxing and waning of yin and yang, giving rise to life and death, conception and nurturing, its mechanism lying between the hexagrams *Fu* (復) and *Gou* (姤). Examined in detail, the seventy-two seasonal markers, the twenty-four qi, the cycles of day and night, darkness and dawn, and the pivotal moments of *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*—all these are fully present within the human body. The marvel of resonance and the principle of mutual influence are supremely empty yet supremely numinous. Thus, though the myriad transformations occur within heaven and earth, they are no less fully contained within the human body. *Mencius* says: *"All things are complete within me"*—how fitting!

The five phases overcome the one qi to give rise to the ten thousand differences. The agent of this process is none other than the creative heavens, yet the human heart responds to it, and the body and hands sense its mechanism, so that movement and stillness are both fitting. How wondrous! Heavenly nature belongs to man; the human heart is its mechanism. Man receives the nature of heaven as his own nature, and in the beginning, this is but a single point of principle-qi. Endowed with this principle-qi, one is born human, and this nature resides within the human heart. Later, it must transform into emotion, arising in response to circumstances—this is the human heart. What heaven bestows is called *nature*; what arises in response to circumstances is called *heart*. Heavenly nature in motion becomes the primordial spirit; the human heart in motion becomes the discriminating spirit. Heavenly nature is empty and without substance. We receive the principle-qi and are born, and within this emptiness lies wondrous existence, yet this wondrous existence remains within emptiness. Empty yet not empty, not empty yet truly identical to emptiness—this is what distinguishes man from beasts. *Mencius* says: *"That by which man differs from the beasts is but slight."* (*Li Lou II*) It is because he possesses heavenly nature within.

Here arises a question: the Buddhist teaching of *"whether a dog has Buddha-nature."* Some say it both has and does not have. Though a dog is a beast, it may cultivate the Dao and attain fruition, so it may be said to have Buddha-nature. Yet from the moment of receiving qi, man and beast differ greatly. Man is the spirit of the myriad things, and the principle-qi he receives is pure, complete, and centered; as for beasts, they lack Buddha-nature and are impure, and thus cannot be said to possess heavenly nature. Consider the tiger and the wolf: fierce and devouring, born with a vicious nature, dull and unenlightened—their lack of Buddha-nature is self-evident.

Let us examine this principle closely. The qi of *Qian*’s origin initiates the myriad things, forming images in heaven; the qi of *Kun*’s origin gives birth to the myriad things, forming shapes on earth. All things in the cosmos with form and appearance arise from this. Yet those born from wombs and eggs first perceive and then respond with qi; those born from moisture or sprouting plants first move with qi and then respond to perception. The former receive qi through self-perception, while the latter are transformed and born through qi’s influence.

Man, the spirit of the myriad things, receives qi in the same way as womb-born creatures, except that at the very moment of perception, he first obtains the true emptiness of the Supreme Ultimate. When male and female unite in essence, reaching the height of joy, all is forgotten, and the heart-body becomes empty and luminous, just like the primordial Supreme Ultimate (or Non-Ultimate). Then, the perfectly round, radiant true emptiness of wondrous existence arises in response to perception, like ⊙. When it later combines with the postnatal spirit and qi of male and female, it separates into *Qian*’s origin and *Kun*’s origin, establishing the root of nature and life. In an instant, the Non-Ultimate, Supreme Ultimate, Two Modes, Four Images, and Five Phases are all fully present—thus it is said that man alone receives the entirety of creation. This true emptiness of the primordial Supreme Ultimate is the heavenly nature bestowed by heaven, and it is also Buddha-nature. Bodhidharma said: *"Each person possesses Buddha-nature"* and *"Directly point to the human heart, see one’s nature, and attain Buddhahood."* The Confucians speak of *"heavenly nature’s empty numinosity,"* and the Daoists of *"the primordial nature’s original emptiness and stillness"*—all refer to this. As for beasts, they merely respond to the qi-mechanism without numinous perception. At the moment of conception, they form only this ⊙, truly lacking what can be called *"heavenly nature."*

This is why both Buddhist and Daoist traditions speak of *"the rarity of obtaining a human body."* The human heart responds to circumstances, manifesting as either good or evil. Mencius taught *"the goodness of human nature,"* while Xunzi argued *"the evil of human nature"*—yet both masters urged emulation of Yao and Shun. Xunzi’s *"evil nature"* refers to the human heart, the acquired nature of food and desire; Mencius’s *"good nature"* speaks of heavenly nature, the primordial essence. Human natures differ due to habitual conditioning: dyed blue, they become blue; dyed yellow, they become yellow—this is the acquired nature of qi and material form, the nature of food and desire.

When the human heart arises, its manifestations vary with timing and opportunity. The extinction of spirit-consciousness and the presence of primordial spirit depend on the moment; the return from spirit-consciousness to primordial spirit also hinges on the moment. The deluded may descend into bestiality in an instant, while the return from delusion to truth likewise occurs in an instant. Following the five thieves leads to mutual generation, while reversing them leads to mutual conquest. Following the sequence, wood generates fire, and from one arises ten thousand, forming the world; reversing it, water conquers fire, and from ten thousand returns to one, merging into the Great Void. For humanity, following the sequence gives rise to mortals, while reversing it produces immortals.

The human faculties of ears, eyes, mouth, and nose—when followed—lead to delusion in the six dusts, spawning every evil, even to the point of patricide and regicide. Thus, some wander in samsara, trapped in the six realms of rebirth, enduring kalpas of suffering without return. But if one reverses the flow, restraining the self and returning to propriety, refining the false to cultivate the true—like the ear’s innate ability to hear, yet making it hear the soundless; the eye’s innate ability to see, yet making it see the formless—extending this to the tongue, heart, and thoughts, all follow the same principle. From delusion to awakening, one may become a Buddha or an immortal, attaining the same indestructibility as the Great Void.

Though all share the same heart and the same principle, the gap between sage and villain may be vast, yet both originate from the same moment. Those who skillfully employ this moment harmonize with heaven and earth, return to the root and restore life, merging the human heart into heavenly nature—thus fulfilling the Dao of humanity. Yet only those who perceive the five thieves can achieve this. The Dao of heaven is established to stabilize humanity. The Dao is heavenly nature; to preserve it, one must establish the Dao of heaven. The Dao of heaven lies in the interplay of yin and yang. By grounding the Dao of heaven in this yin-yang dynamic, the human heart is stabilized.

This depends on transforming spirit-consciousness into primordial spirit. Spirit-consciousness is yin; primordial spirit is yang. Eliminating yin to preserve yang means shedding the human heart to return to heavenly nature. Once heavenly nature is established, the human heart naturally stabilizes. Otherwise, one cannot sustain the birthless primordial spirit to attain the principle of non-arising, and body and mind will still fall into the path of arising and ceasing. Chasing the unnatural spirit-consciousness harms one’s original heavenly nature; when spirit-consciousness runs rampant, the original nature scatters into the realm of nothingness.

Whether one becomes immortal or mortal, human or beast, the moment is subtle—one must discern it early and seize the opportunity. This chapter discusses heavenly nature and the human heart, the return from humanity to heaven to become a sage or good, and the descent from heaven to humanity into delusion and evil. Thus, the sage focuses on establishing the Dao of heaven, on the harmonious arising of heaven and humanity, and on perceiving the five thieves. By distinguishing heavenly nature from the human heart and grasping the moment of their harmonious arising, one can advance in cultivation and attain the Dao, becoming a perfected being. These two lines thus serve as the guiding principle for what follows, and must be read together. A detailed explanation follows.

When heaven releases its killing mechanism, stars and constellations withdraw; when earth releases its killing mechanism, dragons and serpents rise from the land; when humanity releases its killing mechanism, heaven and earth overturn. Only when heaven and humanity arise in harmony is the foundation of the myriad transformations secured.

The *Book of Changes* states: *"The Dao of heaven is called yin and yang; the Dao of earth is called yielding and firm; the Dao of humanity is called benevolence and righteousness."* (See *Xici* in the *Yijing*) Understanding the principles of yin-yang and yielding-firm enables one to embody benevolence and righteousness. Benevolence is yang; righteousness is yin. The generative qi of heaven and earth at the beginning of life is humanity’s benevolence; the destructive qi of heaven and earth in completion is humanity’s righteousness. Mencius said: *"The heart of compassion is the beginning of benevolence; the heart of shame and aversion is the beginning of righteousness."* (See *Mencius*, *Gongsun Chou I*) Humanity’s benevolence and righteousness are heaven’s yin and yang; their waxing and waning lie in the moment of movement and stillness—thus one knows the Dao of yin and yang.

The *Book of Changes* says: *"Movement and stillness have constancy, and the firm and yielding find their proper place."* (See *Xici* in the *Yijing*; original reads *"the firm and yielding are determined"*) Only with constancy can they find their proper place. Humanity is one of the three powers, endowed with the qi of heaven and earth, and thus can seize the creative power of heaven and earth. When heaven and earth release their killing mechanism, it is their movement; when humanity releases its killing mechanism, it is the movement of humanity with heaven and earth. Yet within the killing mechanism lies the generative mechanism—hence it is said *"overturn."* Life reverses into death, and death reverses into life, just as yin and yang exhaust their transformations.

Where there is life, there is death, and death resides within life; where there is death, there is life, and life resides within death. One life, one death—this is one movement, one stillness. Yang moves and generates; yin is still and kills. At winter solstice, heaven’s yang revives, a generative mechanism, progressing from one yang to two and three, until six yangs form pure Qian, the extreme of yang. At summer solstice, one yin arises, a killing mechanism, progressing from one yin to two and three, until six yins form pure Kun—thus the cycle begins anew.

At the summer solstice, the mandate resides in the fifth month, precisely when the generative mechanism reaches its zenith—yet the mechanism of killing already lies concealed within it. Thus, the hexagram *Tianfeng Gou* arrives, and one yin arises beneath the five yangs of heaven. From *Gou* comes *Bo*, and when the mandate reaches "Great Snow," yin attains its extreme. Then *Dilei Fu* arrives, and one yang stirs beneath five yins. From *Fu* comes *Guai*—its principle identical to *"exhaustion gives rise to change, change gives rise to return."*

The mechanisms of generation and killing thus interweave and lie concealed within one another. The killing mechanism of heaven and earth resides within the generative mechanism. During mid-spring, when grasses and trees sprout, the climate is mild, and the generative mechanism flourishes—yet the killing mechanism is already present within it. Witness the sudden fall of elm seeds: in the second month, following the opening of the three yangs, when flowers and trees vie in splendor, elm leaves have just unfurled, yet their seeds swiftly scatter—is this not proof of generation and killing proceeding in tandem? Elm seeds are the seeds of the elm tree, and generally, seeds form only after flowers have bloomed to their fullest, their calyces fallen. Yet here, at the time of leafing, the seeds have already ripened and fallen—this is killing within generation.

During mid-autumn, before the descent of frost, when the killing mechanism is at its height—trees shed their yellowed leaves, and all things wither—yet the generative mechanism is concealed within it. At this time, shepherd’s purse and wheat suddenly sprout—this is generation within killing. Thus do the generative and killing mechanisms of heaven and earth mutually conceal one another. Human fortune and misfortune follow the same transformative pattern as heaven and earth, revolving and intertwining like a ring. Therefore, those who cultivate the Dao must take heed of this.

To seek long life, first seek non-life. If one clings to the notion of craving life, death will surely follow without reprieve. For the mechanism of human life and death admits no gap—at the moment when one yang revives, if one follows the path of reverse cultivation, empties the heart to gather and refine, and transmutes essence into qi, one may preserve one’s celestial authenticity. If one follows the path of sequential progression, this is the union of essence to generate human life, where desire burns and harms the body, hastening death.

This is the mechanism of one generation and one killing. When the acquired spirit governs, celestial nature is lost. If one further follows its flame and nurtures it, one may commit all manner of evil deeds. Yet if one restrains the acquired spirit and does not employ it, one may attain purity and non-action, preserve the uncarved celestial principle, expand one’s capacity, and transform it greatly—thus may one achieve perfection of form and spirit, uniting with the Dao in truth.

The mechanism of generation and killing is one, yet its application differs. As the *Canon* states: *"Within kindness lies harm; within harm, kindness arises."* Kindness resides within harm, and harm within kindness. The human body labors for clothing and food, and through labor obtains life—yet within this labor lie birth, aging, sickness, and death: this is kindness within harm. Those who cultivate the Dao close their eyes and ears, which may seem arduous, yet they extend their years and longevity. Though they suffer in the moment, their future hope is vast: this is harm within kindness.

The mechanism of generation and killing lies solely in inversion. When heaven unleashes its killing mechanism, thunder, wind, and rain arise, heaven and earth darken, and the sun, moon, and stars all conceal themselves and vanish. Thus, the appearance of the sun, moon, and stars is heaven’s constancy, while their concealment is its transformation. Even the fixed stars disappear, and the sun and moon suffer eclipse—Confucius recorded these in the *Spring and Autumn Annals* to mark heaven’s killing mechanism.

The fixed stars, by nature enduring and unchanging, are visible at all times; though veiled by the sun’s brilliance in daylight, they may still be seen from high places or through astronomical instruments. The sun and moon shine day and night, constant through the ages. Yet when heaven unleashes its killing mechanism, even the ever-visible stars conceal themselves, and the ever-bright sun and moon wane—thus one knows that heaven cannot have generation without killing. Violent thunder and fierce winds are minor manifestations of the killing mechanism; the concealment of stars and the darkening of day are its major forms.

Li’s annotation of this "unleashing" as the generative mechanism does not accord with the meaning. The *Yinfu Jing* fundamentally expounds the principle of kindness arising within harm and the reversal of generation and killing. When speaking of the killing mechanism, the generative mechanism is already within it—there is no need to directly call it the "generative mechanism."

When earth unleashes its killing mechanism, "dragons and snakes rise from the land"—this is the time of *Jingzhe* (Awakening of Insects). Before the awakening, dragons and snakes lie hidden in the mountains, in a state of undifferentiated chaos, without knowledge or awareness. At *Jingzhe*, the yang qi stirs, rousing their consciousness to emerge—this is the moment when earth unleashes its killing mechanism, hence the term *Jingzhe*. Yet this "killing mechanism" is spoken of in terms of the Dao of yin and yang. When the killing mechanism stirs, dragons and snakes emerge, transforming hibernation into flight and leaping. In the Dao, this is the killing mechanism, yet for dragons and snakes, it is the generative mechanism.

The concealment of stars in the heavens is the killing mechanism, yet when stars veil their light, they will shine all the brighter afterward—this, too, is not without its generative mechanism. This is what is meant by *"the generative mechanism lying concealed."*

The *Scripture* proceeds from heaven and earth to speak of humanity: *"When humans unleash the killing mechanism, heaven and earth reverse."* These two lines are the crucial key to the entire text. For humans are one of the Three Powers, with heaven as their canopy and earth as their chariot, dwelling between them. Qian’s nature is pure yang, yet it governs the virtue of yin and yang; Kun’s disposition is pure yin, yet it directs the qi of hardness and softness. Humans unite the nature and disposition of Qian and Kun and receive the upright qi of heaven and earth. Thus, the transformations within the human body are the transformations of heaven and earth.

When humans unleash the killing mechanism, it is the moment when heaven’s stars sink and vanish, when earth’s dragons and serpents rise from the land—thus it is called *"heaven and earth reverse."* The mechanism of generation and destruction lies precisely within this *"reversal."* The *Book of Changes* states: *"The noble person works unceasingly all day, for the Way is in reversal."* (See *Xici* commentary on *Qian*.) Only by rectifying the heart and making the will sincere, maintaining purity and constancy, with *"clarity and luminosity embodied"* and *"diligence day and night without remiss,"* can one reverse the mechanism of generation and destruction and advance upon the Dao.

The mechanism of generation and destruction in heaven and earth resembles the solstices: at the winter solstice, destruction reaches its peak and turns toward generation; at the summer solstice, generation reaches its peak and turns toward destruction. This mechanism is revealed in movement and stillness. When stillness reaches its extreme, movement must arise; when movement reaches its extreme, stillness must follow. All things in the world operate through the interplay of movement and stillness—just as humans rise to labor at sunrise and must rest at sunset.

The *Awakening to Reality* says: *"To refine the elixir, one need not seek the winter solstice—within the body, a yang arises of itself."* This *"arising of the one yang"* is the body’s winter solstice, its sign appearing at the moment of extreme stillness, as when yang stirs after deep sleep. When movement reaches its extreme and turns to stillness, true yang ascends, the first yin arrives to meet it, and cool fluid descends—this is the body’s summer solstice.

The moment when the body’s qi mechanism stirs is precisely when the one yang returns. The generative mechanism is the killing mechanism—it can give life or take it: following it gives human life; reversing it yields immortality. When emotions and desires stir and primordial qi transforms into essence, this is the killing mechanism—birth, aging, illness, and death all arise from this. The yang essence within the human body reaches its fullness on the day when the Yellow Path of five thousand four hundred births is complete, an ancient metaphor called *"sixteen zhu in total."* This is the time of utmost abundance, after which it will never regenerate. Once leakage occurs, it diminishes; when exhausted, death follows. Those who ravage it severely die young. The *Awakening to Reality* says: *"There is only one original source—no trifling profit fills the purse."*

When our killing mechanism stirs, if we reverse it, essence reverts to qi, and through gradual refinement, the foundation of the elixir is laid. The term *"inversion"* in elixir texts refers precisely to this. The *Awakening to Reality* states: *"If you understand the killing mechanism and its reversal, you will see that harm conceals grace."* For human movement and stillness follow the qi mechanism of heaven and earth: yang arises in stillness, which is the winter solstice; yin arises in movement, which is the summer solstice. Extending this logic, all twenty-four qi reside within the body.

The *Classic of Yin Conformity* embodies the formless Dao of heaven. By grasping its mechanism and applying it, one can seize the transformations of heaven and earth and reverse them. Heaven’s yang returns in seven days; in humans, it arises from extreme stillness. This *"reversal"* is the turning of heaven and earth’s handle of generation and destruction, the wielding of the power over yin and yang’s waxing and waning. By uniting the human with heaven and reversing its mechanism, one can *"observe the Way of heaven"* and *"grasp the workings of heaven."* The mechanism emitted by heaven and humans is the same, and thus the foundation of the transformations of the myriad things is established.

Master Lü said: *"The living do not live; the dead do not die. To kill the living after it is born and to learn death before dying—this is longevity."* At the moment of utmost emptiness and profound stillness, when knowledge is entirely extinguished and worldly desires do not arise, how can yang stir? This is the mechanism where heaven and humanity unite in emission. Heaven’s yang qi surges, stirring the qi mechanism—hence it is called the *"winter solstice"* or the *"living midnight hour."*

The Supreme Ultimate that governs creation is originally a body of empty chaos. When the human heart becomes empty, it merges with it in substance. Thus, yang arises from emptiness, and the heart of heaven and the human heart resonate as like attracts like. True yang, borne by the yin heel vessel, surges into the *"Gate of Life"* and stirs. This is what is meant by *"primordial true yang arising from the formless,"* or *"summoning true yang to come forth,"* or *"like a magnet drawing iron, penetrating barriers without obstruction"*—all refer to this single event.

When the heart is empty, I become the emptiness of the Supreme Ultimate and naturally resonate with the Supreme Ultimate of the void. When the body is still, I embody the pure Kun trigram and naturally draw that one yang to return. Those who cultivate the Dao must first discern the clarity or turbidity of the water’s source to verify whether it is the mechanism where heaven and humanity unite. Only the yang that arises from extreme emptiness and stillness is clear; the slightest intrusion of emotion or thought renders it turbid, no longer a union of heaven and humanity.

Thus, when we first begin our practice, we must seek deep sleep in stillness. At this time, unaware and unknowing, heaven and earth unite in harmony, sun and moon conjoin, and chaos coalesces into the vision of the Supreme Ultimate. Then, a single point of principle-qi naturally emerges from the void, initiating creation. By employing this mechanism in stillness, gathering, sealing, and securing it, the foundation of all transformations is established.

Zhang Sanfeng said: *"If the human heart unites with the heart of heaven, the inversion of yin and yang takes but a moment."* Shaozi said: *"Within that fleeting radiance, there is no time for words."* Chen Nigan said: *"A mere instant’s effort yields endless ease and joy."* Such is the truth.

When heaven and humanity unite in activation, for ordinary people this is a mechanism of destruction, but for the Dao cultivator it becomes a mechanism of generation—this is the "inversion," the "reversal." The mechanism of generation and destruction lies in a single moment, so to seize it, one must first yield to it. Banishing all thoughts of the heart-mind, one seeks the state of emptiness and stillness—this is what is meant by *"To seek long life, first one must embrace no-life."*

Moreover, the Dao of heaven and earth is the Dao of humanity; yin and yang are none other than benevolence and righteousness, and the vast and unyielding qi is the principle-qi, born of *"accumulated righteousness."* Righteousness is metal, stillness, silence—when stillness reaches its utmost, it becomes trustworthiness. Benevolence is wood, movement, life—life and movement are the arising of benevolence. Righteousness governs solemn stillness; only after accumulating much metal can one resonate with the ceaselessly generating benevolence. The principle of *"heaven and humanity uniting in activation"* is thus in accord with the principle of human life.

When man and woman unite, their passions reach their peak and dissolve into emptiness, their spirits merging into one. Thereupon, they can resonate with the true yang descending from the void to form an embryo—this is the transformative mechanism of one yin and one yang. As for the resonance within the individual body, one employs the yin and yang within to summon the principle-qi from the void. When body and heart-mind become still, they form the Supreme Ultimate, returning to the original face before the parents’ birth. The prenatal true yang then naturally responds and arrives—what is called *nature* in heaven and *destiny* in humanity.

When heaven and humanity unite in activation, this is the moment when the original true emptiness of one’s nature merges with the nature of heaven, and one beholds one’s true nature. For *"heaven and humanity uniting in activation"* is the full realization of humanity in harmony with heaven. Yet nature may be skillful or clumsy, and can be concealed. The depravities of the nine orifices lie in the three essentials, which can be moved or stilled.

Fire arises from wood, and when calamity erupts, it must be subdued. Treachery arises in the state, and when the time comes, it must collapse. One who knows this and cultivates accordingly is called a sage.

*"Nature may be skillful or clumsy, and can be concealed"*—the nature endowed by heaven is the emptiness of the void, the undifferentiated body of the primordial spirit, appearing clumsy yet truly skillful, neither arising nor ceasing. The acquired nature of qi and material form, received from parents and shaped by the karmic roots of past lives’ food and desire, is the consciousness-spirit, appearing skillful yet truly clumsy. The former nature shares the same body as the great void and can lead to sagehood or immortality, thus appearing clumsy yet truly skillful. The consciousness-spirit is the seed of hell and the ancestor of all evils, thus appearing skillful yet truly clumsy. Ordinary people, governed by the consciousness-spirit, in truth turn skillfulness into clumsiness.

Yet the consciousness-spirit and the primordial spirit, though they alternate between skill and clumsiness, mutually conceal and restrain one another. When the consciousness-spirit is extinguished, the primordial spirit revives; when the primordial spirit recedes, the consciousness-spirit runs rampant. The Dao cultivator subdues the consciousness-spirit, returning it to emptiness—this is concealing skill within clumsiness. Though the primordial spirit may seem undifferentiated, it contains vast luminosity. Thus, the clumsy can be made skillful, and even the acquired nature of qi and material form can be transformed and returned to truth—this is why *"concealment"* is essential.

The primordial spirit is yang; the consciousness-spirit is yin. The two mutually conceal and restrain one another, naturally forming the undifferentiated Supreme Ultimate. Thus, at the outset of practice, the *"hidden dragon does not act"*—one conceals and subdues the heart-mind and nature, remaining utterly still. As the *Canticle* says: *"The true person dwells deep in the abyss, floating and roaming within the compass."* Guarding one’s purity, body and heart-mind in non-action, the consciousness-spirit is entirely concealed within the hollow emptiness. Within, one forms a body of pure clarity; without, one assumes the form of pure Kun.

*Laozi* said: *"Attain utmost emptiness, guard deep stillness. The myriad things arise together, and I observe their return."* He also spoke of *"returning to the root,"* *"purity and stillness,"* and *"restoring the root of destiny"*—all referring to the principle of concealment. The *Kun* hexagram, circulating through the six voids, is called *"returning to concealment."* The alchemical texts speak of *"the medicine arising in the Kun direction"* and the saying *"facing the southwest"*—all expressing this same meaning.

Ordinary people cannot conceal because of the *"depravities of the nine orifices."* The *"nine orifices"* are the ears, eyes, nose (two orifices), mouth, external genitals, and anus. Some also count the ears, eyes, nose, mouth, navel, external genitals, anus, heart, and crown as the nine orifices. All nine are gateways for depravity: the eyes see and are deluded by the five colors, the ears hear and are deluded by the five sounds, the mouth craves flavors and is deluded by the five tastes, the nose smells and is deluded by the five qi, while the body is deluded by desires, the heart-mind by emotions and attachments—all are depravities. With these nine depravities, essence, qi, and spirit leak outward, and the human heart-mind becomes scattered, unable to conceal.

If one can close them, one becomes utterly still and unmoving. The consciousness-spirit relies on the nine orifices as its gateways. All human movement, action, thought, contemplation, sensation, and perception belong to the consciousness-spirit. The consciousness-spirit is made perceptive through the nine orifices, hence the term *"depravities of the nine orifices."* Joy, anger, sorrow, and delight, when expressed in moderation, are harmony; when immoderate, they are depravities.

Confucius said: *"Let your thoughts be without depravity"* (see *Analects* 2.2) and *"The noble person has nine contemplations"* (*Analects* 16.11). The ears and eyes cannot think—why then are seeing, hearing, speaking, and bearing all called *"contemplation"*? Because contemplation is governed by the consciousness-spirit. If thoughts are depraved, the nine become depraved; if thoughts are upright, the nine become upright.

The *"three essentials"* are the ears, eyes, and mouth. As the *Canticle* says: *"The ears, eyes, and mouth are the three treasures—close and seal them, do not let them open."*

The union of *dui* does not rely on speech; scarce words accord with the primal chaos. This pertains to the external; as for the internal, it is the three of essence, *qi*, and spirit. Essence flows through the ears, *qi* flows through the mouth, and spirit flows through the eyes—these three interpenetrate. The ears and essence both belong to the *kan* palace; the *qi* of breath enters and exits through the mouth. When *qi* moves, spirit moves—this is the *"three essentials."* If the three essentials are closed and not allowed to flow, then the nature of skill and clumsiness may be concealed; the depravities of the nine orifices may return to the true. What can be moved or stilled are the ears, eyes, and mouth—the three essentials, which are ever in motion and never at rest, yet may be moved or stilled. When in motion, essence, *qi*, and spirit leak outward; when at rest, they are stored within. If one can contain the light of the eyes, congeal the resonance of the ears, regulate the breath of the nose, and seal the *qi* of the tongue, so that what moves becomes still, then the three essentials transform into the *"three treasures."*

For when the three essentials move, the six roots encounter the six dusts, inwardly giving rise to the six consciousnesses and outwardly emitting the seven emotions. Sinking into delusion without return, one falls into the cycle of rebirth. When still, the six consciousnesses return to their origin, the three treasures harmonize, and with courageous diligence, one immediately realizes bodhi. As the *Daodejing* says: *"Of those who follow life, thirteen; of those who follow death, thirteen"*—this is also the principle of *"one transforming into ten thousand, ten thousand returning to one."* The ears, eyes, and mouth are originally for seeing, hearing, eating, and drinking, to nourish this body, and seeing and hearing are especially where intelligence resides. That by which humans are endowed with spirit depends solely on these. Yet the mechanism of life lies here, and the mechanism of death also lies here; skill lies here, and clumsiness also lies here; intelligence lies here, and dullness also lies here. Form, sound, fragrance, flavor, and touch—these are what plunder the heavenly nature of our body and mind. What brings fortune is precisely what brings misfortune, just as fire relates to wood: fire is born from wood, yet when fire burns too fiercely, wood is consumed by it. Thus, what benefits others may turn to harm them, hence the saying: *"Fire is born from wood, yet calamity inevitably strikes."*

Extending this to the state, the principle is the same. If a crafty hero exploits the moment and the ruler trusts and empowers him, nurturing his influence only to ruin the state—such as Wang Mang’s humility and deference to scholars, Dong Zhuo’s merit in suppressing rebels, Xiang Yu’s restoration of Chu leading to its downfall, and Zhu Wen’s defense of Tang leading to its collapse—history records countless such cases. Who can know the mechanism of life and death, fortune and misfortune, and respond to the signs of movement and stillness? Only the sage! The path of cultivation fundamentally lies here. As the *Book of Changes* says: *"To know the incipient is to be divine"* (*Xici* II) and *"The trigger’s release is the master of honor and disgrace"* (*Xici* I). Those who study the sages should know their source.

Heaven gives life, heaven takes life—this is the principle of the Dao. Heaven and earth are the thieves of the ten thousand things; the ten thousand things are the thieves of humans; humans are the Dao of the ten thousand things. When the three thieves are fitting, the three powers are at peace. Thus, *"Eat in season, and the hundred bones are ordered; move the trigger, and the ten thousand transformations are at peace."* The *Classic of the Unseen Correspondence* emphasizes the word *"trigger,"* for life and death mutually depend and alternate. In their reversal lies the workings of creation.

*"Heaven gives life, heaven takes life—this is the principle of the Dao"* means that the Dao originally has no name; it is forcibly called *"Dao"* to guide people along the correct path. This correct path is the natural creation of heaven and earth. Natural creation does not exceed principle; the principle of heaven giving life and taking life *is* the Dao.
