Life and death have their trigger: in heaven, it is the trigger of time; in time, it is the trigger of *qi*. The *Book of Changes* says: *"To know the incipient is to be divine"* and *"The divine is that which subtly brings forth the ten thousand things"* (*Shuogua*). *"Incipient"* is the same as *"trigger,"* and only the divine can know it. The celestial body is empty and luminous, the master of creation—this is the one and divine. In humans, it is the original spirit of our fundamental nature. Only the movement and stillness of the original spirit of fundamental nature precisely accord with the trigger.

When the first yang revives, it is the *"living midnight"*; when the time comes and the spirit knows it, it is the *"living noon."* When emptiness reaches its extreme and stillness is profound, spirit and *qi* naturally harmonize, so when the trigger of *qi* moves, the original spirit knows it. As Ancestor Qiu said: *"When *qi* races outward, spirit also races outward; when spirit returns to the root, *qi* also returns to the root."* If spirit is disturbed, it separates from *qi*, and the heart moves first while *qi* follows afterward—this is different. The movement of *qi* is the movement of the trigger; it is the time when heaven gives life and takes life. To know this is to know the Dao.

For at the winter solstice, the first yang is born; at the summer solstice, the first yin is born—this is the principle of heaven and also the principle of the Dao. The trigger of heaven’s life and death is the trigger of the Dao’s life and death. The ten thousand things of the cosmos are born from the principle-*qi* within the Supreme Ultimate. Principle-*qi* disperses throughout the cosmos, giving rise to the ten thousand things—principle-*qi* *is* the Dao. Heaven’s giving life is the Dao’s giving life; heaven and earth are also one thing within the cosmos, equally born from the Dao. Yet the Dao is formless, while heaven has form, so later people attribute merit to heaven and do not speak of the Dao. *"Heaven gives life, heaven takes life"* is *"the Dao gives life, the Dao takes life"*—the Dao is principle, so it is also called *"heavenly principle."*

Heaven’s having life and death is not that heaven does so; rather, it gives life where it cannot but give life and takes life where it cannot but take life. Why? The cosmos is limited, while the ten thousand kinds are boundless. If there is life without death, there would be the calamity of overflowing fullness.

The transformative *qi* of heaven and earth, if insufficient to nourish the ten thousand kinds, will lead to heaven and earth’s exhaustion. If there were killing without life, the six directions would grow desolate, and the dynamic *qi* of heaven and earth would nearly be extinguished. Thus, life and killing alternate, mutually increasing and decreasing in an endless cycle. As the *Kan Li Contract* states: *"The sun and moon are the Changes; hardness and softness correspond."* The Way of life and killing is the Way of hardness and softness, which is also the Way of the *Changes*. The waxing and waning of yin and yang proceed in endless alternation. When yin flourishes and yang wanes, and heaven and earth are blocked in stagnation, thunder stirs beneath the earth, forming the *Fu* hexagram. One yang arises beneath five yin—this is stillness at its extreme giving birth to yang, life emerging from within killing. Through *Lin*, *Tai*, *Dazhuang*, and *Guai*, one yang grows to six, returning to *Qian*. When yang flourishes, wind sweeps across the world, forming the *Gou* hexagram. One yin arises beneath five yang—this is movement at its extreme giving birth to yin, killing emerging from within life. Through *Dun*, *Pi*, *Guan*, and *Bo*, one yin grows to six, returning to *Kun*. When *Bo* reaches its extreme, it reverts; when *Pi* reaches its extreme, it becomes *Tai*. One yang and six yin circulate endlessly, forming the world. Waxing and waning, fullness and emptiness turn like a ring, never losing their order—Fu Xi named this the "Great Ultimate." *Ji* means exhaustion; when exhausted, it transforms; when transformed, it flows. *Tai* means great, boundless, without inner or outer, beyond all form or likeness. Yet it does not exceed the principle that *"things at their extreme reverse."*

Delving deeper into the waxing and waning of yin and yang, one sees that yin is born of yang, and yang is born of yin. Only through killing does life arise; only through life does killing arise. Cold and heat, day and night, the waxing and waning of the moon—all are but the dynamic of movement and stillness. Without heat, there would be no cold; only with darkness does dawn come. The *qi* of the Five Phases circulates through the cosmos. *Zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you* are the four cardinal directions: *zi* is water, *wu* is fire, *mao* is wood, *you* is metal, which together with central earth form the Five Phases. *Zi* is water, yet water normally overcomes fire, but fire is conceived in *zi*—thus water paradoxically gives birth to fire. *Wu* is fire, yet fire is overcome by water, but water is conceived in *wu*—thus fire also gives birth to water. Water normally overcomes fire, yet fire is conceived in *zi*—thus water paradoxically gives birth to fire. *Wu* is fire, yet fire is overcome by water, but water is conceived in *wu*—thus fire also gives birth to water. *Mao* wood and *you* metal likewise mutually overcome and mutually conceive—this is the principle of water and fire mutually containing life and killing, each lying latent within the other. As life arises, killing arises; as killing arises, life arises. Overcoming is itself generation, and their cyclical nature becomes even clearer.

For heaven’s killing dynamic is but the concealment of vital *qi*—it is not true killing. Why? Heaven’s seasons bring life in spring and growth in summer, harvest in autumn and storage in winter. Spring and summer are the time of vital dynamism; autumn and winter are the time of killing. Yet what is called killing is but gathering and storing; and what is stored is the vital dynamism itself. Today’s storage is tomorrow’s unfolding. Life is indeed life, but killing is also life—only one is concealed within, the other expressed without. Observe the trees: when frost descends and leaves fall, withering in injury, their roots are in fact more nourished than in spring and summer. Digging into the earth to examine the sap within the roots, one finds it thicker than in spring and summer—thus the vital *qi* returns to the root, not perishing. Examining the earth’s substance, one sees that in winter it freezes, as in the north of the Songhua River, where the frost penetrates seven feet deep. Yet seven feet below, it is quite warm, so that those who dwell in caves need not kindle fires. For the yang of spring and summer spreads above the earth...

The yang of autumn and winter lies concealed within the earth. When life’s dynamism prevails, external yang and internal yin form the *Li* trigram; when the force of decay prevails, external yin and internal yang form the *Kan* trigram. *Li* is dark within yet bright without, while *Kan* is bright without yet sunken within; *Li* governs growth, *Kan* governs storage. Whether in growth or storage, all is but theft—stealing the principle-*qi* of the Great Ultimate to bring forth creation.

The Great Ultimate is undifferentiated chaos. In movement, it generates yang, which is principle-*qi*; all things receive this to come into being, hence it is called "benevolence." In stillness, it generates yin, which is metal-*qi*; all things are completed through earth, hence it is called "righteousness." One yin, one yang; one generation, one completion—thus the world is formed, enduring through ten thousand kalpas unchanged.

From this we see that heaven and earth, and the myriad things, all arise from the principle-*qi* of the Great Ultimate. Thus, the myriad things steal the *qi* of heaven and earth to flourish or wither, while heaven and earth in turn steal the *qi* of the myriad things to circulate. Humans steal the *qi* of both heaven and earth and the myriad things, yet heaven, earth, and the myriad things also steal the *qi* of humans. Why is this so?

The myriad things sprout in spring and are harvested in autumn. When they grow, they steal the *qi* of heaven and earth; when harvested, heaven and earth steal their *qi*. The myriad things exist within heaven and earth, and all yin and yang *qi* are the *qi* of the myriad things. Heaven and earth and the myriad things mutually steal from one another: the myriad things steal the *qi* of heaven and earth to come into being, while heaven and earth steal the *qi* of the myriad things to bring about their decay. Only principle-*qi* is the primordial source of all existence, giving birth to heaven, earth, humans, and things. When all existence perishes, this *qi* returns to the Great Ultimate.

Within the heavens and earth, all things—whether born from womb, egg, moisture, or transformation; whether flying, hidden, moving, or still; whether mountains, rivers, or the great earth—flow and rest, all reflecting this principle. Thus it is said: "Heaven and earth are the thieves of the myriad things." Here, heaven and earth stand in place of the Great Ultimate, opposing the myriad things. Yet heaven and earth are themselves but one thing, encompassed within the myriad things. The meaning is that the myriad things steal the *qi* of the Great Ultimate, while the Great Ultimate also steals the *qi* of the myriad things.

As for the saying, "The myriad things are the thieves of humans; humans are the thieves of the myriad things," humans and the myriad things likewise mutually steal from one another. Let us illustrate this with an example. As modern scientists explain, humans and all things depend on breath to live. Humans require nourishing *qi* (oxygen), for only with oxygen can they survive. Plants, however, are the opposite, requiring carbon (carbon dioxide) to live. Humans exhale carbon *qi* and inhale nourishing *qi*; plants exhale nourishing *qi* and inhale carbon *qi*. Thus, humans and plants mutually steal from one another to sustain their lives. This is one example.

Moreover, human life arises from the union of essence, *qi*, and spirit. This corporeal body is governed by spirit, lives by *qi*, and is nourished by essence. Yet the eyes, ears, nose, tongue, body, and mind unconsciously steal and leak one’s essence, *qi*, and spirit. The myriad things, through their colors, sounds, scents, flavors, and textures, further steal the essence, *qi*, and spirit of humans. Unaware, our essence, *qi*, and spirit leak outward through the six senses—this is how humans are constantly stolen from by the myriad things.

Yet human life—clothing, food, shelter, and movement, along with all desires—depends entirely on things. Even jewels, carriages, horses, medicines, stones, birds, and beasts serve our needs, often at the cost of their lives. This is how humans steal from things. Thus, the myriad things steal from humans, and humans steal from the myriad things. Combined with the mutual theft between heaven, earth, and the myriad things, this forms the "Three Thefts."

The Three Thefts embody the natural principles of creation and destruction. The great thousandfold world is formed by these Three Thefts and will likewise be destroyed by them. That they can mutually steal and generate arises from the invisible, empty void, where the natural Dao governs—giving birth to heaven, earth, and the myriad things, yet not perishing with them. As the saying goes: "What has form will ultimately decay; only the formless is true."

The phrase "When the Three Thefts are fitting, the Three Powers are at peace" refers to the Three Powers: heaven, earth, and humans. When the Three Thefts each attain their proper place, heaven and earth are rightly positioned, and the myriad things flourish. The mechanism of generation and destruction is the mutual theft among heaven, earth, humans, and things. "Fitting" means humans align with the timing of the Dao and employ it appropriately.

For instance, joy, anger, sorrow, and happiness, when expressed in moderation, are called harmony. Harmony is the universal Dao of the world.

Silent and unmoving, this is the "center before arousal"; responsive and thus penetrating, it naturally arises in accord with the measure. "Measure" means the seasonal markers—just as the Dao of heaven has its differences of cold, heat, warmth, and cool, each responding to its proper season; so too human emotions of joy, anger, sorrow, and happiness form in accord with the heart’s conditions. Sincerity within takes shape without; stirred in the heart, it issues forth in emotion—thus there is nothing that does not accord with the measure. If one is obscured by private desires from external temptations, then what arises will not accord with the measure, and the "theft" will not attain its fitting place.

Why is this so? Human joy arises from compassion and love, like the generative impulse of heaven and earth in spring and summer; human sorrow and anger arise from grief and indignation, like the destructive impulse of heaven and earth in autumn and winter. What is the same in both is the single heart; what differs in their arising is the stimulus of the qi-mechanism. The *Classic of Difficult Issues* states, "Great anger harms the liver; great joy harms the kidneys"—the movement of the qi-mechanism within the body already distinguishes the five viscera, and what issues forth externally naturally differs as the seven emotions.

Moreover, in ordinary people at times of no particular event, each scene evokes a distinct emotion: encountering the brightness of spring, one’s countenance becomes joyful; facing the gloom of autumn, a hundred feelings arise. This is because the qi they "steal" differs, stirring the human heart and touching the mechanisms of generation or destruction to manifest.

"Eating in season, the hundred bones are ordered"—as the *Record of Rites* says, "Food and drink, male and female: these are the great human desires." One must follow the seasons to align with the fitting moment. For instance, in the heat of summer, one may sink plums and float melons; in the depths of autumn, one may face chrysanthemums and hold crab claws. But one must not force it—forced desire is greed. Extending this principle, one eats melons when summer brings them and bamboo shoots when spring offers them: these are all "eating in season." If one eats out of season, as in the *Liezi*, which says, "Drinking ice in winter—will I not suffer internal heat?"—for in winter one drinks broth and in summer water: this is the human norm. To reverse it and drink ice in winter is something only those with heat illness would do. When the hundred bones are disordered, one eats out of season; and only by eating out of season does one disorder the hundred bones, thus inflicting illness upon oneself and harming one’s life.

This pertains only to the physical form; if we speak in terms of Daoist cultivation, to ensure the "three thieves harmonize," one must first comprehend the fundamental principles of yin-yang creation. Know that all things are born of the *li-qi* of the Great Ultimate, and preserving this *li* allows for enduring longevity. The generation of *li-qi* has no fixed pattern, yet it is indeed fixed—seizing its timely moment, one may reverse the handle of heaven and earth’s life and death. Thus, the *li-qi* of the Great Ultimate may be stolen and drawn forth; the vital force of heaven and earth may be stolen and obtained. "Stealing" means to gather and take—sealing and preserving what is gathered can lead to long life; expanding and magnifying it further can unite one’s virtue with heaven and earth and one’s brightness with the sun and moon. All alchemical texts record this act of stealing: the circulating *qi* of the cosmos is stealing; stealing the *li-qi* of the Great Ultimate is also stealing. Only when one steals *li-qi* is it truly obtained. Once one theft is timely, the Three Powers naturally settle, allowing one to stand between heaven and earth and share their enduring vastness. The *Classic* speaks of "the three thieves in harmony"—this is what it refers to.

What is called "timeliness" here denotes the moment of firing times, the hour for gathering and ingesting the medicine. One who understands the heavenly Dao’s mechanism of life and death may cultivate and refine accordingly—knowing when to gather, when to nurture, and when to perform the Microcosmic Orbit—all of alchemical practice can be encompassed within the single word "timeliness." "Eating in season" is entry into sagehood, hence Confucius is called "the sage of timely centeredness." The heavenly mechanism cannot be seen, yet within timeliness, its workings may be known. When the mechanism moves, the time arrives; when the time arrives, the mechanism moves. Ingesting the great medicine and stealing the true yang of emptiness—there is no time without it, and no mechanism without timeliness. Seizing the mechanism is seizing the time.

This so-called "mechanism" is the mechanism of life and death, the moment for advancing the yang fire and retreating the yin tally. Within a single movement and stillness, both the *qi*-mechanism and the timely moment are complete. For example, the return of the first yang arises from the movement of the *qi*-mechanism—this is also the *zi* hour within the human body. Gathering and ingesting from this point is "eating in season," moving the mechanism. Moving the mechanism is the moment when the *qi*-mechanism first stirs. At this time, there is life and there is death; turning inward in sincerity transforms the death-mechanism into a life-mechanism, while following human desire turns the life-mechanism into a death-mechanism. Moving the mechanism and eating in season naturally strengthens the body—this "timeliness" refers to the living *zi* and living *wu* hours. At the first stir of the mechanism, it is the living *zi* hour; when the *qi*-mechanism flourishes, it is the true *zi* hour. Sensing its movement is the living *wu* hour; perceiving its flourishing is the true *wu* hour. Gathering and sealing it at these times naturally brings all transformations to harmony, and the divine transformations of the elixir are all contained within.

Yet when yang first stirs, one must be most cautious. At this moment, it is the death-mechanism; only when yang returns and is gathered is it the life-mechanism. Thus, when the mechanism moves, one must enter stillness to refine it, allowing true yang to ascend and circulate throughout the body. If the practice deviates from the proper method and leaks midway, it becomes the death-mechanism. In terms of firing times, when yang returns, one must stabilize—stabilizing is the death-mechanism; when returning, one must forget—forgetting is the life-mechanism. All ungathered gathering and unseized seizing occur when body and mind are both forgotten, naturally transforming the death-mechanism into the life-mechanism. Hence it is called "inversion" and "reversal."

People know the spirit’s spirituality but not the reason why the non-spiritual is spiritual. The sun and moon have their cycles, and great and small have their measures. Sagehood arises from this, and numinous clarity emerges. The spirituality of the spirit is the spirit of consciousness; the non-spiritual spirit is the original spirit. Only the original spirit knows when the time arrives. The *Canticle* says: "In sleep, the spirit embraces; in waking, it awaits life and death." To restore the original spirit, one must first cultivate the work of refining the self. Once the original spirit is restored, one then knows the movement and stillness of the *qi*-mechanism. The spirit of consciousness is only clever in thought but cannot sense the *qi*-mechanism in stillness.

People often know the spirit’s spirituality but not the non-spiritual spirit, nor do they seek the reason why the non-spiritual is spiritual—thus their spirit is not numinous. If one allows the spirit of consciousness to roam freely, ceaselessly disturbed, and yet hopes for the spirit and *qi* to embrace and become numinous enough to know the mechanism, it is exceedingly difficult. The method for seeking the non-spiritual spirit lies first in making emptiness extreme and stillness profound, merging into chaos, dissolving the spirit of consciousness. Then the original spirit appears and can respond with sensitivity. For the spirit that is clever in cunning yet cannot silently illuminate is the spirit of consciousness; the one that seems chaotic yet responds wondrously without fixed form is the original spirit—this is what *Laozi* means by "great skill appears clumsy, great wisdom appears foolish."

To seek the reason why the original spirit is spiritual, one must first return the spirit of consciousness to non-spirituality, banishing the six senses, utterly empty and hollow, knowing nothing. Only then does the non-spiritual original spirit become spiritual, able to embrace, to sense, to await life and death. The original spirit is the non-spiritual spirit, hence called the "chaotic original spirit." Only by being non-spiritual is it supremely spiritual; if it were like the spirituality of the spirit of consciousness, it would not be spiritual. Though the original spirit seems non-spiritual, to seize the yin-yang creation of heaven and earth, one must use this non-spiritual original spirit. The original spirit is empty and void, of the same kind as the *li-qi* of the Great Ultimate, thus it can resonate. When the *qi*-mechanism moves, true awareness follows.

The so-called practice of refining the self is precisely the practice of refining the original spirit. The original spirit arises from extreme stillness, adept at perceiving the movement of *qi*-mechanisms, hence called "when the time comes, the spirit knows." The phrase "the sun and moon have their cycles, the great and small have their measures" refers to the numinous nature of the original spirit, born from non-being and emerging from stillness, like the fixed and unchanging cycles of the sun and moon. The cycles of the sun and moon—such as the new moon, crescent, first quarter, full moon, waning phases, and dark moon—are especially evident in the moon’s waxing and waning. On the third day of the lunar month, a sliver of new moon appears, which is "the return of the first yang"; by the eighth day, the first quarter, "three yangs bring prosperity"; at the full moon, six yangs are complete, forming a perfect circle; by the sixteenth, the "first waning" begins, the moon losing a sliver; on the twenty-third, the last quarter, half the moon is gone, marking "the closure of heaven and earth"; by the dark moon, it becomes a pure *kun* state, entirely hidden. The moon completes one cycle each month, while the sun determines the seasons through the four phases, completing one cycle each year. Together, the sun and moon form the *Yi*, the Dao of yin-yang transformation. The transformations of yin and yang follow fixed cycles, and the non-spiritual spirit can know them because it is of the same kind and thus able to resonate. "The great and small have their measures" means yin is small and yang is great: when the small departs, the great arrives, and when the great departs, the small arrives, just like the rising and setting of the sun and moon. The sun completes one celestial cycle in a year, the moon in a month, and the great and small yin-yang complete one cycle each day and night. Because these cycles are fixed, the sage can know the past and infer the future, discern the movement of *qi*-mechanisms, seize the right moment to act, and thus achieve sagehood. By attaining this movement and ascending, sages, worthies, immortals, and buddhas are made, and divine clarity emerges. Only by reaching the utmost emptiness and profound stillness does sagehood arise and divine clarity appear. The movement of *qi*-mechanisms is the resonance of *zi* and *wu*; the sage’s work lies precisely in this moment of *zi* and *wu*, where stimulus and response occur as instantly as a shadow following a pole. The North Pole of the earth is *zi*, and *wu* is the South Pole: when *zi* moves, *wu* perceives, *qi* stirs, and the spirit knows. Without the living *zi*, there is no living *wu*; yet without the living *wu*, there is also no living *zi*. Only when the spirit returns to non-spirituality does the living *zi* arrive, and the living *wu* becomes the very reason the non-spiritual becomes spiritual. This is what is meant by "inevitable response to stimulus": from the utmost emptiness and profound stillness, divine clarity naturally arises, and the work of gathering and ingesting *qi* depends entirely on this. As for the "mechanism of theft," none in the world can see or know it. The noble person who attains it strengthens their body; the petty person who attains it squanders their life. The "mechanism of theft" lies in grasping the moment: to grasp it is to steal, and failing to grasp it means failing to steal. This mechanism of theft is unseen and unknown in the world because there are no sages. If there were sages, they would surely see and know it. Only by emptying the mind of preconceptions can the mechanism be seen; only by extinguishing discriminative knowledge can it be known. If one clings to prior knowledge, they will ultimately fail to see or know it. This mechanism of theft, when attained by the noble person, preserves life and wholeness, allowing them to share the longevity of heaven and earth—hence "the noble person who attains it strengthens their body." As the *Daoist Scripture* says, "Only when the heart dies does the spirit live." That is, the spirituality of the spirit of consciousness must die for the non-spiritual original spirit to be reborn. Only by extinguishing the spirit of consciousness can one recover the original spirit; only when the postnatal returns to chaos can the prenatal be restored. "Only after things are fully realized can knowledge be attained," and "only through overcoming thoughts can sagehood be achieved." As for those petty persons ensnared by material desires, they exploit the cunning of the spirit of consciousness. Even if they hear the Dao, they cannot return the spirit to non-spirituality. If they use the spirituality of the spirit of consciousness to steal the mechanism, it only hastens the loss of their life-essence. Indulging desires and violating natural principles, they cannot restrain themselves and return to propriety, ultimately bringing harm without benefit—hence "the petty person who attains it squanders their life." Those who preserve the original spirit are noble persons; those who rely on the spirit of consciousness are petty persons. The noble person, upon hearing the Dao, believes, accepts, and diligently practices it; the petty person, upon hearing the Dao, dismisses it and treats their life lightly. Some have interpreted "petty person" as one lacking virtue, whose insufficient virtue in practicing the Dao instead shortens their lifespan. This interpretation is logically sound but somewhat superficial.

The *Awakening to Reality* says, "The life-essence should not be lightly toyed with." The life-essence refers to the external yang, upon which human beings rely to become immortals—hence it is called the "life-essence." The term "essence" refers to the prenatal *qi* contained within it. When heaven and humanity unite in the moment of emptiness and stillness, the true yang of the prenatal stirs within the void, rising from the *yinqiao* vessel to enter the *mingmen*, causing the external form to stir. Simultaneously, it surges into the heart-mansion, and the original spirit becomes aware. This prenatal *qi*, which activates the *mingmen* and the heart-mansion, is the life-essence. "Should not be lightly toyed with" applies precisely to this moment. Indulging in reckless dissipation is the ravaging of the postnatal, not "lightly toying." "Lightly toying" means reckless gathering and extraction, where the firing times are mismatched. Petty persons, with their half-baked understanding, fail to explore the true principle and act recklessly—this is "squandering life." The prenatal *qi* is formless and invisible, soundless and odorless—who can see it? Who can know it? As the *Awakening to Reality* says, "It cannot be used when seen, and it cannot be seen when used."

In the midst of the indistinct and vague, they meet; within the profound and obscure, transformations occur. Because it has no form or appearance, it can be used but not seen—once seen, it has already transformed into the postnatal and lost its moment. At the very beginning of returning to the origin, in the primal chaos where yin and yang unite, one knows nothing, sees nothing, yet the *qi*-mechanism stirs therein, and therein one knows of its stirring. At this time, spirit and *qi* have merged, the medicine has been born, the gathering has been achieved, and the *qi* has already returned to its root—thus it can be seen and known, yet this is already after its use.

When stealing the mechanism, body and mind are in non-action, while spirit and *qi* naturally act. How can one know or perceive the actions of spirit and *qi*? Thus, those who attain the Dao truly attain nothing; if one seeks attainment, this is the "lightly toying" with the life-treasure. The blind excel in hearing, the deaf excel in seeing. Cutting off one source of profit yields tenfold returns; thrice returning day and night yields ten-thousandfold returns.

The blind are keen of ear because their eyes do not leak outward, and their spirit concentrates solely on the ears; the deaf are sharp of sight because their ears do not leak outward, and their spirit gathers entirely in the eyes. From this, we know that the nine orifices of the human body are all places where the Three Treasures exit and enter. Only by rectifying the heart and sincerely focusing the intent, cutting off external distractions, can essence, *qi*, and spirit naturally consolidate. One sees without seeing, hears without hearing—thus the true person dwells deep in the abyss, naturally at peace within.

The original spirit and the conscious spirit are fundamentally the same, differing only in movement and stillness. Cutting off external distractions and guarding the spirit in its abode—this is the original spirit as the "spirit that does not act as spirit." Not acting as spirit outwardly means the spirit acts inwardly.

The resonance of *qi*-mechanisms between heaven and earth is inherently swift. For example, the blind and deaf, though impaired in one sense, are keenly perceptive in another. The eye’s ability to see is the work of spirit; the ear’s ability to hear is the work of *qi*. The blind excel in hearing because the spirit extends to the ears; the deaf excel in sight because essence gathers in the eyes. Extending this principle, if we can let go of everything and not be ensnared by things, then ears and eyes will naturally be sharp, the original spirit and original *qi* will naturally return to the Yellow Court, and the Jade Fluid and Golden Fluid will arise accordingly.

"Cutting off one source of profit" means using a single source—this distinguishes the clarity or turbidity of the water’s origin. *Laozi* says, "The highest virtue is like water." This speaks of its purity. Water benefits all things; the Five Phases do not grow without earth, and the myriad things do not arise without earth. Cultivators must use the True One’s water, the purest water, which is the water’s source, the water-*qi*. When obtained, it is the prenatal *qi*. All tangible water is transformed from this *qi*. Cutting off profit from one source and using the True One’s water yields tenfold returns.

"Master" (*shi*), from the *Yijing*’s *Shi* hexagram, represents "earth over water." Using the True One’s water yields tenfold the effect of water within earth. For water within earth is tangible water, postnatal water; the True One’s water is formless water, prenatal water, whose source is utterly pure. The *Shi* hexagram of earth over water takes earth to restrain water, yet the water remains still and thus clear. However, the True One’s water is the water within *Li*, the true water within fire—thus it is tenfold purer than earth-water.

Cultivators must discern the water’s source and use the purest water for gathering; the slightest impurity renders it unusable. How then can it be made pure? It is like the deaf and blind: cutting off external agitation brings clarity. This is the medicine of Dao cultivation, the metal within water, the Qian-metal. Cutting off the external and seeking clarity from a single source, then applying the work of returning, can transform postnatal Kan and Li into prenatal Qian and Kun. Thus it is said, "Thrice returning day and night yields ten-thousandfold returns."

"Day and night" signifies one yin and one yang, containing Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li within. By inverting yin and yang and returning them, one naturally achieves ten-thousandfold the effect of using the master. Obtaining the True One’s water and performing the seven returns and nine reversions yields the Jade Fluid and Golden Fluid.

The *Shi* hexagram is taken as an image of water restrained by earth—still water, calm and unrippled. Yet though this water is clear, it is not the source. Drawing from the water’s source and returning it makes its efficacy manifest. Why is it called "master"? The master pertains to military affairs; this matter is like warfare. *Laozi* says, "Fine weapons are inauspicious." It also takes the image of warfare: Dao cultivation and warfare share the same principle. The empty must be filled, the full must be emptied; the subtlety of application lies in the mind. One must adjust according to the mechanism to achieve victory.

All elixir methods are called "battles"—the "battle of dragon and tiger," the "great battle"—they are fundamentally "intercourse." "Thrice returning day and night" also contains the meanings of *Zhun* and *Meng*. At dusk, *Zhun* retreats the tally; at dawn, *Meng* advances the fire. On one side, water draws lead; on the other, fire adds mercury. Returning is drawing and adding; morning *Zhun* and evening *Meng* are the balance of water and fire.

First obtaining the True One’s water, then balancing water and fire, and thrice returning day and night—this naturally yields ten-thousandfold the effect of using the master.

The heart is born from things and dies from things; its mechanism lies in the eyes. Heaven shows no favor yet gives rise to great favor—even in the clamor of thunder and fierce winds, all things stir unwittingly.

The heart arises from the myriad things and perishes with them. The essence of the heart is fundamentally empty, without form or appearance; it is only when forms and appearances excite it that it comes into being. The heart knows through seeing things and awakens through hearing affairs; it clings to things and follows them, leaving no emotion concealed—like a mirror that reflects a person when it faces a person and reflects an object when it faces an object, only then does perception arise. A child’s heart is originally without things; all knowledge comes from later habitual contamination—this is the heart arising from things. Not only is the heart born from things, but the world itself is formed by them. From the vastness of mountains, rivers, and the great earth to the smallness of grasses, trees, birds, and beasts—without these things, what would the world be? And the heart would not arise either. Precisely because of these myriad forms and colors, the world takes shape externally, and our hearts are born internally. For example, the existence of a stock exchange gives rise to the heart of buying, selling, and speculation; without such a thing, this heart would not arise. Whether tangible or intangible, the heart’s arising from things and affairs is one and the same.

When a person sleeps, they have no knowledge; only when an external thing disturbs them do they awaken. Our original heart is no different from when we sleep—empty and hollow, fundamentally without a single thing. Only when external circumstances touch the heart does the heart pursue them. Thus, before birth, it is the innate original nature; after birth, it becomes the acquired spirit of consciousness. Since the heart has birth and death, we humans have life and death. As worldly affairs grow daily more complex, and one heart must manage them all, people exhaust their essence and labor their spirits among things, giving rise to birth, aging, sickness, and death. If one could remain without knowledge or perception—seeing without seeing, hearing without hearing, eating without tasting—preserving the empty heart, then not being born means not dying.

Yet people let their hearts be enslaved by things, drowning in them. Even for a single trivial object, they exert their minds to seek its refinement. From youth to adulthood to old age, their craving never ceases, continuing until death. The heart is born from things and pursues them, thereby destroying its own life—this is the way of the world. Thus it is said to "die from things."

As for the phrase "its mechanism lies in the eyes," the eyes are where the spirit-light resides; the spirit dwells in the eyes daily. When things enter the field of vision, the heart immediately knows them—the heart’s movement begins in the eyes. Guanyin Bodhisattva has seventy-two transformations, yet cannot change her eyes. Thus, the ancients said that while the human body is entirely acquired, only the spirit-light in the eyes is innate—this is not without reason. The heart’s movement passes through the three essentials, and among them, the eyes are directly connected to the heart. Most movements of the heart enter through the eyes. For instance, when encountering beauty on the road, the eyes see it and the heart is immediately stirred, with extraordinary speed. If one hears lewd sounds with the ears, the sound must travel from the ears to the heart, and only after turning the thought does the heart move. Because the spirit resides in the eyes, what enters the eyes has already entered the heart.

Thus, when a person is about to die, the spirit in the eyes scatters first; in infancy, the eyes’ light is clear. Knowing that the spirit’s mechanism lies in the eyes, one has grasped the method of refining the self.

Heaven does not speak, yet the four seasons proceed and the hundred things are born. The body of heaven is empty and hollow; gazing at it from the ground, one sees only the azure qi of the sky, not its great height. Yet even when climbing to the summit of Kunlun, one still sees no change in its height. For heaven is emptiness—without seeing, hearing, speaking, or movement—yet cold and heat, yin and yang come and go in perfect measure, never losing their order through the ages. Though it seems to show no favor to things, within it lies great favor. Precisely because it is fundamentally empty, it can shelter the cosmos; precisely because it shows no favor, it can nurture the ten thousand kinds.

If the human heart could be as empty as heaven, then the creative transformations of heaven and earth would all be within our hearts. Our hearts would be equal in measure to heaven and earth, and we would stand alongside them. The boundless void has the power to cover and support heaven and earth, and our hearts share the same substance as the great emptiness. Only by being completely empty can one possess all abilities, nurture all things, and encompass all—possessing countless transformations, responding to all stimuli.

Heaven’s creative transformations also do not go beyond the four words "responding to stimuli and achieving communion." For example, in times of flood or drought, if people possess sincere hearts, their sincerity can move heaven, and heaven will respond—as in the prayers for rain at Sanglin or the burial of a child to obtain gold, both clear proofs. Precisely because heaven shows no favor, great favor arises. Thus, even the unwitting clamor of thunder and fierce winds manifests its efficacy within the emptiness, revealing its image.

When winter solstice arrives, "Earth Thunder Reverts"—a single yang arises from yin, thunder stirs beneath the earth, opening the creative transformations of spring. When summer solstice arrives, "Heavenly Wind Meets"—a single yin descends from yang, wind arises across the land, forming the scenery of autumn. Heaven’s life and death lie in the two seasons of spring and autumn. Thunder sounds in the second month and falls silent in the ninth; thunder is the qi of yang, the qi of life. When autumn arrives, the metal wind rises—the metal wind is the qi of killing. Though wind and thunder are unwitting and without knowledge, one hears their sounds but does not see their forms; in truth, they have no sound, but are stirred by things to resound. Yet wind and thunder are the transformations of yin and yang. The accumulation of yang becomes swift thunder; the accumulation of yin becomes fierce wind. Wind and thunder carry out the mechanism of life and death, while swift thunder and fierce winds are the times when life and death reverse.

One constant, one change—how could Heaven possess intent to bring them forth? It merely responds to stimuli, moving unwittingly. In the *Book of Changes*, thunder and wind are the eldest son and eldest daughter, one yin, one yang, succeeding the father and mother of Qian and Kun to govern. Thus, Heaven and Earth’s life and death are revealed only in thunder and wind, and their mechanism of reversal lies herein. Herein too lies the absence of favor that gives rise to great favor. The sage, participating in Heaven and Earth’s transformative nurturance, must seek that which is called "without favor" in order to extend favor to the myriad things. Without favor is without intent; without the intent to transform and nurture the myriad things, the myriad things naturally transform and nurture themselves. Supreme joy leaves a surplus of nature; supreme stillness yields the integrity of nature (alternatively: *then* integrity). Heaven’s utmost partiality is employed with utmost impartiality.

This passage extends the principle of "without favor," which is identical to "non-action." When the heart reaches supreme joy, celestial nature drifts freely and at ease, as if with leisure to spare. When the heart reaches supreme stillness, celestial nature arises spontaneously, as if treading a broad and smooth path. When we refine the self to the state of primordial chaos, the heart suddenly becomes luminous—this is "supreme stillness yielding the integrity of nature." Shortly after, the heart feels unbounded delight, indescribable—this is "supreme joy leaving a surplus of nature." Supreme joy is true joy.

"Heaven’s utmost partiality is employed with utmost impartiality" means that when comparing human nature to celestial nature—though it is private to the individual, as it is received from Heaven—every person is thus, regardless of wisdom, folly, worthiness, or unworthiness, all the same. If what is most private is in fact most impartial, nature equals the vast emptiness of Heaven, utterly impartial and without favor. Thus, the nature bestowed by Heaven has no distinction of good or not-good. When Heaven occasionally sends forth wind and thunder, it may seem highly partial, yet it acts in accordance with the seasons, unchanged across ten thousand ages—this is an example of Heaven’s utmost partiality employed with utmost impartiality.

The restraint of the *qin* lies in qi. Life is the root of death; death is the root of life. Favor arises from harm; harm arises from favor. Sinking into water or entering fire is self-inflicted destruction.

(*Qin* is interchangeable with *qin* [to capture]; the ancient characters are identical. "The restraint of the *qin* lies in qi" means that when qi races outward, to withdraw the heart, one must apply the effort of restraint, as if capturing it from without. By capturing it from without and returning the heart, one receives the seasonal imagery of Heaven, and joy’s surplus and stillness’s integrity arise. This must be achieved by restraining with qi, so the heart remains unmoved and returns to its original substance—this is the meaning of the first chapter of *Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir*: "True earth captures true lead; true lead restrains true mercury. Lead and mercury return to true earth, and body and heart become still and unmoving.")

True earth can capture true lead; true lead is primordial true yang, and true mercury is true yin. When both return to earth, body and heart become still and unmoving. True earth is true intent, the pure heart unstained by the six dusts. This matter first requires refining the self, as in *Journey to the West* when Bull Demon King returns to the truth—this is the moment. For the primordial Great Dao lies entirely in true intent, which must be restrained by primordial qi. As for the acquired spirit of consciousness, which constantly races outward, this is the *Li* trigram, which must be captured and subdued by primordial qi. It is like taming a dragon: primordial qi is metal within water, which is true lead. If one can subdue it, the *Li* trigram transforms into the *Qian* trigram—this is "taking from Kan to fill Li," the work of returning from the acquired to the primordial.

"Death is the root of life; life is the root of death" refers to the mechanism of life and death, mutually concealed within each other. Zi, Wu, Mao, and You mutually overcome and mutually gestate; thus, death is the root of life, and life is the root of death. Favor and harm are likewise akin to life and death, extending to life. If one can sink water into fire, fire is restrained by water and naturally perishes. When Kan water is used to fill Li fire, the spirit of consciousness is extinguished, and the primordial spirit is born. These lines all refer to the spirit of consciousness and the primordial spirit: returning from the spirit of consciousness to the primordial spirit is "favor arising from harm"; extinguishing the primordial spirit with the spirit of consciousness is "harm arising from favor." Self-inflicted destruction means the human heart perishes, and the Dao heart arises, the primordial spirit appears. At this point, the work of cultivating the Dao is complete, hence it is said that cultivating the Dao lies solely in the single step of "refining the self."

The foolish revere Heaven and Earth’s patterns as sagely; I revere the patterns of the seasons and things as wise. People revere foolish vigilance as sagely; I revere the absence of foolish vigilance as sagely.

The foolish revere peculiarity as sageliness; I revere the absence of peculiarity as sageliness. (One version omits the following four lines.) The foolish regard knowledge of astronomy and geography as sageliness; I, however, do not, but instead take the patterns of the seasons and things as essential, knowing the qi-mechanisms of Heaven and Earth’s creation and transformation, as well as the inherent principles of the myriad things. Possessing these two, I deem them wisdom.

People revere foolish vigilance as sageliness, engaging in reasoned discourse and broad debate of past and present; I, knowing nothing of any particular thing, do not regard this as sageliness. People revere extraordinary deeds that astonish others as sageliness; I revere the ordinary and unassuming as sageliness. For astonishing peculiarity is not the Way of the Mean. The Dao lies in harmonizing one’s light with the dust, and only those who know the Way of daily use are sages.

The cycles of Heaven and Earth’s creation and transformation are fixed. Their pivot lies in the qi-mechanism. Scholars, by returning from breadth to simplicity, can grasp this pivot and reduce the myriad differences to a single root; thus, even a thousand or ten thousand generations hence, all may be known. Those who cultivate in accordance with this are the sages who continue the past and inaugurate the future.

The Way of Nature is quiescent, hence Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are born. The Way of Heaven and Earth is gradual, hence yin and yang prevail. Yin and yang interact and transform, and change proceeds in accord. As *Laozi* says: “Humans model themselves on Earth, Earth on Heaven, Heaven on the Dao, and the Dao on Nature.” The Way of Nature lies in quiescence; only in quiescence and non-movement do Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things arise. This is the Way of Nature, which is also the Supreme Ultimate. Empty and still, without movement, hence Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are born from it.

The Way of Heaven and Earth is gradual, meaning it follows Nature’s gradual progression. Heaven and Earth are empty and without intent; when yang ascends, it gradually rises; when yin descends, it gradually falls. Thus, there are times when yin or yang prevails, but the Way of the Supreme Ultimate’s Nature remains impartial and unbiased. Gradualness entails partiality: when yin encroaches on yang, yin prevails; when yang encroaches on yin, yang prevails. Yet this is the principle of mutual interaction and cyclical alternation. Viewed in its entirety, it remains impartial and unbiased. However, humans are thus subject to birth, aging, illness, and death.

“Yin and yang interact”: when yang prevails through interaction, it is spring and summer; when yin prevails through interaction, it is autumn and winter. “Change proceeds in accord” means proceeding in accord with the heart-mind of Nature’s Way. Thus, the sage knows Nature’s Way cannot be violated and therefore regulates it (one version reads: “Thus, the sage knows Nature cannot be acted upon and therefore guards and regulates it with the Dao”).

The Way of utmost quiescence cannot be matched by calendrical calculations. Yet there exists a marvelous mechanism from which the myriad images arise. The Eight Trigrams and the sexagenary cycle conceal divine and subtle workings; the arts of yin and yang’s mutual overcoming are manifestly evident in their images. Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are born from Nature’s Way. Though there are times when yin overcomes yang, all proceed in accord with Nature. The sage knows this Way cannot be violated. If one follows this Way, one naturally becomes an immortal or a sage.

Following Nature’s unchanging Way, one establishes methods of cultivation, sets fixed standards, and formulates oral instructions—all of which are definite and unalterable. Simply embodying emptiness and naturalness, one may become an immortal or a buddha. Nature’s Way arises from the Way of utmost quiescence; through quiescence, creation and transformation naturally arise.

The “Way of utmost quiescence” cannot be matched by calendrical calculations. Within this quiescence lies Heaven’s creation and transformation. By attaining quiescence for even a moment, one may accumulate a year’s qi. Yang’s arising is the winter solstice; yin’s descent is the summer solstice—these are the winter and summer of practice, not the winter and summer of the seasons. By attaining extreme emptiness and profound quiescence, one may grasp creation and transformation in the realm of the indistinct.

There exists a marvelous mechanism from which the myriad images arise; the Eight Trigrams and the sexagenary cycle all emerge from it, which is the Supreme Ultimate: in the world, it is the Supreme Ultimate that generates Heaven, Earth, humans, and things; in the human body, it is the Supreme Ultimate of spirit and qi’s fusion. The arts of yin and yang’s mutual overcoming are manifestly evident to all, not hidden or obscure. Though their principles are not easily understood, their images are readily apparent. This is the Mysterious Pass, the Supreme Ultimate within the human body, which, like the Supreme Ultimate of Heaven and Earth, contains the Eight Trigrams and the sexagenary cycle, concealing divine and subtle workings that are difficult to fathom. Yet their images are outwardly manifest and knowable. By investigating these images to plumb their principles, the source of yin and yang’s creation and transformation becomes as clear as the palm of one’s hand.

**Lecture Thirteen: Master Shi’s Heart-Transmission and the Moon’s Fullness in Oral Instructions**

*East at mao, west at you, south at wu, north at zi.*

The alchemical classics often contain two phrases: “The *zi* of time lies in heart-transmission; the fullness of the moon depends on oral instructions.” These words are of utmost importance in the elixir method. Here, I will explain them in detail as follows:

The Perfected One Bai Ziqing’s *Disquisition on Resolving Doubts* states: “In the superior elixir method, spirit, essence, hun-soul, po-soul, and intent serve as medicinal ingredients; movement, stillness, sitting, and reclining constitute the firing times; and attuning to natural spontaneity is the method of operation.” The firing times are the work itself, and operation is the application. For the primordial Great Dao, the first requirement is to release body and mind, allowing them to follow their natural course—no trace of force may be applied. Any coercion will prevent the elixir from forming. The primordial is the Void; only when one accords with the Void does one attain the primordial. Empty the mind within and the body without; only when both inner and outer are empty can one engage in the practice of emptiness. Nature belongs to the mind, life to the body; when both body and mind are forgotten, nature and life unite, giving rise to creation and transformation. If one forgets the body but not the mind, or forgets the mind but not the body, neither is the primordial. Emptiness is distinguished between true emptiness and dull emptiness. True emptiness is not empty—within it lies wondrous existence—this alone is true emptiness. The mind contains no notion of emptiness; in perfect stillness, this may be verified. For instance, upon just awakening from sleep, all thoughts are empty, and the mind is devoid of any object—this is true emptiness. If one deliberately maintains emptiness in the mind, one cannot attain emptiness; this is dull emptiness. The myriad volumes of elixir classics all teach the cultivation of an empty mind, yet emptying the mind is the most difficult of all. If one forcibly restrains the mind to make it empty, the more one restrains, the less empty it becomes. One must employ the method of naturalness: when a thought arises, immediately become aware of it; with awareness, it vanishes—this is self-emptying. When a thought arises, become aware of it. Awareness can halt thoughts, hence it is said, “It rests in supreme goodness.” Awareness is cessation; supreme goodness is a single awakening. If one recognizes the movement of thought yet adds another thought to suppress it, one cannot attain emptiness, for there already exists a thought of restraint within. The method of returning to the Void is the refinement of thoughts; when thoughts are eliminated, one can return to the Void. Forgetting both externally and internally, one forms a void body, which can then generate existence from non-existence and engender yang from extreme stillness. Once yang arises, the mind must be able to gather and contain it to retreat yin; if it cannot be gathered, yang will not reach the body. Once gathered, one must further retain it; if it cannot be retained, it will vanish in an instant—hence the importance of firing times. Once gathered and sealed, with daily accumulation over months, yang naturally becomes full, and yin naturally retreats, enabling one to revert from old age to youth. What was previously deficient is replenished by newly acquired yang, not only restoring what was lost but also accumulating new yang—thus one can revert and return. When the body returns to the time of five thousand four hundred births of the Yellow Path, it becomes vast and expansive, all things flourish and prosper, teeth regrow and hair turns black, old women revert to maidens, and the decrepit transform into robust men—this is certain. The method begins with reverting to pure *Kun* and forming a void body, yet attaining the void state is not easy, which is why both nature and life must be cultivated together. Forget the mind, and the mind becomes empty and still; forget the body, and the body becomes empty and still. An empty mind is the inner *Kun* hexagram; an empty body is the outer *Kun* hexagram. The union of inner and outer *Kun* is the union of inner and outer emptiness. When the two *Kun* combine to form the *Kun* hexagram, this is the return to the Void, and only then can yang be generated. Yang arises from the outer *Kun* hexagram, yet the inner *Kun* truly responds to it. By gathering it according to the method and retaining it in the body, one can retreat yin and advance yang—at this point, a distinct vision unfolds.

What is meant by the “living *zi* hour”? The *zi* hour is midnight, the time when a day begins at *zi* and ends at *hai*. The *hai* hour is the time of extreme yin. At the *zi* hour, yang begins to revive, though humans remain unaware—only the rooster senses it first and crows upon awakening. The rooster is sensitive to qi; when yang qi arises from the earth, it stimulates the rooster’s body. Receiving this qi, the rooster is startled awake and crows. Yang qi arises from the earth each day without fail, so the rooster’s crowing also remains unchanged. The living *zi* hour is the time of yang’s birth. In the southern provinces, roosters crow at *chou*, while in the northern provinces, they crow at *zi*, demonstrating that yang qi’s generation moves from north to south, with the *Kan* palace receiving qi first. The *zi* hour also derives from the Winter Solstice. As Shao Yong said: “At the midpoint of *zi* in the Winter Solstice, the heart of Heaven remains unchanged. Where the first yang stirs, all things are yet unborn.” The midpoint of *zi* is the midpoint of the *zi* month—this is the time when the heart of Heaven manifests, occurring every year without fail across ten thousand kalpas. Though yang stirs, all things remain unborn, hence the comparison to *zi*. In terms of creation, by the tenth month, all is stripped away. At the transition between *hai* and *zi*, the *Kun* hexagram is pure. Thus, in winter, all things withdraw and conceal themselves, presenting the image of pure yin. At the Winter Solstice, yang returns to revive, forming the *Fu* hexagram. *Fu* signifies the return of the creative transformation of spring. The first half of the month before the Winter Solstice is the time of pure yin, the image of pure *Kun*, derived from the *Bo* hexagram. The *Bo* hexagram pertains to the latter half of the ninth month and the first half of the tenth month, stripping away all yang qi, treading frost and solid ice. While yang qi is not yet exhausted, insects and plants can still barely endure, so in early tenth month, leaves have not all fallen, and insects still make sounds. By late tenth month and early winter, when yang is exhausted, insects and snakes all become torpid. Without yang qi, they cannot move; when yang is exhausted, they hibernate and remain still. When frost and ice strip everything away, the stripping is complete—this is pure *Kun*. At the Winter Solstice, within pure *Kun*, yang is born anew.

The *Fu* hexagram arises from below as renewal, while the *Bo* hexagram strips away from above. When stripping is complete, yang qi retreats and conceals itself below, which is why in the coldest depths of winter, digging six or seven *chi* beneath the earth reveals no frost, and deeper still, warmth is found—this is the yang qi. Heaven’s qi ascends, earth’s qi descends, sealing all in the bitterness of winter. By spring, yang is restored once more.

I once heard Master Chen of geomancy say that in winter, the depth of frozen earth can be measured in *chi*, and when this number is divided by six, it invariably yields a whole number. He tested this by digging in the five northern provinces, and it held true without exception. This demonstrates the subtlety of the *Kun* hexagram’s use of six—a remark grounded in experience, and perhaps not without merit.

In winter, yang qi hides profoundly deep. Grasses and trees wither but do not die, for their roots lie buried in the earth, sustained by yang qi. Insects and snakes exposed to the cold perish, but those hibernating in deep valleys and earthen dens are preserved by yang qi. Thus, in great mountains and marshes, creatures burrow downward to hibernate, consuming their own marrow. Without the yang beneath the earth, they could not survive. As the *Yi* says, “The hidden dragon does not act”—yang lies below.

At the Winter Solstice, the first yang returns, forming the *Earth-Thunder Fu* hexagram. From this point, yang gradually revives. The *Yi* speaks of “renewal revealing the heart of heaven and earth,” for here we see the great cyclic heart of creation. The celestial heart turns ceaselessly in its round; the true yang of heaven and earth appears at the Winter Solstice. Though humans may not perceive it, the qi of heaven and earth has already transformed. At this time, yang grows and yin wanes. The essence of the great yang’s qi lengthens the days. Conversely, when yin waxes and yang wanes, the days shorten—this is the Summer Solstice.

Before the Winter Solstice, the days have reached their shortest extreme. Thus, the first yang returns, born within the earth, gradually reviving and growing. By the time of the second yang, the *Lin* hexagram, this can be observed. Tree branches contain sap: in the eleventh month, it is scarce, but by the twelfth month, it increases. The mulberry tree, which receives qi earliest, sprouts leaves even in winter. In the tenth month, plum blossoms first open on southern ridges, where the earth is warm and yang arrives early.

Though the first yang has returned, the myriad things have not yet come to life. They await the third yang, which arrives in the first month. The *huozi* hour emerges from the Winter Solstice. Considering a single day, day is yang and night is yin. At midnight, the *zi* hour, yang is born; by the *xu* and *hai* hours, yin reaches its extreme. The *zi* hour is the moment when yang first stirs. In alchemical practice, this is the *huozi* hour, the “living *zi*”; midnight’s *zi* hour is the “dead *zi*.” The yang generated within the body is not fixed but alive, arising whenever stillness is attained—hence the term “living.” Even if one cultivates at noon, yang can be generated, making it a *huozi*. For this *zi* hour is the moment when both body and mind are still, and yang is born—not a fixed *zi* hour, but a “living *zi* hour.”

The *Four Hundred Characters* states: “The Summer Solstice is not at *wu*; the Winter Solstice is not at *zi*.” This is precisely the point. The moment when yang is born is likened to the *huozi* hour, for at this time, yang has just begun to stir, and the myriad things have not yet come to life. This is crucial for the elixir’s formation. Beyond the *huozi* hour, there is also the “true *zi* hour”: the *huozi* hour marks the return of yang qi, while the true *zi* hour is when yang, having been born, reaches the body.

At first, its presence is faint—like a single point. It must be nurtured to fullness so that it may grow and be felt throughout the body. If yang is too tender and has not yet reached the body, it cannot be gathered. Nurturing it to fullness is the work of fire timing, requiring the steadfastness of *ding* to cultivate it.

When one first practices and both body and mind attain stillness, yang is born—this is the *Earth-Thunder Fu* hexagram. If it has not yet reached the body, one must remain in *Kun*, for *Kun* is what gives birth to yang. Should there be the slightest deviation from pure *Kun*, yang will cease to arise. Yang is born at specific times, and those who know its root can perceive it. If, upon seeing the signs, one’s thoughts stir and one speculates about the nature of yang qi, yang will not return. For the qi initially obtained is subtle, and one must not move in the slightest. It must be guarded and further nurtured, which is why the work of *ding* is required.

*Ding* means fixing upon one. The spirit within the heart is fixed upon the inner and outer emptiness. When the spirit is fixed externally, completely detached from the body, the body becomes even more empty, and yang qi can then be fully generated. When yang qi is present, signs appear in the body—one feels a pervasive comfort and softness, the moment when yang has grown to fullness. The heart is filled with joy.

Once yang has reached fullness, one must employ the work of *wang* (forgetting). Even the spirit fixed externally must be forgotten. *Wang* is the ungathered gathering—the work of harvesting. *Ding* is the work of nurturing. At this point, the skin and flesh of the entire body may twitch, as if fleas were crawling upon it. One must still forget. Yet when the body is suffused with a melting numbness and forgetting is no longer possible, one must cease the fire, either rising to walk slowly or casually reading a Daoist text. This is the time to cease the fire and seal the elixir. If one does not cease the fire and seal it, the yang qi, having reached its fullest, will generate heat and transform into sweat, dispersing as postnatal qi. Thus, the work of *zhi* (cessation) is most essential and must not be neglected.

There is also a “living *wu* hour.” The *wu* hour corresponds to the Summer Solstice; the living *wu* hour is when the first yin is born. In theory, *wu* is when yin arises. The living *wu* hour pertains to the heart, and the movement from body to heart is the *Li* hexagram.

Within *Li* lies emptiness; when stirred, the *Li* hexagram marks the moment when the first yin is born. When yang arises, it manifests as a vision—first appearing beyond the skin and flesh, then moving inward toward the heart, where awareness awakens. In the state of emptiness, all is profound obscurity; when yang stirs, it becomes a fleeting glimmer. The moment it surges into the heart, one feels the body’s vision as a melting numbness, like the rising of yang—this is the living *wu* hour. *Zi* and *wu* stand opposite; when awareness reaches the heart, it is the living *wu* hour, requiring the practice of *ding* (stabilization).

*Zi* and *wu* differ by six hours, yet in truth they occur in an instant—from *zi* one arrives at *wu*. *Zi* and *wu* are the north and south poles; the moment one is reached, the other is known. The body is the living *zi* hour, the heart the living *wu* hour; to awaken at *zi* and perceive at *wu*—this is their distinction. The living *wu* hour corresponds to the Summer Solstice, represented by the *Tianfeng Gou* hexagram, for six yang lines form *Qian*, occurring in the fourth month. When *Qian* reaches its extreme, yang culminates and yin is born—this is the fifth month, culminating at the Summer Solstice when the first yin arises, descending from heaven as the *Gou* hexagram. How is this known? When yang reaches its peak and becomes excessive, the first yin arrives as *Gou*, and great rains fall in season. Yin and yang unite to form rain and dew; the days are at their longest, and from the Summer Solstice onward, they gradually shorten. By the sixth month, two yin lines form the *Dun* hexagram; by the seventh month, three yin lines form the *Pi* hexagram.

In the third month, the *Zetian Guai* hexagram—five yang and one yin—heralds the blooming of myriad flowers. The fifth day of the fifth month, when the sun shines at its zenith, marks the extreme of yang—hence it is called the "Heavenly Midpoint Festival." Yang at its peak gives birth to yin; thus the ceaseless cycle of heaven and earth’s transformations is revealed. Within the living *zi* and *wu* hours, there are also the *mao* and *you* hours. *Zi*, *chou*, *yin*, *mao*, *chen*, and *si* are the six yang hours; *wu*, *wei*, *shen*, *you*, *xu*, and *hai* are the six yin hours. Since *zi* and *wu* exist, *mao* and *you* lie between them. *Zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you* are the four cardinal directions, occupying the north, south, east, and west—all others are the four corners. Various texts describe these eight directions, adding the central palace to form the nine palaces.

The four cardinals in the celestial heart are the *Tiangang*; the Dipper’s bowl resides at the center as the *Beichen*, also called the *Purple Tenuity*. What encircles and moves is the Dipper’s handle within the North Pole. As the handle shifts, the year’s seasons are divided. The twelve months differ according to the handle’s direction, while the Dipper’s bowl remains unmoving at the center. *Zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you* may also be distinguished thus. The midpoint of the Dipper’s handle is the fixed pivot star—like the body and heart. When body and heart are still, true intention resides at the center, and spirit and qi naturally unite, dividing into *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*.

Yang’s arising is *zi* and *wu*; bathing is *mao* and *you*. *Zi* and *wu* stand opposite as north and south; *mao* and *you* stand opposite as east and west. To mention *wu* is to encompass *mao*; to mention *zi* is to encompass *you*. When yang arrives and is perceived, it is *zi* and *wu*, with *mao* and *you* contained within. As the *Canon* states: “At *zi*, turn left; at *wu*, rotate west.” (Originally: “At *zi*, turn right; at *wu*, rotate east.”) *Zi* and *wu* descend simultaneously; when *zi*’s yang qi reaches *mao*, *wu*’s yin qi reaches *you*. This is illustrated in the diagram. *Mao* and *you* occur together—from *zi* to *wu* conceals *mao*; from *wu* to *zi* conceals *you*.

Heaven has four images: heaven and earth, sun and moon—*Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*—heaven to the south, earth to the north, sun to the east, moon to the west. The elixir method has *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*: *wu* to the south, *zi* to the north, *mao* to the east, *you* to the west—all are but symbols. If distinguished, they become: *zi* moves, *wu* awakens; *mao* rests, *you* ceases. *Zi* to the north, *wu* to the south; *mao* and *you* to the east and west. At *zi*, yang ascends; at *wu*, yin descends. When *mao* and *you* arrive, yin and yang balance—this is the time of bathing. Bathing occurs at the central palace, like the fixed pivot star within the Dipper’s bowl. Here is emptiness: yang ascends from below, yin descends from above. Employing the practice of *wang* (forgetting), one naturally seals the yang qi within, obtaining the elixir.

Such are the teachings of the elixir method—all are but analogies and must not be rigidly adhered to. As the *Four Hundred Words* states: “When it is time for bathing, *mao* and *you* are also empty comparisons.” This is evident. During the great celestial cycle, the movement of yang qi has tangible signs; during the small celestial cycle, the movement of yang qi is still subtle, and the body’s yin qi remains heavy, so one cannot fully perceive it. In the great cycle, the dharmakāya is present, and one knows its ascent and descent; in the small cycle, one only feels a melting numbness.

To forget body and heart is the meaning of *wenyang* (warm nurturing). When one wishes to forget but cannot, yang is full—one must immediately cease the fire. This is the distinction of *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*. The above explains the principle of “the *zi* of time, whose subtlety lies in heart transmission.” Next follows the principle of “the fullness of the moon, preserved in oral instructions.”

Elixir masters take *Qian* and *Kun* as heaven and earth, *Kan* and *Li* as sun and moon, and employ the functions of *Kan* and *Li*. Within the sun is the golden crow; within the moon is the jade hare—thus the crow and hare are taken as the medicinal substances. The principle of the moon’s fullness is the celestial pattern left by heaven and earth for refining the elixir. The sun’s light is eternally whole, unchanging through the ages; the moon waxes and wanes, differing each day, each day presenting a new image. The moon has no light of its own—it is the essence of *Taiyin*, represented by the *Kan* hexagram, internally yang and externally yin.

The outer yin is thus without light, borrowing the sun’s light to shine. Heaven and earth intersect at the summer and winter solstices, while the sun and moon meet once each month. On the day of the moon’s waning, the sun is above in the heavens, the moon below on earth, and they intersect within the earth. Sun and moon face each other, and the moon absorbs the sun’s light. The moon is a body of pure yin, hollow and empty, and by its emptiness can receive—what goes out returns as what enters—thus it absorbs the sun’s light. After three days, it appears in the *geng* direction, gradually releasing its light, its form like a moth’s eyebrow. The *geng* direction is the east; *geng* belongs to *Zhen*, and *Zhen* receives *geng*, hence it is called the *geng* direction. At this time, only a crescent of new moon appears. Three days prior, on the twenty-eighth day, the moon is but a sliver, nearly waning to extinction. The moon first appearing in *geng* is no different in size from the sliver about to vanish.

From the fourth day onward, each day adds a thread of light, no different from the accumulation of yang in elixir refinement, built up bit by bit. By the eighth day, the moon forms a half-circle, appearing in the *dui-ding* direction—the *dui* direction is due west—called the “upper quarter.” It resembles a bow, one side the bow’s back, the other the bowstring. The bowstring faces upward toward the east, hence it is called the “upper quarter,” its edge as straight as a cord. Yin and yang are each half, meaning within the yin there is half yang. If considered as a full circle, half is bright while the other half is hidden; even if unseen, it is because half remains—this is the moon of the eighth day. Thereafter, each day adds another thread of light, until the fifteenth day’s full moon, which appears in the *qian-jia* direction. Moving from west to east, it faces the sun and its light is full.

A song says:
*Toad and hare’s spirit, sun and moon’s qi shine as one.*
*The toad watches the hexagram’s node, the hare spits to give birth to light.*
*The Dao of elixir refinement is complete—bending and stretching, low and high.*

Toad and hare’s spirit, sun and moon’s dual brightness: the toad symbolizes *Taiyin*, crow and hare symbolize *Taiyang*. The toad is the essence of yang, a creature born in water, lifting its head to the heavens to absorb the sun’s qi. The hare, seeing the moon, exhales qi; it has no gender, facing the full moon on the fifteenth day to exhale and conceive, giving birth from its mouth. One inhale, one exhale—symbolizing one day and one month. The *Huangting Jing* says: *“Exhale with the sun, inhale with the moon.”* One inhale and yin descends; one exhale and yang ascends, comparing the ascent and descent of yin and yang. The toad’s inhalation draws inward to gather; the hare’s exhalation turns outward to return. Exhaling outward is likened to the moon: by the fifteenth day, it has exhaled all its yang light.

On the day of the new moon, when sun and moon intersect, a dark shadow falls upon the moon. Thereafter, yang light gradually accumulates and increases, until the fifteenth day when it is full—the moon’s light is the sun’s light. On the fifteenth, the moon is full, and the sun is also full. The dual mention of sun and moon compares essence and life, spirit and qi. By the sixteenth, the moon begins to wane, and yin grows. From the full moon of the fifteenth, yang recedes and yin advances; the abundant *Qian* metal gradually diminishes. By the twenty-third day, it appears in the south, in the *xun-xin* direction, only half remaining, black and white each half—this is the “lower quarter” moon. The bowstring faces downward, within yang there is half yin. From below to above, it resembles the upper quarter, where yang arises from below, and within yang half of yin has already been born.

Thereafter, each day darkness advances and light retreats, until the twenty-eighth day when the moon is but a sliver, and by the new moon it is entirely consumed. This occurs every month without the slightest deviation, and from the moon one can know the waxing and waning of each month. China’s lunar calendar derives from the moon, and by observing this one understands the reasons for each month’s new moon, crescent, quarter, and full moon. The elixir method takes its pattern from the moon: the time when yang qi is fully born is the time of the full moon. The *Wuzhen Pian* says: *“On the fifteenth of the eighth month, admire the toad’s radiance—precisely when metal’s essence is strong and abundant.”* At this time, water is full and metal is replete—the image of the *Qian* hexagram. The full moon is the *wang* day; the pure, hollow body of *Kun* is the new moon. The intersection of sun and moon is the union of spirit and qi, entering the primordial chaos to give birth to subtle yang—like the moon of the third day. The yang born at the living *zi* hour is also like a crescent of new moon. Yin and yang each half resemble the upper and lower quarters of the moon.

The upper quarter moon’s lower half is half yang, the upper half half yin. When yang qi accumulates to half in the body, the body’s yin qi retreats by half—the receded yin is the dark upper half. Yin and yang each form a half-circle, and in the elixir method, the so-called “full moon” is the round moon formed by half yin and half yang—this is the “full moon,” not the usual full body of pure yang, distinct from the full moon of the *wang* day. Now assume the half of yang qi born is the upper quarter, and the half of yin qi receded is the lower quarter; upper and lower combine to form the full moon. The upper quarter is in the body, the lower quarter in the heart; body and heart unite to become the full moon. At this time, metal and water are each half, the hour meets *er ba* (sixteen), the medicinal sprout is newly tender—precisely the moment for harvesting. Proceeding from “stabilization,” one turns to the work of “forgetting.” The *zi* hour turns east to *mao* to form the upper quarter—this is the advance of yang.

The *wu* hour turns west to *you* to form the lower quarter—this is the retreat of yin. When *mao* and *you* unite simultaneously, they form the full moon, precisely the moment for harvesting and completing the elixir; the method here should employ "bathing" and "warm-nurturing." For this is the time when yin and yang are balanced, mercury and lead each at half; metal and wood intersect, bearing both punishment and virtue. Moreover, the upper quarter is the qi of the Green Dragon’s arc, the lower quarter the qi of the White Tiger’s arc; when upper and lower combine, the medicinal sprout is neither overripe nor too tender—just right for harvesting. As the *Wuzhen Pian* says, “On the fifteenth of the eighth month, the metal essence is at its peak”—this is exactly it.

What is called “harvesting” is the harvest of non-harvesting, the gathering of non-gathering, focusing solely on the work of “bathing,” with a single-minded naturalness. Thereby, spirit and qi unite of themselves, metal and wood merge, and the elixir is obtained. This is what cultivators of the Dao must pay closest attention to.

Below, I set forth its meaning in a diagram:
