The martial fire of the water clock is the work of regulating breath and restraining thoughts. Fearing that yang’s rise may attach to the forms of body and mind, one listens to the breath with the ear and silently counts, binding body and mind to prevent dissipation. This is the same as the breath regulation at the outset, as previously explained.

The Buddhist “Six-Syllable True Words” and the Daoist “Six-Syllable Formula” share this intent. The Daoist six syllables for breath regulation are: *chui*, *he*, *xu*, *xi*, *hu*, *xi*. *Chui* treats kidney meridian illnesses; *he* treats heart meridian illnesses; *xu* treats liver meridian illnesses; *xi* treats lung meridian illnesses; *hu* treats spleen meridian illnesses; *xi* treats triple burner illnesses. Each syllable directs *qi* to its respective meridian, allowing *qi* to reach the organs, balancing it and curing disease. The Buddhist six syllables are the same; both Daoist and Buddhist six-syllable formulas can treat illness.

The *Damo Chan Jing* lists four phases: panting, blowing, *qi*, and breathing. In regulating breath, one must pass through these four states. Panting is uneven exhalation; blowing is forceful breath, coarse *qi*—like snoring in sleep. With prolonged practice, one can regulate the *qi*. *Qi* refers to the harmonized postnatal breath; when true breathing is achieved and abides below the navel, this is called obtaining true breathing.

There are also distinctions between sharp and dull roots. Sharp roots refer to those with deep foundations and past wisdom; dull roots are the opposite. During practice, there are phenomena and images. Dull roots do not perceive forms until reaching true form; sharp roots see many forms, but these are illusory. The first phenomenon one should perceive in practice is the primordial chaos of the Supreme Ultimate and the Two Modes. It is present at all times—when spirit and *qi* unite, it appears. Sharp roots perceive it often; dull roots see it rarely, or not at all.

Bodily sensations are phenomena; what appears before the eyes are images. Alchemical texts say, “Each step has its phenomena, each step has its images.” Most alchemical texts rely on images for explanation, making them difficult to understand.

The *Heart-Transmitted Rhymed Verses* speak of the primordial Great Dao, recording only the words of verified cultivation—never discussing dharmic phenomena. Yet in its dialogues, it often touches upon such phenomena, where the terms "that" (*bi*) and "self" (*wo*) most frequently lead to error. "That" is spoken in relation to others, and many mistakenly take it to mean a woman—how could they know that "that" is no person at all, but rather refers to empty space? Since there is no way to describe the forms one perceives, the word "that" is used as a substitute. For example, when awareness arises and the perceiver sees before them a "self," it is called "that"—the one who perceives is "that," not the "self."

The phrases *"An echo resounds in the empty valley; a shadow appears when the pole is raised"* are often used in alchemical texts. Where there is an aperture, sound arises—emptiness gives birth to the music of heaven. Thus, every empty valley can transmit sound. The sword is wisdom-light; with this wisdom-sword, one slays all manner of demons and ghosts. As the *Awakening to Reality* says, *"A flash of lightning slays demons a thousand miles away"*—this is precisely it. Nine is yang; wisdom-light is the light within the formless. The stringless lute means it speaks without being played.

**Lecture Sixteen: The True Water and True Fire’s Sevenfold Internal Union**

**The External Union of Metal and Wood, Zi and Wu: The Ninefold Return**

The Ninefold Return and Sevenfold Reversal of the Minor Celestial Cycle have already been explained: internally, Kan and Li unite in the north and south; externally, Qian and Kun unite at Zi and Wu. The union of Kan and Li in the north and south occurs as the internal exchange between heart and kidneys; the union of Qian and Kun at Zi and Wu occurs as the external exchange between spirit and qi. When true yang stirs within the body and the external kidneys rise, this is the *living Zi hour*; when the true fire of the Gate of Life surges upward to the crown and is perceived, this is the *living Wu hour*. At this moment, both internal and external unions have already taken place. True yang emerges from the void, and the void lies beyond the body—hence it is called "external."

The union of Qian and Kun at Zi and Wu constitutes the Ninefold Return, while the union of Kan and Li in the north and south constitutes the Sevenfold Reversal. In truth, the union of Qian and Kun at Zi and Wu is also the union of Mao and You—this is the conjunction of Metal and Wood. The Mysterious Aperture is the gateway of Mao; Mao is the true void within me, the lesser void. You is the greater void, indescribable in form, thus provisionally named "that." Yet "that" is truly the "I" of "I."

From the womb, one receives one’s nature, and nature arises from the void. "That" is the master of the void, and the void is the master of me—thus it is said, *"That is truly the I of I."* Here, a diagram may clarify: the lesser void connects to the greater void, and qi emerges from within the void. Therefore, "that" of the void is the master of me. Moreover, in the postnatal realm, the colored body is taken as the self—this self is the consciousness-spirit. Combined with "that," it becomes the true self. The consciousness-spirit that governs the colored body is merely the provisional self.

The union of Zi and Wu is also the union of Mao and You. Mao pertains to Wood, the spirit of Wood-Mercury, its color red—also called *Red Mercury*. You pertains to Metal, the true yang of Lead, its color white—also called *White Lead*. When the living Zi hour arrives, true yang reaches the body, the external kidneys rise, and my true perceiving spirit of Mercury emerges to unite within the Mysterious Aperture, drawing in the true yang of Lead to merge with it—thus it is said, *"Lead arrives, Mercury welcomes."* The spirit-awareness of Mercury naturally greets the true yang of Lead—this is the union of Mao and You, the external exchange.

Internally, when yang stirs and surges to the crown, this is the union of Wu and Zi. The welcoming of Lead is the work of spirit-awareness, not the consciousness-spirit. If the consciousness-spirit is employed, it remains postnatal and cannot generate transformation. The phrase *"With intent, one welcomes; with intent, one sends"* refers precisely to spirit-awareness, not the intent of the consciousness-spirit.

Externally, Mao unites with You; internally, Wu unites with Zi. Once both exchanges are complete, the congealed fluid descends to the Gate of Life, the external kidneys retract—this is Zi arriving to unite with Wu, the internal exchange. Externally, the spirit of Wood-Mercury stabilizes within the Mysterious Aperture, and true Lead once more arrives to unite with it—this is You uniting with Mao. Initially, Mercury emerges to welcome Lead; subsequently, Lead arrives to unite with Mercury—both exchanges occur within the external Mysterious Aperture, constituting the union of Mao and You.

Internally, Wu first unites with Zi, fire coming to unite with water; then Zi unites with Wu, water coming to unite with fire—this is the internal union of water and fire, the exchange between heart and kidneys. One internal, one external, both unions occur simultaneously—thus it is said, *"Internally, Kan and Li unite in the north and south; externally, Qian and Kun unite at Zi and Wu."* The union of Zi and Wu is the union of Mao and You.

The internal union of water and fire constitutes the Sevenfold Reversal; the external conjunction of Metal and Wood constitutes the Ninefold Return—this is the Ninefold Return and Sevenfold Reversal of the Minor Celestial Cycle. From the perspective of the entire alchemical method, the Minor Celestial Cycle is the Sevenfold Reversal, the *Jade Fluid Returned Elixir*; the Major Celestial Cycle is the Ninefold Return, the *Golden Fluid Great Returned Elixir*. If considering only the Minor or Major Celestial Cycle individually, then within the Minor Celestial Cycle, both Sevenfold Reversal and Ninefold Return are present; within the Major Celestial Cycle, both are likewise present.

In the entire alchemical method, the Minor Celestial Cycle is designated the Sevenfold Reversal because it is governed by the union of Kan and Li in the north and south. The true fire of the Gate of Life surges upward to the heart palace—this is Kan returning to Li. The cool fluid from the heart descends to the Gate of Life—this is Li returning to Kun, called the *Internal Elixir*, also known as the *Jade Fluid Returned Elixir*. Jade nourishes the self; having reverted to the youthful body, one attains longevity—thus it is called *refining the self*, which is *nurturing the self*. The cool fluid descending from the crown is the Jade Fluid. At this stage, Li has transformed into Qian, and the Jade Fluid can repair the body’s broken vessels, restoring it to wholeness—hence it is called the *Jade Fluid Returned Elixir*.

As for the *Golden Fluid Great Returned Elixir* of the Major Celestial Cycle, the work of reverting to youth has already been accomplished, and the practice now resides entirely within the void. Nine is the number of yang; one gathers true yang from the void—this is also gathering the Qian Metal of "that," hence it is called the *Ninefold Return*, though distinctions must be made. If considering a partial alchemical method, then within the Sevenfold Reversal of the Minor Celestial Cycle, the Ninefold Return is also present; within the Ninefold Return of the Major Celestial Cycle, the Sevenfold Reversal is likewise present. In the Major Celestial Cycle, when yang qi ascends posteriorly and descends anteriorly, this is the Sevenfold Reversal.

Why distinguish between the union of Kan and Li in the north and south and the union of Qian and Kun at Zi and Wu? Because the human body contains two layers of heaven and earth, four yin and yang—this distinction arises from the division between the prenatal and postnatal. The prenatal has its heaven and earth, its yin and yang; the postnatal likewise has its heaven and earth, its yin and yang. In the prenatal, Qian is south and Kun is north, Li is east and Kan is west; in the postnatal, this shifts to Li in the south and Kan in the north, Qian in the east and Kun in the west. The prenatal yin and yang reside within the void—this is the conjunction of Metal and Wood at Mao and You, the union of east and west, which is also the union of Qian and Kun.

In the postnatal realm, Kan enters the body as the Gate of Life, while Li resides at the crown of the head. The yin and yang of Qian and Kun govern as the formless, ruling yin and yang; the yin and yang of Kan and Li, however, constitute the yin and yang within the human body. As the lesser void connects to the greater void, the great qi permeates the body’s orifices, and the prenatal lingers within the postnatal—thus, in the formless, Qian and Kun preside over Kan and Li.

Man’s breath communicates with heaven and earth; day and night, he draws in the great qi to invigorate it, enabling movement and action without perishing—otherwise, he would be lost. Man’s spirit of consciousness also depends on the great qi; sustained by it, the spirit may long abide in the body as its master—else, it scatters to the four directions.

Man is born through this formless heaven and earth, its yin and yang—hence the saying, “two layers of heaven and earth, four yin and yang.”

Li Qing’an’s comparison of man to a marionette is most apt. He states: “Consider the marionette—its limbs move, its steps shift in myriad ways. Yet the marionette does not move of itself; it is the strings that pull it. Though the strings are tied to its joints, it is the puppeteer who moves them. Do you recognize this puppeteer?”

The marionette represents the physical form; the strings, the breath of the body; and the puppeteer, the true master within. The marionette moves because the strings move it; the strings do not move on their own—they are set in motion by the puppeteer. This puppeteer is the void itself, and thus the void is called the true master. Lead exists within the void, unseen by the eye; only the void, only this void, is the true master. The puppeteer stands outside the marionette, just as the master of the void lies beyond the body—hence the elixir method names it the “master from without.” One must allow this master to enter the body and become its lord; this is the inception of receiving qi, the true nature of Qian’s origin. Only this can serve as one’s enduring master; the spirit of consciousness cannot.

In *Journey to the West*, Sun Wukong styles himself "Grandfather Sun from Without"—precisely the master from without. When Sun Wukong subdues the Green Bull, the beast possesses a white ring that ensnares all manner of treasures; the Ruyi Jingu Bang of Sun Wukong, the demon-slaying sword of Prince Nezha, and even the fire implements of the Fire God—none escape its grasp. Though neither water nor fire could harm it, and Li Tianwang entreated Sun Wukong to steal it, the Bull Demon wore the bracelet on his wrist, rendering theft impossible. Thus, they journeyed westward to beseech the Tathāgata. The Tathāgata dispatched eighteen arhats, each bearing cinnabar elixir sand, to aid in the endeavor. The elixir sand could transform the earth into emptiness to ensnare the demon, yet still they failed. Following the Buddha’s counsel, they ascended to the Heaven of Separate Regret to seek the Most High Lord Lao, who revealed the demon to be the Green Bull and the white ring to be his own Diamond Bracelet, capable of transmuting demons. The bracelet had been stolen by the Bull while the cowherd boy slept, descending to sow chaos in the world. Lord Lao still possessed his Banana Fan, which had not been stolen; had it been taken as well, even he would have been powerless. With the fan, he fanned the Bull, whose strength melted and sinews numbed, revealing its true form. The Diamond Bracelet was then threaded through the Bull’s nostrils, subduing it. This ring is the Supreme Ultimate, the very "Center." The Supreme Ultimate is a void, supreme and without peer, from which all things arise; thus, it absorbs all things, and nothing can overcome it. The Buddha’s cinnabar elixir sand is also a void, hence its power to transform the earth into emptiness. The Banana Fan is true yin, and only true yin can restrain true yang. The Tathāgata comes from nowhere and goes nowhere, embodying the true emptiness of neither coming nor going. Only emptiness gives rise to wisdom, enabling recognition of the demon’s original form. Only within emptiness can the elixir coalesce, hence the cinnabar sand issues from the Tathāgata’s treasury. True cinnabar sand lends power—it is true yang. Finally, threading the Diamond Bracelet through the nostrils signifies the generation of medicine within the mysterious aperture, the very "Center." Every line in *Journey to the West* carries allegorical meaning.

When Tang Sanzang and his disciples cross the Heaven-Reaching River, they ride upon the back of an ancient turtle, white in color and vast as a canopy—this canopy is the origin of Qian and Kun. A cord is threaded through the turtle’s nostrils, resembling a bridle; this cord signifies unbroken continuity. The breath of respiration lies outside the nostrils, yet the primordial spirit settles here—this is the interdependence of heart and breath. When Sun Wukong finds himself within the belly of the Lion Demon King, he recalls bringing a collapsible pot from Guangdong, which he sets upon the three-pronged bone to cook a stew. He then thrusts his Ruyi Jingu Bang into the crown of his head, piercing a hole to serve as both skylight and chimney. This three-pronged bone is the yin heel aperture, connected to the upper mysterious aperture—together, they form the upper and lower "Centers." The Ruyi Jingu Bang opening the skylight symbolizes the thrusting vessel, linking to the external void. The yin heel aperture is encircled by the eight extraordinary vessels and the nine meridians, communicating with the upper mysterious aperture. The yin heel is the root of life, the mysterious aperture the root of nature; the dual cultivation of nature and life, the communion of the two Centers, is true intent. The union of midnight and noon is the firing times. Setting the furnace upon the three-pronged bone and using the Ruyi Jingu Bang to open a chimney for smoke clearly illustrates the activation of living midnight and living noon, unifying the qi—its meaning is evident and easily understood.

Nature is fundamentally emptiness. When it receives the mandate of Heaven, it resides within the body; but the spirit of consciousness usurps its place, forcing it to retreat and conceal itself without, obscuring its luminous essence. Thus, it remains external. "Grandfather Sun from Without" precisely points to this external nature. The great void is that, the intermediate yin is I; nature is that, emotion is I. The deluded fail to comprehend, mistaking "that" and "I" for personal pronouns and thus misinterpreting them as male and female. Yet in the classics of immortals and the scriptures of Buddhas, the terms "that" and "I" often have no relation to persons. "I" denotes emotion, while "that" represents nature. The void defies description, hence it is also denoted by "that." If I am the essence of the void, then "that" opposite me is naturally also void. The void within our bodies is likewise denoted by "that." Only with this void can the embryo form, and the intermediate yin enter to dwell within. The intermediate yin of emotion is the master of my body; the void of nature is also my master. The master of masters is called "that." Later generations, fearing misunderstanding, appended the word "home" to "that."

*The Awakening to Reality* states: "This supreme treasure exists in every household, yet fools fail to recognize it fully." It also says: "Every household possesses this household treasure." The human body is substantial, yet only the void can contain it. There is no person without a home, and no home without this void. My home is my void—the small void within the crown aperture; that home is the void of that—the great void of Heaven and Earth. Both my home and that home refer to the void. Later generations misinterpreted "that home" as referring to women. Only Li Xianhun’s words are most precise and clear: "That home is void, made substantial by my reality."

"That home is void, made substantial by my reality."

From these two phrases, one may attain thorough understanding.

When Qian (Heaven) and Kun (Earth) unite at the zi-wu axis, it is the convergence of Metal and Wood—also the fusion of the two soils, Wu and Ji. In the *Li* trigram, the central line is Ji soil, one’s own soil; Wood-Mercury is thus one’s own soil. Placing one’s own Ji soil within the void to seek Wu soil—namely, the single line within the *Kan* trigram—*Kan* is emptiness, nothingness. From nothingness arises being, generating this one line of Wu soil, which is true Lead. True Lead, Wu soil born of emptiness, arrives at the mysterious aperture to unite with Ji soil, and the two soils form the *gui* elixir.

Moreover, Lead is Metal, which is also Qian Metal; one takes Qian Metal as the *daogui* (knife and square). Daoist medicinal substances are named "daogui," and this meaning is also derived from this. The nine returns and seven reversions involve internally the union of Water and Fire—namely, the union of *Kan* and *Li*; externally, the union of Qian and Kun—also the harmonization of the two soils, Wu and Ji—this is true intention, and true intention is also the true embryo. When true intention is established, true breath is likewise established.

Ordinary intention unites with ordinary breath; true intention unites with true breath. When the Tang monk journeyed to the West to fetch the scriptures, he rode a dragon-horse—this dragon-horse is true breath. Guanyin Bodhisattva then sent the local earth deity to deliver a saddle, and this saddle is true intention. Breath and intention cannot be separated even for an instant.

Sha Wujing (Friar Sand) also represents true intention, while his eight-treasure monastic staff symbolizes true breath. Wujing never parts from his staff, just as the dragon-horse never parts from its saddle—both illustrate the principle of interdependence between mind and breath.

Within the mysterious aperture resides a warm, gentle qi—this is true intention—while the true breath in the abdomen opens and closes. Externally, there is one Yin and one Yang; internally, there is one Yin and one Yang—these are the two layers of Heaven and Earth, four sets of Yin and Yang. The body possesses postnatal Yin and Yang, while externally there exists prenatal Yin and Yang—are these not four sets of Yin and Yang?

As for the Heaven and Earth of the body: the crown is Heaven, and the abdomen is Earth. The external Heaven and Earth are the cauldron of the Great Void and the furnace of the Supreme Ultimate—these too form a Heaven and Earth. In truth, the cauldron is the furnace, and the furnace is the cauldron. The cauldron is not originally a cauldron, nor is the furnace truly a furnace. Master Wu said: "The cauldron is no cauldron at first; the furnace is not a jade furnace." From this, one may understand.

In the practice of the Dao, there are two centers: true intention and true breath. True breath resides in the fixed center—embryonic breathing opens and closes within the space one *cun* and three *fen* below the navel, "facing the navel wheel in front and the kidneys behind, with a true golden cauldron in between"—this is the "fixed center." True intention, however, dwells in the realm neither inside nor outside, between the inner void and the outer void, now rising, now descending, circulating within its bounds—thus true intention is the "unfixed center." Yet within it lies a point of intersection between the vertical and horizontal axes, which may be fixed here. Though unfixed, it still possesses fixity, for its bounds—above and below, front and back—are interconnected.

Yan Yuan said: "The more I look up at it, the higher it seems; the more I delve into it, the harder it becomes. I gaze ahead, and suddenly it is behind." (See *Analects*, "Zihan") This also means: when one looks up, its vastness knows no outer limit; when one looks down, its minuteness knows no inner boundary. Between above and below, front and back, there exists a center—this is the "unfixed center." If one establishes something towering, then it stands towering—unfixed, yet still fixed.

The *Great Learning* states: "What you dislike in those above, do not use to govern those below; what you dislike in those below, do not use to serve those above; what you dislike in those ahead, do not place before those behind; what you dislike in those behind, do not follow after those ahead; what you dislike on the right, do not apply to the left; what you dislike on the left, do not apply to the right. This is called the Dao of the compass and square." The compass and square define the rules of measurement—within above and below, front and back, left and right, a "center" is concealed. This aligns with the meaning of the *Huangting Jing*.

The *Huangting Jing* says: "Above is the soul-spirit, below is the Pass of Origin; on the left is Lesser Yang, on the right is Greater Yin; behind is the Secret Gate, ahead is the Gate of Life." Here, too, a "center" is concealed within the words. Scholars may contemplate this—all refer to the "unfixed center."

Lecture Seventeen: Spirit and Qi, Essence and Intention, Body and Mind.

The "Three Families Meeting" refines true gold—what is called the "Three Families Meeting" refers to the Small Celestial Cycle. The nine returns and seven reversions are precisely the Three Families Meeting. The *Jade Emperor Scripture* takes spirit, qi, and essence as the Three Treasures, naming them the Three Grades; when the Three Grades harmonize, it is called the convergence of the three into one, yet the Three Families are not these Three Grades. Since they are named "families," each contains more than one. Of the three, one is internal, one external, and one central: internally, water and fire unite as one family; externally, metal and wood merge as one family; and centrally, the two earths form a jade tablet as one family. These are the Three Families, comprising six elements—water, fire, metal, wood, and the two earths. Water and fire pair as yin and yang; metal and wood likewise pair as yin and yang; wu and ji (the two earths) are one yin and one yang, uniting in harmony.

Verifying this with the principle of the *River Diagram*: the east’s three and the south’s two sum to five, forming one family—the east’s three is nature, the south’s two is spirit; nature is quiescent, spirit is dynamic, with distinction between them, thus one family. The west’s four and the north’s one sum to five, forming one family—the west’s four is emotion, the north’s one is primordial essence; emotion is quiescent, essence is dynamic, thus one family. The central wu and ji, each bearing the number five, form a family of their own—these are the Three Families.

The south’s two is fire, li, yet the true mercury within li belongs to wood, the wood of the eastern direction—this is wood within fire, akin to Zhu Wuneng in *Journey to the West*; the east’s three and south’s two form one treasury. The north’s one is water, kan, yet the true lead within kan belongs to metal, the metal of the western direction—this is metal within water, akin to Sun Wukong; the north’s one and west’s four form one treasury. Wu and ji are true earth, akin to Sha Wujing, with the central two fives forming one treasury. When the three and five unite, only then is the complete Tang Sanzang formed.

The north’s one and west’s four are the primordial true lead, lead that comes from without; the east’s three and south’s two are true mercury, mercury that issues from within; wu and ji are the Central Palace, the true intention harmonizing between the two, thus serving as the Yellow Dame who brokers the union, causing true lead and true mercury to merge in the Central Palace.

The east’s three is yang, the south’s two is yin; the north’s one is yang, the west’s four is yin; wu is yang earth, ji is yin earth. These distinguish nature, emotion, spirit, essence, and intention: nature and spirit unite as a couple, emotion and essence unite as a couple, wu and ji form a couple of their own—these are three pairs of husband and wife. When husband and wife unite, they form one family, thus the Three Families, each with one husband and one wife.

Metal, wood, water, fire, and earth are the Five Phases; once paired, they revert to the Three Families. When the Three Families meet, they return from three to one; reverting three to one relies entirely on the function of true intention.

The true lead residing without and the true mercury residing within are harmonized in the Central Palace, where they reunite as husband and wife, only then able to form the elixir. Thus, true lead is called the "White-Faced Gentleman," true mercury the "Green-Clad Maiden," and true intention the "Yellow Matron." With the Yellow Matron as matchmaker, she draws together the inner and outer true mercury and true lead, pairing them as husband and wife—this is the Dao of uniting inner and outer, the meeting of the Three Families.

As *Wuzhen* says, "This supreme treasure exists in every household," for only through the union of one husband and one wife, the coupling of yin and yang, is life generated. It is not the solitary yin or isolated yang within the body, hence it is called a "household." When the Three Families meet in the Yellow Court’s divine chamber—within the Central Palace’s Yellow Court—the three treasures of inner essence, qi, and spirit naturally merge into one. This is the meeting of the Three Families, also known as the nine returns and seven reversions.

The outer medicine is lead; the inner medicine is mercury. When inner and outer unite, the two earths form the *gui* tablet. True lead is the one yang within *kan* (Water), *wu* earth; true mercury is the one yin within *li* (Fire), *ji* earth. When the two earths merge, the Three Families become one family. Returning from three to one accords with the principle of Heaven and Earth.

First there was the Limitless, then the Supreme Ultimate was born. From nothing, something arises—from the extreme of stillness, the One and the Spirit emerge. This is the Supreme Ultimate’s primordial *qi*, the root of creation, thus called the "Ancestral *Qi*," also the *Taiyi*’s true-contained *qi*. *Taiyi* is Heaven’s One, contained within water, hence the "true-contained *qi*" is the "gold within water." When movement reaches its extreme and stillness arises, yin *qi* is born, forming the Two Modes—Heaven above, Earth below. The void between them retains the Supreme Ultimate, identical in essence to the Limitless’s truth. Thus, three is one, and one is emptiness.

Once the Five Phases are established, they transform from five to three, and from three to one. This function resides entirely in the central void—the true emptiness of the Limitless, the primordial *qi* of the Supreme Ultimate. In human cultivation, the Five Phases within our bodies follow the same principle, their function centered in the Central Palace’s true earth. Earth is intention, and this intention is an undistracted, singular focus. Our firing times depend entirely on the regulation of the Central Palace’s intention; true intention is spiritual awareness.

As *Wuzhen Pian* states: "There is a natural true firing time—no need for kindling or forced breath." This firing time arises spontaneously. If one acts contrary to nature, water and fire cannot merge, metal and wood cannot unite, and the path becomes deviant and false.

As the *Canticle* says: "What is naturally so cannot be a false path." The *Daodejing* states: "Heaven follows the Dao, the Dao follows nature." This is non-action that accomplishes all—when the mind is empty and vast, fundamentally without action, it embodies the primordial chaos of the Great Void. Within the Central Palace, metal, wood, water, and fire naturally merge and unite, acting without acting.

When body and mind are without action, spirit and *qi* naturally act; only when body and mind are without action can spirit and *qi* truly act. As *Wuzhen Pian* says: "At first, action is unseen by others; only when non-action is achieved do all recognize it." Within the formless and shapeless, the elixir coalesces naturally, unseen.

For non-action pertains to body and mind, while action pertains to spirit and *qi*. Only through non-action can there be action—action in spirit and *qi*, which others cannot perceive.

Among the aforementioned nature, emotion, spirit, essence, and intention, there is a distinction between prior and posterior Heaven. The east’s three is nature, the true yang’s primordial *qi*, also emptiness. Within true emptiness lies wondrous existence, entirely of prior Heaven’s prior Heaven. The south’s two is original spirit, also prior Heaven. The west’s four is emotion, which at conception, when male and female emotions and spirits unite, becomes the nature of temperament. The north’s one is original essence, prior Heaven within posterior Heaven. Thus, the north’s one and west’s four are both prior Heaven within posterior Heaven. The central *wu* and *ji* are *wu* as prior Heaven and *ji* as posterior Heaven.

Therefore, nature and spirit meet as prior Heaven, emotion and essence meet as prior Heaven within posterior Heaven, and intention meets as posterior Heaven with prior Heaven—this describes the virgin body. For those who have broken the body and cultivate the Dao, the situation differs.

The east’s three, true nature, hides in the realm of profound obscurity, unseen. The south’s two, original spirit, transforms into consciousness, and nature and spirit become posterior Heaven. The *kun*’s primordial emotion ascends to *qian*, prior Heaven recedes, and consciousness takes control, turning emotion into nature. Emotion and essence, as prior Heaven within posterior Heaven, also shift. Only *wu* and *ji* remain unchanged—one prior Heaven, one posterior Heaven.

*The Journey to the West*, from the first to the seventh chapter, symbolizes cultivation in the virgin body, hence its ease. From the fourteenth chapter, "Two-Border Mountain," to the end, it symbolizes cultivation after breaking the body, hence its difficulty.

Sun Wukong is true yang, the nature of prior Heaven. Zhu Bajie is the nature of gluttony and lust, prior Heaven within posterior Heaven. The Buddha says parents conceive the body through desire, carrying that desire, so Bajie loves wine and women. A true spiritual nature mistakenly entered a pig’s womb, thus his true nature is obscured by consciousness.

For those cultivating after breaking the body, true yang from the void must enter the body, consciousness must revert to original spirit, and after stillness and stability, true intention arises, forming the true embryo. Thus, Sha Wujing is the last to attain in *The Journey to the West*.

Only after achieving the Nine Returns may one proceed to the Seven Reversions, attaining the true intention of the Central Palace, where singular focus remains unscattered—this is the principle.

Zhu Bajie was born at Fuling Mountain in Cloud Chain Cave. *Fuling* signifies the spark of true nature, the fortune of man; Fuling Mountain is also the Vermilion Mansion. *Cloud Chain Cave* denotes a sealed cavern mouth, as true nature is obscured by gluttony and lust, hence symbolized by cloud and chain.

Sun Wukong was born on Flower-Fruit Mountain, the primordial ancestral *qi* from which all things arise. Beneath the Water Curtain Cave lies an iron bridge, signifying metal within water. *"Square Inch Mountain of the Numinous Terrace"* refers to the square inch of the numinous terrace; *"Cave of the Slanting Moon and Three Stars"* is the character *xin* (心, "heart") in full—this is the original heart, not the fleshy organ. The *"Slanting Moon and Three Stars"* is also called the Crescent Moon Crucible, where the slanting moon is the small void, and the three stars are the square inch, the gateway. Observe the two diagrams below to grasp the meaning of *"Slanting Moon and Three Stars."*

Sun Wukong’s return from the Patriarch to Flower-Fruit Mountain signifies drawing from *kan* to fill *li*; stealing weapons from the Ao-Lai Kingdom represents accumulating metal. Obtaining the golden-hooped staff from the Dragon Palace is the Nine Returns; erasing his name from the Register of Life and Death at King Yama’s court signifies attaining immortality through cultivation. His appointment as *Bima Wen* (Protector of the Horses) denotes the work of warming and nurturing. After forming the inner elixir, a year of warming fire is required—*ma* (horse) is *wu* (noon) fire, and *tianma* (heavenly horse) is *qian*. The title *"Great Sage Equaling Heaven"* signifies immortality and longevity equal to heaven. Only then can one project spirit, hence the *"Great Sage Equaling Heaven’s"* palace contains the *"Peace"* and *"Tranquility"* bureaus. The uproar at the Peach Banquet and the havoc in Heaven represent the Great Celestial Cycle. Erlang is true yin, Sun Wukong true yang; true yang meeting true yin results in no victory or defeat. The three heads and six arms signify the *qian* and *kun* hexagrams. The golden bracelet is the primordial Supreme Polarity; true yang is the primordial unity of *qi*, akin to the Supreme Polarity, thus able to subdue Sun Wukong’s family. Pressing him under the Eight Trigrams Furnace is the work of nursing; the Buddha pressing him beneath the Five Phases Mountain is the work of returning to the void.

The role of *Bima Wen* is most crucial. After the embryo is formed, a year of warming work must follow before undertaking the Great Celestial Cycle. This year precedes the Great Celestial Cycle, not follows it. The medicine of the Small Celestial Cycle is born then gathered; the medicine of the Great Celestial Cycle is gathered then born. Only when metal accumulates to fullness can one transform into a body of pure yang. The nursing work of the Great Celestial Cycle spans three years. After the Small Celestial Cycle, a year of warming yields the red orb. After spirit projection, nine years of wall-facing work follow. The so-called year of bathing, three years of nursing, and nine years of wall-facing all employ yang numbers, roughly indicating the pace of progress, which depends entirely on the diligence of one’s root *qi*—no uniform standard applies.

The Small Celestial Cycle forms the inner elixir, also called the yin elixir, sealed and secured an inch and three-tenths below the navel. This is the return of the great medicine to the furnace, the inner elixir sealed within, revolving like a wheel. Breathing becomes natural, opening and closing like a fetus in the womb—ordinary breath reverts to fetal breath. Advancing further, one refines *qi* into spirit, fetal breath reverting to true breath. True breath is breathlessness; only when the six pulses fully cease can one perform the fire times of the Great Celestial Cycle and project spirit—this is the completion of life through the inner elixir. Upon its formation, the old revert to youth, white hair turns black. Women practicing this also regain youth. Men retract the external yang; women sever the red dragon. The red dragon is *tiankui*; reverting to a maiden’s body and cutting off *tiankui* is also called *"transforming the female into the male."* The distinction between male and female lies in *tiankui*; severing it renders one no different from a man. Men manifest the hidden phase of the horse’s yin; women sever the red dragon—this is the fetal immortal. The *Yellow Court Classic* states, *"The zither’s heart, thrice layered, dances the fetal immortal,"* referring precisely to this. If preserved without loss, one may attain ageless longevity, called a human immortal. Further progress yields an earth immortal, a terrestrial transcendent. Earth immortals possess transformative abilities; human immortals do not. Forming the inner elixir makes one a human immortal; after a year of warming work, one becomes an earth immortal. After this year of warming, one achieves longevity, impervious to illness and harm. Without warming work, after sixteen years, the five thousand four hundred days of the yellow path will break the body. For men, this spans five thousand four hundred eighty days; for women, five thousand forty-eight days. Breaking the body again reduces one to a child’s state, no different from an ordinary person. Thus, warming work is most essential—without it, one endures only fifteen years. With a year of warming, the body need not break again, a natural transformation beyond further dissolution. Yet if the mind-horse and heart-ape are unchecked, ruin may still follow, as with the Red Lotus Monk of the Jin dynasty. During Zheng Chenggong’s time, a monk possessed Daoist arts, invulnerable to blades. Chenggong, troubled, consulted his officers on how to defeat him.

An adjutant then spoke, saying, "This poses no difficulty. I have a method to break him." He summoned courtesans to fill the room and tested the monk, who remained unmoved as before. Next, he ordered the courtesans to chase one another naked and to engage in intercourse before him. Seeing the monk suddenly close his eyes, the adjutant knew his heart had stirred. Seizing the moment, he raised his blade from behind and struck—whereupon the monk’s head fell to the ground. This is an example of ruin through corruption.

Thus, one must undergo a year of warming work to face circumstances without attachment, seeing form yet not as form. Yet in extraordinary situations, such as the example above, one may still be undone. Therefore, one must cultivate the work of completing the nature to achieve eternal existence. The Great Celestial Cycle is precisely the work of completing the nature.

External elixir completes life; internal elixir completes nature. External elixir forms the inner cinnabar; internal elixir forms the outer cinnabar. Only by forming the outer cinnabar can one depart the bodily shell and transform freely. After the inner cinnabar is achieved, with a year of warming, should one face inevitable death—whether through events demanding utmost loyalty or filial piety, or encountering disasters and treacherous schemes—though the body perishes, the primordial spirit remains luminous and indestructible. Like loyal ministers and filial sons who die to preserve their integrity, or those who succumb to karmic retribution or ambush, after death, the primordial spirit retains its agency and may seek rebirth by seizing a womb.

Once the body dies, the luminous primordial spirit naturally discerns where to find immortals and selects a family of ancestral virtue for rebirth. During the parents’ union, it enters the womb first, preventing other intermediate beings from occupying it—this is "rebirth," choosing a womb and entering it ahead of others. As for "seizing a womb," the luminous primordial spirit perceives which family possesses virtue and can produce a human immortal. Calculating the moment of birth, it stirs the intermediate being within the fetus. At the moment of delivery, it seizes the womb from the intermediate being, expelling it and entering itself—this is "seizing a womb."

Only yin spirits can perform rebirth and womb-seizing. Though superior to ordinary spirits, they remain yin spirits and thus may employ these methods. At this stage, one’s innate spiritual root enables the cultivation of immortality from a child’s body. All who cultivate immortality from a child’s body originate from rebirth or womb-seizing. The Venerable Mahākāśyapa of the Western Regions, during the Six Dynasties, was reborn into the Xiao family—evidence that in his native land, his work remained incomplete. Śākyamuni declared that he had cultivated for seventeen lifetimes across seventeen kalpas, each time emerging through rebirth or womb-seizing.

All who have established the foundation can perform rebirth and womb-seizing. Combined with "old dwelling" and "relocation," these constitute the "Four Fruits of the Path." In Buddhism, they are known as Arhat, Sotāpanna, Sakṛdāgāmin, and Anāgāmin. "Old dwelling" corresponds to the Arhat fruit; "relocation" refers to reviving in a corpse. If one’s yin spirit is cultivated but not yet perfected, one may borrow another’s recently deceased body, reanimating it to restore human form—this is "relocation," akin to a person changing residences.

Immortal Li Tieguai was originally Lord Ningyang, who had already cultivated the ability to project his yin spirit. One day, summoned by the Celestial Most High, he prepared to depart his body in spirit, instructing his disciple to guard his physical form. He cautioned, "If I do not return in seven days, you may bury my body." On the fifth day, the disciple’s mother fell gravely ill, leaving him torn. Though he dared not leave, on the sixth day, his uncle arrived, urging him to return home due to his mother’s condition. The disciple, bound by duty to guard his master’s body, still refused to go. As they deliberated, calculating that only eight hours remained until the seven-day limit, they assumed the master would not return and buried his body before the disciple hurried home. Upon arriving, Lord Ningyang returned to find his body interred. Nearby, a beggar had frozen to death outside a mountain cave. His primordial spirit entered the corpse, reviving it as a cripple.

He then visited his disciple’s home, where the disciple wept bitterly. Hearing his name called, the disciple exclaimed, "How your voice resembles my master’s!" Lord Ningyang revealed, "It is I, your master. You may recognize me by my voice. I do not blame you for burying my body. I foresaw this— the Celestial Most High had already informed me. I can revive your mother." With a breath, he restored her to life. Overjoyed, the disciple believed him to be his master. Thus, Lord Ningyang became the Cripple Immortal.

Having first cultivated the yin spirit, he could ascend to heaven and attend gatherings like the Peach Assembly. The Celestial Most High had previously advised him to refine his yang spirit. After becoming a cripple, he resumed cultivation of the yang spirit and attained immortality. These Four Fruits of the Path are not the ultimate attainment. In Buddhism, they are termed the "Hearers and Solitary Realizers" of the lesser vehicle. Unable to return to emptiness and merge with the void, they achieve only earthly immortality, incapable of divine transformations for universal salvation. Buddhism regards them as "self-liberated ones," concerned only with their own liberation.

Advancing further, one becomes a bodhisattva, possessing divine powers to save the world and ferry beings across suffering. Heavenly immortals, like buddhas, embrace the vow of universal salvation, becoming "Great Awakened Golden Immortals" who transcend the Three Realms and endure eternally without decay. The Most High Lord Lao has already undergone seven cycles of primordial chaos without dissolution. True Man Zhang Sanfeng said, "People often speak of worldly affairs as unfinished. To complete them, one must transcend the world."

If one’s parents are still living and major affairs remain unfinished, one may first lay the foundation and complete the initial stage of practice. Once these affairs are settled, one may then proceed to the great elixir practice—this is also permissible. Those who aspire to cultivate but neglect to lay the foundation risk sudden death with no time to remedy it.

In this world, three things are most difficult to attain: first, the rarity of obtaining a human body; second, the scarcity of encountering the Dao’s turning (Dao yun), for as Buddhism often says, “the Dao’s turning is hard to meet”—without it, cultivation rarely succeeds; third, the difficulty of finding a true teacher. Given these three challenges, one must cultivate without delay, lest in old age one’s lead and mercury diminish. By then, primordial qi will have been exhausted, and the elixir cannot be returned.

The first step in Daoist practice is most crucial. To establish the initial foundation of the elixir, one must first set aside sixty days—whether beginning with sleep practice or seated meditation—engaging in six sessions daily to gradually attain proficiency. Proficiency means natural spiritual awareness, which is the natural firing process. Only when one reaches this natural firing of spiritual awareness can the return be achieved; otherwise, the water source remains impure.

At present, practice begins with effort, then progresses to smoothness, and finally to effortless mastery. Once effortless ease is attained, success comes readily. Daoists call this stage “entering the chamber,” while Buddhists term it “closing the retreat.” Only after completing these sixty days of practice can one proceed; otherwise, it is exceedingly difficult.

If we cannot yet enter the chamber, we must first purify the heart. However, to reach proficiency requires three years of practice—at least one year. Thus, it is wise to first undertake several months of chamber practice. If entering the chamber is not yet possible, one must subdue distracting thoughts, making the heart as vast as the great void. Practicing four or five sessions daily will facilitate progress, with each session lasting twenty to thirty minutes. One may set the heart aside, completing practice before attending to other matters.

In refining the self, one must practice at least six times daily, once every two hours. During practice, the heart must be fully released, and both body and mind stilled. Within five minutes, yang will arise, and each session of twenty to thirty minutes will suffice to complete the work. One must never begin diligently only to grow lax later. Daily practice should be frequent, and within stillness, the heart must remain lively. Only through frequent practice can one gradually attain naturalness.

Initially, there is no fixed forgetting; one must first subdue thoughts. If a thought arises and cannot be stilled, one should cease practice until the thought is dispelled. If thoughts arise during seated meditation, the practice will be ineffective. Only when the heart is still and thoughts do not arise can one proceed.

How to attain proficiency depends entirely on personal realization—others cannot assist. The terms “comprehension” and “awakening” are often discussed. During practice, the heart must be free of affairs yet not attached to emptiness, like the state upon waking in the morning—utterly devoid of thoughts, yet without the thought of being thoughtless. When one realizes the state of no-heart and no-thought, yang will naturally arise. However, this process may be slow, as it requires accumulation over time—hence the need for one to three years.

To attain proficiency, one must first accumulate essence and refine qi. Only when the abdomen holds gold can proficiency be achieved. As the *Awakening to Reality* states, “Not knowing to refine lead and first fill the abdomen, I teach you to guard the hall of gold.” Returning to emptiness is the work of accumulating gold, which is also the practice of emptying the heart and filling the abdomen. Emptying the heart and filling the abdomen is the work of returning. First, true emptiness; second, wondrous existence—these are interconnected, and both involve subduing the heart.

How to subdue the heart is most thoroughly explained in the *Diamond Sutra*. The next lecture will continue with the *Diamond Sutra*, which may clarify these matters.

**Lecture Eighteen: Subduing the Heart Begins with Subduing the Four Marks**
*The Diamond’s Prajñā Leaves Gold and Discards Dross*

How to abide in the heart and how to subdue it is difficult to express. Even with a thousand words, clarity is not easily achieved. A heart set on something is not the Dao, nor is a heart devoid of all. Between having and not having, there lies the true meaning of neither clinging to existence nor to emptiness. The heart may exist, yet leave no trace; it may seem absent, yet not entirely so—like a mass of true intent, undispersed and unyielding, its appearance that of primordial chaos.

Master Qiu said, “If one can grasp the secret of no-heart, one will surely become a Great Luo Immortal.” Yet a heart that is utterly dead is not permissible. There is a true secret here: it must be lively and empty, for only then is it the primordial spirit. If the heart is too rigid, not only will the acquired spirit perish, but the primordial spirit will also fade.

Between existence and emptiness, between weight and lightness, one must cultivate realization. Seeing the heart as the Buddha is possible only in primordial chaos. The *Book of Documents* states, “The human heart is perilous; the Dao-heart is subtle. Only with utmost refinement and singular focus can one truly hold to the center.” (See *Book of Documents: Counsels of Yu*)

“The human heart is perilous”—any attachment to the heart makes it human. “The Dao-heart is subtle”—discarding the human heart reveals the Dao-heart. If the heart is weighed down, it is the human heart; the Dao-heart is exceedingly subtle, while the human heart is exceedingly perilous. Between peril and subtlety, one must carefully cultivate realization, adhering to the center with utmost refinement and singular focus. Refinement is subtlety; by realizing the subtlest depths with sincerity and concentration, one can “truly hold to the center.”

The work of "holding to the center" manifests in the practitioner’s body as the center containing the One. The *Dao De Jing* states, “Grasp the One, and the myriad affairs flourish.” Between the center and the One, there is a distinction: consider the kernels of peach and plum, each formed by two halves—these halves are the one yin and one yang. The hollow between them, slightly raised, is the true center, and within this emptiness resides a single *qi*. Without this *qi*, life cannot exist.

Within the center lies the One. Confucius said, “My Way is threaded through with a single principle.” (See *Analects: Li Ren*) The center is emptiness; the One is being. The center is true emptiness; the One is the singular *qi*, the primordial *qi* that cradles the true. Only emptiness can generate boundless transformation; only the One can foster inexhaustible growth. If there is the One but no emptiness, it cannot be properly established.

Nature is true emptiness, yet within it lies wondrous being; life is wondrous being, yet within it lies true emptiness. If one attains the secret of no-mind, one can grasp the true meaning of true emptiness. From true emptiness alone can wondrous being arise. When the discriminating mind vanishes, one returns to primordial chaos—the state before heaven and earth were sundered. Only then does emptiness give birth to true yang. Though this true yang arises from the resonance of emptiness, it is emptiness shaped by human effort. Through this resonance, a single *qi* emerges, birthing true yang. Without true emptiness, wondrous being cannot arise; true yang *is* wondrous being.

The postnatal body, broken and corporeal, cannot attain emptiness, so one must subdue the heart—akin to taming the dragon and tiger, a task of no small difficulty. Here, we turn to the *Vajracchedikā Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra* for elucidation. “Thus have I heard”: this Dharma is as such, and I have heard it from the Buddha.

“The Tathāgata was in the Jetavana Grove with a great assembly of twelve hundred and fifty bhikṣus.” At this time, the Tathāgata took his meal. In Buddhist practice, one must beg alms from seven households before partaking, “proceeding in order from door to door,” then returning to eat. After the meal, he washed his feet, for having gone forth to beg, they were soiled with dust; only after cleansing them until not a speck remained would he expound the Dharma. Subhūti, “baring his right shoulder,” rose and addressed the Buddha, saying: “Rare is the World-Honored One.” *Rare* here is a term of reverence.

The central purport of this sūtra lies entirely in the arising of the *anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi* mind. *Bodhi* means awakening; *samya*k is rightness; *sama* is equality; *anuttarā* means supreme—thus, “the mind of supreme right and equal awakening.” “World-Honored One, how should the bodhisattvas and good men and good women who have set forth in the Mahāyāna generate the mind of supreme right and equal awakening? How should they abide in it? How should they subdue their minds?”

Supreme right and equal awakening is spiritual awakening—natural, divine awareness—unsurpassed in goodness, supreme beyond compare. The human mind is most mutable, like a dragon; as a Tang poet said, “In meditation, tame the poisonous dragon” (Wang Wei, *Passing by Xiangji Temple*). If even the dragon is called *poisonous*, its mutability is extreme—how then to subdue this dragon? The tiger is a man-eating beast, most fierce and savage—how then to tame this tiger? To make the mutable unmoving and the savage docile is spoken of in terms of the human mind. Only by subduing the human mind can one abide in this right awakening.

What does it mean to *abide*? It means to dwell in this *samyak-saṃbodhi* mind. How can one generate this mind of right and equal awakening and abide in it permanently? Even those of the postnatal, broken body may at times generate this mind, but it lasts but an instant before shifting; *abiding* means permanence. The Buddha replied: “They should abide thus, thus subduing their minds.” *Thus* means just so. Beyond these words, there is no language that can describe it; thus he answered in this way. *Thus* refers to the mind’s original nature. The mind of *samyak-saṃbodhi* is inherently one’s own, merely obscured by discriminating consciousness and thus unseen. By clarifying the mind and perceiving one’s nature, one naturally accomplishes this—purely a matter of natural subduing and natural abiding. If the mind is forcibly restrained, it is not empty; even if right awakening arises, it is not true, for the mind is already obstructed.

The mind without mind is the original mind; the awakening without awakening is true awakening. When the fundamental nature is quiescent and extinguished, Buddhists call this “the *nirodha-samāpatti* of extinction.” Only after extinction can great samādhi be attained. In the first dhyāna, thought is stilled; in the second, breath is stilled; in the third, the pulse is stilled—meaning the six pulses cease entirely. Breath and thought depend on each other; when thought is stilled, one forgets thought; when breath is stilled, breath ceases—thus, with the cessation of respiration, true breath is established. When breath and pulse are extinguished, there is no-thought and emptiness.

“Of all the kinds of sentient beings—whether born from eggs, wombs, moisture, or transformation; whether with form or without form; whether with thought, without thought, or neither with nor without thought—the Buddha causes them all to enter *nirvāṇa* without remainder and thus leads them to extinction.” This is the meaning of “the *nirodha-samāpatti* of extinction.” “Though he thus leads all sentient beings to extinction, in truth, not a single being is led to extinction by him.” For those beings led to extinction are not extinguished by the Buddha but extinguish themselves. Not to be born and not to perish is *nirvāṇa*—the prenatal nature. Though countless beings enter this *nirvāṇa* and preserve their unborn, imperishable nature, it is not that the Tathāgata possesses a method to extinguish them; all beings liberate themselves.

In the *Śūraṅgama Sūtra*, Ānanda faced calamity, trapped in the chamber of demonic women, nearly destroying his precept-body. The Buddha rescued him, and Ānanda, seeing the Buddha, wept. The Buddha said to Ānanda: “You have not yet attained the *āsrava-kṣaya-jñāna*, so you could not avoid this calamity.” Ānanda wept and said to the Buddha: “I am the Buddha’s beloved disciple, and the Buddha cherishes me most. I thought the Buddha’s Dharma had been fully transmitted to me, yet I had not attained the *āsrava-kṣaya* and could not avoid stumbling.” His heart was filled with despair. He did not realize that this matter requires first extinguishing the discriminating consciousness—it lies entirely in one’s own clarification of the mind and perception of nature, and cannot be accomplished by another.

Why is it said that no being is led to extinction by the Buddha? Because one liberates oneself by liberating others; first, one must eliminate the four marks. Removing these four marks cannot be done by another. If the Buddha were to extinguish beings, the Buddha would still possess the four marks. The mark of self is one’s own mind; the mark of others is the perception of others in one’s mind. With these perceptions of self and others, the mind cannot be empty. The mark of sentient beings is the mark of form; the mark of lifespan is the mark of cultivation. With these four marks, how can one accomplish the Dao?

A bodhisattva must eliminate the four marks. If the Buddha were to extinguish beings, the Buddha would not have removed these four marks. Only by beings liberating themselves can the four marks be absent—this is what is meant by “subduing the mind,” which is to eliminate these four marks. Of these, the mark of self is the heaviest; the others follow. Laozi said: “Inwardly observe the mind, and the mind has no mind”—this is eliminating the mark of self. “Externally observe form, and form has no form”—this is eliminating the mark of others. “From afar observe things, and things have no things.”

"is the mark of sentient beings. After extinguishing these three marks, one must further eliminate the mark of lifespan. Only by removing these four marks can the mind be subdued; otherwise, one cannot arouse the mind of supreme perfect enlightenment. After eliminating the four marks, we then speak of generosity. A bodhisattva’s generosity should not abide in the mark of form. Internally, the six senses; externally, the six dusts—these a bodhisattva should not abide in, yet still practice generosity. Practicing generosity is performing merit; generosity without abiding in marks yields boundless merit. Eliminating the four marks is to be without mind; without mind, there are no marks; merit without marks is true merit."

The Buddha asked, "Subhūti, what do you think? Can empty space be measured? Can the four directions and above and below be measured?" The empty space between the four directions and above and below is the center; the size of the center cannot be measured. The merit of a bodhisattva’s generosity without abiding in marks is likewise like empty space, immeasurable. A bodhisattva’s mind should abide thus: not abiding in marks, abiding as "taught"—abiding in no-marks, yet abiding in non-abiding. Non-abiding abiding is true abiding; non-fixed samādhi is great samādhi, for abiding is samādhi. One should proceed thus to break through all marks.

"Subhūti, what do you think? Can one see the Tathāgata by means of bodily marks?"
He replied, "No, one cannot see the Tathāgata by means of bodily marks." The bodily marks spoken of by the Tathāgata are not marks of form. "All marks that exist are false and illusory"; whatever has marks in heaven and earth is illusory. Only without marks can one see the Tathāgata. Here, "marks" includes dharma-marks. The Tathāgata is without coming or going, utterly empty; to have marks is not it.

First from the four marks, then the six senses and six dusts—extending this, all marks that exist are false and illusory. To cling even slightly to marks is not supreme perfect enlightenment. Subhūti addressed the Buddha, saying, "Will there be sentient beings who can generate faith?"
The Buddha replied, "Do not speak thus definitively. In the last five hundred years, there will be those who uphold the precepts and cultivate the Buddha Way. Such a person has already planted good roots before ten thousand buddhas over countless lifetimes, and thus can generate reverent faith. Otherwise, they cannot. The Tathāgata fully knows and sees those who can generate faith in these words; their merit is boundless."

One who can believe these words has already relinquished the marks of person, self, sentient beings, and lifespan, as well as dharma-marks and non-dharma-marks. The marks of person, self, and so on are real marks; dharma-marks are illusory marks. When cultivating, the marks one perceives are illusory marks, that is, dharma-marks. Non-dharma-marks are the marks of not clinging to any marks. If one has the mind that does not cling to marks, one clings to non-dharma-marks.

Why is this? When sentient beings separate the mind from marks, is this not clinging to marks? When the mind moves, this is clinging to marks. Only in non-knowing and non-perception can one not cling. If one clings to dharma-marks, one clings to person and self. Clinging to illusory marks is clinging to real marks; clinging to non-dharma-marks is clinging to the four marks. Marks arise from the mind; once the mind arises, there is no difference from clinging to marks. Therefore, one must see marks as no-marks; neither dharma nor non-dharma should be clung to.

You should know that the Dharma spoken by the Buddha is like a raft: "Even the Dharma should be relinquished, how much more so non-Dharma." Thus it is said, "For forty-nine years, I have not spoken a single word." In *Journey to the West*, obtaining the wordless scriptures is also good, but for fear that people of the Eastern Land would not understand, this is the meaning of not speaking even a single word.

"Subhūti, what do you think? Has the Buddha attained supreme perfect enlightenment? Is the Buddha expounding the Dharma here?"
He answered, "There is no fixed Dharma of supreme perfect enlightenment to be attained; since there is no fixed Dharma to be attained, there is also no fixed Dharma to be expounded." For the Buddha-Dharma is not something to be grasped or spoken. What is spoken is not the Dharma, yet it is the Dharma. Thus buddhas and immortals take "nothing" as the Dharma, possessing not a single thing. "Taking nothing as the Dharma, yet within it there are distinctions." The root source is only the word "nothing," yet within the firing times there are distinctions. "Taking nothing as the Dharma"—the entire meaning is already pointed out here. Those who cultivate the Dao should take non-mind as their foundation; only the merit of non-mind is the greatest.

The Buddha asked Subhūti, "If one were to give alms with the seven treasures filling a trichiliocosm, would the blessings be many?"
He replied, "Extremely many." Yet blessings are not the nature of blessings; thus if one has the mind seeking blessings, they are not many. "However, if someone receives and upholds this sutra and explains it to others, their blessings surpass those of giving the seven treasures filling a trichiliocosm." For all buddhas and the Dharma arise from this sutra and these four-line verses: "What is called the Buddha-Dharma is not the Buddha-Dharma"—it still takes no-Dharma as the Dharma.

Next are the "Four Fruits." The fruit of Stream-Enterer is called "entering the stream," not entering the six dusts of form and so on. Only with the mind free of the six dusts can one attain the fruit of Stream-Enterer; if one has a mind, one cannot attain it. The Stream-Enterer is the fruit of the yin-spirit; even this requires non-mind, how much more so the yang-spirit, which requires even greater non-mind. The Once-Returner is "no coming and going"; having no coming and going is non-mind—if one has a mind, one cannot attain it. The Non-Returner is called "not coming," "yet truly there is nothing that does not come."

The term "not coming" truly has no such "not coming"; if one has a mind, then there is "not coming," and one cannot attain it. The Arhat is the yang-spirit, only then is it called the "Dao/Way," and it is not called a "fruit." The Arhat is named "truly no dharma exists"; if one has thoughts, one cannot attain this Dao/Way. To think of attaining the Arhat Dao/Way is to be attached to the four marks—how can one attain the Dao/Way? To have the mind of cultivating the Dao/Way is to be attached to the mark of the long-lived; to think, "I want to attain the Dao/Way," is to be attached to both the mark of self and the mark of the long-lived.

Subhūti attained the "Samādhi of Non-Contention," which is the practice of departing from desire. Departing from the desire realm, with no desire and nothing remaining in the mind, one attains the "Foremost Departure from Desire." Precisely because there is no such thought, one can attain the Dao/Way; if one thinks with the mind, desiring to attain this Dao/Way, the World-Honored One would not call such a one an attainer of the Dao/Way. "Not practicing a single dharma is called the practice of Āraṇya," which is the practice of non-contention.

The Buddha asked Subhūti: "When the Tathāgata was with Dīpaṃkara Buddha in the past, did he obtain any dharma?" He replied: "The Buddha obtained nothing." If it is not adornment, then it is adornment; without the mind of adornment, this is adornment. "One should thus generate a pure mind, and should not generate a mind that abides in form, sound, scent, taste, touch, or dharma"—to generate a mind is to be impure. Only by generating a pure mind is it adornment. The Buddha’s mind is Anuttarā Samyaksaṃbodhi; removing the mind of form, sound, scent, taste, touch, and dharma, "one should generate the mind without abiding in anything." To generate a pure mind is to be without abiding, and thus not attached to marks.

Throughout the twelve hours of the day, the mind generated without abiding is a pure mind, is the mind of the Dao/Way, and is also spiritual awareness. "Having a body like Mount Sumeru" is the Dharma-body. When not a single thought arises, it is called "pure"; when the mind’s terrace is empty of things, it is called "clean"; the mind without mind is the pure mind. What follows speaks of the merit of no-mind. Precisely because of no-mind is it great.

The number of grains of sand in the Ganges is already exceedingly many; if each of these grains of sand were another Ganges, the number would be immeasurable. If one were to take the seven treasures of as many trichiliocosms as there are grains of sand in these immeasurable Ganges and offer them in charity, the merit would also be immeasurable. Yet compared to the merit of one who receives, upholds, or explains this *Vajracchedikā Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra* to others, the latter’s merit is even greater.

Asuras belong to the demonic realm. "All the devas, humans, and asuras in the world should make offerings to this sutra and its four-line verses." If one can receive, uphold, recite, and read this sutra and its four-line verses, one has already attained no-mind and may be said to have truly attained. The Buddha taught the supreme One Vehicle of the dharma of no-mind, the merit of which is unsurpassed.

This sutra is called the *Vajracchedikā Prajñāpāramitā*, one volume among the 579 volumes of the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Sūtra*, indestructible for ten thousand kalpas. Only by cultivating with the mind of no-mind can one attain it. Otherwise, one cannot. The dust of a trichiliocosm is not dust—only then is it called dust; it is not a world—only then is it called a world. Whenever there is a mind of right and wrong, of duality, one cannot become this thing. Only by having no right or wrong, no duality, can one accomplish it.

For example, if there is a teacup here, and someone says it is a teacup, another will say it is not a teacup and deny it. As soon as there is a name, there is right and wrong. Only by negating everything can one accomplish and stand alone. Thus it is said, "A dust-mote is not a dust-mote," "A world is not a world"—only then can this dust-mote and this world be established.

"One cannot see the Tathāgata through the thirty-two marks." The thirty-two marks are illusory marks, and also marks of what is not. Only by having no marks can one see him. To give one’s body and life in charity as many times as the grains of sand in the Ganges would yield great merit; yet the merit of one who receives, upholds, recites, and reads this sutra is even greater.

Those who hear the name of the *Vajracchedikā* and generate faith "immediately generate a pure mind and immediately generate the true mark." The true mark is not a mark; it is the wondrous existence born from emptiness, and thus it is the "foremost merit." Because to depart from marks is to be without marks, and to depart from all marks is to be a buddha, it is the "foremost rare merit" and is the "foremost pāramitā." To attain the foremost pāramitā is not the foremost pāramitā, for having the true mark is to be without marks.

In the past, when the Buddha was in the time of King Kali, he was dismembered limb by limb by the king yet had no trace of the four marks, and thus was called the "Kṣānti Ṛṣi." "One should generate a mind without abiding," which is also "one should generate the mind without abiding in anything." The mind of a bodhisattva is the mind of merit and giving. One should not abide in marks but, for the benefit of sentient beings, practice giving with no-mind. Those with mind and form are not sentient beings; only those without mind and form are sentient beings.

Only by not abiding in form when giving can one see form; if one has a mind, one does not see it. Those who know how to cultivate the Dao/Way see form and generate their mind, and their marks immediately vanish. Those who receive, uphold, recite, and read this sutra can attain immeasurable and boundless merit. If there are good men and good women who give their bodies in charity day after day for immeasurable hundreds of thousands of myriads of koṭis of kalpas, the merit would indeed be great.

Yet this still does not compare to the merit of transcribing, reciting, and reading this sutra, which is even greater. "This sutra is inconceivable, its boundless virtue beyond measure." Such a person has already attained the anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi of the Tathāgata.

Those who cling to marks or delight in lesser teachings cannot cultivate this sutra, nor transcribe, recite, explain, or expound it for others. Wherever this sutra resides, it should be revered as a stūpa or temple. If one who upholds this sutra is nonetheless despised by others, it is due to the karmic offenses of past lives. Yet now that one upholds this sutra, those past karmic offenses are immediately extinguished, and the Way is accomplished.

When the former Buddha was with Dīpaṃkara Buddha, though he made offerings to all buddhas and performed various meritorious deeds without idleness, compared to the merit of receiving and reciting this sutra, it does not equal one part in a thousand myriad koṭis. If the full extent of such a person’s merit were described, those who heard it might "fall into madness and doubt," unable to believe and accept, instead generating karmic offenses. For this sutra contains inconceivable meanings, and thus its karmic retribution is naturally inconceivable as well.

**Lecture Nineteen: The Buddha is Perfect Awakening; Only the Self Can Liberate the Self**

Forms are not like lightning or dew—what is called the "anuttarā-saṃyak-saṃbodhi mind" is precisely the pure mind free from the four forms: no self, no person, no sentient being, no lifespan. Extending this principle, "all forms whatsoever are illusory." Only by not clinging to the slightest form does one attain this mind of perfect enlightenment.

The *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Sūtra* is the supreme treasure among Buddhist scriptures, just as the *Yijing* is to Confucianism and the *Lingbao* to Daoism. The Chan school, in particular, takes the revelation of *prajñā* as its core, making it the orthodox teaching of the Mahāyāna. Though schools like Tiantai and Pure Land also belong to the Mahāyāna, they differ in being provisional methods.

*Prajñāpāramitā* means "the wisdom that arrives at the other shore." Through this *prajñā* wisdom, one is born onto that shore. The term "that" here shares the same meaning as in Daoist references to "that realm" or "that self." The *Vajracchedikā* and the *Heart Sūtra* are both essential texts of the *Prajñā* corpus. The nature of *prajñā* wisdom is fundamentally empty. The six-hundred-fascicle *Mahāprajñāpāramitā* begins by expounding this emptiness in its first fascicle and culminates in the *Vajracchedikā* by fascicle five hundred seventy-nine. As the entire scripture nears its conclusion, it thoroughly elucidates the work of returning to emptiness: inwardly, the three minds are relinquished; outwardly, the four forms are extinguished. Ultimately, all forms are illusory, and the mind becomes pure.

The six senses interact with the six dusts, giving rise to the six consciousnesses. Only when the six consciousnesses are completely dissolved does one attain this pure mind. Only purity is non-action. "Non-action" does not mean idleness; sitting motionless while the mind generates chaotic thoughts is still not non-action. One must first purify the mind. With a pure mind, the body becomes non-active, and naturally, through non-action, nothing is left undone.

The body and mind of non-doing and non-action belong to the realm of spirit and *qi*. Only when the mind is pure can the spirit and *qi* act effectively. This pure mind is the natural spiritual awareness. When the mind is unmoving, it is emptiness; when it is about to move, it is natural spiritual awareness. Only this natural spiritual awareness can generate transformation and creation, for emptiness devoid of all things is the Dao, and the Dao is this natural spiritual awareness. Only at this stage can one gather external *qi* to obtain the elixir, refining it with spirit.

Bai Yuchan said: "Refining the elixir with fire to form the cinnabar is like guiding *qi* with spirit to accomplish the Dao." (See *Ziqing Zhixuan Ji: Xuanguan Xianmi Lun*) In truth, elixir and fire are one: when obtained, it is the elixir; when refined, it is fire. The entire process is the function of this natural spiritual awareness, which alchemical texts call the "true intention" or the "Dao-mind." It is self-so, requiring no deliberate effort yet capable of spontaneous action.

Through effort one attains ease. As Lü Zu said: "From striving one reaches ease; from subtlety one enters transformation." When practice reaches ease, the great transformation is called spirit. This spirit naturally guards the aperture. This natural spiritual awareness is both the elixir substance and the mysterious aperture.

Zhang Ziyang said: "The elixir substance arises in the mysterious aperture; the fire-timing ignites the yang furnace." (See *Jindan Sibai Zi*) Only with the elixir substance does the mysterious aperture arise. "Mysterious" is yang; yang arises within emptiness. Externally, it is called "image"; internally, it is called "reflection." This internal reflection is the "mysterious aperture." The aperture arises from the fire-timing of natural spiritual awareness, which is also the function of this natural awareness.

"The elixir substance arises in the mysterious aperture" is the cauldron; "the fire-timing ignites the yang furnace" is the furnace. Cauldron and furnace are two yet one: first the cauldron, then during refinement, it becomes the furnace. This is the natural spiritual awareness—the pure mind untainted by sound, form, scent, flavor, touch, or dharmas.

The *Daodejing* states: "These two emerge together but differ in name; together they are called the mysterious. Mystery upon mystery—the gateway to all wonders." Yet within this, the sequence of pre-heaven and post-heaven is inverted. One must distinguish whether it is the pre-heaven of pre-heaven or the pre-heaven of post-heaven, though in essence they are one. Through the provisional one cultivates the real, giving rise to true mercury and true lead. The entire process relies on true intention—this natural spiritual awareness, which is also the pure mind untainted by the six dusts. This will be discussed in detail later.

The latter part of this sutra mirrors the former. Beginning with "subduing the mind," Subhūti asks the Buddha: "How should one abide? How should one subdue the mind?" The Buddha answers that one should generate the mind in this manner. Generating the mind without clinging to sound, form, scent, flavor, touch, or dharmas is how one subdues and abides. Not clinging to the six dusts is purity of mind; with a pure mind, one naturally abides without attachment.

Not abiding in the six dusts is the method; not clinging to the four forms is the practice. The mind is like the dragon and tiger: only by not abiding in the six dusts can one subdue it. Once subdued, one can extinguish the four forms and abide. Abiding is immovability—this is the meaning of "generate the mind without abiding anywhere."

"I should liberate all sentient beings across the three times, yet in reality, not a single being is liberated." For if I harbor the mind to liberate others, the four forms arise.
