Liberating others with a sense of self entails the form of self, the form of others, and the form of lifespan in cultivating the Dao. Not only does this fail to achieve buddhahood, it also fails to achieve bodhisattvahood. Only without the mind to liberate others can one truly liberate sentient beings. Sentient beings, too, must relinquish the four forms to receive liberation and liberate themselves.

The *anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi* mind—the mind of supreme perfect enlightenment—cannot in truth be aroused by any method; its arising is entirely spontaneous and cannot be compelled by external force. Since this awakened mind cannot be aroused by any means, it likewise cannot be used to liberate others. Beings must awaken and liberate themselves, yet even in beings, the thought of self-liberation must not arise, for where there is mind, there are the four forms. Neither liberating others nor liberating oneself can be achieved through such means.

"Subhūti, what do you think? Was I liberated by Dīpaṃkara Buddha? Did I receive any dharma from him?"
"No, World-Honored One. When you met Dīpaṃkara, there was no dharma to be obtained, nor was it transmitted by him."
"Just so, just so! Had there been any dharma to obtain at that time, Dīpaṃkara would not have given me the prediction: ‘In the future, you will attain buddhahood.’" Where there is dharma to obtain, the four forms arise. At that moment, mind corresponded with mind, and true awakening arose naturally—thus the prediction of buddhahood was conferred.

The Tathāgata is without coming or going, for the dharma of non-action is neither born nor destroyed. All dharmas are without birth or extinction. Some say the Buddha attained the dharma of *anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi*, yet in truth, the Buddha attained nothing. It is as if there were something, yet in reality, there is nothing—utterly empty and void, existing in the realm of neither reality nor unreality. This is the "middle" spoken of by the sages of the Three Teachings. The middle is emptiness; the dharma abides within true emptiness, and thus there is no dharma.

All dharmas are the Buddha-dharma; all dharmas are not the Buddha-dharma, for dharmas are without coming or going, without reality or unreality. The Tathāgata speaks of "a great body," yet it is not great—it is merely called great. For once "great" is spoken, there must be a limit: ten feet is great, ten thousand feet is also great. If it reaches ten thousand feet, it is already the utmost in greatness, yet since ten thousand implies a limit, it is not truly a great body. Only that which is limitless, like empty space, is the great body.

When a bodhisattva vows to liberate sentient beings, to say, "I shall liberate beings," is no longer a bodhisattva. "I" is the form of self; "shall" is the form of others. With the four forms present, how can one be called a bodhisattva? Only where there is no dharma can one be called a bodhisattva. Departing from the four forms, utterly empty, one subdues the mind and generates a pure mind—only then may one be called a "bodhisattva."

If a bodhisattva has the mind to adorn a buddha-land, the buddha-land cannot be adorned. Only without the mind to adorn is there true adornment. "One who thoroughly understands the dharma of no-self is a bodhisattva." Where there is no self, there is no dharma—thus it is said, "there is no dharma to liberate beings." Of the four forms, the form of self is the heaviest; only without self can there be dharma, and one becomes a true bodhisattva.

The Buddha asked Subhūti: "Does the Tathāgata possess the divine eye?" The divine eye is the divine-eye penetration. "The wisdom eye?" The wisdom eye pervades the trichiliocosm. "The dharma eye?" The dharma eye illuminates past and future worlds, surpassing the wisdom eye. "The buddha eye?" The buddha eye surpasses the dharma eye; its nature is true emptiness, illuminating all without exception, perceiving first and then illuminating. Subhūti replied: "The Buddha possesses the divine eye, the wisdom eye, the dharma eye, and the buddha eye."

The Buddha further said: "In worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, the minds of all beings are fully known and seen by the Buddha." For all minds are not minds, and thus the Tathāgata "knows and sees them all." The mindless mind is fully known and seen by the Buddha.

"The past mind cannot be obtained, the future mind cannot be obtained, the present mind cannot be obtained." This encompasses the three minds. The past is recollection, an afterthought; the present is thought; the future is anticipation—none can be obtained. The original mind of beings is true emptiness, and thus cannot be obtained. The three minds are also the buddhas of the three times, belonging to the trichiliocosm. The three times arise from the mind, giving rise to recollection, deep thought, and excessive worry. Abandon these three, and all thoughts become empty—only emptiness is the buddha-mind. With these three minds, one cannot attain *anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi*. Only by first eliminating the past and future, then the present, does the mind become empty. This is what is meant by "subduing the mind"—it is the effort of unifying the three minds.

To give alms with the seven treasures of the trichiliocosm yields great merit, yet only when given without mind is the merit said to be abundant. With mind, it is not abundant.

The Buddha asked Subhūti: "Can the Tathāgata be seen by means of the physical body?"
"No, World-Honored One. Where there is form, there is no form; the Tathāgata cannot be seen by means of the physical body." Only where there is no form can one see him, for what the Tathāgata speaks of is the dharma-body, which cannot be seen by a form-bound body.

Again he asked: "Can the Tathāgata be seen by means of marks?"
"No, World-Honored One. Where there are marks, there are no marks. All marks are empty—only then is it the true mark. Within true emptiness, nothing is lacking. Only true emptiness can give rise to transformation." The so-called "thirty-two marks" are transformations within true emptiness.

The Buddha further told Subhūti: "The Tathāgata’s teaching of the dharma is no dharma to be taught—this is called teaching the dharma." The dharma is fundamentally empty; since there is no dharma, teaching is also not teaching. At that moment, as Subhūti listened to the Buddha’s teaching, his wisdom opened, and thus he was named "Life of Wisdom."

Subhūti addressed the Buddha, saying: "In future ages, will there be sentient beings who, upon hearing the Buddha’s teaching of the dharma, generate faith?"

The Buddha replied: "The term 'sentient beings' is but a provisional designation. The nature of sentient beings is empty—there is not only no sentient-being nature, but also no Buddha-nature. To be identical with sentient beings is to be a sentient being; to be identical with the Buddha is to be the Buddha. One who realizes the emptiness of nature is the Buddha; one who fails to understand the emptiness of nature is a sentient being. Thus, one may be called a sentient being, or one may not. Those who comprehend this sutra and generate faith are the Buddha, not sentient beings. The Tathāgata sees all sentient beings as identical to the Buddha, yet they do not depart from being sentient beings—all is self-invited by sentient beings themselves."

Subhūti again addressed the Buddha: "When the Buddha attained anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi, was it a case of nothing being attained?"

The Buddha said: "Just so, just so! The Tathāgata attains nothing in all dharmas. Not only anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi, but even the slightest dharma in all dharmas is unattained. Only when all is utterly empty, with not a single thing grasped, is it the pure mind."

The Buddha told Subhūti: "The Buddha-dharma is equal, without high or low." Only the supreme One Vehicle is the Buddha-dharma. The Sixth Patriarch said: "The Buddha-dharma is but the One Vehicle; the other two are not true." (See the *Platform Sutra*) The Middle and Small Vehicles are not the Buddha-dharma. By relinquishing the four marks, the mind becomes pure of itself; naturally, spiritual awareness arises, inherently without great or small, high or low. All wholesome minds are the pure mind unattached to the six sense-objects. "All wholesome dharmas are the dharma of attaining anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi." There is no dharma that is the wholesome dharma. The Buddha speaks in metaphors; this profound principle must be carefully contemplated.

The Buddha told Subhūti: "In one world, there is one Mount Sumeru, also called the summit, located in the Western Regions. If one were to offer the seven treasures of Mount Sumeru, king of the three-thousand great-thousand worlds, in almsgiving, the merit would be great indeed. Yet the merit of receiving, upholding, reciting, and explaining this sutra to others does not compare to even a thousandth or ten-thousandth part of it. This sutra speaks entirely of the merit of formlessness. Since it is formless, it is identical to the Supreme Ultimate. When the mind is thus, its merit is boundless. One who is without mind unites in virtue with Heaven and Earth, and shines together with the sun and moon. Confucius said: 'What does Heaven say?' (See *Analects*, *Yang Huo*) Only by being without mind does Heaven achieve its greatness. When a person is without mind, they too are identical to Heaven—how could their merit be measured? Mencius said: 'All things are complete within me!' (See *Mencius*, *Jin Xin Shang*) For when it is extended, it pervades the six directions. If one can relinquish the three minds, extinguish the four marks, and be without mind, then their merit is of the same substance as the great void. Three thousand eight hundred worlds are encompassed in an instant—such merit cannot be calculated by words or numbers."

"Subhūti, I have no such thought as 'I shall ferry sentient beings across.' If there were sentient beings whom the Tathāgata ferried across, this would be attachment to the four marks. 'One who has a self is not the true self.' The self of the Tathāgata is not the self of the form-body. The self of the Tathāgata is empty, of the same substance as the great void. Emptiness is the self; the self is emptiness. 'Ordinary beings' are spoken of in contrast to cultivators. When the mind is truly empty, one is the Buddha. Thus it is said: 'Ordinary beings are not ordinary beings—this is called ordinary beings.'"

"Subhūti, what do you think? Can the Tathāgata be observed by means of the thirty-two marks?"

"To observe" means to look upon. "If the Tathāgata were observed by means of the thirty-two marks, then the Wheel-Turning Sage King would be the Tathāgata." The Wheel-Turning King is distinguished by gold, silver, and copper—one should not observe the Tathāgata by means of the thirty-two marks. To observe the Tathāgata by form is heterodox and cannot see the Tathāgata. Form is the yin spirit; one cannot see the Tathāgata by this. The Tathāgata is empty—hence the thirty-two marks.

"Subhūti, you should not cling to the notion of complete or incomplete marks in attaining anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi. If one clings to the notion of incomplete marks and arouses the mind of correct awakening, then all dharmas would be cut off. Correct awakening is the pure mind. Even if all marks are complete, though pure, they are not cut off. Attaining this and being empty like the great void, the marks and excellences are naturally complete, neither arising nor ceasing—one must not speak of the mark of extinction."

"Subhūti, if one were to offer worlds of seven treasures as numerous as the sands of the Ganges in almsgiving, and another were to empty themselves entirely, perfecting forbearance—having emptied the four marks and realized 'no self in all dharmas'—they could endure unmoved. Even if their body were dismembered, because the mind is not in the body, they would not die and would perfect forbearance. If there were a mind, even a slight injury to the body would be unbearable. The merit of such a forbearing bodhisattva is greater than that of the almsgiving bodhisattva. Because the bodhisattva does not receive blessings and merit, though performing meritorious deeds, they are unattached to marks. With a mind free from craving, this is true emptiness. Only the merit of true emptiness is the greatest."

"The Tathāgata neither comes from anywhere nor goes anywhere—hence the name Tathāgata. If someone were to say that the Tathāgata comes or goes, sits or lies down, they would not understand the dharma spoken by the Tathāgata. For the Tathāgata is utterly empty, without coming or going."

The Buddha addressed Subhūti: "If the trichiliocosm were pulverized into motes of dust, would these motes be numerous?" To pulverize into motes is to shatter even emptiness. Subhūti replied: "Exceedingly numerous." These motes arise from nothingness; their nature is fundamentally empty, and only thus can they be ground into countless billions of particles. Were they truly substantial, they could not be so shattered. The trichiliocosm of which the Tathāgata speaks also resides within the mind—it is not a world in itself. If a world were truly substantial, it would be but mountains and rivers formed by the illusory interplay of yin and yang.

"This is called the *unified appearance*, yet it is not truly a unified appearance." The unified appearance refers to the union of spirit and qi, the interdependence of mind and breath. The chaotic fusion of yin and yang, the interdependence of mind and breath—this is the unified appearance. When yin and yang meet, they coalesce. This unified appearance arises from nothingness, its essence fundamentally empty, and thus it is said to be "ineffable." Form arises from the formless, and form and formlessness merge. Yet only when neither existence nor nonexistence is established, when the mind holds neither existence nor nonexistence, can they unite as one. Its ineffability stems from this.

"Ordinary beings cling to such matters." Ordinary beings readily attach to appearances—if not to existence, then to nonexistence; if not to dharma-appearances, then to non-dharma-appearances. Thus, they cannot attain immortality. Only when neither existence nor nonexistence is established, forming the unified appearance, does one become a fully awakened golden immortal. Possessing the dharmakāya formed by the union of existence and nonexistence, one then pulverizes it into countless billions of transformation bodies. Thus, the Buddha ultimately presents the unified appearance, only to declare it "ineffable." For in truth, it is the formless unity born of existence within nothingness and nothingness within existence.

"Subhūti, people often speak of the Buddha’s teachings on the views of self, person, sentient being, and lifespan. Do you think they understand the meaning of my words?" Subhūti replied: "Such people do not understand the Buddha’s meaning." For ordinary beings interpret these "four views" as truly existent, whereas the Tathāgata speaks of view without appearance—responding to circumstances with stillness, moving in accord with stimuli, a seeing without seeing. This is the difference.

The Buddha then explained: "Those who arouse the mind of supreme perfect enlightenment and attain purity of mind should know and see thus: respond when circumstances arise, cease when they pass. One’s faith and understanding of the dharma must be the same—without generating dharma-appearances or the mind of cultivation. When the time is ripe, one responds naturally, without preconceived intent. The Tathāgata’s teaching that 'dharma-appearances are not dharma-appearances' refers to the appearance of the formless, for no views or perceptions arise.

The Buddha told Subhūti: "The merit of upholding this sutra surpasses that of almsgiving. Even if one were to fill measureless asaṃkhyeya worlds with the seven treasures and offer them in almsgiving for asaṃkhyeya kalpas since beginningless time, the merit would be immeasurable. Yet if one were to receive, uphold, recite, or expound this sutra for others, the merit would surpass even that." For one does not grasp at appearances, and the sutra itself is without the appearance of a sutra. In receiving, upholding, reciting, or expounding, one does not cling to the appearance of upholding the sutra. The mind remains empty, like movement without movement, stillness without stillness, neither empty nor non-empty. Thus, its merit is even more immeasurable.

"All conditioned dharmas are like dreams, illusions, bubbles, shadows, like dew or lightning—all pass as swiftly as a glance. All immortals and buddhas are distinguished by the unconditioned dharma. Conditioned dharmas are viewed as illusory. The wondrous observation of my mind equates all dharmas to flowers in the sky. Thus, all monks, nuns, laymen, laywomen, and even earth-bound demons who hear the Buddha’s words rejoice greatly, believing, accepting, and practicing them.

In these thirty-two chapters, all appearances are emptied—from the four appearances to dharma-appearances and non-dharma-appearances, all are empty. Yet from nothingness arises the unified appearance, the root of heaven, earth, and all beings. Having attained this unified appearance, one must still transcend existence and nonexistence, abiding in unmoving suchness. Only then is it in accord with the dharma. This is the primordial qi, the original nature of Qian, the nameless beginning of heaven and earth, the mystery within mystery, the gateway to all wonders.

Having attained this unified appearance, one must further shatter it into countless billions of transformation bodies. Then the work is complete, and the task is finished. Scholars must contemplate this deeply." Lecture Twenty.

The inversion of Qian and Kun, Kan and Li—this "inversion and reversal" is a phrase often used in Daoist texts. Yet from ancient times, those who study the Dao have mostly failed to deeply comprehend its true meaning, thereby giving rise to heterodox interpretations. Some take heaven below and earth above as the hexagram *Earth-Heaven Peace* (*Ditian Tai*), and in human terms, consider a man yielding to a woman as inversion—thus leading to misunderstandings. In truth, these two characters hold profound significance; the entire function of Daoist cultivation lies precisely here. The transformation from the primordial to the postnatal, and the return from the postnatal to the primordial, are never separate from inversion and reversal.

From the moment of birth until the breaking of the body, the single yang line in *Qian* descends into *Kun* to become *Kan*, while the central yin line in *Kun* ascends to become *Li*—from above to below, from below to above, this is inversion. After the breaking of the body, the cultivator draws from *Kan* to fill *Li*, seeking to restore the youthful body—is this not also inversion? *Qian* is nature, *Kun* is destiny. When the single yang line in *Qian* descends into *Kun*, it becomes *Kan*; the yang within *Kan* is destiny, the primordial true yang. All that a person uses in a day, all actions and movements of the body, are the functioning of this true yang. Yet the more it is used, the more it diminishes, until the body becomes purely yin. This true yang is the metal of *Qian*, the innate nature bestowed by heaven. The postnatal physical form struggles to preserve destiny and fulfill nature. By sixteen, after the breaking of the body, by sixty-four, the true yang is entirely exhausted.

The postnatal qi from without enters the body through its orifices, lingering with the primordial qi to preserve the true yang and sustain life. Yet as it is used day by day, the true yang is depleted, and death follows. The true yang in our bodies is already deficient, though not yet exhausted, varying in degree from person to person. Those deficient must replenish it; that which has descended must be raised, which is the meaning of "drawing from Kan to fill Li"—the act of lifting upward.

This involves two layers of effort: first, absorption—since what we have used is insufficient, we must absorb the true yang from the void to replenish our own, restoring the youthful body; second, elevation—extracting the true yang sunken in *Kan* and raising it back to *Li*. Yet the yang that has descended is deeply hidden and invisible; it must be stirred by the true yang from the void, which resonates with the true yang in the Gate of Life, mutually responding until they merge and ascend. Relying solely on the body’s yang qi is ineffective and difficult to elevate, hence the need for both internal and external cultivation. Focusing solely on either inner or outer practice makes success unlikely.

The elevation of the true yang from the Gate of Life is inversion; the absorption of true yang from the void is also inversion. Foundation-building and absorption belong to the primordial within the postnatal. Since our bodies are postnatal and incomplete, what is drawn forth is thus the primordial within the postnatal. In the Great Celestial Cycle, it becomes the primordial within the primordial. In essence, they are the same; the distinction arises only from the differing states of the body.

The first step is returning to emptiness—emptiness so profound that stillness is absolute, and the true yang arrives to be absorbed. In the void, all is purely primordial; when it resonates with the body, one may feel arousal or tingling numbness. Once sensed, it becomes postnatal; after absorption, the primordial transforms into the postnatal. The true yang born in the void is a perfect, unadorned thing; when it merges with the body, they become one, hence postnatal. Relatively speaking, it becomes postnatal; in its essence, it remains primordial—this is the principle of "the primordial transforming into the postnatal."

The true yang in the void is what was received before the parents’ birth—this is the primordial ancestral qi. When the true yang resonates and essence arrives, the parents’ blood and essence envelop it, forming the embryo. This primordial ancestral qi is the original ancestral nature. What now arises in the void is the same substance; we absorb it to establish destiny, not nature. Before the parents’ birth, it is nature; now, we use it to establish destiny. Nature and destiny are the same thing, differing only in name.

At the moment of primordial conception, nature and destiny are undivided, for there is no physical form. Now, with a physical body, we call it "destiny." Master Lu Xianqian said: "Fulfilling nature depends on the realm of destiny; fulfilling destiny depends on the realm of nature." In the primordial state, they are one; from the postnatal body after breaking, we rely on absorption to restore the youthful body and thereby establish destiny—yet nature and destiny remain one. This is what is truly meant by inversion: destiny inverted is nature, nature inverted is destiny. That which dwells in the Heavenly Valley is nature; that which dwells in the Responsive Valley and Spirit Valley is destiny—nature and destiny are one.

The phrase "inversion and reversal" appears often in texts. When one reaches the state of no-mind, the primordial establishes destiny, which is then used to cultivate nature. In the boundless obscurity of chaos, seeing nature is seeing destiny. Thus it is said: "Exhausting principle and fulfilling nature to reach destiny."

As stated in the *Yi Jing*’s “Shuo Gua”: “nature and destiny are originally one,” hence the phrase “to reach destiny.” Nature and destiny are but one inversion and one reversal. From the perspective of nature, it is “prior to Heaven, and Heaven cannot contravene it”—the primordial qi gives birth to Heaven, and since Heaven is born of this, it cannot contravene it. From the perspective of destiny, it is “posterior to Heaven, yet in accord with Heaven’s timing”—once Heaven exists, this qi lingers in the void, giving rise to the myriad things in due time, hence “in accord with Heaven’s timing.” The so-called “Heaven cannot contravene” and “in accord with Heaven’s timing” both refer to this single qi of the Supreme Ultimate within the void. Prior to Heaven and posterior to Heaven invert and reverse one another; the myriad things arise from this. The generation of the myriad things is posterior to Heaven, while their original substance is prior to Heaven—these two are one.

Heaven and Earth, the myriad things, are all encompassed by this qi, yet nothing can be seen or heard of it, thus it is said: “Its greatness has no exterior; its smallness has no interior.” As Ancestor Lü said: “In its vastness, it embraces the cosmos; in its minuteness, it hides within a mustard seed.” This is precisely the reason.

The nature and destiny of the human body are likewise one. Thus, the Daoist texts say: “Nature is established by destiny; destiny is completed by nature.” Before we are born, there is no such thing as nature; only after the physical body exists do we know that where there is this thing called destiny, there is nature—hence “nature is established by destiny.” Yet our destiny is formed by receiving true yang at the moment of conception—hence “destiny is completed by nature.” These two are in truth one body and cannot be separated. Thus, in cultivating nature, destiny is inherently present; in cultivating destiny, nature is inherently present.

The supreme vehicle of the primordial Great Dao in cultivation lies entirely in emptiness and quiescence, wholly natural and spontaneous. This so-called emptiness and quiescence reside entirely in chaos. To reach the state of chaos is to return to the prior-Heaven. The crux lies in whether one can return to the void—if one can return to the void, one can naturally attain chaos. Whether the primordial qi can be felt in the body depends on whether one can achieve emptiness and quiescence. Only when one attains emptiness and quiescence can the qi be felt in the body and yang arise. Once yang has arisen, if one can remain in emptiness and quiescence without stirring, one can retain the yang qi in the body without loss. Otherwise, destiny cannot be established, and nature will also be lost. Therefore, in cultivating the primordial Great Dao, one must take emptiness and quiescence as the foundation—both the heart must be empty and the body must be still.

If one can be empty at all times, the true yang of the primordial qi can arise at all times. If one can be still at all times, the primordial true yang can arise and reach the body at all times. Thus, the first step in Daoist cultivation is the stillness of both body and mind, whereby the spirit’s awareness naturally generates true yang, which can then be gathered and preserved without loss. Otherwise, if it does not adhere to the body, it will adhere to the mind. Though yang may arise, it cannot be gathered; even if gathered, it will be lost in an instant and cannot be preserved.

This involves the question of whether the source of water is clear or turbid. Only when one attains emptiness and quiescence is the source clear; otherwise, it is turbid. The clear is genuine, while the turbid is false. When the true yang from the void reaches the body, it stirs the Gate of Destiny, and the yang organ rises—this is the clear. When the mind harbors images and the yang organ rises, this is the turbid. When yang first arises, it is originally clear, but if the mind becomes fearful or the hands and feet flustered, the clear immediately turns turbid, for the moment an image arises in the mind, it becomes turbid. Once turbid, it cannot be used and cannot be gathered. If one fails to distinguish carefully and gathers this turbid qi, the consequences will be immediate: headaches, heart vexation, red eyes, and other ailments will arise, for the true yang has transformed into ministerial fire, which flames upward and causes these illnesses. But if both body and mind are still, and one gathers the true yang in tranquility, the source of water remains clear, the body feels light and at ease, and the mind and spirit are serene—this is the difference.

Water and fire are originally one: water contains fire, and fire contains water. When body and mind are clear and still, and one gathers the clear, what is obtained is the metal within water. If one fails to do so and gathers the turbid, what is obtained is ministerial fire. A hair’s breadth of difference leads to a thousand miles of error. Thus, one must rely on natural spirit-awareness, whereby body and mind can remain still and unmoving. Otherwise, body and mind will separate, and one will cling to images.

When we first awaken, the water is originally clear, but the moment we awaken, it transforms into the posterior-Heaven, and the source of water becomes turbid. The alchemical texts’ saying “the medicine has aged and cannot be used” refers precisely to this. Once spirit and qi embrace and one awakens, why do they immediately separate and change? It all depends on whether one’s daily work in refining the heart is mature. If thoughts cannot be stilled, even if spirit and qi momentarily embrace, the moment one awakens, thoughts arise, and the clear turns turbid—this is where the work lies.

For beginners, who have just commenced their cultivation, it is naturally difficult to maintain the perpetual embrace of spirit and qi. A method is needed to refine it—this is the martial fire of the water clock. The water clock was an ancient device where a bronze vessel dripped water, marked to measure time. The phrase “listening to the water’s sound and counting the clock’s marks” refers to the method of listening to the breath. When spirit and qi embrace, yang arises and one awakens, but as soon as thoughts stir, one employs the work of listening to the breath. By slightly lengthening the breath and listening with the heart, thoughts can be stilled and remain unmoving. This is what is meant by applying the martial fire of the water clock during the gathering process.

With this method, clarity naturally arises, and the water becomes clear once more. This method of stabilizing the mind is indeed most essential. However, it must be applied the moment a thought begins to stir; if several thoughts have already arisen, it cannot be used—or if used, it will be ineffective. This martial fire of the water clock may also be employed in daily life to still the mind. If one listens to the breath at all times, even ordinary breathing can be heard. Ordinary breathing is the breath of formlessness; by this method, thoughts may be severed even as they arise.

We who study the Dao are all those who cultivate while managing worldly affairs. If we cannot detach from mundane duties, we cannot hear the breath except in stillness. The "martial fire of the water clock" is spoken of in contrast to the "cultivating fire of steaming warmth." As the saying goes: *"The civil refines, the martial restrains."* Refining is harmonizing; restraining is halting. When stabilized in emptiness, one may faintly sense a warm, misty white qi lingering outside the nostrils—this is the civil refining, the cultivating fire of steaming warmth. To call it "steaming" is to liken it to the heat rising from a steamer, suffusing the body with a tingling numbness, as if on the verge of sweat yet not sweating. When yang arises, holding fast to stillness and forgetting all else is the cultivating fire of steaming warmth. At the moment before yang stirs, counting the water clock’s marks to still the mind is the martial fire—the martial fire of the water clock. Thus, the martial comes first, the civil follows, and at the end, the fire is withdrawn. For beginners in cultivation, there are no more than these three steps: martial, civil, and cessation.

After yang stirs in stillness and one rises to gather it, one must move slowly to prevent the stirring of emotions and thoughts. Upon rising, one should listen to the breath to preserve the clarity of the water’s source. Those who do not understand the martial fire of the water clock mistake it for mere breath-counting. In daily practice, if one seeks to still the mind with martial fire, one must not rely solely on counting breaths—rather, one must listen with the ears, not count with the mind. This distinction cannot be overlooked.

The first step for the student is to still both body and mind. To awaken with body and mind still, one must first sleep with body and mind still. As the *Canon* says: *"In sleep, spirit and qi embrace; upon waking, one attends to presence and absence."* That is, in sleep, spirit and qi embrace, and one remains alert, knowing the living zi and living wu. If one cannot be still in sleep, stillness upon waking will be even harder to achieve. Thus, for beginners, sleep practice is most essential.

We are all novices in cultivation and should devote special care to sleep practice. When sleeping, one must not dwell on the past or worry about the future. As soon as one lies down, one should fall asleep, naturally achieving *"in sleep, spirit and qi embrace."* There is a way to test this: if one falls asleep the moment one’s head touches the pillow, that night’s dreams will be peaceful and the spirit serene. But if one lies in bed lost in thought for a long time before sleeping, dreams will be chaotic and the spirit muddled. Therefore, those who cultivate the Dao must first tame the demon of sleep.

The thoughts one has before sleep become the dreams one has during sleep. Many thoughts lead to many dreams; few thoughts, to few dreams. The dreams of Tang Sanzang in *Journey to the West* are dharmic manifestations, not mere dreams. When we sleep, if we begin to think the moment our head touches the pillow and cannot fall asleep, we must employ an expedient method: draw a circle ○ and gaze at it with an intention that is neither too strong nor too weak; or imagine a circle ○ lingering outside the nostrils, holding it there with an intention that is neither too strong nor too weak. This will still the mind and allow one to sleep. With practice, this becomes habitual, and thoughts will naturally cease. Mastery comes only through repetition and habit.

When first falling asleep, one must focus the mind without distraction. Between intention and non-intention, unite spirit and qi in the emptiness outside the nostrils. Then spirit and qi will embrace, yang will return, and awareness will stir naturally. This is called *"when the time comes, the spirit knows"*—as soon as yang stirs, one awakens. Upon waking, one should immediately sit up and practice. If one remains lying down, one will either sink into torpor or grow restless. Torpor means yang has reverted to the postnatal; restlessness means it has been lost. One must rise slowly and sit up, applying the martial fire of the water clock. When sitting up, one must stabilize, and once stabilized, forget all else. Whether the legs are crossed or not is of no consequence. However, clothing and the like must never disturb the external kidneys—this is most critical and must not be neglected.

**Lecture Twenty-One: Fulfilling Nature to Reach Destiny is Called the Celestial Origin**

The Utmost Simplicity and Ease of the Supreme Vehicle of Chan

All aspects of the precelestial Great Dao—the supreme vehicle’s method, including its firing times and other details—have now been thoroughly expounded. Though some minor points may remain unaddressed, the essentials are complete. By diligently applying these teachings, the practitioner may penetrate all truths. I had intended to proceed to the *Wuzhen Pian*, but since the *Huangting Jing* has already been discussed, the *Wuzhen Pian* should be readily comprehended, and thus will not be further expounded. Today’s conclusion marks the end of this lecture series. Last year’s opening coincided with the winter solstice, and today’s closing falls on the Buddha’s Enlightenment Day—both occasions arising from the convergence of human affairs and celestial timing. Only afterward did I realize that the lectures began on the winter solstice, and now conclude on the Buddha’s Enlightenment Day, a felicitous alignment unanticipated beforehand. This reveals the workings of Heaven’s intent, urging us to seize the moment with resolve.

Today, I shall briefly distinguish between the Celestial Origin (tianyuan) and Human Origin (renyuan) methods, as well as the ease and difficulty of cultivating the higher and lower virtues, thereby bringing these lectures to a close.

The contents of the Celestial and Human Origin elixir methods have already been addressed above. Li Xianhanxu’s *Nine Layers of Heart Refinement Discourse* describes the Human Origin method, which requires the practitioner to forcibly apply personal intent in cultivation, gradually refining it into true intent. In the "Second Layer of Heart Refinement," it is said that postnatal humans, burdened by excessive thoughts, cannot directly enter profound stillness or penetrate the mysterious aperture. The method lies in wielding the sword and forming the mudra, using one’s intent to dissolve awareness. When yang arises and solidifies, intent must guide it to the coccyx, ascending to the Niwan Palace.

The Human Origin method begins with intent guiding the process. At the outset, one focuses on the point one *cun* and three *fen* below the navel, directing the breath there with inhalation and exhalation. Thus it is said: *"It halts behind the navel hall, before the Gate of Life, within a circle of emptiness."* And again: *"My heart rests here, and I inwardly observe it."* On the in-breath, intent directs it inward; on the out-breath, intent welcomes it, halting at the external point one *cun* and three *fen* below the navel. Once the internal qi congeals behind the navel, the exhalation is further directed outward through the nose, stabilizing externally. This "guiding of intent with inhalation and exhalation" first unites the mundane intent and breath of the postnatal. Once united, they merge into primordial chaos, then circulate within the bounds of the compass and square. The "compass and square" refer to the Golden Crucible, while "square and circle" denote the apertures of square and round as previously discussed. After congealing, the heart-spirit stabilizes in the external emptiness beyond the nose. When yang arises, intent guides it to the coccyx, ascending to the Niwan Palace—this is the work of refining essence into qi, distinct from the Celestial Origin method.

The Celestial Origin is purely a practice of emptiness, free from arbitrary intent. While intent may arise, true intent does not. The Human Origin begins with deliberate effort, extending the postnatal destiny. Only after completing the destiny work does one cultivate the nature. Though this may seem easier initially, the later resolution of nature is far more difficult, for the source of water is impure.

Those who begin with the Human Origin must ultimately undertake nine years of wall-facing practice to refine spirit and return to emptiness, achieving true emptiness and resolving nature. Only after nine years will their nature become perfectly round and luminous. Daoists speak of beginning with the refinement of form—form being the body. Those who cultivate the Celestial Origin start with the true emptiness of form refinement, thereby attaining both nature and destiny. The Human Origin first refines form to prolong life, then cultivates destiny. After completing the Great Cycle, three years of "nursing" practice follow, after which the spirit becomes efficacious. Combined with the hundred-day foundation-building and nine years of wall-facing, the entire process requires thirteen years.

At the outset, personal intent serves as the premise, employing single-minded focus to generate transformation within, such as opening the pass and expanding the aperture—all guided by intent. Intent, the master of the postnatal physical form, is primordial essence before birth and becomes intent after birth. When intent moves, spirit, qi, and essence move with it. If intent is concentrated, the postnatal essence, qi, and spirit naturally unite—this pertains to destiny work.

As for the work of nature, nature is inherently empty. Without first returning to emptiness, one cannot perceive nature. The Human Origin first resolves destiny, then nature. Only during the three years of nursing and after nine years of wall-facing can one shatter emptiness, a sequence distinct from the Celestial Origin.

The Celestial Origin is the supreme vehicle shared by the Three Teachings, embodying *"exhausting principle and fulfilling nature to reach destiny."* Principle refers to the generative principles of Heaven and Earth’s yin and yang, born from the li-qi within the Supreme Ultimate. The character *li* (理) is interchangeable with *li* (礼, ritual), as the ancients used *li* (礼) where we now use *li* (理). Examples include Confucius inquiring about ritual from Laozi and *"restraining the self to return to ritual."* The cosmos’ generative transformations arise from the function of li-qi. Humans are conceived through the influence of li-qi. This qi is destiny in Heaven and nature in humans. Heaven bestows it as destiny; humans receive it as nature. Principle and destiny are one! Though originally an empty body, it is the root of all diversity. By single-mindedly cultivating the One, body and heart become still, inner and outer both empty. From this, a single point of li-qi may be regenerated, gathered, and sealed—thus both nature and destiny are attained. This achievement may reduce the need for three or nine years of practice.

At the outset of refining the self, one tempers the form with true emptiness. After the great celestial cycle, the spirit may depart and attain spiritual communion—this is the attainment of nature, with destiny inherent within. Thus it is said: *"Exhaust principle, fulfill nature, to reach destiny."* Nature is the li-qi within the Supreme Ultimate. To fulfill one’s nature is to fulfill the nature of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things.

The Supreme Ultimate is undifferentiated; when it moves, it generates yang—this is li-qi, the root of Heaven. When it is still, it generates yin, dividing into the Two Modes, the root of Earth. From Non-Ultimate to Supreme Ultimate, from yin-yang to the Five Phases, the myriad things are transformed and born. Therefore, to fulfill one’s own nature is to fulfill the nature of others; to fulfill the nature of others is to fulfill the nature of things; to fulfill the nature of things is to assist in Heaven and Earth’s transformative nurturance. Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are fundamentally one body, sharing the same root as the self. Mencius said: *"The myriad things are all complete within me."*

The primordial li-qi is inherently possessed by humans, but it is constrained by temperament and obscured by worldly desires, scattering midway. The practice of the Dao lies solely in recovering this scattered li-qi. Though the streams of the Three Teachings diverge in myriad ways, their source is rooted in this, and they are not different.

Buddhism speaks only of nature and not of destiny, for when nature is attained, destiny is naturally attained—this is not a matter of neglect. Yet one must not mistake the cultivation of nature for the refinement of the yin spirit. Refining the yin spirit is the nurturing of the postnatal consciousness, enabling it to attain spiritual communion. However, being yin, it remains subject to arising and ceasing; lacking the work of returning to emptiness, it does not attain the primordial nature. Those who focus single-mindedly on nurturing the consciousness without uniting it with qi achieve only the spiritual communion of the yin spirit, a result inferior to the cultivation of the Heavenly Origin. The distinction in levels must be understood.

The practice of the supreme vehicle of the primordial Dao—the method of the Heavenly Origin—begins with returning to emptiness, prioritizing the refinement of the heart. The work of refining the self is boundless, for differences in individuals’ capacities, wisdom, and mental ground lead to variations in speed. Those with deep roots and great merit find refinement easy, requiring little time; otherwise, it is difficult and protracted.

Those who practice the method of the Human Origin first engage in foundation-laying, beginning with the generation and acquisition of the medicine. By uniting one’s intention with the qi of breath, the medicine is generated—this is the postnatal medicine. All that is gathered and attained remains within the postnatal, which is the work of foundation-laying. The refinement of the self comes afterward, requiring two additional steps.

Those who practice the method of the Heavenly Origin to cultivate the primordial Dao need only the single step of refining the self; all other practices proceed and are attained naturally, without deliberate effort. The Buddhist cultivation of prajñā, the Daoist supreme vehicle, and the Confucian *"exhausting principle, fulfilling nature, to reach destiny"* are all thus, without distinction.

For those cultivating the Human Origin, the initial work is easy, but the final stage is difficult. Some practice for many years without attaining ascension, all due to insufficient cultivation of nature. The *"untrammeled, weightless"* superior elixir method described in Bai Yuchan’s *Disquisition on Delusions* refers to this Heavenly Origin great elixir. Its method is simple and may be completed in a year, transmitted through the heart-mind.

The middle-grade elixir method pertains to the Human Origin, taking three years to complete. The lower-grade elixir method can only dispel illness and does not extend life; its duration of practice is uncertain, with no guarantee of attainment. The Human Origin returns from the postnatal to the primordial, while the Heavenly Origin is purely primordial.

Since ancient times, those cultivating the Heavenly Origin have been many, while those practicing the Human Origin have been few. After the Tang and Song dynasties, the Human Origin flourished among Daoists, though Chan practitioners continued to refine the primordial, leading to mutual criticism with Buddhists. By the Yuan dynasty, Wang Chongyang’s emergence revived the Heavenly Origin Dao, its content largely aligned with Buddhism, leading some to claim the Heavenly Origin elixir method originated from Buddhism. In truth, the supreme vehicle is the same across the Three Teachings, fundamentally undivided. Thus, the principles of the Heavenly Origin resonate with Confucian classics and Buddhist scriptures, which may be consulted for mutual verification.

The Daoist scriptures handed down from antiquity all expound the Heavenly Origin, differing from the Daoist texts of the Tang and Song. The ignorant have debated their superiority, but this arises from a lack of deep inquiry. The Heavenly Origin Dao is certain to yield success, which is why Bai Yuchan asserted that this path is assured and this elixir is certain to form. As long as body and heart are still, a natural spiritual awareness will govern the firing process without error.

The Human Origin is otherwise: when false intentions are not exhausted and true intention has not arisen, the firing process requires constant vigilance against danger. Thus, it is difficult to practice and difficult to complete. Qing dynasty Daoists regarded the Heavenly Origin as pure and the Human Origin as grafting, requiring supplementation when true yang was insufficient. Those cultivating the Human Origin rely on their own heart-mind to govern the process, often forcing it. The Heavenly Origin, however, is purely natural: body and heart are still and pure, non-doing yet all-accomplishing. A single spirit and a single qi govern it, not one’s own actions.

Those cultivating the Human Origin, after obtaining the great elixir, must further attain the mysterious pearl—the pearl of nature—to complete the final work of nature. In the postscripts to the *Three Commentaries on the "Awakening to Reality"*, the *Authentic Transmission of the Golden Elixir* states that the refinement of the self follows foundation-laying.

After nursing the great elixir, one then obtains the mysterious pearl—this is the separate cultivation of nature and life, first completing life and then completing nature. As for the Way of the Heavenly Origin, to attain nature is to attain life; wherever nature is attained, life is attained there as well—these two are one. If one focuses solely on the work of the Human Origin without advancing to the Heavenly Origin, this is what Ancestor Lü called "a fool whose lifespan equals heaven’s."

Those who cultivate the Human Origin attain the five spiritual penetrations during the three-year nursing period. Those who cultivate the Heavenly Origin, however, vary in timing according to their root capacity and merit. Those of the highest root capacity may attain it even before the foundation-laying is complete; those of weaker capacity will surely attain it during the foundation-laying and warming-nurturing phase. After attaining the penetrations, one must further undergo the great celestial cycle—this is where the Heavenly Origin and Human Origin differ.

During the Qing dynasty, various schools of learning flourished, and many innovations emerged in the methods of elixir refinement. Those who achieved the elixir and became immortals were not few, leading some to regard the Heavenly Origin as the divine elixir and the Human Origin as mere alchemical practice, with the cultivation of the Heavenly Origin as true Daoist practice. They held that those of superior root capacity could cultivate it directly, while those of slightly lesser capacity must first cultivate the Human Origin before advancing to the Heavenly Origin—this was their teaching.

The Buddhist view, however, holds that all ordinary beings can cultivate the Heavenly Origin, that only the Heavenly Origin is the path of the Great Awakened Golden Immortal, and that it alone ensures one’s attainment is permanent, leading to Dao. Those who cultivate the Human Origin remain within the ranks of the ten kinds of immortals described in the *Śūraṅgama Sūtra*, still bound within the three realms and unable to transcend them. Only by learning Buddhism—becoming a Great Awakened Golden Immortal—can one transcend the three realms and remain indestructible for endless kalpas. Thus it is said, "There is only one vehicle; the other two are not true." The other two refer to the pratyekabuddhas and śrāvakas, attained through the cultivation of the Human Origin.

From the first to the seventh chapter of *Journey to the West*, Sun Wukong’s cultivation is that of the Heavenly Origin. From the Mountain of Two Frontiers to the end, Tang Sanzang’s cultivation is that of the Human Origin advancing to the Heavenly Origin—far more difficult than Sun Wukong’s. Only after passing the Flaming Mountains can deluded intent return to true intent; only after subduing the rhinoceros demon can nature be completed. The ninety-nine trials arise from errors in the firing process, making success all the more difficult. Thus, the cultivation of the Heavenly Origin is sudden and complete, while that of the Human Origin is gradual—another point of difference between the two.

The method of cultivating the Heavenly Origin begins with emptying one’s nature, that is, the emptiness of the two natures. For nature is divided into good nature and non-good nature. The Buddha taught that "natures are also the same"—these are all postnatal natures. The prenatal nature is the original ancestral nature; only by emptying the postnatal nature can one attain the prenatal nature. The original ancestral nature is true awakening.

Those who cultivate the Human Origin first eliminate the non-good nature, yet still retain the good nature. Only after nine years of wall-gazing do they eliminate the good nature, achieving the emptiness of the two natures and thus true awakening. The Buddha spoke of the "mind-generated body," a term of vast scope. Our physical form is a "mind-generated" body. When male and female unite, a single point of intent arises—this is emotion and thought. As emotion and thought interact, they attract external principle-qi to the body, giving rise to a human form. When the two emotions reach the peak of joy, thought dissolves into formlessness, forming a purely yin body—this is true intent. This intent can stir the purely yang qi, condensing it into an embryo. This is the ordinary process of human birth. Those who cultivate the Dao reverse this process to become immortals, attaining the dharmakāya. The circumstances are broadly similar, differing only in whether one cultivates the Human or Heavenly Origin.

Those who cultivate the Human Origin rely primarily on focused intent, using intent to guide qi to a point one inch and three fen below the navel, causing spirit and qi to merge and condense into a single mass of qi. At this point, deluded intent is exhausted, and true intent returns. Through true intent and this mass of qi, one attracts true yang to the body, condensing it into the dharmakāya—this is the "mind-generated body" of the Human Origin.

Those who cultivate the Heavenly Origin, however, take as their first step the elimination of deluded intent. Thus, by returning to emptiness, they attain true intent, which can then attract external true yang to condense into the dharmakāya. From the outset, they do not employ deluded intent, making their path more direct than that of the Human Origin—this is the "mind-generated body" of the Heavenly Origin. Thus, whether ordinary body or dharmakāya, all are generated by intent, all by true intent—this is their commonality.

Ridding oneself of deluded intent is exceedingly difficult. This deluded intent is the Bull Demon King in *Journey to the West*. The Bull Demon King was originally the great white ox of Snow Mountain, for the ox represents kun earth and also intent. When intent departs from truth, it becomes delusion, and the ox becomes a demon king. Returning delusion to truth transforms it back into the white ox. The same applies to intent: from delusion it becomes a demon, from truth it becomes a buddha. True intent turning into deluded intent is demonic; deluded intent returning to true intent is buddhahood.

To subdue the Bull Demon King, one must first encircle him with the vajras, signifying the attainment of the qian metal qi, which alone can return delusion to truth. Only by returning delusion to truth can one attain the vajra body, indestructible for endless kalpas. In *Journey to the West*, the vajras appear only once—this is the most crucial point to observe.

Nezha the Third Prince is the Great Spirit of the Three Altars and Sea Assembly, representing the spirit of consciousness within the heart. Only when the spirit of consciousness returns to the primordial spirit does true intent arise. Beheading him only to have another head grow in its place illustrates the human mind—thoughts arise ceaselessly, unsevered. His mouth spewing lotus blossoms and his crown emitting azure smoke signify that true intent remains unstained amidst the mire of delusion; even within deluded intent, shedding delusion allows a return to truth. Binding him with the demon-taming rope through his nostrils—where the nose is the central mountain of the body, earth, and intent—demonstrates the return of intent-earth to truth, thus transforming delusion back into truth. When deluded intent returns to true intent, true yin and true yang meet, enabling the acquisition of the Plantain Fan to cross the Flaming Mountains.

For those cultivating the human elixir, only after traversing the great celestial cycle does true intent emerge; refining the self and completing nature both follow the great celestial cycle. First building the foundation to obtain the elixir, then refining the self to complete nature—this sequence in human-elixir cultivation has its own rationale. Considering the rarity of obtaining a human body and encountering the great Dao, and how life is as fleeting as a candle in the wind, if one does not first build the foundation to prolong life, should death unexpectedly arrive during cultivation, one’s aspirations would go unfulfilled, leading to a life of vain dreams—how could regret not follow? Thus, one should first use techniques to extend life, then employ the Dao to refine nature. Building the foundation first stabilizes the physical form; once the foundation is secure, preserving a lifespan of one hundred and twenty years allows wholehearted cultivation to complete one’s nature. Extending life requires action, hence it is called "technique." Yet when deluded intent is not fully dispelled and true intent not yet attained, the foundation cannot be firm, for the heart’s refinement remains impure. To illustrate: encountering a beautiful woman in a secluded chamber or suddenly obtaining gold at dusk—those with weak resolve cannot remain unmoved, and their elixir foundation is ruined. Those cultivating the human elixir, prone to the flaw of haste leading to failure, may find direct cultivation of the celestial elixir more straightforward. This marks another distinction between celestial and human elixir cultivation.

For those practicing the primordial great Dao and celestial elixir methods, before refining the self, one must dispel deluded intent, returning delusion to truth so that intent-earth is attained, then expand and magnify it. Transforming ordinary breath into true breath and the spirit of consciousness into the primordial spirit naturally awakens spiritual awareness, enabling the completion of both nature and life. At the inception of prenatal destiny, there was but a single principle-qi; in the postnatal state, it divides into three and transforms into five, arising in profusion. Before the body’s birth, only principle-qi existed, and principle-qi was still a thing; before the Supreme Ultimate stirred, there was nothing—it was an empty body. Only by returning to the formless state of the Non-Ultimate does true emptiness arise. Thus, one must gather the five phases to assemble the three families, unite the three families into the two modes, return to the Supreme Ultimate, and revert to the Non-Ultimate to return to the root and restore life.

The work of building the foundation involves returning the five phases to the three families; traversing the great celestial cycle involves returning the two modes to the Supreme Ultimate; and the final return to emptiness involves reverting the Supreme Ultimate to the Non-Ultimate. This stage encompasses refining essence into qi, qi into spirit, and spirit into emptiness. For those cultivating the celestial elixir, when refining essence into qi, it includes refining spirit and qi to merge with primordial essence; when refining qi into spirit, it encompasses refining essence to merge with primordial spirit; when refining spirit into emptiness, it involves uniting essence, qi, and spirit with emptiness. Thus, the three are interconnected—one in three, three in one.

True intent is the Yellow Dame; yellow is the color of the center, and the dame embodies compassionate heart, representing kun earth and true emptiness. Through her mediation and union, the three treasures—essence, qi, and spirit—are harmonized by true intent, naturally coalescing to form the elixir. Moreover, the spirit in the heart is the sun, the qi in the body is the moon, and the central true intent is the star; with true intent present, the sun and moon naturally conjoin to form the elixir. The *Book of Changes*, a Confucian text on the Dao, combines the characters for sun and moon to form *yi* (change); Buddhism elucidates the principle of illuminating the mind and seeing nature by combining sun and moon to form *ming* (brightness), akin to Daoism’s *dan* (elixir)—all converge in union. The essence lies in merging, for union naturally achieves truth.

Yet to attain true intent, one must first return to emptiness. Laozi said: "Attain utmost emptiness, abide in profound stillness." Letting go of all entanglements, forgetting both body and mind, and persisting in practice will naturally return delusion to truth, yielding true intent. Utmost emptiness and profound stillness are interlinked—only by attaining the extreme of emptiness can one abide in profound stillness; without utmost emptiness, stillness cannot be profound. The *Great Learning* states: "Only after stabilizing can one attain stillness." If stillness is not achieved even in stability, further effort is needed to attain motionless stillness. Primordial true yang arises only through emptiness and stillness. The *Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* says: "From emptiness arises white snow; from tranquility grows the yellow sprout." Yellow is earth; from earth, the sprout arises—this is primordial true lead, born of true intent. White snow is the same substance; lead is qian metal, inherently white, as in "white qian, black kun." Being primordial and luminous, it is called "white snow." White snow arises from emptiness, the yellow sprout grows in tranquility.

When emptiness reaches its extreme and stillness becomes profound, the false returns to the true. The acquired spirit fades, and the primordial spirit emerges; spirit and qi naturally unite. The medicine is born, and the mysterious aperture likewise arises.

True intent is the natural spiritual awareness; the firing process, governed by this natural awareness, will not err. If the false intent presides, the firing process will often go awry. The methods of human-origin and celestial-origin elixir formation are largely the same; the difference lies solely in the firing process. The celestial-origin follows nature, while the human-origin involves deliberate effort. Thus, the duration of cultivation, the magnitude of attainment, and the fruition achieved differ greatly.

Yet the intent employed in the human-origin method is also an "intent without intent," not false intent. If false intent is used, it becomes deviant fire, which not only fails to achieve the Dao but may also cause illness—this, too, must be understood.

Those who cultivate the human-origin method open the passes and expand the apertures with intent; establish fetal breathing with intent; and guide the river chariot with intent. Those who cultivate the celestial-origin method proceed differently, allowing everything to unfold naturally without imposing intent. All actions—opening passes, expanding apertures, and the like—occur spontaneously.

Once refinement of the self is perfected, the foundation is firmly established, and no separate foundational work is needed. This marks another distinction between celestial-origin and human-origin cultivation.

In the pursuit of the Dao, humans are divided into those of superior virtue and those of inferior virtue: the former are those who cultivate as virginal youths, the latter those who cultivate after the body has been broken. Those of superior virtue, having never broken the body, retain primordial true essence and true qi within, possessing no transformed physical essence. Thus, their celestial authenticity is abundant, and their desires are shallow.

The acquired spirit in humans is a stray spark of the great yang, ever seeking to depart. Only primordial qi can restrain it. Since primordial qi is present to control this acquired spirit, it does not stray outward, and the seven emotions and six desires of the posterior heaven do not become excessive.

As for those of inferior virtue whose bodies have been broken, their primordial qi has already stirred and entered the Gate of Life, transforming into physical essence that constantly leaks outward. The acquired spirit, unrestrained, becomes agitated, leading to many cravings and a dull spirit.

The purpose of Dao cultivation is to restore primordial spirit and primordial qi to subdue the acquired spirit. In those of superior virtue, primordial spirit and qi remain within; when active, it is the acquired spirit, when still, the primordial spirit—far surpassing those whose bodies have been broken.

Thus, those of superior virtue may achieve the Dao in a single year, having already established a foundation. They need only return to emptiness. Daily, they remain in a state of effortless ease, allowing body and mind to move naturally, practicing non-action that accomplishes all. By aligning their own emptiness with the emptiness of heaven and earth, they can spontaneously shed the mortal coil and ascend as spirit, without needing additional effort.

Those whose bodies have been broken must first establish the foundation, then practice non-action—far more difficult than for those of superior virtue. The latter need only employ the work of "forgetting," forgetting both body and mind, entering primordial chaos. Spirit and qi become clear, returning to emptiness and merging with the undifferentiated, naturally achieving success.

On Mount Emei, there was once a thirteen-year-old girl named Song who daily stood outside her door, gazing at the sky. Through long practice, she attained the Dao. Her primordial spirit and qi remained within, merging her own spirit and qi with those of the great void, thus completing her cultivation. This was due to her great karmic capacity, having come with vows. Her actions unconsciously aligned with the Dao, cultivating without cultivating, naturally achieving the Dao.

Those who cultivate the celestial-origin great elixir restore the virginal body. After ten months of gentle nurturing, they continue with the virginal-body method of true cultivation, aligning their own emptiness with the emptiness of heaven and earth. By the time they refine the self, false thoughts and intentions have already been purified, so they need only practice returning to emptiness to succeed.

Those who cultivate the human-origin method, however, have not fully dispelled false thoughts nor fully restored true mind, and cannot proceed so directly.

For those who cultivate the celestial-origin great Dao, every moment—whether moving, dwelling, sitting, or lying down—is cultivation. By emptying the heart, the belly naturally fills. As Zhang Sanfeng said, "Play and frolic—all is cultivation." In play, they cultivate unknowingly, and the Dao is achieved.

Those of deep karmic roots may establish the foundation in just one or two years, then add the work of gentle nurturing. Choosing a secluded mountain forest, they practice the work of equanimity in stillness and forgetting. Thereafter, they focus solely on forgetting, and the Dao is naturally attained.

Bai Yuchan spoke of "achievement in one year," excluding the time spent refining the self: a hundred days to establish the foundation, ten months for the great celestial cycle—combining these makes a year, hence "achievement in one year."

Obtaining the elixir does not require much time; with perfect refinement of the self, the elixir can be obtained in an instant. Those cultivating the human-origin method, however, require thirteen years. For the celestial-origin method, only the refinement of the self is timeless; the speed of success varies with karmic capacity. The key lies in unwavering faith—sooner or later, achievement is certain.

Faith is the mother of the Dao’s source and merit. With deep, unshaken faith, even those of shallow karmic roots will one day achieve greatness—though the time required may differ slightly.

The merit of three thousand and eight hundred deeds must be fulfilled by cultivators. Yet one must not cling rigidly to method. If the aspiration to universally ferry beings is held, a single thought can fulfill the vow.

In former times, when Master Zhongli transmitted to Ancestor Lü the method of refining the earthly-origin elixir, the earthly-origin was a tangible, material alchemical practice—namely, the art of transmuting base metals into gold and silver. Ancestor Lü said, “This would harm people five hundred years hence; I shall not do it.” Master Zhongli replied, “Five hundred years from now, each will have their own karmic conditions. Though the substance may decay, it will not obstruct their affairs, and one may reclaim it in advance. Yet your single thought of not wishing to harm those five hundred years hence fulfills the three thousand and eight hundred merits within this very thought.” This serves as proof: one must not cling rigidly to a single view but must indeed accomplish the three thousand and eight hundred virtuous deeds. When the earthly-origin elixir is refined, it can transmute stone into gold and, when ingested, confer the attainment of earthly immortality. To become a celestial immortal, one must further cultivate from the stage of earthly immortality. The earthly-origin transforms tangible, material substances into intangible, immaterial ones, and moreover produces wealth; thus, only those with the requisite karmic fortune can undertake its refinement. Formerly, Ancestor Zhang transmitted this method to Shen Wansan, for Shen shared the same birth year, month, and day as the Ming sovereign Zhu Hongwu. Zhu’s destiny was to become the Son of Heaven, while Shen’s was to be the wealthiest man in the realm—hence the transmission of the earthly-origin elchemical method. When the earthly-origin elixir nears completion, mountain spirits and demonic entities often come to seize it; therefore, only those of great fortune can refine it. Even if refined, they may not succeed, and even if successful, they may not enjoy its benefits. In the cultivation of the human-origin, celestial demons may also come to disturb the practitioner, but in the celestial-origin, this does not occur. For the celestial-origin begins with the refinement of inherent nature, and when the pearl of nature is perfectly luminous, it becomes a purely yang substance, capable of subduing all yin and malevolent forces—thus, demons and spirits all conceal themselves and avoid it. Only when passing the great celestial cycle do the yin demons accumulated over one’s kalpas appear before one; yet if one remains unmoved in heart, they vanish in an instant, and all demons retreat without peril. Moreover, by this stage, one possesses natural spiritual awareness, ensuring the heart remains undisturbed and the elixir furnace unoverturned. These matters must be understood, and thus I have expounded upon them at length.

From last year to this day, I have lectured forty-nine times in all—a perfect seven sets of seven—fittingly accordant with the principles of creation. Here, I conclude these teachings. May scholars each exert themselves diligently in their cultivation.

*—The Direct Exposition of the Unitary Celestial Mechanism* concludes.
